Purpose of the Software Engineering &
Architecture Guide
Goals
One of Capgemini’s core competences is our expertise in the design of good software architectures.
The definition and introduction of Quasar — the quality software architecture from
Capgemini — represented the first guidelines for the design and implementation of applications in
software projects. Its successor, the Architecture Guidelines for Application Design, addressed a
number of architecture-related topics in more detail. In the past decade, however, we have seen a
rapid increase in various new modern architectural styles, design methodologies, and technology
options. Therefore, this guide will now evolve into a Wiki of architecture guidelines rather than a
consistent document released in versions. It builds on those given foundations, many of which are
still applied with success in our projects, adapts them where necessary, and enriches them where
useful.
In these guidelines, we provide introductions and comments from a Capgemini point-of-view,
which may help architects find appropriate solutions for recurring problems in the design of
applications, both large and small. This will allow architects to concentrate more specifically on the
particular challenges posed by the application in question. At the same time, such guidelines
provide criteria that can be used to evaluate the quality of an architecture.
The rules and comments on solutions presented here are intended as guidance. They should not be
used or enforced automatically. Under no circumstances can they replace the work of the architect,
who is always responsible for making the difficult choices within the project’s specific context and
constraints. This is because the design of a viable technical or logical architecture is always a
creative process that defies automation, and one for which the experience and expertise of
Capgemini’s architects is a vital requirement! It depends on the specific customer needs, and our
ambition should always be to fulfill these needs.
Target Audience
The target audience for these guidelines are our delivery architects and software engineers,
especially in the context of our C&CA practice. However, it will also be a useful resource for our
business and enterprise architects to understand in more detail how we design software
architectures.
Relation to other Standards
iSAQB Certified Professional Software Architecture
The iSAQB CPSA certification is a well-known standard most prominently in the EMEA region. It
closely aligns with our architecture competence and own methodologies. Therefore, we see a good
fit to our own point-of-view on software architecture. In particular, we don’t see a substantial need
to diverge from the terms and architecture guidelines provided by it. Instead, we seek to extend this
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Goals | 1
standard where it adds real value.
TOGAF / IAF
We see TOGAF and IAF as more relevant in the field of enterprise architecture. As this guide is
focussed on custom software architecture, it can be seen as complementary to these more high-
level standards.
Usage of the Guide
Directions for use of the SEAGuide in customer contexts (Copyright and
Intellectual Property)
The SEAGuide consolidates Capgemini’s experience on designing mission-critical systems. It
contains concepts that refer to publicly available knowledge/standards and that only deliver value
when applied in a specific context. When using the SEAGuide, please consider and ensure the
following:
• Copyright The contents of SEAGuide are generally copyright protected, which should be stated
when copying text from the Guide: “Software engineering and architecture guide (SEAGuide) -
Copyright © <current year> Capgemini. All rights reserved.”
• No Exclusive Rights When using the SEAGuide or its contents for customers, you need to be
clear in the customer specific contract that Capgemini cannot and does not grant exclusive
rights to the SEAGuide and its contents. If we have a contract that generally demands exclusive
right for the customer for the Capgemini services and works, you are not allowed to copy texts
from the SEAGuide. In this context you need to create new customer specific deliverables from
scratch, but of course you can use the knowledge/concepts from the SEAGuide to do so.
• Quality Assurance When sharing texts with customers, suppliers, business partners or other
third parties, you need to ensure the quality of the parts you share. Please check and confirm
that our efforts to ensure quality in terms of correctness, understandability and correct
referencing of external sources are still in place and up to date. In particular, we don’t copy
public text to the SEAGuide, but rather reference it. Still, we cannot guarantee the level needed
to directly copy all contents without your review before sharing it externally. This is up to you.
• Local law The statements are based on German law. When using the SEAGuide in other
countries, local law needs to be considered, inter alia regarding the protection of copyright.
Assuming the directions above are followed, you can:
• Use the SEAGuide to demonstrate our capabilities to customers
◦ Reference the SEAGuide, e.g. use the OnePager
◦ Create an excerpt and share it with customers
• Use the SEAGuide to design software systems and systems landscapes
◦ Refer to the concepts as our Capgemini expertise, copying or restating it in the specific
design context
◦ Design and evaluate customer-specific solutions based on the SEAGuide
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Usage of the Guide | 2
• Use the SEAGuide when setting up architecture guidelines on customer side
◦ Use parts for customer internal documentation
Understanding and using the guides' content
The general structure of the guide is oriented towards the Arc42 standard for architecture
documentation.
Since this standard is gaining more acceptance within our customer base, structuring the guide
accordingly offers guidance where it is needed for the architecture work being done. The first
section lays the foundations for the objectives and principles of our architectures. The core part will
be architectural patterns, which will help you during the design of the software. We design
software from different perspectives and addressing different aspects of the architecture. Arc42
proposes four different views to present the results of these design activities. We therefore organize
our patterns along those perspectives where they are applied:
• Context patterns
• Building block patterns
• Runtime patterns
• Deployment patterns
The crosscutting concepts will highlight best practices on solutions across multiple building blocks.
Last but not least, articles on quality assurance will provide guidance on how to ensure the quality
of our custom applications.
The overviews in the different sections will try to give a complete picture, listing
 also possible future topics not yet covered in the guide. Please take this as an
invitation to contribute to the guide in case you have any kind of input on these
topics (see below).
Separate to this structure, additional pathways are provided through the guide for specific usage
scenarios (e.g. bid support for specific contexts, relevant content for a training, setting up a new
project, project transformations, etc.)
Contributing to the Guide
We encourage and welcome everyone to contribute to the guide! If you are interested, please send a
request to the following distribution list:
DL DE seaGuide - Admins
We will then provide you with a user account for you to access and contribute to the guide. The
contribution will be moderated and quality assured by the CTO office.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Contributing to the Guide | 3
Architectural objectives
Software is built to solve problems in a functional domain. The solution for these problems is
expressed in terms of requirements that describe the needed functionality. In addition, the software
needs to meet certain quality goals and characteristics. Both the functional requirements and
quality criteria (sometimes also called "non-functional requirements" or short NFRs) differ from
project to project. Therefore we need to establish the architectural objectives for our software
upfront for every new project individually.
Fundamental considerations to validate every choice
during your work
The following two very basic principles should guide you to pick wisely on whatever kind of choice
you make. They build the foundation for everything else you or the team will do or decide during
the architecture and implementation work.
So, always ask yourself the two check questions:
• Is my choice suitable and appropriate to meet the goals/objectives?
• It the solution simple enough (and not unnecessarily over-complex)?
The following sections elaborate further on these questions.
Suitability
Project-specific requirements demand suitable or appropriate architectures. The question of when
an architecture is particularly suitable or appropriate cannot be answered by simply counting the
applied principles and rules. An optimal architecture for a project is characterized to a far greater
extent by the fact that, overall, it implements the requirements placed on it in the best possible way
given the constraints. It is the architect’s goal to achieve this. In the case of competing
requirements, in particular, the architect must weigh the trade-offs involved.

Always use all the architectural principles and rules appropriately.
Example 1. example for suitability
The requirements placed on the software we develop are often similar: custom software
systems should have the longest possible lifetimes and they must therefore be easy to
maintain, for example. However, this requirement should not be generalized. In a mini-project
for "disposable software" which is only intended to be used once, it would be a design error to
pay particular attention to ease of maintenance at the expense of the more important
objectives such as budget and deadline.
Simplicity
One of the important design objectives is embodied by the KISS principle. KISS stands for "Keep it
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Fundamental considerations to validate every choice during your
work | 4
small and simple" and is thought to have originated in NASA’s Apollo project. As far as software
architecture is concerned, it means reducing the complexity of the systems to a minimum and
ensuring that the employed structures are clear, simple and traceable.
Design the software system as simply, clearly and easy-to-understand as possible at

all levels. If there are two ways to solve a problem, all other things being equal,
choose the simpler.
Setting and documenting Architectural Objectives is
important
The primary purpose of defining and documenting architectural objectives is to ensure that the
critical quality aspects relevant to your software receive focused attention and are met.
Typically these quality objectives are specified in terms of quality characteristics (such as efficiency,
maintainability, or security). In most of our projects a large variety of these quality attributes need
to be fulfilled at the same time. Unfortunately they are in parts mutually opposing to each other.
Fore example, introducing multi-factor authentication to a login page or requiring a VPN
connection in order to access a system improves security, but degrades user experience and
satisfaction.
The functional requirements, on the other hand, could theoretically also be implemented without a
good architecture. It is important to highlight the dependency of the architecture on the quality
characteristics, because in essence a good architecture ensures these attributes, but does not
impose them on the software unnecessarily (see Fundamental considerations to validate every
choice during your work). The architecture needs to aim for the software to be economically
manufacturable. This means that it should ensure that the software can actually be built within the
existing budget constraints.
Architectural objectives build the foundation for all of our architecture work that will follow.
Therefore, it is absolutely crucial to discuss and establish architecture objectives at the very
beginning of a project. The architecture objectives provide the guardrails to validate and assess
each subsequent decision. Thus, architecture objectives provide the basis for the architecture
principles that give fundamental design rules and are discussed in the following main section.
Additionally, architecture objectives form the starting point to review an architecture. On top of the
architecture principles the architecture itself is designed and build. However, we need to check all
our architecture decisions against the principles and (transitively) the objectives. (For more
information on architecture decisions cf. Architecture Documentation and Communication on
"Architecture Decision Records", ADR).
The following drawing depicts the "stack" that forms the basis of an architecture and
implementation:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Setting and documenting Architectural Objectives is important | 5
Figure 1. How fundamental considerations, architecture objectives, principles, and decisions stack on each
other
That means:
 Establish architecture objectives at the very beginning of a project and document
them explicitly.
If your settled objectives do not seem appropriate anymore and need a rework: Tackle the rework,
align them, document them, and communicate — again!
When documenting, don’t just write "maintainability is important". Elaborate more on what you
have in mind and why. Like:
Example 2. explain your thoughts and document more than a mere (half-)sentence.
maintainability is important
The software will live several years and will experience constant incremental (agile)
enhancements over the time. Also the maintainance will be done by a far-shore team with
rather junior developers. Therefore high maintainability of the code is important and the code
must be well documented and written in such a way that junior developers are able to
understand and maintain it later on.
More on architecture documentation can be found in the chapter about Architecture
Documentation and Communication. Especially at an early stage when the architectural objectives
are discussed and documented, it’s recommended to have a look at the Architecture Inception
Canvas which captures the most important information of an architecture at an early stage.
Stakeholders for architectural objectives
Three major stakeholders have to be involved in formulating the architectural objectives and
finding agreement: The architects themselves, the engagement manager, and the client; the latter
since the software’s architecture builds the foundation for providing value to the client. So align
them within your engagement. Also the customer has to agree because of their fundamental
importance.
Whatever you decide: Don’t treat the objectives as a state secret! On the contrary: Document them
openly and also communicate them to the whole team. They will fundamentally affect and
influence your whole system, so everybody has to be aware of them.
However, maybe not all of these can be discussed openly with the client. Not all goals and all set
ups are good for 100% openness: For example, if you are on a fixed price project, stating openly to
the customer that you want to make as much profit as possible might not be the best strategic
choice. So open your eyes and chose and wisely how and what you communicate to whom. But be
as open as possible.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Setting and documenting Architectural Objectives is important | 6
Where to find input for architecture objectives?
Fortunately somebody took the task to identify a catalog of quality characteristics for you. So your
first look should be to ISO 25010:
ISO 25010 is the standard that defines quality in software products: "The
quality model is the cornerstone of a product quality evaluation system. The
quality model determines which quality characteristics will be taken into
account when evaluating the properties of a software product."
— ISO 25010
The ISO 25010 standard is part of ISO 25000: System and Software Quality Requirements and
Evaluation (SQuaRE). Most of the time you can chose the relevant characteristics for your software,
be happy and walk.
However, be aware that there can be additional important drivers for your software not captured
by ISO 25010. Architectural objectives, such as achieving a short time to market for the MVP, cost-
effectiveness within a tight budget, suitability for multi-shore implementation, or creating a
throwaway prototype, can also serve as primary goals for your project. As stated before, it is always
a good idea to talk to your engagement manager about them and to discuss them jointly.
Limit the number of objectives to a manageable size
Limit yourself to a handful of architectural objectives, e.g. 3 to max 7. Thus, you have a manageable
size of priorities. Sometimes it is very hard to limit yourself (or the customer) to such a seemingly
low number. The higher your number of goals is, the less guidance you will get when later
validating a design/architecture decision against your foundations (=architecture objectives). Your
decisions become more difficult if not impossible to match: With an increasing number of
objectives they will be more and more mutually contradictory. Additional restricting factors are
time and budget. You will be forced to focus and prioritize your objectives to make it work with the
given constraints.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Setting and documenting Architectural Objectives is important | 7
Tip how to keep the number of objectives limited
Do a small workshop or brainstorming session with your relevant stakeholders:
1. agree on a max number of objectives upfront — e.g. 5 (don’t start too high!)
2. participants may add new objectives to the list as long as your limit has not
been reached.
 3. when somebody proposes a the 6th objective:
a. discuss if that one is more important than the others on the list and which
of the existing suggestions should be replaced instead. (If it’s not black or
white: maybe a slight rewording of an existing objective can "merge" both
ideas?)
b. Update your list accordingly
4. continue until there are no new suggestions and hopefully, everyone is happy
and agrees
LASR (Lightweight Approach for Software Reviews) provides some material (German only) to
identify architecture objectives on their home page as a card deck: Playcards for Quality Goals.
More on LASR can be found in the Architecture Reviews section.
Further information
• ISO 25010
• LASR-Reviews - Leightweitht Approach for Software Reviews (German Only) — here especially
the "Support Material": Playcards for Quality Goals
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Further information | 8
Architectural Principles
Introduction
You can find a lot of definitions on software architecture. When talking about software architecture
and architectural decisions, we focus on the quality of the resulting software product.
Definition: Software Architecture
Software Architecture is about designing and structuring a system so that it meets quality
expectations.
Since quality is an important goal and thus architecture is an important task, we share Ralph
Johnson’s definition (quoted by Martin Fowler):
"Software Architecture is about the important stuff. Whatever that is."
— Ralph Johnson
Our custom applications on the other hand have a common surrounding that impacts good
software architecture, which (usually) is:
• Mission-critical systems
• Long-term use and frequent adaptation to (new) business models
• Global/distributed organizations
• Large user base
• Critical data
• Central backend-systems, need for integration
• Large, global, and changing project teams
Along these common challenges, we have found: Good software architectures are always
characterized by the same attributes, and we have collected these in our architecture principles.
The 11 Principles of Software Architecture
Good software architectures are always characterized by the same attributes. The concrete
identification of architectural principles should help architects to use these characteristics
consciously in their work and thus provides an orientation for discussing architectural aspects and
finally making decisions. Thus, you may think of these principles as a heuristic: A heuristic is a
guideline that provides direction and a shortcut towards (at least) a good solution. It is important to
understand principles as general quality guidelines that need discussion and involve trade-offs in
the project’s specific context.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Introduction | 9
Definition: Principle of Software Architecture
An architectural principle is a guideline for the design of an application landscape, an
application or, more generally, a "building block".
Principles can be applied irrespective of the technology employed, the business logic or the
size of the software in question. Architectural principles are elementary in that they reflect
only one of the structural characteristics required for thoughtful architectures.
An architectural principle is not a "must" and should not be blindly applied. A good architecture
can only be created by applying principles sensibly and appropriately.
Finally, architectural principles can be used at all levels of software design: They apply to
application landscapes, entire applications, and, at a smaller scale, even to the internal structure of
a system. Therefore, we use the term "building block", which can be any structural level considered
in software architecture, be it a component, a service, a class or other types.
Architectural principles are formulated at a high level of abstraction. When applied to the concrete
situation facing the architect, they provide direction by reducing the scope of possible solutions,
and consequently make it easier to develop a thoughtful architecture that is appropriate for the
required task.
Below, we indicate the architectural principles that we use in Capgemini:
First-level principles
1. Separation of Concerns
2. Minimize dependencies
3. Dependency inversion principle
4. Information hiding
5. Homogeneity
6. Control redundancy
Second-level principles
7. Different software categories (A/T)
8. Layering
9. Design by contract
10. Data sovereignty (CQRS)
11. Re-use
The first six of these principles are particularly important since the other principles can be derived
from them. We therefore refer to these as first-level principles. The other principles build on these
first-level principles and represent a more concrete expression of them. The sections below define
and explain these principles.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini The 11 Principles of Software Architecture | 10
Separation of Concerns
Architectural Principle: Separation of Concerns
Subdivide the system into building blocks: Every building block is intended to perform
precisely one task. This task is also only performed by this building block. The task must be
defined precisely and clearly, and the distinction from other building blocks must be clearly
identifiable.
The separation of concerns makes a system easier to understand. It comprises both business logic
aspects (order management, invoicing, after-sales) and technical aspects (GUI framework interface,
specific middleware characteristics, database interface) and, more generally, the separation of
business logic and technical considerations.
The Single responsibility principle "A class should have one, and only one, reason to change", coined
by Robert C. Martin as the first of the SOLID principles for object-oriented software development, is
commonly interpreted as an application of Separation of concerns on a lower level of abstraction.
Minimize Dependencies
Architectural Principle: Minimize Dependencies
Minimize the dependencies between building blocks, e.g. by combining building blocks or
decoupling elements. Avoid cyclical dependencies.
The content of a building block must exhibit strong internal cohesion. In contrast, the coupling
between the individual building blocks should be loose — the principle is therefore also known as
"Strong Cohesion & Loose Coupling".
Minimizing dependencies therefore reduces the complexity of the application. By combining this
principle with the principle of separation of concerns, it is possible to organize a project team and
roadmap along the resulting elements and their dependencies. A high level of dependency between
building blocks is reduced by breaking down the elements in a different way — either by imposing
strong cohesion or loose coupling.
In addition to loose coupling, avoiding cyclic dependencies is needed for maintainability: It is much
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini The 11 Principles of Software Architecture | 11
easer for humans to understand hierarchical dependencies than cyclic ones. See also the
dissertation from Carola Lilienthal.
Dependency Inversion Principle
Definition: Dependency Inversion Principle
The fourth of the SOLID principles for object-oriented software development coined by
Robert C. Martin:
1. High-level modules should not depend on low-level modules. Both should depend on
abstractions.
2. Abstractions should not depend on details. Details should depend on abstractions.
The dependency inversion principle is a design principle that states that high-level modules should
depend on abstractions rather than concrete implementations. In the same way, low-level modules
should depend on the abstraction and not the other way around. Therefore, the name may be
somewhat confusing, as the dependency between two modules is not simply inverted, but split.
Applying this principle helps to decouple the high- and low-level modules, making it easier to reuse
modules and to change them without affecting other modules.
High-level module High-level module
Abstraction
Low-level module Low-level module
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini The 11 Principles of Software Architecture | 12
Information Hiding
Architectural Principle: Hide Internal Data and Business Logic
Encapsulate the internal knowledge in a building block and do not reveal it to other building
blocks. The users of the building block need only know what is strictly necessary for them or
obtain a special view of the functionality in the building block which is used by them. The
users don’t have to care about internal logic.
The principle of information hiding extends the principle of separation of concerns by adding the
requirement that a building block should hide as much internal knowledge as possible from other
building blocks. This internal knowledge includes the data used by the building block as well as the
corresponding structures, flows, and internal states. The principle of information hiding therefore
also supports the principle that requires the minimization of dependencies. The users of a building
block have only a restricted view of this element (the what), but they should not be concerned
about the implementation details (the why and how).
Homogeneity
Architectural Principle: Similar Problem, Similar Solution
Use similar solutions to solve similar problems and use similar dimensions and units within
one and the same structural level.
The building blocks at the same structural level should have similar dimensions, and the internal
structuring of building blocks should follow a similar design. In practice, this means that once
design decisions have been made, these should be pursued when similar cases arise. It does not
mean, however, to blindly repeat patterns without regard to the specific needs. But if there are
similar needs, similar solutions should be used.
The principle of homogeneity makes it possible to respond to certain design questions on the basis
of decisions made in the past. It makes it easier for (new) people in the team to understand the
architecture of an area when they are able to recognize the used patterns. Disregard for this
principle leads to systems that are difficult to understand since design decisions for new problem
situations become increasingly difficult to make.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini The 11 Principles of Software Architecture | 13
Control Redundancy
Architectural Principle: Don’t Repeat Yourself (without need)
Implement identical logical functionality that has the same business owners in one building
block (service, component). Identify and isolate shared functionality in building blocks so that
it can be reused easily by other building blocks.
For example, certain algorithms, data types, and entities that appear in different building blocks
can be relocated to their own building block to be used jointly by the original building blocks.
It is key to be very detailed when evaluating the potential for reuse and the reason to use it in
different building blocks. The following two examples evaluate the pros and cons of reuse:
An algorithm that is defined and owned by a certain business department and that needs to be used
by several other ones is a good candidate to implement only once and access for reuse. Example:
The algorithm to calculate taxes when purchasing a new vehicle can be implemented once and
used from different services in quoting and ordering process. Due to legal constraints, there is no
room for individual derivations, deadlines for implementation are usually given.
Master data that has a common core but which, depending on the functionality, needs additional
data for this functionality might be "redundant". Example: Master data for a sales organization
might be defined by one responsible department and maintained/distributed through a central
service. Consuming services might need to again implement functionality to add data to that
organization (e.g. discount agreements in pricing, delivery locations in distribution).
Trade-offs need to cover aspects of cost, speed, responsibility, and legal obligations (e.g. data/logic
must be available on a certain date).
This principle of controlled redundancy is also known as DRY - Don’t repeat yourself.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini The 11 Principles of Software Architecture | 14
Differentiate Software Categories (A/T)
Architectural Principle: Differentiate Software Categories
Assign precisely one software category to each building block. Here, we distinguish between
the two fundamental software categories:
A-software: The building block implements application and business logic requirements and
possesses no knowledge of the technical characteristics of the system.
T-software: The building block shapes the technical character of the system. It possesses no
knowledge of the logical requirements to be implemented.
The principle of differentiating between software categories represents a concrete expression of
the principle of separation of concerns (Separation of Concerns). Software components which can
be assigned to both software categories should be avoided, as a mix will lead to a drop in
maintainability and portability. This architectural principle has been extremely characteristic for
Capgemini in the past and differentiated our architecture from that of our competitors and other
products. In the meantime, driven by DDD and hexagonal architectures, the industry has begun to
pick up similar concepts.
Logic (A) Logic (A)
Technical (T) Technical (T)
General (0)
General (0)
Layering
Architectural Principle: Arrange Into Layers
Layering is an architectural principle helping to manage dependencies of building blocks. It
assigns each building block to exactly one layer. The layers are arranged vertically with the
restriction, that building blocks may only depend upon building blocks of its own layer and of
lower-level layers.
An essential task of architecture is to manage dependencies. Layering is an easy, helpful and often
applied principle to simplify the management of dependencies. Using layering as described above,
one can easily avoid cyclic dependencies spanning layers since dependencies "going up" are strictly
forbidden.
In a strict layering, a dependency is not allowed to "skip" a layer, so that building blocks may only
depend upon building blocks of its own layer and of the ony layer below its own.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini The 11 Principles of Software Architecture | 15
There are two flavors of layerings corresponding to the two flavors of dependencies: static and
dynamic. In a static layering, only static dependencies are relevant. In Java this would be the
import dependencies. This flavor would allow for calls "going up" which is e.g. necessary to
implement callbacks for a reactive programming style. In a dynamic layering, the relevant
dependencies are the call relations. For this kind of layering, if the layers are physically separated,
they are called tiers.
An additional benefit of layering is its support of separation of concerns and homogeneity:
Layering often leads to typing the building blocks where layers and types can be mapped one-to-
one. The types help to implement the principle of separation of concerns at the functional level as
they facilitate to decide, for example, which buildings blocks perform certain checks or
transformations. They also help to implement the principle of homogeneity as building blocks of
the same type have similar structure. This can be enforced by applying a layer supertype.
For examples of layers, see
• Layered architecture ,
• multitier architecture,
• service layer,
• section "Layered Architecture" in Domain-Driven Design, and
• section "Kopplung gemäß Kategorien" of chapter 5.4.4 in Quasar Enterprise.
S1 E3
E4 Service S1 S2
S2 Process P2 P1 P3
P2 P1
Entities E1 E2 E3 E4
E1 E2 P3
Design by Contract
Architectural Principle: Design by Contract (API-Driven Design)
Fully describe the system in terms of a set of interface contracts.
To assist in the understanding of this architectural principle, we first define the term "interface
contract":
Interface Contract
The interface contract is a contract between the provider and the consumer: It defines
everything that the consumer needs to know in order to make use of the provider functionality
described in the interface contract (in particular, what rules have to be observed). This
definition comprises the syntax, the associated semantics as well as non-functional properties.
Interface contracts should be drafted in such a way that they are as specific as necessary and as
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini The 11 Principles of Software Architecture | 16
general as possible. An interface contract is good if its implementation can be replaced.
Even though interface contracts should provide the most precise information possible concerning
functionality and utilization — i.e. expected prerequisites, guaranteed conditions after
implementation, errors — they should also disclose as little detail as possible concerning the
implementation of the functions which they describe (in accordance with the principle of
information hiding, see section Information Hiding). Interface contracts make the implementations
of building blocks replaceable as consumers make themselves dependent on the interface contract
and not a specific implementation. In the software development field, the use of interface contracts
ensures that individual parts of a system can be implemented and tested independently of one
another.
Contract
Data Sovereignty (CQRS)
Architectural Principle: Data Sovereignty
Make sure that exactly one building block is responsible for each element in the data
resources. This building block defines the structure and modeling of a section of the data
resources and ensures the integrity and consistency of this section when the data is accessed.
Data sovereignty defines one owner in the sense of one writing building block, which is responsible
for current and correct data. It is related to the pattern "Command and Query Responsibility
Segregation" (CQRS). Write operations should only be possible via the interface contracts belonging
to the building block which possesses data sovereignty over the associated section of the data
resources. Read operations might be solved with interface contracts or — in distributed
systems — by distributing data to other building blocks (events). In the latter case, eventual
consistency has to be considered in the discussion to balance cost, flexibility, and speed in
development with consistency needs.
Data sovereignty is a direct consequence of the principles of information hiding and separation of
concerns. It is of crucial importance to ensure that dependencies between building blocks can be
minimized. It is also essential to guarantee integrity and consistency in order to maintain the
serviceability and operability of a system.
So in short: Data sovereignty cares about "who owns and changes data" while allowing distribution
and local data storage for read access and extension of data for local needs.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini The 11 Principles of Software Architecture | 17
Re-use
Architectural Principle: Re-use Existing Solutions, Patterns, Reference
Architectures
Use existing (standard) solutions, design patterns, and reference architectures when
implementing the system’s functionality. Find reference solutions for the typical logical and
technical problems posed by the application and make sure that these are used.
On the one hand, the principles of re-use mean that it is preferable to use existing solutions to solve
certain problems rather than develop new solutions. On the other hand, it requires architects to
design reference solutions for the typical technical and logical problems that occur within a project
and make sure that these are used. Experience shows that the re-use of logical solutions is rarely
possible in the field of custom software. Examples of re-usable technical building blocks include
design patterns and reference architectures as well as technical libraries and services.
How Architecture Principles Support Software Quality
(ISO 25010)
Overview
Our architecture principles provide guidance on the major aspects to consider and decide on while
architecting software. The overall goal, of course, is to achieve a high quality, yet adequate product.
This article maps our architecture principles to the ISO 25010 standard in order to make that
support for quality clear.
ISO 25010 is the standard that defines quality in software products: "The quality model is the
cornerstone of a product quality evaluation system. The quality model determines which quality
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How Architecture Principles Support Software Quality (ISO 25010) |
18
characteristics will be taken into account when evaluating the properties of a software product."
This ISO standard is part of ISO 25000: System and Software Quality Requirements and Evaluation
(SQuaRE).
Mapping our Principles to ISO 25010
The following figure illustrates the architecture principles that are relevant for each of the quality
goals defined in the ISO 25010 standard:
ISO 25010 Aspects
Principles Functional Performance Compatibility Usability Reliability Security Maintainability Portability
Suitablility Efficiency
P1 Separation of concerns + + + + +
P2 Minimization of dependencies + + + +
P3 Information Hiding - + + + +
P4 Dependency inversion + +
P5 Homogeneity + +
P6 Zero redundancy + + + +
P7 Software categories + + + + +
P8 Layering - + + +
P9 Design-by-contract - + + + + +
P10 Data Souvereignty - + + + +
P11 Re-use + + +
Figure 2. Support for the ISO 25010 quality characteristics through the individual architectural principles
The table roughly indicates the relation between a principle and a quality goal: A "+" indicates a
positive supporting relation; a "-" indicates that a principle has a negative impact on the quality
goal; and an empty field indicates that we don’t see clear relationship, neither positive nor
negative.
As you can see, there is mainly one quality goal - Performance Efficiency - that is negatively
impacted by some principles. This observation reflects the trade-off that is inherent in the different
quality goals: To achieve maintainability, reliability, portability and (partly) security, concepts used
in software design put additional code in the software that needs to be executed. In the end, the
visible (negative) effects show that we need to trade-off quality goals in our software design.
Furthermore, it is evident that not all aspects of software quality are covered to the same extend by
these principles:
• Aspects "functional suitability" and "usability" are subject to the domain of business analysis
and thus not covered in this guide.
• Aspect "security" is covered in more detail in an own section of the architecture guide,
highlighting the specific principles relevant to security.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How Architecture Principles Support Software Quality (ISO 25010) |
19
• Aspects "performance efficiency" and "compatibility" are partly supported by our principles,
but need also further consideration on coding level. Also, a high level of efficiency may often be
incompatible with the quest for an easy-to-maintain architecture.
• Aspects "reliability", "maintainability" and "portability" are strongly supported by our
principles.
The exaggerated use of the architectural principles and the other rules in this document may result
in the over-complication of simple things and thus lead to unsuitable designs. Therefore we used
the two major objectives of simplicity and suitability to make clear that applying principles should
always be done having the objectives in mind.
Architecture Trade-offs
Introduction
Architecture trade-offs summarize major decisions needed to find a good software architecture.
Usually, decisions are needed when there are competing goals, either between quality attributes
and project constraints or between two different quality attributes.
Typical Trade-offs
In this section, you can find trade-offs that you might frequently face in your project so that you are
aware of the range of decisions that need to be made and enable you to discuss them properly in
order to gain alignment.
Flexibility vs. Cost
When designing a system for flexibility, this flexibility comes at a cost. This cost might come from
high-level architectural decision such as a microservices architecture (MSA) with intended
redundancies, but also from low-level architecture with broad use of flexible configuration abilities
vs. hardcoding. You should run a proper cost-benefit analysis for major architectural decisions in
this area to clearly explain the cost effects of flexibility to enable the customer to make a well-
informed and well-founded decision.
Complexity/Suitability vs. Cost
As we mainly architect systems for large organizations, these organizations might have purchased
products that need to be used for developing, deploying or operating software. To make use of these
products and achieve the targeted savings, we often have to consider certain products as required
by our customer. As a consequence, we frequently face either increasing complexity of the solution
or even negative impact on requirements due to such constraints. Even though it will be hard to
change these constraints, you should always make the customer aware of their impacts to enable
them for other decisions.
Future Proof vs. You Ain’t Gonna Need It
This trade-off can apply to high-level architecture decisions, such as data volumes and scaling.
While we could assume a certain growth of our customer and try to design for this scenario, we
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Trade-offs | 20
should never overfulfill the non-functional requirements. If there is a need to re-discuss those
requirements, they should be adjusted. In a low-level architecture case, this trade-off often is part
of discussing hard-coding vs. system configuration vs. user configuration. This trade-off usually
needs to be discussed with the customer.
Homogeneity vs. Suitability
While too little homogeneity makes it difficult to understand the system and its architecture, too
much homogeneity might lead to patterns that do really serve the problem. It is important to have
standards, still you should reflect if their usage will create major complexity or even limit the
functionality.
Reuse vs. Innovation
Reuse of common solutions is one of our architecture principles needed to ensure consistency and,
by extension, maintainability in our system architectures. As architects we need to carefully revise
these used solutions along with known issues in regards to new and better options. Even though we
might find a lot of possible improvements, we need to limit ourselves to improvements with a very
high benefit, as changing a solution always comes with the cost of redesign or increased complexity.
Structured Trade-off Analysis through architecture reviews
To tackle the trade-offs of software architecture in relation to quality characteristics and/or
architecture objectives, we recommend to conduct an architecture review. There are multiple
methods and tools to do so. For further information see the Architecture Reviews article within the
SEAGuide.
Capgemini Assets
Further Information
The following articles will help you on the mentioned tasks, always assuming your architecture also
fits these needs.
How to start a new project
Starting a project is often a phase with little time, a lot of tasks, and, in that combination, stress.
Even though we have started a lot of projects as a company, you as an architect might do it for the
first time or have done it the last time some time ago. This article provides guidelines, practical tips,
and reusable templates to focus, speed up, and reduce stress.
Focus has two dimensions: Doing the right/urgently needed things (scope) and doing them only
when needed (timing). Guidelines on scope are provided in the checklist referenced at the end. The
following overview helps in understanding the timing aspect:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a new project | 21
Leadership Team Technical Architect (TCD) Leadership Team
Architects (FCD+TCD)
#5
Technical Baseline
#2 #7
#1 Engagement #3 #4 Business Architect (FCD) Complete
Preparation Context Organization Minimum viable #6 Setup
architecture
BA Baseline
Initialization Sprint 0 Sprint 1+
As you can see, there are three major phases in setting up a project:
1. Initialization: Prepare the very basic foundations to organize the project setup and later phases
2. Sprint 0: Ensure basic setup to deliver a first sprint
3. Sprint 1+: While teams start to deliver, focus on remaining tasks for project setup
At the end of this article, you will find a link to a reusable checklist, which contains the relevant
tasks in detail. In the following sections, you get a basic understanding of the goals and areas to
focus on in each step and thus better understand the checklist provided.
But before, we have two major hints for you:
Use the checklist
The checklist does not only list the tasks to be done, but also supports with additional links to
• Knowledge nuggets / guidelines
• Templates
• Capgemini standards
that help you accelerate and setup quickly.
Additionally, you will always find information where to document content created and decisions
taken during the setup phase. The setup phase is about defining the very basic things to successfully
start delivering, so it is of course important to also communicate this to your team. Therefore, you
should make sure, that these basics are documented so they can be found and accessed by the team.
But you should also keep it lean: A graphic or simple table is often "just enough documentation" - in
general, but even more at the beginning.
Use standards
We highly recommend getting started with the provided templates and standards and adopting
them for your project later. You should protect yourself and your project: avoid putting too many
details and adoptions in the setup time, it also makes sense to learn first and then adopt according
to these learnings. Too often, we see architects getting lost in customizing templates, guidelines and
processes on a very detailed level in early phases, thinking of "what could be needed". As it cannot
be made clear enough:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a new project | 22
MVP Principle: Working solution first, details later
 Start with existing standards and templates provided by our company. Keep focus
on the crucial tasks for project setup. Start delivering, learn and only then start
adopting your processes and tools to the project needs!
Preparation
To support the work done in the setup phase and its documentation, it is helpful to have a basic
collaboration tooling at the beginning.
With a documentation tool and structure right from the beginning you can directly document in
that central space. If documentation tooling and structure is delayed to a later point in time,
projects usually end up in documenting first guidelines in PowerPoint, Word, OneNote, Teams, …
and there is an increasing extra effort needed to consolidate this distributed knowledge at a later
point (and often even not fully possible anymore). Therefore, this point is made number one.
It might also be an option to make these tasks part of the step "Organization", which then requires
to post-document your learnings from "Engagement Context" afterwards. So - of course - it is not a
blocker, if you can’t or don’t want to do it as first step. But you could, esp. when using standard
tools and templates as suggested in the checklist.
Engagement Context
The engagement context set’s the scene as it provides the boundaries as well as the "desires" (=more
formal: goals and vision) of the application or architecture. Therefore we must be aware of all these
factors as early as possible. To help to structure them, it’s recommended to have a look at the
Architecture Inception Canvas as discussed in the chapter about Architecture Documentation and
Communication. It is a good, concise starting point which later can (and should) be transferred to a
fully grown arc42 documentation.
Goals and vision
A good and shared understanding of business goals and vision is key to align larger teams on
successful delivery. It also ensures that basic settings, such as the high-level architecture, support
these goals and vision. So before going into any details of architecture, the goals and vision from
the contract should be revised. If there are any additional needs to clarify goals and vision, we
should urgently take that step right at the beginning, to avoid wrong architecture decisions, wrong
team setups and unsatisfied customers.
The goals are ususally expressed as the quality goals which will very much affect your architecture
as architecture objectives. They should be your scale to measure and assess all your further
decisions. See section Architecture Objectives for more on that topic including how to identify
them.
Constraints
Given constraints are important to know, as they influence your architecture and project setup.
Initially, constraints may be defined in the contract and/or can be a result of first alignments with
the customer on architecture. So, taking care for constraints early is also important to reveal the
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a new project | 23
needed effort during project startup for clarifying important aspects not yet defined by the
contract.
Customer stakeholders
Beside goals and vision and existing constraints, your stakeholders of course are also important to
understand the engagement context. They are those you will need to align with for all upcoming
delivery. They might influence if the project is considered to be successful even more than the
contract. So, you should know them, get into contact as early as possible and gather first
expectations from them that influence the architecture and project setup.
System context/environment
Often, we start new projects in the surrounding of already existing projects or within existing
programs. If this is the case and you’re not yet familiar with this surrounding, you should get. This
is expected by our customers, even if they don’t explicitly tell us to do it.
But be careful: With Capgemini internal communication we should focus on knowledge transfer. As
soon as it comes to decisions, it is important to include the customer side (mostly of both
engagements).
Organization
We recommend handling all organizational points with an onsite leadership kickoff-workshop.
Basic team setup
Our projects are led by the leadership team. This team usually consists of the engagement manager
and the lead architect(s) (two, if business and technical is separated). There might be further
management roles part of that team, depending on the project’s needs.
As an architect, it is crucial to act as part of the leadership team, so we have all experience in place
to find efficient delivery models and project organizations that deliver value to the customer. As the
architect in the leadership team, you should put special focus on areas related to architecture,
SDLC/delivery efficiency and quality. Not only you’re influencing the way our delivery teams work,
but also you have a responsibility to ensure personal growth in your area of responsibility.
First planning of project setup
After having now understood the engagement context and defined a basic team setup, it is time to
get a first idea on efforts and skills needed. In first place, you should focus needs on skills and
capacity on the project setup phase, as this is the more urgent thing to do (no surprise, you are
already in that phase).
To get the needed support, it is not only important to highlight support needs early, but even more
to make it specific. "I need a specialist on technology xyz at least for task a and b" is much more
specific and has quite higher chance of success than only "this is too much to do, I need senior
people".
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a new project | 24
Operations
Operations is one of the topics we recommend starting early, because experience has shown we
collect debt and lacks if postponed. The first step is about clarifying responsibilities (what is the
dev(ops) team doing? What is done by other teams/partners? What are the interfaces/interactions
between them?). Having this clarity, you can set up communication and start this communication
with a basic alignment.
Minimum viable architecture
Activities in this cluster are needed to define a basic setting, that enables teams to successfully
deliver a first sprint. The goal of the tasks in this area is to ensure a common mindset, but even
more to ensure that “things that are hard to change”, such as fundamental architecture decisions,
are aligned with the business goals, product vision and existing constraints.
If we don’t take the time for this minimum viable architecture early, we can end up with technical
debt after the first sprint. Even though we want to prevent this situation, it is of same importance to
limit decisions made upfront to the needed minimum. This minimum is related to the level of detail
(What is most crucial to be defined for all teams before starting to work?) and the timing (What do
we need to decide NOW?). Not deciding now should always produce a significant pain later. If this
is not the case, you should postpone the decision).
With this approach, we want to ensure “Just in time – just enough architecture (JIT-JEA)”.
Some approaches of how to apply JIT-JEA and build up a minimum viable architecture can be
found here.
Technical Baseline
Activities in this step are needed to enable teams for successfully delivery of a first sprint incl.
fulfilling the DoD. Remember: the first sprint should deliver first value, as small as it might be. But
without a proper technical baseline, teams will either completely fail to deliver or you have risk of
ending up with quality issues or first technical debt. And don’t forget to communicate to the team,
which takes a big part in this area. Definition and setup are needed, but not sufficient as long as the
team does not know they exist and/or how to use it.
Again: You should not take decisions that aren’t needed now and/or that could be given to the
team(s).
Conventions and Guidelines
Conventions and guidelines always bear the risk of spending too much time on them. On the other
hand, experiences show that once started without them, it is very hard to catch up. Thus, we
recommend starting with standards and templates provided in the checklist and to extend and
adopt later as much as possible.
Project Technology
From all aspects of the technical baseline, this one will take most time. Therefore, you should
especially focus to pull in people and skills and avoid doing too much on your own. This would cost
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a new project | 25
you a lot of time, you’d miss for other important tasks during the setup phase. And - of course -
starting with out-of-the box standards where possible and optimizing later also applies here.
Quality
Not setting up quality measures right from the start, does not cause immediate blockers in the
delivery process, but issues will either block successful delivery (functional aspects) or accumulate
and produce high-cost or high trouble later (non-functional aspects)! Therefore, we recommend
putting special focus to it: If you don’t care, nobody will and problems will arise later - promise!
Operations
After having understood responsibilities regarding operations before, it is now time to understand
their major requirements and prepare a setup supporting them. Same as with project technology, it
is about providing tooling and processes that enable the team for delivery of operable software.
E.g., having basic monitoring on test environment right from the start speeds up the analysis of
problems and generates learnings how helpful the monitoring is.
Business Analysis Baseline
The prework in this area should focus on enabling efficiency and quality of business analysis work
from the beginning of the engagement. Beside defining and setting up the basic tooling and
processes for their work, it is key to also communicate both to the team.
Aspects without need to decide now should be postponed to the phase "Complete the project setup",
e.g. system documentation or convenience tooling (e.g. Draw.io Plugin for Confluence. You can start
with manual adding it). Guidelines, such as naming conventions, fall under the category of "not
mandatory to deliver Sprint 1, but hard to catch up later". So, again, please use standards provided
to have it place and put a special focus on providing just enough guidance right from the start.
Complete the project setup
After having focused on the bare minimum to successfully start the project and sprint 0 is
successfully delivered, the leadership team now can start to focus on all activities needed for a
complete setup, while the team can already start delivering.
In this section, the checklist will reuse task names you found above, where there is still open work
to finalize. When prioritizing remaining tasks, we recommend using two questions:
1. How urgently does the team or customer wait for this task? How big is the pain, if the task is not
done immediately?
2. How much work will accumulate, if this task is done later? Is it possible to catch up and are the
needed efforts planned?
Notable Tools
• Checklist in Word Format
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a new project | 26
Capgemini Assets
▪ Global Architect Community - Agile Architecture
Architecture Documentation
Overview
Even the most brilliant architecture will get worthless if it is not known, agreed and understood.
Therefore, communication plays a major role in architects work - might it be the conversation
during design or written documentation for implementation and maintenance.
While written documentation is extremely important to keep the product maintainable, there is
also a risk of having too much documentation. In this case, it gets hard for a reader to find the
content and it also gets hard for the project, as they usually struggle to keep large documentations
up-to-date. As soon as some parts get outdated, the reader might perceive the complete
documentation as outdated and even the still good parts will not be used anymore. Thus, as shown
in the picture, usefulness of communication and documentation will drop if major things are
missing, but also if it is too much of documentation.
Usefulness
Amount of documentation
In the following article, you can find guidelines for good communication and minimal
documentation of software architecture, helping you to find the sketched optimum between "too
little" and "too much".
As the two really good and broadly used standards, we propose to use the C4 model for structured
and easy-to-understand written communication in design phase, completed with arc42 for
lightweight documentation. We won’t repeat these standards, but give you a brief reasoning for
that choice and highlight how they should be used in combination.
The most important point to successful documentation in a project is to build it on diagrams and
other artifacts used for communication during design. Of course, those artifacts might need to be
reworked according to changes discussed and implemented after design. Nevertheless, there should
be some stability and reusing the assets from communication not only lowers efforts for
documentation, but also has a strong recognition effect for stakeholders/readers.
General Rules for documentation
The following rules summarize, how to realize a lean, yet helpful communication and
documentation in your project. This section is inspired by the 7 rules for good documentation as
described in [Zoerner2021].
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Documentation | 27
Keep it easy to understand
Rule
Write from the readers point of view
For an easy-to-read writing style, imagine your reader, his/her knowledge and background and also
the situation where he/she wants to access the information. Help your future colleagues by using
terminology they can understand, focus on the really important content and to keep it short and
easy to understand. The best way is to imagine this new colleague and to write as you would
explain to him/her.
Rule
Use easy to understand language
Follow basic rules of simple language. Documentation is a helper for your colleagues, not a
showcase of your eloquentness. Basic rules for simple language are: Active Language, short
Sentences, no unnecessary repetitions, no ambiguities, no filler words.
Rule
Use standard structuring.
Having standard structures is helpful as a reader, as you need less time for orientation in
documentation you access the first time. If possible, you should use commonly known standards,
esp. ARC42 which is the standard we propose for structuring your documentation (SEE BELOW). If
there is a need and value to derive from this standard, at least you should make sure to reuse
structures in your context (e.g. same structure for each product/component/service).
Keep it lean
Rule
Documentation does not intend to master problems that should be solved at the source.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Documentation | 28
If things are hard to understand, we often use documentation to overcome this issue. One
prominent example is vastly used JavaDoc to explain code that should be self-explaining. So in this
example, you should aim for readable code using e.g. better naming / naming conventions. The
same issue can be transferred to architecture. If you need extensive documentation to explain your
architecture, it is obviously not self-explaining (which it should be). We should not start to solve
such an issue with additional documentation.
Rule
Prefer well structured and documented code over additional documentation outside the code.
In any case, where documentation can be done as part of the codebase, you should choose this as
an option. Additional architecture documentation needs to focus on adding extra-value by
documenting context and other major information needed to understand the system. Especially
detailed graphical modeling should be avoided. During design phase, consider communication
supported by less formal drawings as the better alternate. If detailed diagrams are needed during
maintenance, generating them from the existent codebase will give a realistic and up-to-date view,
which usually cannot not be kept in very detailed, additional documentation. Any other knowledge
on class level should be documented within the code.
It’s all about maintainability
Rule
Focus on new team members
Especially when it comes to documentation, we need to focus on a minimal set required for a
general onboarding (overviews). This is the most frequent situation where documentation is
accessed, so it is the most important use case. Additionally, and brief additional documentation
helping new colleagues to get the important information on areas they are working on is helpful.
Additional documentation not intended for team rotation and maintenance should only be added if
required by the customer.
Rule
Document decisions along with their reasoning
First of all, this is needed to avoid derivations, as the team can access decisions and get an
understanding of the reasoning. Additionally, it will be easer to adjust decisions when knowledge or
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Documentation | 29
premises change - which of course needs a documentation of the knowledge/premises that led to a
taken decision. Regarding documentation of decisions, please refer to Architecture Decision
Records (ADR), which are also used by the arc42 standard (see
[_structured_documentation_with_arc42]).
Rule
Build-in documentation to your delivery process
To keep documentation up to date, it needs to be kept up-to-date. As soon as parts get outdated, a
reader might assume that all documentation is outdated. The best way to keep it up-to-date is to
directly add changes while delivering them. It is like the kitchen: If you clean up your plate directly,
its a matter of minutes. If you postpone it, your kitchen becomes a mess.
Consider using the practices of Living Documentation wherever possible to safeguard an up to date
and relevant documentation.
Use Graphics
Rule
Make use of graphics and explain them.
Graphics are easy to understand and you might have noticed that good graphics are often used in
projects, e.g. to onboard new colleagues or to discuss the effect of changes to the architecture. They
can be developed jointly in a discussion with the customer, which also helps making it a jointly
known and owned asset. We highly recommend to concentrate on a (limited) set of helpful graphics
that can be used in several situations. In regards of a lean documentation, we recommend to not
repeat in text what is visible on the diagram, but to only give additional information.
Rule
Helpful diagrams are clean and focus on one topic
Diagrams are key to successful communication and documentation. Often started as a draft on a
whiteboard, a limited amount of great diagrams support communication during customer and
team alignment, and is also helpful for onboarding of new colleagues. In general, please consider
the following rules for helpful diagrams:
• Understandability over Completeness (One aspect only)
• Always use a legend (Even if only referring a standard, e.g. "this is a UML Class Diagram")
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Documentation | 30
• Avoid colors, keep it simple
Regarding the proposed diagrams, please refer to section
[_visual_communication_and_understandable_graphics_with_c4].
Collaboration based tooling
Beside the question of content, level of detail and used standards, you need to choose your platform
and tooling for communication and documentation.
You should consider a collaboration-based tooling, as everything else will become a pain in
distributed, large delivery teams. There is no one best solution, but always the same questions to
answer:
• Who are the readers? Is the customer reading the documentation? Which profiles at the
customer?
• Who are the writers? Which tooling are they familiar with?
• In general, we recommend a tooling that allows direct feedback by readers. Is there any reason
in your setup not to allow direct feedback?
Often, there is no one and single answer. There might be different answers when it comes to more
business related information in comparison to technical information. While business related
information often is written by business analysts and meant for the customer business department,
technical information usually is written by an meant for people with technical background.
Business people usually vote for a WYSIWYG-Editor with possibility for direct feedback. Technical
people usually vote for a toolset incorporated in the codebase like ASCIIDoc, using the same
"feedback/review options" as used for code reviews.
It is the individual task of a project to define the best fitting setup for the individual needs. Often,
we see a mix of solutions:
• High-Level documentation in Confluence, detailed in the codebase
• Business related documentation in Confluence, technical in the codebase
We have also a lot of other solutions in place, especially as we have documentation that is much
older than Confluence. This includes special solutions/programs for documentation as well as
Office/Document based solutions. Seeing the limited support for parallel writing, collaboration and
the pain to create system documentation with these toolings, they are of course not recommended
anymore for new setups.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Documentation | 31
Structured documentation with arc42
Arc42 is broadly known and used standard structure for communicating and documenting
software architectures and is promoted be the International Software Architecture Qualification
Board (iSAQB). Arc42 has a high focus to keep documentation lean, yet helpful ("painless
documentation", that even can be further reduced). It puts a high focus on limited, but very helpful
graphics, major listings and little additional text.
Seeing the maturity and spreading of arc42, as well as considering it helpful for our project
contexts, we recommend to use arc42 for documentation. Especially helpful is a ready-to-use
Confluence Template, you may use in your project.
Additionally, you can use arc42 it in your project as a checklist during design to ensure all major
points have been discussed and aligned.
A very good starting point at the beginning of a new project is an architecture inception canvas. It
can help you to gain a quick understanding of the most important factors that will influence your
architecture.
Architecture Canvases
You can look at the architecture canvases as specialized views or projections of your architecture
documentation. They provide a subset of an full blown arc42 documentation. Currently three
canvases are proposed on the canvas home page on arc42. We recommend to have a closer look
especially to the first two.
The arc42 open source organization claims to maintain, support and host these canvases. So they
are part of the arc42 eco system. They are compatible with the arc42 template and you can easily
map the sections of the canvases to arc42 sections.
Architecture Inception Canvas
The Architecture Inception Canvas is a tool for defining and conceptualizing new software
systems. It focuses on the goals of new initiatives, helping to derive architectural
decisions/hypotheses and identify major technical challenges. These insights can be integrated
into the arc42 document and further elaborated there later.
Architecture Communication Canvas
The Architecture Communication Canvas on the other hand is a "bird’s eye view" or “elevator
pitch” of an architecture. It is valuable for communicating and explaining the architecture to
others as it provides a compact documentation of the key elements of the system’s architecture.
Thus, the Architecture Communication Canvas is a condensed excerpt of a full arc42 document.
Even if you don’t adhere 100% to the layout of the canvas, it is a good idea to have a short
presentation of your architecture covering those aspects anyway.
Tech Stack Canvas
The Tech Stack Canvas was additionally proposed by INNOQ and has the scope to capture the
most important cornerstones regarding the technology stack of a new application or existing
architecture.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Documentation | 32
Architecture is a team sport!
You don’t have to fill out the canvas(es) alone on your own. On the contrary! You
shouldn’t try it solitary. Bring together the relevant stakeholders or knowledgeable

people and discuss the canvas' topics together. Thus you can pick more brains at
once and as a positive side effect you can get a buy in from all relevant people at
the same time.
This is especially most helpful when starting a new project.
Mapping arc42 and Quasar
This section is especially meant for colleagues with some history in our company, that (still) know
Quasar (QUality Software ARchitecture), to enable mapping of this former methodology to arc42. As
we still use the mentioned views, please find a description on the architecture views (A/T/TI) in the
article on functional decomposition. If you do not know Quasar, you can ignore this chapter.
arc42 Quasar Views

2. Constraints
3. Context and Scope
4. Solution strategy

12. Glossary
Mapping of arc42 and Quasar:
The documentation we see in most of our project goes back to our specification method, project
individual standards and Quasar with its separation of A-, T-, and TI-Architecture Views. While we
have seen a proven benefit from these documentations, we also see trouble in keeping these very
large documentations up to date. The decision for Arc42 was taken as we value the combination of
keeping the good (e.g. the architecture views) but removing the poor (inability to keep very large
documentations up to date).
Visual communication and understandable graphics with C4
The C4 Model focusses on visualizing software architecture in a lean and easy to understand way. It
proposes four major diagram types, along with few supplementary diagrams:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Documentation | 33
• Core diagram types: Context, Container, Component and Code (Therefore C4).
• Supplementary diagrams: System Landscape diagram, Dynamic diagram, Deployment diagram
The C4 approach to have the core diagrams as an order to zoom in makes it really easy to drill
down depending on the stakeholder and discussion and to focus on "one aspect only" for a certain
diagram. The following picture shows the core diagrams used in C4 and how level are zooming into
one aspect of the higher level.
The major reason to use C4 in our projects is about the content and scope of different views and
diagrams. The C4 model has collected best practices in useful and understandable diagram types,
which are worth using in our projects.
While we focus on the diagram contents when proposing C4, the actual modeling language used
still can be chosen in a project. C4 proposes a clean and nice looking language with very few
symbols, but also provides guideline how to use whiteboard/moderation techniques, UML or
ArchiMate for modeling C4 diagrams.
The creator of C4, Simon Brown, has a strong mission to keep as much documentation in the code
and to focus on core diagrams for documentation. While we share the usefulness of the proposed
diagrams, other building blocks of this work might be worth a look, but are not explicitly proposed
by us. This especially includes his approach for diagrams as code, architecture as code and the
corresponding product Structurizr.
Mapping arc42 and C4
C4 gives guidance to helpful diagrams that are developed in discussions with the team and
customer. These diagrams can and should be reused in the documentation of the intended system,
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Documentation | 34
and even more in the architecture documentation for future reference, esp. for onboarding new
colleagues.
The picture below shows on the left, how C4 diagrams are created while zooming into the solution.
Usually, diagrams as proposed by C4, are developed during design as part of alignment between
team members and customers. Even though the order of Levels in a sense of "zooming in" makes
sense, it is the choice of the project to choose an order that is helpful for the discussions.
On the right hand side, these C4 diagrams are mapped to the arc42 structure. This mapping of
diagrams to the documentation structure should help reusing existing artifact from
design/discussions in documentation.
