Interaction of architecture and managing an
engagement
Your high-level component architecture is not only needed for your solution and maintainability. It
also is a major input used to manage other aspects of an engagement, as you as architect are part of
the leadership team. Beside, this dependency to project setups also is a linking back to the
functional decomposition, enabling you to check if the architecture chosen also supports successful
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interaction of architecture and managing an engagement | 36
delivery.
Overview
The baseline to component management of course are components resulting from Functional
Decomposition. For these resulting components, we recommend to focus on three additional
aspects to be successful as architect: Security/Data Protection, Complexity and Scalability. Those
aspects should not only be known/classified, but also taken into consideration while performing a
functional decomposition, in order to achieve a managable architecture as well.
More to come

The following aspects are a starting point to this topic, but most probably not a
complete list. If you have aspects/experiences to share, please do so!
Security and Data Protection
Not only for an overall application, but also for each of major components, we should know a basic
security and data protection profile.
Of course, there are basic good practices for secure systems that always should be considered,
while additional measures, such as penetration testing should be focussed on the right spots.
We recommend to use the following classifications to define a security and data protection profile
for each (high-level) component. Having these classifications for components makes it easier for an
engagement to focus efforts in order to achieve a compliant architecture:
1. Exposure: Public Services, Company Public, Company Restricted. Should be used to focus
additional efforts to ensure security (e.g. Penetration Testing).
2. Data protection needs: Sensitive Company Data, Personal Data Should be used to focus
additional efforts to ensure compliance (GDPR Conform Information and Deletion Processes)
Beside just classifying components, it is also a good practice to consider these two aspects in the
functional decomposition and to avoid having "a little exposure and personal data" in each
component resulting from your macro-architecture. Managing the architecture and the project gets
much easier, if only some components are exposed/handle personal data.
Complexity
This aspect describes the component complexity. Again, we suggest to keep it simple, but you may
extend the classification based on your needs:
• Simple: Simple CRUD Functionality, single data set manipulation, limited or no dependencies
and checks.
• Medium: Easy Algorithms, e.g. simple calculations, mass data handling, some dependencies
• Complex: Complex Algorithms (e.g. optimization, mass data handling), lot of dependencies
Usually, we have a team build as a pyramid, with more and less experienced people. The complexity
classification should be considered when defining teams and their product responsibility. Complex
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interaction of architecture and managing an engagement | 37
components should be handled by experienced teams, while simple components can be covered by
less experienced teams or teams with higher change/fluctuation.
Of course, you can and should also turn around this aspect, make it a design need: In order to
properly manage an application in a team with mixed seniority, focussing/encapsulating
complexity is helpful to better control quality along with the given team setup. Often, this can be
aligned with functional needs and might be in place without explicit consideration, e.g. master data
maintenance usually is comparable simple, while calculations using this data often is complex. Still,
again having this small check on your high-level architecture under the aspect of complexity
classes is recommended.
Scalability
This aspect describes the component scalability needs. Again, we suggest to keep it simple, but of
course you may extend/adopt on your project needs:
• Normal: E.g. use cases for backoffices / master data maintenance / no time criticality / little
volume..
• High: E.g. use cases for customers / backoffice use cases with high processing volume, but short
process time given / …
Most probably when architecting a system for cloud, you may have already considered this in your
functional decomposition. From a scalability perspective, it can be better to have some
encapsulated functionality with high and some/more with normal scalability needs. To say it
otherwise: If all of your components end up having some functionality with high needs, it will get
more expensive in the cloud. In best case, you may have an option to improve the functional
decomposition in a way you can encapsulate functionality with high scalability needs.
If you manage to end up with components having different scalability needs as recommended
above, it is good practice to make this classification visible to the team. This is not only helpful to
keep the architecture clean and avoid unforeseen increase in operating cost in the cloud, also it
helps to focus efforts for load tests.
Managing Technical Debt
The term „technical debt“ is used to refer to all kind of technical issues that negatively impacts
software development regarding time, cost and quality.
Overview
Often, the reaction of people with little software development expertise is: This is the problem of
the supplier, you should have known better. But, as we know, it is not that simple. It is worth to take
a deeper look into reasons for technical debt to understand how we can manage them together
with our customers.
Considering the analogy to monetary debt, the term technical debt can be understood better. People
take financial debts to buy something in advance before they actually afford it. At some point in
time, they need to pay back these debts, and usually getting the money earlier comes at some cost –
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Managing Technical Debt | 38
the interest to be paid additionally to the borrowed money. As with monetary debt, technical debt
increases over time, accumulating interest making it increasingly difficult to address; the longer
you wait, the more difficult and therefore costly it can get.
This essence of "gaining speed for cost" is also key to technical debt. To get into the different types
of what is understood as technical debt, we need to differ
• Regarding the attitude that made them arise: Careless or Thoughtful
• Regarding the intention that made them arise: Conscious or Unconscious
The combination of the two aspects leads to the technical debt quadrant by Martin Fowler (see
image). You can already see in the picture that we usually consider only technical issues arising
from thoughtful processing as technical debt and therefore this article focusses on this area. On a
side note, the original meaning from Ward Cunningham who coined the term was only bottom
right quadrant (s. Video).
Still, we also give a short overview on reasons and prevention on issues arising from carelessness.
They can be handled similarly to debt in most regards, so you still might get some ideas on
managing those from this article.
Careless Thoughtful
We'll skip design without We have to deliver fast
further alignment on risks. and clean up later.
Now we know how we
What is layered architecture?
should have done it.
suoicsnoC
suoicsnocnU
Technical Flaws Technical Debt
Figure 3. The technical debt quadrant (by Martin Fowler)
Basic Considerations
Technical debt vs. technical flaws
Consciously taken Technical Flaws
Technical issues arising from this area should not happen, as consciously doing wrong things and
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Managing Technical Debt | 39
knowing about the arising problems obviously is not professional behavior. If, for example, we
need to skip a task due to time or budget pressure, this should happen as an aligned decision with
our stakeholders, at least with project management that then needs to plan for preventing and
handling the arising risk of technical flaws.
Unconsciously taken Technical Flaws
In case of “unconscious sloppiness”, it is also our task to setup project training and quality
measures to prevent such situations. We might also have taken a cautious decision to go for a
project with a junior team, but this needs, as mentioned, the according planning for training,
quality management and – maybe – also more bug fixing.
So it is our task to prevent and control this area with at least proper project planning and
automation in quality assurance. Still, there will happen a few mistakes, but “in a normal range”.
Those are covered by warranty. Only, if we internally took more debt than is covered by our
warranty, it becomes us who is paying the bill. This might be with budget overshoot, but also with
unsatisfied customers and the potential loss of a project.
Consciously taken Technical Debt
The most common reasons for consciously taking technical debt is time pressure and/or a lack of
budget.
If the go-to-market is of much higher importance than the cost coming along with technical debt, it
is absolutely ok to take it. This leads to the more dangerous reason for technical debt: lack of
budget. Same as finance, if there is no budget to pay back the debt and the interests, it will lead to
trouble. Thus, we highly recommend to never solve budget issues with technical debt, but with
reduced (functional) scope.
Unconsciously taken Technical Debt
Even if everything is perfectly planned and well aligned and cautiously decided, technical debt can
arise. It is to take account for the fact we learn things, nobody knew before while delivering
complex, large and long-running systems. Examples from our projects are:
Increase of volume
We might have designed a sales system handling orders for a certain amount of markets and
products. We might even have considered some buffer. Still, ten years later the company with their
products and markets might have grown to an extend that the current system cannot handle the
volume anymore and thus suffers from decreasing scalability, reliability and/or performance.
Unplanned Rollouts
We might have designed a system used for a local acting company. At the beginning, a purely local
system without internationalization was decided to save time (and budget). At some point in time,
this company may decide to extend to international markets, starting offsite production or sell the
software as SaaS product to other companies. This this scenario, the system cannot handle yet
internationalization and my suffer from missing data silos and tenant management.
Learn on the way
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Managing Technical Debt | 40
Using agile delivery, it is a design pattern to deliver fast and accept needed rework based on
learning. It accounts for the fact, that we cannot know everything upfront and that speed and
learning is of higher importance than “perfection with the first release”. This may of course also
lead to situations where we learn more about technical/non-functional requirements on the way –
same, as we allow to learn about functional requirements. Agile goes for speed at the cost of
potential rework.
As you can see, technical debt may have been taken with intention or they may arise due to
changed requirements, but in all cases, they are not related to missing craftsmanship.
No "Unforeseeable upfront design".
Please note that - independent from the delivery model (Agile/Waterfall) - is NOT
our goal to provide architectures and designs for all possible requirements that
 may arise in future. Designing for requirements that are not yet known, nor
detailed, usually leads to over-complex or unused code (if there is no requirement
coming) or it does not match the needs, as they are not known yet. It is task of the
project to know about upcoming needs and to balance "no design for unknown
requirements" with "don’t block the (known) future".
Architecture vs. code debt
The types of debt can be distinguished between architecture and code/devops debt. Architecture
debt usually are much more expensive to resolve, as the impact on existing code and the need for
refactoring is much higher, as it is for code debt.
Example Architecture Debt
It was decided to handle messages within each component individually. If the decision is later
changed to a common message handling, refactoring of all error producing code areas will be
needed.
Example Code Debt
In a specific situation it was decided for a hard-coding to fix a bug quickly. Later, this code area
(only) will need to be adopted to implement a proper solution.
We recommend tracking technical debt separated by their type. Not only it impacts the efforts
needed to fix them, also the responsibility might be with different persons. While architecture debt
effects the architect as well as – usually – all development teams, code debt often can be related to
the one team that is responsible.
Consequences of technical debt
As we now have learned, why there is technical debt, we have to understand their impact. How big
is the interest and what if we don’t pay back?
So, of course, paying back the core debt is doing at later point what was skipped at the beginning. If
it was decided not to implement a proper internationalization at the beginning, the core cost is to
do it later. Additionally, we have the interest: We need to refactor existing code, e.g. hard coded
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Managing Technical Debt | 41
labels, run (broad) regression testing and managing the fixes which – in total – makes a higher cost
than doing it correctly from the beginning. But of course, we had gained speed.
If we don’t pay back the technical debt, this might lead to:
• Increased time-to-market Due to technical debt – usually unneeded complexity - the product
becomes less maintainable. This leads to increase of time-to-market.
• Increased cost Along with a longer time-to-market, the cost increases to make changes to a less
maintainable system. Also, operating cost might increase, due to high resource consumption.
Lastly, support cost may also increase due to increasing number of reported tickets, e.g. due to
performance issues or a lack of reliability.
• Decreased user satisfaction Due to technical debt, users might rate the product having a bad
quality, esp. if it becomes unstable due to performance and scalability issues. In worst case,
issues arising from technical debt might lead to outages (problems in reliability).
• Compliance Issues We might have decided to solve user management with simple system
mechanisms supported by very few (IT) experts maintaining them. As soon as this
organizational setup changes and user management needs to account more for compliance, the
system might not support this need anymore.
Managing technical debt
Why managing technical debt?
Our goal should be to always have an overview on the technical debt, so we
• Have an overview on the software quality
• Can initiate activity where needed to prevent the mentioned consequences
If we have an overview, it is important to make the management a part in our customer
conversions that we actively bring in. We need to enable the customer to have a proper
understanding of the current amount of technical debt and the related risks. Only then we can
collaboratively work on improvements and prevent surprises on customer side. As always, it is
about avoiding (bad) surprises.
Budgeting technical debt
As defined above, technical debt is not about technical flaws (mistakes), and so technical debt needs
to be budgeted by the project sponsors. By this, it is ensured that the debt usually taken for higher
speed is paid back by those taking the debt. This holds true for conscious taken debt (“We go fast
now and fix later”) as well as unconscious taken debt (“We go fast and learn on the way”).
The budgeting of course needs to have a relation to existing volume of debt. Still, we recommend to
keep the budget lean to enforce prioritization and prevent resolution of technical debt with a bad
or even negative cost/benefit ratio.
Define your scope of technical debt management
Before starting, it is important to align on a common picture on what is seen as technical debt.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Managing Technical Debt | 42
Therefore, the overall project goals/priorities and quality goals must be known. If they are not yet
clear or fully aligned, we recommend taking that step before starting more detailed analysis.
As a result, we can consider everything a technical debt which hinders achievement of the project
goals and principles as well as the derived quality goals. If there is no problem, there is no debt.
For example: Poorly maintainable code in a system about to be shut off is no debt, as
maintainability is not a priority for this system.
Project Goals
What are major achievements the project is aiming for? This is especially taking care about plans
for the future.
Examples:
• If there is a plan for rollouts to a very large user base, this knowledge will impact how we rate
technical debt effecting usability.
• If there is a plan not to enhance a system anymore – maybe along with an already planned shut
down – this knowledge will impact how we rate technical debt effecting maintainability.
Beside project goals, priorities should be clarified. An easy way to prioritize might be using the
known triangle of project management and put them in order: Time/Speed, Quality and Budget.
This helps in prioritization of technical debt later.
Quality Goals
Based on project goals and priorities, quality goals should be aligned. Often, they can be derived
directly as indicated with above examples. Nevertheless, there is a need to align those quality goals
with the project stakeholders to avoid surprises. The easiest way is to use the ISO 25010 Quality
Map, and align with the stakeholders on the level 1 aspects (e.g. irrelevant - relevant - highly
relevant). Of course the input might already be present in a project, especially if conversations on
quality have already been taken.
Identifying technical debt
Consciously taken technical debt
If we decide for taking technical debt explicitly, this is the best moment to document technical debt.
When the customer decides or agrees for a quicker solution that produces (a usually roughly
known) amount of debt, this debt should directly be added to the debt backlog. As usually we
already have all information on stakeholders, budget owner, impact and solution as part of
decision making, we should directly document this complete knowledge. Of course, it is a good
practice when documenting this early to add links to existing material, such as evaluation slides
made for decision making, ADRs or meeting minutes.
Unconsciously taken technical debt
Still, it might be needed to collect technical debt later on. One reason might be that documentation
has been missed so far (e.g. when we take over a project or within a consulting activity for an
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Managing Technical Debt | 43
external project), the other one of course is learning that might lead to unconscious technical debt.
For unknown, audits on architecture and code are usually the best approach to identify technical
debt. These audits should be done from people not delivering the project to get an external view.
Audits usually need two major inputs to determine technical debt, as also illustrated in below
picture:
• Implicit knowledge of the existing stakeholders (Humans). A lot of issues are known by the
development team and stakeholders, but rarely this knowledge is already consolidated and
available. To collect the existing knowledge, mutual or written down at multiple places,
interviews are the best and most common way to collect known debt.
• Explicit knowledge of the existing stakeholders (Backlogs). Some issues might be already
documented in Issue Tracking tools (e.g. JIRA) and/or List (e.g. Confluence, Sharepoint, Excel).
These existing documentation needs to be collected and consolidated.
Beside, in a project due-diligence or as consultancy, tool support cant be helpful. In our delivery
projects, this tool support should be build into our CI/CD pipeline along the needed processes to
handle issue. This provides permanent prevention of technical debt.
Tools used in these area can be classified accounting to architecture vs. code debt:
• Static Code Analysis Tools such as Sonarqube and comparable tools that support analysis of a
code base to find "code smells"
• Architecture Analysis Tools such as ArchUnit that checks the architecture of your code to find
"architecture smells"
It is very important to make sure, your static code analysis tool is configured to reflect project and
quality goals, so only real issues are reported.
To enable ongoing management of technical debt, the debt catalog should take the form of a
managed backlog, integrated to same system used for feature backlog.
Figure 4. How to collect technical debt (consulting focus)
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Managing Technical Debt | 44
Managing technical debt
After identifying technical debt, the usual result is a long or very long list of debt. Especially when
introducing static code analysis at a late point in time, this might lead to a huge number of findings.
To evaluate – and by that manage – technical debt, we propose the following steps. The aim of these
steps is to reduce the list as early as possible and by that to avoid unneeded efforts in management
and to only focus on debt where a resolution pays off.
As already mentioned before, these steps are usually part of decision making and thus directly
documented. Having the following flow explicitly is needed for one-time activities or to tackle
upcoming issues usually reported as bug (e.g. performance issues due to unforeseen user base
increase).
Step 1: Document and classify technical debt
As first step, additional data should be added to manage the technical debts.
• Stakeholders Who needs to be involved for prioritization?
• Classification What is the quality impact on the system (Referring to ISO 25010 map:
Performance, Compatibility, Usability, Reliability, Security, Maintainability, Portability)?
• Impacted Systems or System Components What is impacted?
• Brief Description Provide basic understanding of the debt.
• Change tracking When and by whom was the item last updated? Only relevant when using
tooling that does not support automatic versioning.
Of course you may adopt classifications to your needs.
Step 2: Reduce list according to strategic relevancy
According to the project overall priorities and quality goals, a first round of sorting out should
happen before spending efforts on impact and solution evaluation. If there are quality aspects not
relevant for the project context, the accordingly classified technical debt should be discarded
(“Won’t solve”, along with a short reasoning).
Step 3: Reduce list by items with little or no impact
For all remaining debt relevant for the project, a rough evaluation on the impact is needed. An
impact can be a problem, or it can be a risk with an additionally given likeliness. Unlikely or not
harmful debt should be sorted out after having clarity on the impact.
Step 4: Evaluate Solution
For each of the remaining technical debt, a solution sketch along with cost indication is needed:
• Solution Description What needs to be done? What is the risk/what are expected side-effects of
solving the debt?
• Solution Cost We recommend to use classifications such as High-Medium-Low or T-Shirt sizes.
Still, more detailed cost could be used if already known. Spending too much effort on detailed
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Managing Technical Debt | 45
cost if a rough estimation is sufficient for prioritization should be avoided.
Step 5: Prioritization according to Cost/Benefit
Based on the evaluation benefit and solution cost, items must be prioritized. If there is enough
clarity that benefits will not cover the cost, items should not be de-prioritized, but directly be
discarded (again, with an according comment).
Who is prioritizing?
The prioritization should be done together with the customer. In all scenarios, resolution of
technical debt has an impact on the delivery of features – might it be due to needed capacity or
dependencies. It is essential, the the overall alignment on usage of development capacity is aligned
with the customer. Without alignment, it will be close to impossible to prioritize and resolve
technical debt, which again will lead to exponential growth of debt.
Beside prioritization, is has been regarded as beneficial to resolve debt when changing related
functionality anyhow (boy-scout principle). This saves efforts for testing and also ensures, there is
no clean-up for the sake of it, but only when needed. Nevertheless, this only helps for small issues,
as all others are hard to deal this way.
From our projects, we can see the following three setups:
Mixed team (Capgemini, Customer Business and IT)
This is by far the best and recommended way, as it ensures the buy-in from all stakeholders. The
prioritization in this setup is done by the business department, supported by Capgemini and the
IT department. The supporters need to enable the business to understand benefit and solution
cost of a debt, not necessarily the debt itself.
Mixed team IT only (Capgemini, Customer IT)
In this setup, usually a certain capacity is fixed for technical debt and the prioritization for usage
of this capacity is done by the IT. This setup only is successful if there is a strong trust between
business and IT department, which is needed for successful capacity management. The business
needs to trust that the usage of the reserved capacity carefully used and capacity would be given
back if there is not urgent things to do while the IT needs to trust that in case of urgent and
bigger issues, more capacity can be allocated.
Team only (Capgemini)
Same as the second approach, this might only work if there is enough trust and comes with the
same risks and downsides. Prioritizing technical debt without customer involvement at all is not
recommended. If there is a need, because the customer prefers to delegate.
How to prioritize
• Decide on Solution Type (Tolerate, Change, Replace, Retire) The need for such a classification is
basically given with the chosen tooling. If debt management is and integrated part of backlog
management as recommended, this classification should be replaced with either directly closing
the issue with a comment (tolerate) or incorporate it into the solution sketch (change, replace,
retire).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Managing Technical Debt | 46
• Roughly planned time period for resolution (e.g. Year, Release) if needed.
Resolving technical debt
There are two major aspects to consider when organizing the resolution of technical debt: Who is
doing it and when is it done. Regarding the responsibility, we know two major approaches: Either it
is a task of the development team or a dedicated team. Considering the timing, technical debt
reduction can be done as one-time activity or incorporated in the ongoing development – either
with dedicated capacity in feature sprints/release development or with fully dedicated sprints /
releases. For agile development, it is of course the sprints to consider, for non-agile delivery we
might need to think of the development for a release.
Our experience on the different approaches when combining the options for “Who is doing it” and
“When is it done” is:
• We recommend approaching technical debt using the development teams and an integrated
(ongoing) approach, as this ensures fast learning and is preventing more debt
• It can be an option to organize a one-time activity, if for any reasons a larger amount of
technical debt has been grown and there is a large pressure to reduce technical debt quickly.
This is only recommended as fallback, reacting to a situation with a certain amount of technical
debt. This approach should not be for “we don’t care now, lets do later”, assuming there might
be a time in future where we have time and capacity to so.
• We recommend NOT to use any ongoing activities with dedicated teams, as this leads to ongoing
organizational challenges that impact efforts and quality negatively (ongoing KT needed,
ongoing management of dependencies needed)
When talking about the development team, we also include other roles and their tasks than only
the developers. In particular, the development team includes BAs facilitating the process for
identification, evaluation and prioritization of technical debt and testers to test the changes done
(esp. regression testing).
Keep the customer up-to-date
This is an obvious, but too important not mention here: We actively need to report out on the status
of technical debt. We recommend to focus on the discussion and classification on new technical
debt, but especially to resolved debt and gained quality.
Notable Tools
• Your best friend: your projects' standard backlog management tool ;) (JIRA or any other tool of
choice)
• ArchUnit to prevent / find architecture debt
• SonarQube to prevent / find architecture and code debt

How to start a project using JIT-JEA and hexagonal
architecture
Agility is the defacto-standard in our projects. Short time to market is the main driver for agility.
Especially in the project start phase, it is crucial that productive and usable results are achieved
quickly in order to create a business value for the customer as early as possible. In a classic context,
however, complex and comprehensive architectural concepts were created at the beginning of a
project before the first code could be written and the first functionality delivered.
One approach that counteracts this problem is just-in-time, just-enough-architecture (JIT-JEA). JIT-
JEA describes that at a defined point in time only enough architecture needs to be available that
makes it possible to develop initial functionality without building up large amounts of technical
debt. This enables the project to start quickly while maintaining a minimum viable architecture
(MVA). However, an MVP might become part of a larger landscape and in which case, the MVA
should also consider this future situation.
JIT-JEA originally emerged from the area of enterprise architecture and many formulations or
examples are also aimed at this form of architecture. For example, the approach of the Architecture
Runway. Both the intentional architecture from the perspective of the enterprise architect and the
emerging architecture from the team perspective are brought together. JIT-JEA can also be
transferred very well to the solution architecture of software systems. Here, too, an MVA is
developed by the architect, but it must be regularly compared with the developments from the
teams and adjusted if necessary.
In the second part of the JIT-JEA approach, various examples are shown of how this approach was
applied in practice. Based on Example 4 this article shows one example how the architecture style
of a hexagonal architecture supports JIT-JEA by enabling a later transformation from a modulith to
microservices just when it is needed.
Use Hexagonal architecture to transform over time from a modulith to
microservices
At the start of a project the macro architecture has to be defined, as subsequent changes at this
level are often highly complex, expensive or even uneconomical.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a project using JIT-JEA and hexagonal architecture | 48
In many of our projects a layered architecture with modular monolithic pattern is used. There are
use cases in which a modulith are very well suited, for example with high performance
requirements or with a manageable size of the overall domain.
Alternatively, it is possible to start directly with a microservice architecture, but this also requires a
large number of decisions to be made and methodologies to be developed early on, such as
distributed tracing and how to assure transactions that span multiple services (see SAGA pattern).
Starting with a microservice architecture also contains the risk, that the split of services might be
wrong leading to a more effortful refactoring compared to a modulith. Both aspects can slow down
the initial delivery of artifacts, especially in agile contexts with high time-to-market pressure.
When changing the marco architecture from a modulith to microservices using the layered
architecture, two central problems often arise:
1. The boundaries of the layers are not maintained correctly e.g. the mapping into transport
objects is blurred into the business logic. This happens primarily because the dependencies
cannot be clearly controlled and therefore access is not intended in principle, but still arises
over time. This blurring of boundaries makes it difficult to replace the adapters/interfaces
during a later refactoring, as the business logic cannot be clearly differentiated from other logic,
e.g. REST adapters.
2. Access to other business modules is not clearly regulated or possible through direct access
without there being abstraction levels through additional adapters. This creates a close-meshed
connection between the modules, which corresponds to the technical access rules, but leads to a
complex coupling of the modules and can only be divided with great effort. In addition, the
mapping of Java classes to database tables does not respect the boundaries of the business
modules.
The following approach is intended to describe a way in which a backend macro architecture can
be transformed over time by using the hexagonal architecture, based on the JIT-JEA idea. The
fourth example of the second part of the JIT-JEA approach is also taken up. The hexagonal
architecture offers advantages here and, taking into account a few regulations, makes it possible to
start with a simple modular backend architecture and, over time, transform it into a microservice
architecture with increasing technical complexity.
Applicable projects
This approach is aimed primarily at projects in which there is a high time-to-
market pressure and the initial MVP needs to be released quickly and there is not
enough time and resources to build a complex microservice architecture upfront.
At the same time, however, it is foreseeable that the application will become more

complex over time and higher requirements for differentiated scaling will be
necessary. However, there are other reasons to start with this approach, for
example if there are uncertainties regarding the domain split or if the effort
required for implementation in a microservice architecture is difficult to estimate
in advance. The approach is not necessary if JIT-JEA is not relevant in the project
due to a lack of business agility requirements.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a project using JIT-JEA and hexagonal architecture | 49
Start small
To start implementation, the business components for the MVP need to be defined. These
components are typically defined through data-driven decomposition or strategic DDD and can
change over time and changing business needs. In order to be able to deliver the MVP as quickly as
possible, the modular monolithic pattern is initially used in combination with the hexagonal
backend architecture. By this, decisions regarding events, distributed transactions and tracing, but
also compatibility between individual microservices only have to be dealt with regarding the
external systems of the application.
The following two characteristics of hexagonal architecture are crucial in this context and must be
obeyed:
1. The core should be as technology-agnostic as possible and only implement application or
business logic. Depending on the project context, technical frameworks such as Spring can be
defined as 0-software and therefore also used in the core.
2. Incoming and outgoing connections to the core are realized by adapters.
This allows the individual business modules to be implemented according to the hexagonal
architecture and communication to the core is clearly regulated via adapters.
Get more complex over time
Additional business components are added with further requirements and possibly already for the
MVP. It’s crucial that communication between the business components is implemented explicitly
via adapters, even if both components are within the same application. These internal adapters
represent the necessary abstraction layer between the business components and are a breaking
point for a later transformation into a microservice architecture. Another important aspect is that
data sovereignty is maintained, i.e. only business objects from your own component are modified.
This can be technically prevented through the appropriate use of modules (in Java Maven or
Gradle), even if all components of the application share the same persistence schema at this point.
The graphic below shows this structure as an example for two business components. In addition to
internal communication, incoming web and event adapters as well as an outgoing adapter for
persistence are also shown.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a project using JIT-JEA and hexagonal architecture | 50

Figure 5. Module-based hexagonal component architecture with multiple modules
With an increasing number of business components and differentiated scaling requirements, it is
necessary to transform the existing macro architecture.
Break down complexity
Transforming the macro architecture from a modular monolith to multiple microservices is now
significantly easier compared to the previously described scenarios because:
1. Communication between business components is explicitly defined by ports and implemented
via adapters. This high degree of decoupling between the components enables easier
separation. Communication via adapters has another helpful side effect. Due to the higher effort
(additional mappings and data structures), communication with other business components
becomes more conscious and explicit. This reduces overall communication between business
components and reduces the effort for a later refactoring.
2. Since the application core is as technology-agnostic as possible, logic for mapping and
transforming into transport objects is only in the adapters and not mixed with the business
logic. This allows for easier exchange of communication technology.
When transforming the macro architecture, individual business components can be pulled out into
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a project using JIT-JEA and hexagonal architecture | 51
new microservices. To do this, the internal adapters must be rewritten to include communication
technologies such as REST or event-based communication. Since the core of the business
components is free of framework-specific content, the conversion is limited exclusively to the
adapters. A key advantage is that the existing interfaces/adapters are already value-based through
the use of DTOs and mappings, making switching to other communication technologies less
complicated. See also 'Value and Reference-oriented Interfaces' in Interface Design.
Further challenges of transformation
A transformation from a modular monolith to microservices always includes
many other aspects, such as the division of the data schema, distributed
 transactions, resilience or performance. This approach primarily addresses the
problem that the backend-micro architecture does not allow for later division and
transformation into microservices because the dependencies are tightly meshed.
The aforementioned challenges are not explicitly addressed here as they must be
solved independently of this approach.
The following graphic shows the division of the previously described business components into two
separate applications / microservices. It should be noted that the previous internal adapters were
replaced by web adapters, thus switching to http-based synchronous communication.

Figure 6. Module-based hexagonal component architecture with microservices
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini How to start a project using JIT-JEA and hexagonal architecture | 52
This approach shows a way in which the JIT-JEA idea can be realized by using the hexagonal
architecture and explicit communication via adapters. On the one hand, it makes it possible to start
quickly with an MVP without having to deal with the complexity of a microservice architecture, but
at the same time to remain future-proof and later migrate to microservices if the growing
requirements make it necessary.

Functional Design Patterns
Overview
The following articles provide some guidelines and experiences useful for business clarifications
and functional design. In projects, we often experience lack of clarity on how much technical
design is impacted by business demands. "This is technical" is often used when it comes to details of
implementation, while some of these details need to be defined together with the business
department.
To support you better in functional design, the below articles explain basic concepts you should
know, along with guidelines on information sources, questions to clarify with business, design and
documentation guidelines, and finished by helpful tips for quality assurance. As usual, articles
provide helpful links to tools, Capgemini assets and (external) links.

will be larger updates to add more images and to make the texts shorter and more
precise.
Nevertheless, the content is already helpful and can be used.

Functional Design: User Interfaces - Dialogs
The user interface summarizes all parts of the system that is visible to the end user. This includes
dialogs, but also printouts, generated documents, automatically sent e-mails and much more. This
article focussed on the dialog part of user interfaces.
Overview
A dialog is a self-contained element of the user interface, separate from other dialogs, which is used
by the application to support a user action by providing a meaningful part of its functionality for
interactive utilization. The design of dialogs defines the user interface incl. static elements and the
dialog behavior.
The dialog design is almost always required for enterprise information systems. It is a central
element in the solution design, since it describes how the user communicates with the application
and carries out the desired actions of the use cases. Often, it heavily influences how satisfied users
are with the designed system.
Basic Concepts
Dialogs
In general, a dialog is considered to be a self-contained element of the user interface, separate from
other dialogs, which is used by the application to provide a meaningful part of its functionality for
interactive utilization. Such a part usually comprises the functionality specified by a
• bounded context / a building block resulting from functional decomposition;
• use case or by a series of related use cases; or
• related features and user stories.
The purpose of the dialog is to make this functionality available to the user at the user interface by
presenting the corresponding data and functions. “A dialog forms a unit of human / machine
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Capgemini Assets | 55
communication in the sense that it makes related data and functions available to the user. Its data
view is an important aspect.”
A dialog should be designed so that a self-contained task can be carried out in a single dialog where
possible. Typical breaks in a task should correspond with dialog changes, or at least with natural
steps in a task (a credit application may be saved and then subsequently approved the next day). As
a result, a corresponding relationship often exists between dialogs and business processes, e.g.
business process corresponds to a dialog or, in case of breaks in a task, several dialogs.
Sub-Dialog (Views)
A dialog can have a hierarchical structure with sub-dialogs. In projects, sub-dialogs might also be
called tabs, masks, forms or views of a dialog.
In general, we consider a sub-dialog (or dialog) to be a self-contained area of the screen which
allows a coherent part of the functionality that corresponds to the dialog to be utilized. A sub-dialog
frequently corresponds to exactly one window, mask, form or one of the tab pages in a window.
Figure 8. Example of Sub-Dialogs (Views) of one Dialog
The example from the mobilePASS Application illustrates the difference of a dialog (window) and
sub-dialogs (views). The dialog in all three screenshots is the same, it is the same window and
application used. Still, you can change the view completely by either being on the initial screen, the
“authentication screen”, the “application information screen” or the “create token screen”. This
example thus represents one dialog with four sub-dialogs.
Please note that sub-dialogs are optional. Of course, we still can have a dialog having only one sub-
dialog, which actually means: there is only one view, one dialog.
The concept of dialogs and sub-dialogs is especially useful if there are dialog elements needed in
the same form but in different areas. In the above example, there is always the same dialog name,
orange heading part und logo footer.
Style Guide
A style guide aims to provide a unique user experience. Specifically, this means: A user can find
same elements at the same place with the same look and feel across different dialogs. Additionally,
style guides ensure that regulations (e.g. limitation of barriers / “Barrierefreiheit”) are followed.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Dialogs | 56
In the example below, you can already gather the style guide for Microsoft Office products. They
use the same icons, same labels, same orders and locations for same functionality, no matter if you
use Word, Excel or Powerpoint:
Figure 9. Example of implemented style guide (Microsoft Office)
Typical elements of a style guide are:
• Appearance
◦ Fonts
◦ Text sizes
◦ Color palette
◦ Icons
• Layout Patterns
◦ General Design Guidelines, e.g. where to place a Save-Button
◦ Dialog types, e.g. patterns for master data dialogs, settings dialogs, …
• Dialog Elements
◦ Used dialog elements, e.g. Datepicker (Calendar), Radiobuttons, Tree Views, …
• Behavior Patterns
◦ Responsive design needs (Do screens need to adopt to PC, Tablet, Mobile usage?)
◦ Usage of Modal and non-modal dialogs (Are modal dialogs allowed/enforced?)
◦ Resizing (Can windows be resizable?)
◦ Shortcut keys
◦ Tab behavior/order
• Naming conventions
• Multi-Language Support
For dialog types and dialog elements, the following subsections provide further details.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Dialogs | 57
A style guide usually is set up at project start and adopted/extended with the arising project needs.
It should be aligned and approved by the customer to meet corporate design guidelines and
customer expectations.
Dialog Types
Dialog types summarize equivalent (dialog) layouts and behavior, so they do not have to be
redefined every time. The concrete dialog description refers to these dialog types, so that standard
behavior no longer has to be described. The layout may be documented with a reference to the data
model. The existing description of the dialog type is also referenced regarding the dynamics. This
approach has been proven for:
• Standard actions such as cancel, save, delete, close, search.
• Standard dialogs such as master data administration, searching datasets, displaying datasets.
Layout frameworks as well as dynamic behavior, standard key assignment, standard dialog
transitions, etc. are specified for each dialog type. Dialog types are described in the dialog design
using the same tools utilized for the concrete dialogs themselves. In principle, dialog types can
already be finalized in the project-specific dialog design guideline.
The design of dialogs mainly takes the form of structured text, which is supplemented by graphics
to visualize the established layouts. Dynamic characteristics of dialogs can be modeled with UML
diagrams. However, it is essential to ensure customers can read UML notation if UML diagrams are
used.
Dialog Elements
Individual fields for the input or output of information by or for the user are called dialog elements.
Typical dialog elements are:
• Textbox (Single/Multiline)
• Dropdown
• Radiobuttons
• Checkboxes
• Switch
• Table
• Datepicker (Calendar)
• Maps
• Tree Views
• …
The style guide defines the elements used in a certain application. During technical design, these
elements are usually provided using libraries to make them reusable.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Dialogs | 58
Modal and non-modal dialogs
While non-modal windows can be activated and left open while navigating to other parts of the
application, a modal window blocks all other windows (of the same application). The other
windows of the application can only be used after the modal window is closed. This means that if a
modal dialog is active, no other dialog of the same application can be active at the same time.
Conversely, the user can work in several non-modal dialogs of an application at the same time.
Figure 10. A typical model dialog: Settings in Word block other actions
Presentation states and dialog states
There are two different types of states in dialogs: The presentation states and the dialog states.
Presentation states usually refer to sub-dialogs. Sub-dialogs usually have many parallel
presentation states, e.g. “the checkbox is selected” or “the button is available”. Presentation states
define presentation characteristics that may be independent of each other (e.g., the fields for
company information are locked for private individuals). However, presentation states may be
dependent on dialog states.
On the other hand, the dialog states refer to the dialog. They are used to specify the behavior of a
dialog more precisely in different dialog contexts. This means dialog states represent different
states of a dialog. We differentiate between dialog states such as “empty dialog”, “data read”, “data
changed”. Dialog states are exclusive, e.g., a dialog is always in precisely one dialog state.
For example, a dialog is in the “initial state” after the window with the empty display opens. If the
user enters a key value, the dialog changes to the state “data changed”; or if the user activates the
Search button, the dialog changes to the state “search data”, in which certain dialog control
elements may be activated or deactivated.
Distinguishing between dialog states and presentation states prevents these two levels of the dialog
design from being mixed, which would result in complex descriptions for the presentation states
which are only meaningful for dialog states.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Dialogs | 59
Collaborative Functional Design
This chapter helps you to prepare and understand all relevant questions that may occur and need
alignment with the users regarding user interfaces / dialogs.
Sources
The sources for preparing customer interaction and designing dialogs are:
• Dialog design guideline (aka style guides): The dialog design guideline underlying the project
determines the basic “look and feel” of the user interface dialogs being specified. Details include
the design of widgets and their utilization; general designs include the definition of dialog types
or general guidelines for window or mask layouts. Dialog design guidelines usually are
prepared with ergonomics/UX experts that can ensure dialogs of an application are uniform
and especially functional and ergonomic.
• Business processes: Business process models show how the functionality supported by the
application is embedded in the customer’s business processes and workflows. Use cases are
derived from them, and they also supply valuable context information regarding the
environment in which the application will be deployed.
• User stories/Use cases: When a user story/use case has a human actor, there generally needs to
be a dialog that supports the actions of this use case.
• Logical data model and data types: Dialogs that represent data must be in line with the (logical)
data model. More precisely, each attribute from the logical data model must specify how it is
populated: Loaded from an interface, calculated by a use case or entered in a dialog.
• Interviews with the business department, user observation.
• The legacy system supplies designs for information that must be available in a dialog, as well as
the workflows supported to date.
• Standard products / competitor products.
• UI Framework and technical limitations.
Interaction Guidelines
Using prototypes for alignment
The design of dialogs usually takes place in parallel to the design of the business logic. In many
cases, the dialogs help department employees to develop an understanding of the business logic. It
is crucial to use prototypes to align on a common understanding – “a picture tells more than 1000
words”.
Perfect prototypes can provide valuable feedback regarding user interface design, especially the
sequence of dialogs. However, they can also cause the customer to “play” and therefore lose sight of
the objective for the prototype. In this case, it may be better to explain the fundamentals of
navigation to the customer in a workshop based on a sketch-like prototype that already shows “This
is not the final dialog”.
Developers frequently chose to display random data in prototypes. However, meaningful logical
data should be obtained for a prototype. Otherwise, acceptance problems and the risk of
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Dialogs | 60
misunderstandings may arise. Of course, this also applies to screenshots used in the dialog design.
Reduced level of detail for discussions
If possible, discussing the concrete appearance of the dialogs with the customer too early should be
avoided. Nearly all ergonomic experts recommend initial discussions based on sketches or paper
prototypes (also known as paper mock-ups). Paper prototypes simulate the application with the
help of paper. Discussions using an actual dialog lead to the premature consideration of details and
people loosing themselves in details of design. Sketches facilitate the discussion of the general
dialog sequence, handling and information blocks on the dialogs.
Layout (Static Characteristics)
The structure or layout of a dialog is established for each dialog (and possibly for each dialog state).
The following points must be considered in the alignment on menus, input / output fields, lists, and
tables:
• How do labels need to be named, so they can be understood?
• Where is data stored / where does displayed data come from (relationship to the logical data
model)
• For new data: What is the data type and default value to be displayed?
• Is the field optional, required or an output field only?
• Are there special plausibility checks, validations and data verifications needed?
• Is there a need to define new shortcut keys?
As already mentioned, the style guide should already preset a lot of these questions. Especially for
plausibility checks and data types, conversation needs focus on “special cases/the non-obvious”. If
there are date fields for a “From date” and a “To date” and the style guide defines the elements to
use for date picking, there is no need to discuss these fields for each dialog. It is obvious which
layout to use and that the “To date” cannot be before the “From date”. But if, for example, there is a
rule that the "From date" must be in the future, this would be a rule to align on.
If the style guide defines freedom to choose, the following points additionally need to be aligned on
for the window in which the dialog is displayed:
• It needs to be established whether the window can be resized and / or whether minimizing /
maximizing is possible.
• If the dialog of the user interface is based on a window system, we can use both modal and non-
modal dialogs in the logical design of the user interface.
Behavior (Dynamic Characteristics)
The dynamics within a dialog are described by actions and the resulting interface changes. An
action is triggered when the user activates a control element (menu, button) or initiates a specific
keyboard event (using the tab key to change fields), or if all spaces in a certain field are filled.
General Behavior
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Dialogs | 61
• Which action is/which actions are triggered?
• Is user input persistent (stored in the database)?
• Which parts of the action can be performed in the background (separate transaction)?
• How is a transaction started/discarded? Save/cancel button? “Auto-commit at each click”?
• When saving in all other dialogs that also contain data of the dialog to be saved, is an update
carried out or does the user have to update manually?
• What happens when two dialogs with the same logical object are open and the first one is
saved?
• If the title bar and status bar include dynamic elements: What should be displayed?
Dialog states
Used to specify the behavior of a dialog more precisely in different dialog contexts.
• What should the dialog look like when opened (initialized)?
• What should the dialog look like after specific actions, e.g “data read”, “data changed”? For
example, a dialog is in the “initial state” after the window with the empty display opens. If the
user enters a key value, the dialog changes to the state “data changed”; or if the user activates
the Search button, the dialog changes to the state “search data”, in which certain dialog control
elements may be activated or deactivated.
Presentation states
• Is data depending on user rights (visibility/editability)?
• Is data populated based on other fields?
• Are there preconditions before a certain action (button) can be triggered?
Design Guidelines
Design frequently-used dialogs as efficiently as possible
The use cases should be examined to determine if a particular use case (or a sequence of use cases)
is executed frequently by a user. In this case, highly segmented dialogs are often cumbersome, and
an additional “quick input dialog” may be required. This means that in such cases, dialogs or tab
pages are provided – for example, according to the user groups – which contain a different
combination or selection of the same data model relationships, so that the user does not have to
click through several masks or tab pages during processing.
Focus sequence for input fields
Screen navigation details, such as the use of the tab or other input keys and keyboard versus mouse
navigation, are frequently very important to the user. These details have a significant impact on
user productivity! This is why it is important to clearly establish which field receive focus first
(which may depend on the displayed data), how the focus is changed, in which sequence the fields
receive focus etc. These questions provide information regarding special cases and dynamic
behavior requirements, which may result in significant additional implementation costs.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Dialogs | 62
Take steps to ensure usability
We strongly recommend taking steps to ensure usability diligently and on a regular basis. External
usability experts should be consulted for this purpose. As an initial step or in smaller projects,
actually stepping through the masks in earnest is often sufficient. This may be done using paper
prototypes (paper mock-ups), PowerPoint slides, Visio graphics or implemented interface
prototypes. In this context, it may also be necessary to take the “Verordnung zur Schaffung
barrierefreier Informationstechnik nach dem Behindertengleichstellungsgesetz BITV” (directive for
the development of barrier-free information technology according to the laws for the equal
treatment of disabled persons) [BITV] into account.
Consider Styleguide and (technical) limitations
The design of UI needs to consider the style guide, but also the used technologies, frameworks and
architecture decisions. You should know or check at least during design the project style guides to
ensure their compliance. If you feel you need to derive from the style guide or extend it, contact the
responsible UX-Expert/Architect. Beside the style guide, technology is important. What can be done
with the UI Framework, what cannot be done? If, for example, the architectural guideline defines a
pattern to have logic only in the backend and keeping the UI simple, you should avoid defining
complex calculations on UI without having the according discussion with your architects.
Verify dialog consistency
Large projects should have a committee that verifies dialog consistency during development and
quality assurance. The chief analyst and chief architect should be members of such a committee.
While the dialog design guideline establishes the standard, verification is important.
Documentation Guidelines
As concise as possible, as detailed as required
Designing trivial dialogs too detailed in advance usually waste effort, as for trivial dialogs, a lot of
information is not needed. Especially, a 1:1 mapping between a dialog field and an attribute is
normally not required if the field label and attribute name are the same and by that self-
explanatory. In this case, a dialog sketch and references to the entity types might be sufficient.
Diagrams may be too abstract
Activity diagrams or state diagrams represent a formal notation for the description of dialog
sequences. Not all customers accept this notation or have the ability to read the diagrams. In this
case, it is better to describe the dialog sequences verbally or possibly using a rule-based approach.
Quality Assurance
Take advantage of expert knowledge
Quality assurance for mask designs should be provided by ergonomic experts or screen designers
in a timely manner. This is especially important for large projects or those with a large number of
dialogs. If the related know-how is not available internally or in the customer’s ergonomics
department, it needs to be obtained externally as required. If possible, an initial review should be
conducted before the masks are discussed with the customer for the first time – once the customer
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Dialogs | 63
department has seen a mask, it is often “cemented”.
Functional Design: User Interfaces - Documents
The user interface summarizes all parts of the system that are visible to the end user. This includes
dialogs with user interaction, but also elements without further user interaction like generated
documents and automatically sent e-mails. This article focussed on the latter part of user interfaces:
documents, incl. e-mails and other generated output intended for users.
Other than dialogs, print outputs often are facing users external to a system, e.g. the e-mail being
sent to a customer, the order agreement letter sent to the business partner or a tax summary report
PDF.
Overview
The design of a print output includes its layout and data. The generation of documents can be
configurable by a dialog, for example, to include or exclude certain information from the
document. These configuration dialogs are specified like any other dialog. Having such a
configuration ability in place needs well consideration, as the configuration abilities tend to
increase complexity during document generation and may become a severe issue.
The structure of print output definitions largely corresponds to dialog definitions, only a document
of course does not possess any dynamic component.
Basic Concepts
Document Data
The document data contains all data that needs to be printed in an output, categorized by:
• Text blocks: Fixed texts for the print output (changes once in a while, e.g. legal statements,
company address, members of board)
• Master Data: Names and translations of data shown (changes regularly, e.g. product names)
• Process data: Data created as result of a process (changes frequently) e.g. calculated prices
Templates
Templates are the definition of the document including the definition of styles (typo, fonts, sizes,
colors, …) and the layout (placing text, define text placeholders).
Document Engines
Document generation is a generic task independent of specific business needs and thus can and
should be solved using existing document engines. Document Engines, also called document
generators, are used to create print outputs based on templates and data transformed to the
representation needed by the engine.
As document generation thus usually combines custom software with commercial-off-the-shelf
(COTS) building blocks, clean architecture to separate these two parts is especially important.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Documents | 64
Legal Requirements
Certain documents may have legal requirements, such as contract, tax or financial related
documents. Requirements in this area are usually known by the business department and should
be carefully considered.
Typically, legal requirements either relate to archiving needs or data/content of the document.
Collaborative Functional Design
This chapter helps you to prepare and understand all relevant questions that may occur and need
alignment with the users regarding print outputs.
Sources
The sources for preparing customer interaction and designing print outputs are:
• Workflow information: When is print output generated and by whom? When is print output
required and by whom?
• Corporate Identity / Brand and Marketing / Style guides and company specifications establish
standards for the design of print output. This is especially important for form letters etc.
(specifications for layout, font, …).
• Dialog design: The discussion of dialogs results in requirements for print output with specific
information. At the same time, it is necessary to ensure that print output can also be initiated;
this means dialogs for the administration of print output may need to be specified.
• Logical data model and data types: Print outputs that represent data must be in line with the
(logical) data model. More precisely, each attribute from the logical data model must specify
how it is populated: Loaded from an interface, calculated by a use case or entered into a dialog.
• Interviews and coordination sessions with the business department provide feedback for the
layout of print output. At the same time, the logical discussions may result in new information
regarding the data model and the use cases.
• Examples prepared by users - this usually is easy to do for documents
• The legacy system supplies information. Depending on the change strategy, the given
documents either serve as to be implemented example (no changes should be visible to
document receivers) or only as baseline to define a new document design (improvements to be
provided to the document receiver).
• Standard products / competitor products.
• Document generation framework and technical limitations.
• Specifications for reporting tools and hardware supply constraints for the design of print
output.
Data Security
• Batch: Batch processes produce print output, especially for logging purposes.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Documents | 65
Interaction Guidelines
Functional requirements / Questions to clarify
• What is the layout of the print output?
• How does data on the layout relate to entities and attributes of the data model?
• Which data needs to be determined on-the-fly during document generation (algorithms)?
• Is there any grouping of data needed and if so, what are the grouping rules?
• User authorization requirements: Who is allowed to create the document? Which data is a user
allowed to see (e.g. a country can only create documents with data owned by this country)?
• Are there any relations to use cases, dialogs or batches (e.g. the document is an invoice and the
successful invoice creation triggers payment)?
• Organizational requirements: Where is the output generated? When is it generated (online,
batch, using an archive system or document management system…)? How does it reach the
recipient?
• Is there any business state related to the creation of a document (e.g. when creating an invoice
setting an order to "invoiced")?
Non-Functional requirements / Questions to clarify
• How fast must print output of this type be available? Is it e.g. directly handed out to a customer?
(Performance)
• How much print output is required in a certain period of time? (Quantity structure and
throughput requirements)
The use of print output types results in a simpler functional design if there are several similar print
outputs. A print output type jointly describes print output with a similar layout and content.
Characteristics described for print output types include the following:
• Common layout: E.g. representation of individual datasets in a table and information about the
list contents.
• Selection criteria: Selection parameters for the datasets included in the print output.
• Sorting: Fixed sort order for datasets of this type or sort criteria that can be selected.
• Grouping: Dataset grouping according to certain criteria, calculated summaries such as sums or
representation of grouping in the layout if applicable.
• Fixed Text in Template or Configurable?
The below table shows typical aspects that need to be defined as part of functional design:
Aspect Description
Title Used for the unique identification of print output.
Brief description What are we talking about?
Trigger Who triggers the print output?
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Documents | 66
Aspect Description
Preconditions What conditions need to be met at the outset?
Input What information (search criteria etc.) is passed to the print output?
Scenarios What happens during the process?
Results What logical content is returned by the print output?
Execution How often is the print output generated?
frequency
Quality What are the quality requirements for this print output?
requirements
Notes What other relevant information should be included?
Legal and regulatory aspects
Other than dialog, for print outputs it might be needed to consider further legal and regulatory
aspects. Examples for these kinds of aspects are:
• An invoice document is only allowed to be created once with a unique invoice number.
• An order must state certain information, e.g. the full name and address of the ordering person
or organization.
• Data privacy must be especially considered, as data once printed might be visible to people not
eligible.
Using prototypes for alignment
In many cases, the dialogs help department employees to develop an understanding of the business
logic. It is crucial to use prototypes to align on a common understanding – “a picture tells more than
1000 words”.
Balance the detail

Be detailled with the data / Business case shown on the prototype, focus on
completeness not detail for the layout.
The graphical representation shows the layout of the future print output. This may range from a
simple table to a complex output structure with various page headers, grouping changes etc. All
output fields, literals, graphics etc. of the future print output are shown. The goal of the illustration
is to come as close as possible to the artifact being implemented. However, the preparation of the
layout should focus on completeness as well as the sequence of the elements, and not their precise
visual representation. If different layouts are possible due to input parameters or calculations
during the process, all layouts must be represented with a reference to the corresponding scenario
in the description.
Design Guidelines
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: User Interfaces - Documents | 67
Replacement of legacy systems
When legacy systems are being replaced, it is essential to investigate what print output is
distributed to whom and in what form. These outputs must be taken into account for the functional
design of the new system. In some cases, legacy systems include numerous templates for print
output that is no longer used. Attention: In many cases, there are also “custom solutions” that
generate printed lists from the legacy database. It is necessary to determine what will be done with
these lists.
Using document generators
Many customers use report generators as standard tools to prepare certain types of print output.
The operating conditions for these report generators must be examined. Requirements for the
interfaces and layout of print output can be identified.
• Static text, configuration and master data and process data
• Description of the requirements to generate print output (hardware requirements, performance
requirements, software requirements such as reporting tools, …).
• Prototype for the approval of print output by the users.
• Relationship to dialogs or batches where the generation of print output is initiated.
• Relationship to use cases where print output is processed.
• Description of the layout.
• If applicable, a description of the processing logic for print output data (data sources, summary
and grouping rules etc.).
Documentation Guidelines
Quality Assurance
Technically experienced departments can also verify the assignment of fields on print output to the
entities and attributes of the data model. The developer requires the layout and the assignment of
fields to the attributes of the data model in order to implement the print output.
Notable Tools
• Document generators used in project: Please refer to C3PO Technical View, where you can find
used frameworks in column User Interaction (Green Elements).
Functional Design: Batch Processing
A batch program conducts independent processing without direct user intervention during the
process. The logic to be processed should be described in the same way as logic triggered by dialogs
or system interfaces (see Use Cases and Application Functions). Thus, clarifications needed for
batches focus on the automation part of triggering as well as mass-data processing, which is often
the case for batches.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Batch Processing | 68
Overview
Batches are artifacts that need to be specified for an application. While they usually do not occur as
often as dialogs, use cases, data models, etc., they are still a critical core element of the application
in most cases. Just like all other artifact types of an application, batches are specified separately.
One of the characteristics documented for a use case is whether this use case is executed directly in
connection with a human actor or viewed as a batch. One example is a “payment”, which may be
individually processed online and/or by bulk processing as a batch.
To design a batch, the following stakeholders may be relevant:
• business department
• operations
• developers
The focus of their interest is very different; the description has to meet the needs of the following
two groups. The business department focuses on the logical principles, timing, volume and
dependencies of batch programs. Employees in the production environment want to ensure that
the batch programs can be integrated into the processes of the existing application structure.
Operations are interested especially in resource consumption as well as handling of batches in case
of failure.
Collaborative Functional Design
This chapter helps you to prepare and understand all relevant questions that may occur and need
alignment with the users regarding batch processing.
Sources
The sources for preparation are:
• Results of the use cases design.
• Interfaces of external applications, logical data model and logical data types components. Input
and output files for batches frequently represent application interfaces defined in the
specification of interfaces of external applications. When batches use database tables, these are
defined in the data model and data type index. This information is referenced by the batch
processing component.
• The business department defines requirements for data evaluation, printed lists and reports to
be implemented as a batch.
• Employees in the production environment formulate requirements for processing time, the
design of the batch-user interface, the restart procedure and configuration options.
Interaction Guidelines
• What is the trigger to start?
◦ Time-triggered (cron-job)
◦ Event or message based
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Batch Processing | 69
◦ Manually by user interface
• Are there any relevant dependencies?
◦ Do other batches need to run first?
◦ Are there batches not allowed to run in parallel?
◦ Are there further conditions to be considered for a batch run?
◦ Does the batch need to be configurable? What for?
• What data needs to be accessed, what data is produced?
◦ Input data: Which data does the batch need to access (e.g. database, files, …)?
◦ To what database tables or files is the output data written, and in what format? Which user
rights are needed for the batch to operate successfully?
◦ For printing batches: Reference to the print output component.
• What are constraints for performance?
◦ What is the estimated data volume?
◦ Runtime behavior of the batch: Does the amount of data affect the processing time of the
batch?
◦ Is the processing time a linear function of the amount of data being processed?
◦ Do large data volumes cause central memory problems?
◦ Are resources utilized within acceptable limits?
◦ Data throughput to external application(s)?
◦ Communication speed across networks?
◦ Batch processing may be subject to special quality requirements (e.g. regarding the
processing time for individual batches).
• How robust does the batch need to be?
◦ What happens in case of failure?
◦ How flexible is batch processing in case of an error? Does the batch stop or log, skip and
continue?
◦ How should the batch be restarted in case of a stop? Does it only process the data that were
not processed previously (restart)? Can it be restarted so that all data are processed again
(rerun)?
• What needs to be aligned with operations (if not operated by a DevOps Team)?
◦ How can a batch be monitored and by whom?
◦ Is it operators only (management console) or is there a need for business users to manage
batches (user interface within application)?
◦ What is the form and content of logs and error logs?
Design Guidelines
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Batch Processing | 70
Early involvement of technical experts
As batches may cause complexity based on the non-functional needs, it is especially crucial to early
involve technical experts and discuss in input gathered from the customer if these experts are not
part of the customer conversation anyway. During this alignment, additional questions and hints
for design sketches may arise that need again clarification with the customer.
Documentation Guidelines
Table style description
Usually, a batch can easily be documented using tables like the following example:
Aspect Description
Brief description Batch for mass-invoicing based on orders
delivered since last batch run.
Trigger and Frequency Time-Based Trigger, Nightly at 11pm
Preconditions Import of delivered orders must be completed
and ended successfully.
