Functional Design: System Interfaces
System Interfaces handle all design needs for interfaces between a service and the external
applications/services it needs to communicate with.
Before preparing a specification for a new logical system, it is necessary to clarify which business
processes or use cases will be supported by which system. This analysis then establishes the precise
scope of the specification. Which systems communicate with each other is also established. Two
cases must be differentiated:
• The new system utilizes data or services, e.g. application functions of another system. In this
case, the new system is the “caller” / user of the interface.
• The new system provides data and services to other systems. In this case, there are use cases in
which the external applications appear as actors (e.g. initiators of the use cases). The new
system is activated via the interface.
There is also a section more focused on the design of internal interfaces.
Overview
The design of a system interface covers the needed knowledge on external applications,
communication process sketch, data handling incl. transactional needs, identification of business
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: System Interfaces | 72
processes, and usage scenarios of that external application interface. The definition of external
interfaces focusses on the contract regarding the interaction between the systems that needs to be
aligned.
For the alignment on system contracts, we usually need to consider:
• Business department: functional and non-functional requirements
• IT department: Interface and system communication governance
• Contract partners: Responsible persons for the external application, usually represented by
customer employees or other suppliers
• Developers: Implementing the interface contract
• Project Management: Needs knowledge on impacted external systems for project planning and
risk management
Take care and plan for external interfaces
The design of interfaces to external applications is just complex, but often cared less about as they
are supposed to be of technical nature only. This article summarizes all aspects that might look
technical at first sight, but do have severe impact on business processes. Therefore, we recommend
spending as much care and effort on system interfaces than you do on user interfaces! It is
especially crucial to have sufficient time to coordinate and review with contact persons responsible
for the external applications. A comprehensive understanding of the external applications is
required for the specification of interfaces of external applications. This knowledge must be
developed early in the project. The analysis of external applications is very time-consuming:
Comprehensive documentation must be examined, and the users, developers and operators of the
external applications must be consulted. In some cases, very little documentation is available so
that documenting the external applications is required. In addition, the contact persons for the
external applications are not actively involved in the project and may therefore be difficult to
reach.
Manage project dependencies
In many cases, errors and misunderstandings are only revealed by integration tests when it is too
late to make changes without exceeding deadlines or budgets. If possible, integration should be an
ongoing process during development and not only at the very end. This approach frequently
reveals problems during the developer test. The availability of the external applications for testing
is required. This must be considered during project planning.
Names and brief descriptions of the interfaces are important for project planning and risk
management in the project. Each interface of an external application is an additional project risk!
Information about the complexity of the interface, requirements for the external application and
names of responsible contact persons are of interest for management purposes.
Basic Concepts
Interface Contract
For the customers of the system being developed and the persons responsible for the external
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: System Interfaces | 73
application, the interface document is contractual in nature: It describes what is expected of the
external application and / or what the external application can expect from the system being
developed. The customer also enters into a contract with the vendors who develop the specification
and develop the system. The customer ensures that the external applications will provide the
required services, which has an impact on the warranty obligations of the vendors.
To avoid subsequent misunderstandings, the specification developer should inform the customer of
the contractual nature of the specification for interfaces of external applications in a timely
manner prior to acceptance of the specification.
The interface documentation also describes necessary modifications to the external applications.
Therefore, the interface descriptions must be as precise as possible. If applicable, separate
“interface agreement” documentation can be used for a contractual agreement between the system
and external application. These documents refer to the interface specification.
Utilized interface vs. interface offered
The own system utilizes an interface of an external application if it determines the point in time
when the exchange of data is initiated. In the reverse case, the own system offers an interface to the
external application. In the special case of publish / subscribe services, the user of an interface
registers for a distribution list; the interface provider then sends messages to all registered
recipients at a point in time chosen by the provider.
Online vs. offline
With online interfaces, the exchange of data results in immediate processing by the target system.
With offline interfaces, a data structure (message, file, …) is stored for the target system for
processing at a later point in time, e.g. as part of an overnight batch run.
Synchronous vs. asynchronous
With synchronous interfaces, the calling process locks until the called system has sent back a
response. With asynchronous interfaces, the calling process immediately continues with additional
local steps. If a response is expected from the called system, it must be integrated into the process
using an asynchronous mechanism such as an inbox.
Stateless vs. stateful
For stateless interfaces, each exchange of data is self-contained and independent of all other
exchanges. Stateful interfaces differentiate between various data exchange steps that may only be
executed in a defined sequence (protocol).
Public vs. private
Public interfaces offer system functionality in the form of reusable services. In principle, the
connection between the calling and called systems is only established at runtime. Private interfaces
connect two systems by means of a point-to-point connection. The interfaces are optimized to meet
the requirements of one or a few business processes supported by a fixed pair of systems.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: System Interfaces | 74
Legacy system as external system
A second scenario for system interfaces might appear when replacing a legacy system:
• The legacy system might not entirely be replaced; instead, parts of it are retained.
• The legacy system might be entirely replaced at some point in time, but shifting happens step by
step.
In both cases, the legacy system becomes (interim) an external application to the new system, so
that the information above also applies to the legacy system:
Detailed knowledge regarding the functionality of the legacy system is required for the
specification of interfaces. While this may be difficult to communicate to the customer (the legacy
system is supposed to be replaced; why analyze it again), it is definitely essential.
Collaborative Functional Design
This chapter helps you to prepare and understand all relevant questions that may occur and need
alignment with the users regarding system interfaces.
Sources
The sources for the preparation of alignments on system interfaces are:
• Business processes and use cases
• Data model
• Documentation of external applications.
• Contact persons for external applications.
• Documentation, code and contact persons for the legacy system if applicable.
Interaction Guidelines
• What validations are conducted?
• What happens if they fail (default values, error messages, ignore etc.)?
Knowledge about the external application
For the design of the interface, we need the following information about the neighboring system:
• Identification and brief description of the external application (if possible, the logical role in the
overall process, special logical role in respect to the system being developed and logical /
technical classification within the application structure).
• What does the system do? What is the status (individual software, new system under
development, standard software – expected new releases…).
• Identification of contact persons / responsible persons for the external application.
• System platform of the external application (operating system, TP monitor or application server
if applicable, middleware etc.).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: System Interfaces | 75
Knowledge about the interface of an external application
The description of an interface of an external application includes the following information:
• Brief description of the interface.
• Evaluation of the logical complexity of the interface.
• Classification of the interface (classification criteria see below).
• Logical interface description.
• Data structure for the exchange of data.
• Communication process.
• Interface utilization profile.
• Transaction concept and error handling.
If the interface is subject to foreseeable changes, we identify the possible changes, provide
information on the expected frequency of changes or note points in time and contact persons from
whom we can subsequently obtain information on changes.
Data structure for the exchange of data
A logical description of the data being exchanged is provided for each step in the communication
process. The following information is included in the detailed specification for each data element:
• Identification of the data element.
• Data type and value range.
• Representation in the data model of the system.
• Optional / mandatory element? If applicable, complex conditions under which the element must
be present.
• Comments
The data models of the external application and the own system normally deviate from each other.
This is why the above-mentioned logical descriptions are respectively required for both systems. In
addition, mapping must be specified in order to show how the representation of data in the
recipient system can be derived from the representation of data in the originating system. Any
required filters, splitting or concatenation between the data that is sent and received must also be
described. Additional information includes:
• If documentation for the interface exists, the existing documents are referenced.
The data structures may be described with the help of data models, e.g. in the form of UML class
diagrams. The relevant section of the data model is shown for each interface. A detailed description
of data elements uses “mapping tables” similar to those in the dialog specification.
Design Guidelines
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: System Interfaces | 76
Interface communication process
The communication process is described by the following information:
• Description of the communicating entities: What are the smallest identifiable entities
communicating with each other? (Application systems, representatives of a business process
entity, abstract communication channel end points.)
• Description of the communication process: Who initiates communication? Who does what, and
in what order? Graphics (sequence diagrams, activity diagrams) with additional explanations
are used in the description. Each possible process must be comprehensible for the reader!
• Assurances regarding the order in which messages are received. Example: The communication
process uses a channel concept; it is guaranteed that a message from the channel is only
processed after the preceding message from the same channel was processed successfully.
However, messages in separate channels can overtake each other.
• Lifespan of the communication relationship if applicable, connection pooling.
UML sequence diagrams or activity diagrams may be used to describe the technical data exchange
protocols.
Aspects to cover in an interface contract
The content of the interface contract between systems:
• Identification and characterization of external applications
• Communication process (coarse-grained).
• Identification of business processes and use cases that utilize the interface.
• In case of master data interfaces: Identification and characterization of master data.
• Error situations and error handling from a logical perspective (this also includes the logical
effects of technical errors).
• Transaction support
• Quality requirements.
We avoid the redundant specification of information already described in the use cases, the data
model or the quality requirements. References to this information are included as required.
Interface utilization profile
Based on the interface utilization profile, the quality requirements that need to be considered in the
specification will vary. Users and providers of an interface have to reach an agreement regarding
the load placed on the provider system by the users. The following aspects may be considered to
characterize the load:
• Characterization of the period type for calls (points in time or periods when calls take place).
• Number of calls per unit of time during periods of maximum load.
• Number of calls per unit of time as a long-term average.
• Number of simultaneous calls (especially for state-based interfaces).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: System Interfaces | 77
• Scope of transferred data per call.
• Measure of data complexity or measure of the processing load in the provider system.
The following aspects may be considered to characterize the user’s expectations for interface
performance and availability:
• Response time for online interfaces.
• Maximum duration of batch processing for offline interfaces.
• Maximum throughput (ultimate load).
• Tolerable downtime due to system errors.
• Minimum requirements for regular online time.
Transaction concept and error handling
The description of the transaction concept and error handling includes the following information.
The focus is on logical errors. This also includes the logical effects of technical errors.
• Interface support for transactions that can be rolled back:
◦ (No support.)
◦ For each call, a compensating call can be defined that reverses the original call in case of
successful execution.
◦ The use of the asynchronous interface for a transaction only leads to the dispatch of
messages when the transaction is successfully concluded by commit.
◦ The interface represents an XA-compatible resource for distributed 2PC transactions.
• Error semantics: Even in case of system crashes, the following is guaranteed:
◦ Each asynchronous message sent with a unique message ID is only processed exactly once.
◦ Each call is automatically repeated until it has been successfully concluded at least once
from a technical perspective.
◦ Each call was executed no more than once.
◦ (No guarantee.)
• Classification of recognized error situations (e.g. technical error to be corrected, fatal technical
error, logical error etc.) and logical interface responses.
• Logical error handling mechanisms:
• Timeout.
• Maximum lifespan of messages.
• System response when the external application is not available: Should the system also be
halted? How can processing continue?
• Error logs and their structure.
• Flow of information in case of errors.
• Effective radius of errors:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: System Interfaces | 78
• Restart capability / restart points for batches.
• Deactivation of the interface for additional calls in case of errors.
With some external applications, changes cannot be rolled back once they have been made.
Therefore, it is necessary to specify how continued data consistency will be guaranteed in such a
case.
Documentation Guidelines
Logical interface description
The logical interface description includes the following information:
• In what use cases is the interface required?
• In case of master data interfaces: What master data is supplied over the interface?
• What are the requirements for the quality and timeliness of master data?
• What logical services, e.g. application functions, are provided by the interface? (Through
external applications or the own system.)
• What is the logical background of the data being exchanged?
Quality Assurance
Notable Tools
• Please refer to the BA Toolbox.
Functional Design: Use Cases
A system use case specifies one or more process steps of a business process that have to be
supported by the application. It describes the behavior and interaction of an application as a
reaction to a specific query or action by an actor. The description of a use case explains the
externally visible as well as the detailed internal application behavior in the language and from the
perspective of the user.
Overview
Use cases always include an interaction of the specified application with at least one external actor.
This actor may be a human user, another use case (such as a batch), or an external or partner
application. The interaction itself may be initiated by the actor or by the application. This means the
trigger can be an external event (e.g. a call by the user) or an internal event in the specified
application (e.g. a timed event). The individual steps within a use case can be referred to as an
activity or an action. There is no generally applicable definition in the corresponding literature.
Cockburn ([Coc01]) for example speaks of “Action Steps”, UML [Oes05] describes “activities” and the
online help system in Enterprise Architect describes “[…] a real world sequence of operations”. We
will use the term “action” throughout this document.
Each use case serves to communicate with the user and is used as the basis of design and testing.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Use Cases | 79
This means there are three basic target groups: The customer, the designers and the testers.
• The term "user" is somewhat vague: In our case, it refers to the department that supplies
information for the future application and the department that will subsequently use the
application. These may, of course, be different departments. It is important to note that one
cannot always assume that the department has the same knowledge regarding the specification
of software systems as we do (method, approach, UML etc.). This means the use cases must be
easy to understand but formally precise. They need to be fully comprehensible to the customer,
since it is the customer who ultimately approves them.
• The use cases form the basis for the design. This in turn means that the use cases must be fairly
detailed so that the designers can implement the application correctly.
• Based on the use case definition, the testers prepare the test case specification as well as the test
cases. This means the use cases have to contain all relevant information for the testers.
Although the testers normally do not have detailed technical knowledge, they still require
extensive details in order to specify appropriate test cases.
Thus, three different perspectives have to be considered in the development of use cases: The
logical view, which is usually somewhat more coarse-grained, the design perspective, which
requires very detailed descriptions, and the test perspective, which falls in between the other two.
All the target groups must be taken into account when preparing use cases. Meeting the needs of all
stakeholders and perspectives is the art of writing use cases.
Basic Concepts
Use Case vs. User Story
There are quite some views on the difference. We found the view of the blog article User Story vs.
Use Case quite helpful. Considering this blog entry and internal experiences from our projects, we
see the following approaches:
• Agile: User story only, no use cases
• Agile: User story for alignment, use case for documentation
• Agile: Use Case for features/epics, user story for stories
• Incremental/Waterfall: Use case for coarse and detailed concept, no user stories
• Incremental/Waterfall: Use case to address IT stakeholders (coarse, structural view), user story
to address business (detailed, user centric view)
Collaborative Functional Design
This chapter helps you to prepare and understand all relevant questions that may occur and need
alignment with the users regarding user interfaces / dialogs.
Sources
The sources for preparing customer interactions to discuss use case details are:
• Descriptions of business processes
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Use Cases | 80
• Interview with the business departments
• Interviews with the developers of legacy applications
• Observation of processes in the area where the application being developed will be used
• Handbooks
• Specification documents of the legacy application
• CR documents of the legacy application
• Project definition / contract specification
Interaction Guidelines
Design Guidelines
Identification of use cases
The first question that arises in the preparation of use cases is “How and where do I find use
cases?”. The sources of use cases were already identified in Section 11.1 Characteristics: Interviews,
handbooks, observation etc. All of these represent data sources that can be used to derive use cases.
Precisely defining what constitutes a use case in the information provided and what does not is
more difficult. Unfortunately there are no clear rules. The only way is to approach the search in a
manner similar to the preparation of objects and classes in object-oriented development – with
heuristic knowledge, common sense and the idea to find certain words.
Identification of a use case
Use cases represent the actions of a user, a batch or an external application executed during
interaction with the application. Therefore it is important to find and describe all actions of the
potential users. The focus is on the logical actions and not so much on technical processes. We are
primarily interested in how the administrator calculates the total price for a complex invoice, but
not whether the interim result of such a calculation has to be stored as double, int or long. These
are elements of data modeling, not the specification of use cases. While we are looking for nouns
when modeling classes (“customer”, “vehicle”, “booking” etc.), verbs identify an action for the
identification of use cases: “book”, “print”, “close”, “cancel” etc. It is no wonder that the name of a
use case includes a verb. In the specification, a use case is described from a purely logical,
conceptual view. For example, merely “searching” for information does not constitute a separate
use case, unless a user begins a process for the sole purpose of searching without intending to
execute a subsequent activity. The use case “Approve invoice” always includes the search for the
corresponding invoice. The use case “Record invoice” presumably also includes the search for the
invoice to be recorded. This means individual steps may be described multiple times in the
specification. If these steps are complex (e.g. due to numerous search criteria or complicated
matching of the search criteria against the data model), this description may be incorporated in a
separate application function.
Workshops & interviews
It is expedient to initially use workshops, interviews etc. in order to collect and briefly describe all
these actions – if possible without evaluating them right away. Instead, all of these ideas should be
put on a wall and subsequently sorted into cohesive logical groups. The goal of this approach is to
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Use Cases | 81
see all use cases in context in order to maintain a general overview as well as comparing the
granularity of the use cases identified so far.
Documentation Guidelines
Textual Description
The use case description is the basis of any documentation for a use case. It includes all information
the reader requires to understand the use case in text form. The description is provided in a
structured manner. We use attributes to precisely define which information we record where. This
approach also makes it easier to record information with software tools, which normally provide
these attributes or allow the user to define them.
The following attributes are required:
Aspect Description
Title Unique identification of a use case. It should be phrased to provide a succinct
indication of the use case objective.
Brief description What are we talking about? A brief summary of the use case in a few
sentences. This text serves to clarify the content in overviews, tables,
evaluations etc.
Actors Who triggers the use case? The actors of a use case distinguish the human or
technical users. The actor triggering the use case is referred to as the primary
actor. In many cases, additional actors referred to as secondary actors are
involved in the execution of a use case. If different behaviors depending on
different actors are required, we have to create either separate scenarios or
(even better) separate use cases. Scenarios should only be used, if the logic in
differing only slightly to find a good balance between simple system design
and design efforts.
The list of actors contains the actors that interact with the application via the
use cases. Actors embody a role assumed by a human user or a technical
system (e.g. an external application) in respect of the application. The list
identifies all actors and briefly describes them with keywords. Names for the
actors are based as closely as possible on the names established in the
customer’s domain. It is possible that, for example, there are various
specializations of the customer clerk role with different rights. If this is the
case, we create several actors for this purpose and organize them in a
generalization hierarchy.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Use Cases | 82
Aspect Description
Trigger What causes the use case to be executed? What is the reason? The trigger is
the logical cause for the execution of a use case. It is the starting point for
interaction with the application. For example, the trigger for a use case
“Record reservation” could be a phone call or visit by a customer to a travel
agency. Only logical triggers are mentioned: Initiating an action by selecting a
menu item is not a trigger. This would be a technical trigger which is not
relevant here. We are interested in the reason why the menu item was
selected in the first place. If there are several possible triggers for the
execution of a use case, they are explained separately.
Preconditions What conditions need to be met at the outset? The preconditions describe all
prerequisites that must be met so the use case can be executed. If there are
several preconditions, they are listed and described separately. All
preconditions must be met. Example: PrintOrder • The order must have the
status “created” • The order must have at least one position If one of the two
preconditions is not met, the print output will not contain correct data. If state
diagrams are also used to document the use case, it is meaningful to describe
the preconditions based on states; e.g. “…the booking must have the status
‘Open’”.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Use Cases | 83
Aspect Description
Scenarios What happens during the process? The scenarios of a use case are the core
element of the use case description. They describe the sequence of actions
performed by the actor and the application, which is required in order to
achieve the objective of the use case. The description is in text form, divided
into individual steps (“actions”). Each step is numbered and includes
information on who or what initiates or executes this step, e.g. “The user…” or
“The application…”. In order to maintain readability and improve
comprehension, a scenario has only very few branches. In general, the
description is also in text form when using a tool to record use cases. However,
if sufficient options to create comprehensible documentation are provided, the
description may also be prepared in the form of diagrams with notes in text
form and / or a separate description.
In the description, we distinguish between success and alternative scenarios.
The success scenario of the use case is the error-free success case identified by
the objective of the use case. Each use case has exactly one success scenario. In
addition to the success scenario, alternative scenarios can also be specified.
These describe separate special cases, e.g. in case of special constraints, as well
as errors in text or table form. In regards to errors, we limit ourselves to
logical errors. We do not describe technical errors such as the failure of a
network connection
These scenarios are described separately. This results in a concise
representation and reduces the complexity of the individual scenario
descriptions in the specification documents. The ordinal number from the
main scenario is added behind the title in brackets, so that the reader knows
where the alternative scenario branches off the main scenario. Within the
alternative scenario, numbering restarts at 1. If a “return” into the process of
the main scenario takes place at the end of an alternative scenario, the return
point is specified by its number. Otherwise, the results of the alternative
scenario must be included in the attribute Results. Note: Very complex use
cases may also have several success scenarios of equivalent importance (in
terms of several main scenarios). However, most cases involve subordinate
alternative scenarios (e.g. sub-scenarios). In the specification, we explicitly do
not use the terms main and sub-scenario for classification since this is
sufficiently expressed by the terms success and alternative scenario. If several
success scenarios are identified, segmentation into several independent
specializations of the use case should be considered.
Relationship to the data model The use cases refer to the underlying logical
data model where possible. In particular, the entities affected by an action are
identified for application actions. This relationship facilitates the logical
understanding of the reader and helps verify the consistency of the use case.
In general, only the object should be identified but any existing attributes
should not. Updates for possible subsequent changes would only result in
unnecessary additional effort. Therefore, it is sufficient to say the following:
“Customer address information is obtained from the object
[component]:customer”. If it is necessary to specify the exact attributes for
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Use Cases | 84
Aspect Description
Result What information is supplied by the use case? The result of a use case includes
two areas: The description of the affected entities and the description of return
values.
Description of the entities The state of all entities affected by the use case is
described. Which entities have changed directly, which have changed
indirectly by calling use cases within this use case, etc. The description of
changes must not be on the attribute level, but must inform the reader about
all relevant changes. E.g. a change to the attribute “Street” is not relevant; the
following statement would suffice: “The customer address was changed.” But
if the customer ID is changed and is a logical key, this must be described.
Description of return values If a use case obtains data and returns it to the
caller, these data are specified here. However, we are not talking about an
exact definition of a return object in the technical sense. Instead, the
information expected by the caller has to be described at a high level. For
example, a result might be described as follows: “The use case returns a list of
objects with entity type service”. Unless all scenarios return the same results,
the results must be described per scenario for all existing scenarios.
Furthermore, along with the results we return logical errors. Logical errors
are errors that result from incorrect logical behavior, e.g. by the actor: Input of
a non-existent customer number or input of an invalid combination of search
criteria. These errors must be predictable; otherwise, they could not be
documented here. If such an error can occur, it is described in an alternative
scenario and the results, e.g. the message, documented here in this section.
Technical errors like “Database not available” or “Server not responding” are
not documented with the use case. They are subject to the design phase.
Execution How often is this use case executed within the application? The execution
frequency frequency specifies how often the use case is executed. The frequency either
refers to certain events (e.g. once each time the application is launched) or to
time intervals (e.g. ten times per administrator and month).
Quality What are the quality requirements for this use case? Quality requirements are
requirements usually described in a separate general document, e.g. response times, security
etc. If there are quality requirements that apply to this use case in particular,
they are documented in this attribute – possibly in the form of a reference to
the requirements specification. A reason may be that for the use case stricter
or less strict requirements apply.
Notes What other relevant information should be included? The attribute Notes
includes everything that has not been documented in the other attributes or
where no separate attribute was defined in the project. Examples include
review notes, history descriptions etc.
This list represents the recommended minimum of attributes for the description of a use case.
Attributes may be omitted on a project-by-project basis. However, it is important to examine
precisely why this is necessary. On the other hand, other attributes can be defined in addition to
these (logical) attributes when required. From the project management perspective in particular,
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Use Cases | 85
information such as status, assigned project team member or scope is relevant. The attributes are
individually described in the following sections.
Activity diagram
The activity diagram can be structured by swim lanes. We then use two swim lanes: One for the
actions of the primary actor and one for those of the application. In order to avoid the risk of
prematurely specifying a technical design, we do not differentiate between the client and server. If
the level of complexity permits, all scenarios should be depicted in an activity diagram. This allows
the reader to discern all possible process paths of a use case at a glance. A scenario will then form a
path through the activity diagram of the use case, with the smallest possible number of branches.
The scenarios may be highlighted or differentiated through the use of color if applicable. Should the
diagram become too complex if all scenarios are integrated, a separate diagram must be prepared
for each scenario.
State and sequence diagrams
As an alternative or in addition to the flowchart, other types of diagrams may help document the
business logic in special cases: State diagrams and sequence diagrams. State diagrams are used if
the state of objects has to be described in detail. Sequence diagrams show the process used to
exchange messages between objects. These diagrams focus on the time sequence of this exchange.
Superordinate Documentation
In addition to the documentation of an individual use case, it is necessary to document information
that is generally relevant for several use cases on a higher logical level as part of the specification of
use cases. This information relates to two areas: The joint representation of use cases in the form of
lists and diagrams and the documentation of actors in the form of lists and actor goal tables.
Use Case Diagram
Examples for use case scenarios and validation
Textual description
Customer pays by credit card.
1. The user selects “Payment type credit card”.
2. The application reads the credit card types recorded in the master data and provides a list of
them.
3. The user selects the credit card type.
4. The application validates the input and requests information according to the credit card type
selected.
5. The user enters the required data (card number, checksum etc.).
6. The application validates the input data and verifies them against the bank server.
7. The application displays a success message to the user.
An example of an alternative scenario for the preceding example would be as follows: Bank server
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Use Cases | 86
validation (6).
1. The validation of the checksum fails.
2. The user is prompted to re-enter data.
3. The input is validated again.
4. If successful, resume the main scenario at step 7.
5. In case of error: Terminate the use case.
The description of a use case is a structured text description in table form. It includes the attributes
listed above: Brief description, Actors, Trigger, Preconditions, Scenarios, Results, Execution
frequency, Quality requirements and Notes.
Flowchart
Flowcharts are used for the graphical representation of a use case. UML activity diagrams (also see
the following section) are suitable for this purpose. Less formal graphical notations for process
descriptions may be used for additional, concise overview explanations of the processes. There is
no specified format; the chief analyst selects the notation that adds the most value for a specific
project. Other types of diagrams may be used in order to precisely specify data dependencies, state
models or interaction sequences.
Formal graphical process descriptions such as UML activity diagrams, Petri networks or program
flowcharts can be used in order to improve the readability of use cases. Flowcharts allow us to
describe the control flow of a use case in graphical form. The graphical representation of the
control flow makes it easier to comprehend the sequence of individual actions in complex
branching structures. It is also possible to include responsibilities in these diagrams. In principle, a
graphical process description that contains a separate diagram for each scenario (success and
alternative scenario) is prepared for each use case. However, a decision has to be made whether
the diagrams can be combined depending on the complexity. This is definitely feasible and also
recommended for “small” use cases. Very complex flowcharts usually indicate that the use case
design may not be entirely appropriate. If the preparation of complex diagrams cannot be avoided,
these can have a hierarchical structure for easier comprehension.
Notable Tools
• Please refer to the BA Toolbox.
Further information
• Blogarticle "User Story vs. Use Case"
Functional Design: Application Functions
Application functions are functions that execute the logical calculations in an application. They are
called in services in order to execute a possibly complex logical function.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Application Functions | 87
Overview
Application functions are required whenever services contain complex processing algorithms.
These complex processing tasks are described by application functions. Certain functions of an
application may be required in many different contexts, so that a uniform description at a central
location is helpful (e.g. searching for order data with search patterns). In the following technical
design, architects need to trade-off, if a single implementation (re-use) or redundant
implementation (reduced dependencies) should be used.
The definition of application functions is mainly used by business departments and by the
developers. The business departments have to decide whether the functionality was understood
and described correctly, so the most current knowledge can be considered for implementation. The
description/documentation must be readable for the business departments in order to be approved.
In addition to readable descriptions, developers mainly require a precise and unambiguous
definition of the functionality.
Basic Concepts
Types of algorithm
You should be aware of the type of algorithm as this may impact your implementation heavily. A
proper design should always be done together with technical architects / design:
• Simple formula: Easy to describe and to define acceptance criteria
• Complex algorithms: Needs some more consideration, especially on if and how they may be
split in several (independent) steps. Acceptance criteria may get complex, so scenario-based
definition might be needed.
• Optimization problem: Focus on parameters and their weights for optimization. Acceptance
criteria can be hard to define. Might need additional efforts to make optimization steps visible
for acceptance.
Collaborative Functional Design
Sources
The sources for the development of application functions are:
• Use cases / User Stories
• Cross-cutting concepts
• Consultations with customer departments
• System specifications and documentation provided by the customer
• In re-engineering projects: Documentation and functionality of the existing system
Interaction Guidelines
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Application Functions | 88
Incremental and/or iterative approach
Depending on the project delivery mode (incremental or iterative), it might make sense to define
and build an application function incremental. Starting with the major scenario, alternate
scenarios might be added step-by-step. For nearly all cases, there is now need to iterate to a correct
calculation as formulas are well defined and not a matter of experience collection or definition
based on results. Of course, in an incremental (agile) setup, a calculation might be build and
reworked iteratively, if the customer needs more time to align correct calculations. Other than user
interaction, this is not a matter of "learning on the way", but to gain speed while needed
clarifications are done.
Use examples and scenarios to align with the customer
Including one or more examples is often worth its weight in gold to improve comprehension by the
business department. Running through scenarios in the form of interaction diagrams or adding
appendixes with tables to the specification document are alternatives, that provide some benefit,
but are not as strong as a common session with the customer running through specific examples.
Design Guidelines
Functions are usually known
The application function should be described as precisely as possible as application functions
usually are not a matter of to be defined user needs, but origin in - more or less - known existing
algorithms from business management, laws or other regularities.
Consider cases - be comprehensive
The alignment with the customer should include success and alternative scenarios. Alternative
scenarios include special cases or errors.
Important data and object flows may also be represented, although this should be done very
sparingly to avoid increasing the complexity of the process description unnecessarily.
Focus on performance needs
Especially when performing complex and thus resource consuming calculations, it is important to
carefully determine cases that lead to a situation where further calculations for either a single
dataset or a set of data is not needed anymore. These abortion cases have to be aligned with
business in order to avoid waste of resources.
Relationships between application functions
In technical design, application functions my be decomposed and calling each other. The
hierarchical decomposition of application functions during business solution design is not
recommended. It makes it more difficult to understand the business alignments and preempts the
design phase.
Documentation Guidelines
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Application Functions | 89
Pragmatics for trivial functions
Application functions are only described for comprehensive, complex processes or functions that
are used frequently in different contexts. Describing an application function for every application-
side activity is not required, esp. simple CRUD functions should not be described in detail.
Describe functions precisely, but without pre-empting the implementation phase
A coarse-grained process description is usually sufficient. Describing the calculation algorithm of
an application function precisely is more important than describing the process of calculation. This
is why only (!) the algorithm is explained in detail, e.g. in pseudo code or with mathematical
calculation rules. However, pre-empting the programming phase is a risk related to the use of
pseudo code. This is why a declarative description is preferred to a procedural one, and pseudo
code should only be used if actually required for the establishment and customer approval of the
algorithm. For the transition to the design phase, it is important to note that any pseudo code
provided is not a replacement for a draft of the functionality being implemented in the design. The
implementation team is responsible for designing the algorithm being implemented.
Simple calculations: Structured Text
If the process is very simple (e.g. if it only covers a handful of steps and does not include any
significant control structures) and can be specified in a comprehensible and efficient manner with
text alone, an explicit graphical representation is not essential. A list with names and brief
descriptions of the application functions is the most basic type of documentation.
Additional description with UML
Formal process description languages are recommended to supplement a structured text
description. We recommend the use of flowchart languages in particular. Flowcharts – preferably
UML activity diagrams – or pseudo code illustrate and specify the process behavior of an
application function. Other UML behavior diagrams such as interaction diagrams (e.g. sequence
diagrams) or state diagrams (e.g. for the main identity processed by the application function) may
also be suitable.
Pseudo Code
In addition to graphical modeling languages, pseudo code may be used to describe the application
functions. Please choose carefully, if this proceeding is helpful as it often used broadly without
need. Pseudo code is meaningful when the function uses complex algorithms since this form of
representation tends to be clear and unambiguous. However, it bears the risk of quasi pre-empting
the implementation and taking the responsibility from the developers that should design and own
the correct implementation. Pseudo code is also difficult for the business department to read. Due
to the given reasons, Pseudo Code should only be used sparingly.
Tables
Tables are suitable for the presentation of essential information when the algorithm itself is
relatively simple, e.g. for price lists, product master data, validation rules with an application
context, validation catalogues per product etc. For complex algorithms, tables may be used to
define cases resulting from the combination of used input data.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Application Functions | 90
Mathematical notations
For calculation functions in the mathematical sense, the corresponding mathematical notation
should be used. Of course this also needs to be comprehensible for the business department.
Example for documentation
In an application that handles tourism processes, the use case “check availability” checks if there is
an available contingency for an object that matches the primary search criteria. This functionality
is offered by the booking component as an application function “check contingency”. A sample
flowchart is not provided at this point. Description of the application function “check contingency”
Aspect Description
Brief description Checks if a contingency is available for a certain
contingent service
Trigger Booking - Check Availability
Preconditions The object being checked is a contingent service
(e.g. also of the corresponding entity type).
Calling use cases/Used in Verify availability
Input Object being checked (e.g. hotel), time period,
restriction (e.g. type of room such as single or
double).
Process (1) The first step is to verify whether the object
meets the restrictions. (2) Subsequently, vacancy
of the desired object is verified for each day
during the specified time period. The capacities
assigned to the entity type “contingent services”
are checked for this purpose. An available
contingency for the object only exists if the
object is vacant on all days.
Result Alternatives:
Yes, the object is vacant
No, the object is not vacant.
If the object that has been checked is vacant, it is
flagged to indicate that it is part of an
availability check that is still in progress.
Quality requirements Since the check is carried out frequently, it is
essential to ensure it can be completed quickly
(max. 10 ms).
Notes The result of this function is merely a snapshot.
The requested object is not reserved.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Application Functions | 91
Quality Assurance
For complex, yet known and understood calculations, a desk test may help to ensure correct
understanding and consideration of the relevant cases before (usually also complex and expensive)
implementation.
To run a desk test, you need:
• the algorithm to test
• the data for testing the algorithm
The algorithm should be used directly from the written description in place, as this is the item to
test (presentation, document, user story, specification, …). The data needs to be prepared for such a
session. The data should cover all scenarios - the standards but also alternate scenarios. To find all
relevant scenarios, please perform the following steps:
• Based on the data used in the calculations, gather:
◦ Value ranges, out of value ranges and boundary values
◦ Combinations of data to create scenarios that bundle data variations
◦ Methods like Equivalence class partitioning help finding scenarios to test.
• Prepare handouts / slides containing the data set for the each scenario of the desk test
• Often, an excel to run the calculations with the test scenario data is helpful - especially in virtual
setup. If you want to use an excel, make all intermediate results visible, so that users are able to
follow the calculation steps and verify.
• For a live setup, you may decide for a more collaborative way of testing (XXX).
The defined scenarios also should be used as baseline for test cases / acceptance criteria.
Functional Design: Data Setup
When replacing system functionality, data must be set up accordingly. This includes functional
configuration, master data, but often also migration of process data from old systems or services.
Without data, a system cannot run properly. Still, the task of data migration is often not considered
early enough in a project. Missing a proper concept can lead to:
• Overtime and/or shift of deadlines, even though the software is ready
• Discussions and frustrations on customer side, as the often are not aware of the efforts they
need to spend
• Late missing requirements, as data often reveals missing use cases
Overview
A migration concept is required whenever the application being developed replaces one or more
legacy systems. Migration requirements that need to be implemented by the concept are
determined as soon as the basics of the system being developed have been established: The logical
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Data Setup | 92
data model, the initial list of use cases and the list of external applications.
For the alignment on system contracts, we usually need to consider:
• Business department: definition of configuration data, defining and/or validating master and
process data to be migrated
• IT department: Support of migration activities, setup of environments for data setup (opt)
• Contract partners: Providing data to be migrated from services/systems under replacement
• Developers: Implementation of migration scripts and procedures
• Project Management: Needs knowledge on migration activities and involved stakeholders for
project planning and risk management
If the complexity of migration requirements is limited, the migration concept can be specified in
the system overview or along with the project, implementation and data model requirements. If the
migration requirements are very complex, it may be wise to conduct the migration in a separate
subproject with more comprehensive documentation.
Basic Concepts
Data Migration as part of replacement of Legacy Applications
Data migration is a (usually large) subtopic for the replacement of a legacy application.
For data migration from a legacy application, it is needed to describe the details of how data is
transferred to the new application when a legacy application is replaced. This is a data migration
process that generally takes place once and may also be completed in stages.
The strategy for the replacement of legacy applications is defined as a project plan that describes
the individual replacement steps. Early in the project, the definition of the replacement strategy
may be comparatively coarse-grained and abstract (e.g. without the identification of concrete
dates). The detailed definition follows in a subsequent version of the project plan. This plan is
supplemented by definitions and explanations of system sections. It may also be necessary to
compare various replacement strategies and explain their advantages and disadvantages.
Configuration Data
All data that configures the system and system behavior, e.g. default language for a country,
applicable discounts for a product, legal category of a sales organization, … Changing Configuration
data sometimes can be done by a user directly, but often needs additional activities, such as
organizational change, data migration and others.
Master Data
Data explicitly maintained by a user, e.g. Product Data, Organizational Data, Assets, Document
Types, … Master data usually does not change on a daily basis.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Data Setup | 93
Process Data
Data produced by the system through user interaction, e.g. aprroving an order will lead to an
approval data entry. Process data is continuously produced by systems.
Collaborative Functional Design
This chapter helps you to prepare and understand all relevant questions that may occur and need
alignment with the users regarding needed activities for data migration.
Sources
The sources for the preparation of alignments on system interfaces are:
• Analysis of legacy applications: Structure and value ranges of datasets, as well as data quality in
the legacy application
• Data model of the application being developed, as a target structure
• Consultations with the customer as well as operators of legacy applications and the application
being developed (constraints for possible migration strategies)
• If already available: Implementation and migration strategy; requirements for the transfer of
legacy datasets can be derived
• Also, if already available: A document with migration requirements
Interaction Guidelines
How to analyze legacy datasets
The data migration is based on a description of the current situation and a thorough analysis of
data in the legacy application. This analysis examines the structure, value ranges and quality of the
data. Special data values must be examined, e.g. the misuse of values such as “-1” for entirely
different purposes. The assumptions and prerequisites to transfer data from the legacy application
to the new application must be described. The treatment of datasets that cannot be automatically
migrated must be clarified with the customer. An agreement has to be reached regarding the extent
to which the dataset has to be automatically convertible and how much manual post-processing is
acceptable. If data from the legacy application have to be transformed into another data format
prior to the transfer to the new application, the complexity of the transformation must be evaluated
in addition to a mapping of the transformation. If the transformation cannot be completed
“overnight”, for example due to time constraints, a (very elaborate) step-by-step approach must be
planned and specified. It is also necessary to clarify who is responsible for acceptance and the
availability of responsible persons.
Analyze the quality of legacy data in a timely manner
The best data transfer algorithms are useless if the data structures only follow the rules in 20% of
all cases and consist of 80% exceptions. This is why the quality of data being migrated should be
analyzed early on.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Data Setup | 94
Close coordination with the responsible persons and administrators of the legacy application
When a legacy application is being replaced, maintaining good contact with the customer’s
employees who are familiar with the legacy application is important. Unfortunately they are
frequently not highly motivated to cooperate since the application they are familiar with is being
replaced and changes result in uncertainty.
Design Guidelines
TODO: To be collected here for future versions.
Documentation Guidelines
The section on data migration from a legacy application describes the details of how data is
transferred to the new application when a legacy application is replaced. This is a data migration
process that generally takes place once and may also be completed in stages. Its consists of the
following three parts that are described more in detail in the subsections:
• List of data mappings for data to be transferred from the legacy application, and the rules for
mapping these data elements from the legacy application to the application being developed
• Additional list of complex mappings for the transfer of data. Complex mappings include
concatenating or splitting fields, inserting standard data, operations for normalizing and de-
normalizing data and grouping data
• List of data validations
List of data elements to be transferred
The list of data elements to be transferred from the legacy application and the mapping rules
should include the following information for each data element:
• Name of the data element in the legacy application
• Data type and value range in the legacy application
• Alternative formats in the legacy application (e.g. numeric values with or without leading
zeroes, text with or without leading spaces)
• Mapping to the data model of the legacy application to the application being developed;
reference to the definition of a complex mapping if applicable (see below)
• Definition whether the transfer of data is mandatory or optional
• Comments
List of complex mappings
The list of complex mappings consists of the following information for each mapping:
• Name of the mapping
• Name of the data element in the legacy application
• Name of the data element in the application being developed
• Definition of the mapping
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Data Setup | 95
• Error handling information
• Logging information for the mapping (e.g. output of datasets with errors)
List of data validations
The list of data validations consists of the following information:
• Name of the validation
• Definition of the validation
• Error handling information (e.g. error output text, applicable default values, rules for ignoring
errors)
• Information for validation logging
Relationship between the data models of the legacy application and the new application
The data being migrated results in requirements for the design of the data model of the new
application. It must be possible to transfer all data from the legacy application to the new
application, e.g. suitable data structures must be provided. It should be possible to largely fill the
structures for the new application using data from the legacy application. Manually editing old
datasets is usually not acceptable. This is why meaningful default values for parts of the new data
structure that cannot be filled may need to be defined. These considerations must be included in
the design of the new data model. The detailed data migration, especially the definition of transfer
algorithms, is completed after finalizing the introduction and migration strategy and preparing the
data model. Additional changes to the data model may be required. The project manager has to
decide how much effort to invest in the initial analysis of the legacy application in order to
minimize the risk of subsequent model changes, which may result from new information obtained
during the analysis of the legacy application while preparing the data migration.
Quality Assurance
Include Stakeholders
For data migration, it is crucial to involve all needed stakeholders. This includes:
• Business departments, IT department or other suppliers that provide data for migration
• Project management to support time planning, timely follow up of tasks and risk management
Notable Tools
• Please refer to the BA Toolbox.
Capgemini Assets
• Template for migration concept
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Functional Design: Data Setup | 96
Architecture patterns
We organize our architecture patterns via the architecture views as proposed by Arc42.
Context patterns
The context view shows the system as a black box, with the integrations to other relevant
system. We plan to collect here useful integration patterns in the future.
• Synchronous vs. Asynchronous integration
• Guidelines for API design based on open standards
◦ REST
◦ GraphQL
◦ gRPC
◦ OData
• Message integration patterns
Building block patterns
The building block view looks into the architecture of a system, i.e. into the black box and
decomposes it into several building blocks.
Deployment patterns
The deployment view illustrates with what, where and how the application is being operated.
Runtime patterns
Patterns useful for the runtime view showing interactions between components might be
collected here in the future.
Building Block Patterns
In our project it is established practice to differentiate between functional and technical building
blocks (s. Architectural Principles). Accordingly we organize building block patterns in the
following sections, together shaping the building block view:
• Functional Decomposition: addressing first the business or application architecture of the
system serving as a foundation
• Macro Architecture: based on the functional decomposition make high level decision on the
architectural style and principles applied on the overarching level affecting all building blocks
• Micro Architecture: depending on the macro architecture chosen, different patterns might still
be applicable per building block which are collected here
Typically the design of the building block view should follow these steps in the given order.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 97
Functional Decomposition
Most of our projects deal with building a custom software application for the business of our
customers. They are custom built since they are tailored to best support the processes of the
customer for which no standard software exists. Typically, such kind of systems cover some
business domain with a lot of functionality and inherent complexity.
In order to tackle the complexity especially with regards to maintainability, we explicitly design an
application architecture (s. Architecture objectives) applying our Architecture principles. In order
to deal with the complexity of the business and the technology independently, we apply the
architecture principle Differentiate software categories (A/T), i.e. we define two views on the
architecture: the application/business architecture and the technical architecture. These views are
disjunct and highlight different aspects of the same software. Please note, that this means
duplication of same aspects should be avoided (such as a functional and technical data model,
which is mostly the same). They are different perspectives on the one architecture:
A-architecture
Contains functional building blocks and their dependencies as well as interfaces between each
other. These building blocks are solely responsible for a business or application concern, i.e. a
certain functionality the software implements. They are completely free of technical details such
as protocols or technical infrastructure services.
T-architecture
The technical architecture complements the application architecture. It defines a blueprint on
how functional building blocks are physically represented in the code and how each building
block should be structured. In addition, it defines technical building blocks such as those for
monitoring, logging, security and other cross-cutting concerns. These views are disjunct and
highlight different aspects of the same software.
The functional decomposition is about identification of the functional building blocks of the A-
architecture. Some of the other architectural principles that we propose for software are fulfilled
by classes of object-oriented languages such as Java or C#. However, classes are not a suitable basic
building block unit for functional modularization. They are much too small! If the class is used as
the basic design unit then the result is that large systems are subdivided into countless classes and
the understanding of the employed architectural principles is completely lost.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 98
Figure 12. Designing with classes
Hence a higher level of abstraction is needed for a solid modularization. Since the business is what
matters, we see a higher priority on the A-architecture and how to decompose the business into
different building blocks. Therefore, the functional decomposition is one of the first activities being
performed also by our business architects. Naturally, we have some overlap between our two
methods ADAM and the SEAGuide as this point.
The decomposition can be performed in different ways, which are collected below.
Patterns
• Strategic Domain Driven Design: as advocated also by ADAM
• Data Driven Decomposition
• Functional decomposition via business capabilities (to be described)
• Functional decomposition via user story maps (to be described)
Internal links
• Agile Design Applied Method: our design methodology toolbox also relies heavily on the
strategic domain driven design
Strategic Domain Driven Design
Overview
Domain Driven Design is an architecture approach with a strong orientation towards the business
domain. It is based largely on a book by Eric Evans (blue book), later on refined by a book from
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 99
Vaughn Vernon detailing the concrete implementation of DDD in an organization (red book). Its
goal is to establish a ubiquitous language across domain and IT experts based on which the
architecture or design of a system can be build. The following figure gives an overview of what
DDD is about.
Figure 13. Overview of Domain Driven Design
As you can see it can roughly be split into two parts: the strategic design (content of this article) and
the tactical design (content of this article). The DDD Reference gives a good summary of all DDD
concepts.
The goal of strategic design is to decompose a large, complex domain into subdomains and
consequently each subdomain into bounded contexts. Dependencies between bounded contexts are
then explicitly traced in a context map and implemented by using one of the provided strategic
design patterns.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 100
Decomposition into subdomains
The actual decomposition into subdomains is a business architecture topic and several methods for
this exist. Most notably big picture event storming can be used as a collaborative format for
identification of subdomains. Typical candidates for this are the parts of the big picture between
pivotal events and swim lanes (s. Event Storming). Each subdomain thus identified can be classified
into one of the following:
Core subdomain
the true business of a company, where it differentiates itself in a unique way from competitors
in a complex context. The uniqueness necessitates that no existing software exists to support the
business as done by the company. It brings a competitive advantage and therefore the
willingness to invest in this subdomain, since it can yield a positive ROI.
Supporting subdomain
the part of the business where no off-the-shelf product implementing this functionality exists,
but where the company cannot really gain a competitive advantage. Caution is advised in those
cases where a supporting subdomain is becoming complex, because it is then actually becoming
a core domain, or the added complexity should be questioned against the business value.
Generic subdomain
the part of the business, where industry solutions simply exist and no gain in own solutions can
be identified.
A very good, more detailed explanation on the above can be found here. The DDD crew provides a
good heuristic for classifying subdomains in two dimensions:
Figure 14. Core Domain Charts technique from the DDD crew
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 101
Decomposition into bounded contexts
Whereas the domain and its subdomains purely address the problem space of a business challenge,
bounded contexts aim at the solutions space. Typically within a large domain, the ubiquitous
language cannot be applied canonically across all contexts. Between the different subdomain
different understandings on the same terms may exist.
A typical example would be the customer: the sales subdomain handles a customer in a completely
different way then the after sales. For the sales subdomain the business opportunities and how to
actually win them is important. The after-sales subdomain is interested however in the bought
products and what service level the customer acquired.
DDD does not aim towards a holistic ubiquitous language, but realistically only aims towards such a
common language understanding in a bounded context.
Bounded Context
the bounded context establishes a ubiquitous language expressed via a domain model in a single
subdomain
The core design task in strategic DDD is to identify these bounded contexts and then to relate them
with each other where dependencies between them exist. For identification event storming is again
recommended within the industry and also by ADAM.
They can be further defined by using a canvas approach as for instance this one (ADAM also
provides an own version of this):
Figure 15. Bounded Context Canvas from DDD crew
For each bounded context, a different architecture can then make sense depending on the
requirements and also the subdomain it lies in. It is especially noteworthy, that Tactical DDD is
often seen as especially valuable for the core subdomain. For bounded contexts in generic or
supporting subdomains, a simple CRUD-based or low code architecture might be a better choice.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 102
Context Mapping
Bounded contexts do not exist standalone, but depend on each other and thus require integration.
Strategic design defines different patterns for mapping and integrating one context with others as
can be seen from the overview above.
Again we only point to the DDD Reference for the definition of those patterns and to this summary
given below by the DDD crew. ADAM in addition provides guidance on how contexts can be related
between each other.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 103
Figure 16. Summary of context map patterns
Relation to component-oriented architecture
Strategic DDD is not yet widely used within C&CA Germany. Most of our large-scale projects instead
successfully applied a component-oriented architecture using a combination of the modular
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 104
monolith and Layered architecture patterns. The domain was thereby decomposed into subsystems
using data-driven decomposition including a break-down into components. A business component
or subsystem in such an architecture could be interpreted as a bounded context. In such an
architecture we also addressed different types of subdomains by classification of different business
component types:
Figure 17. A-Component Type Overview
Depending on the concrete requirements, a different type with differing guidelines was used.
Comparing both approaches however we can see that the DDD approach is more business value
oriented and also does not try to apply the same technical architecture to each bounded context. On
the other it does not make sense to allow complete freedom of architecture styles per bounded
context in an enterprise due to economic considerations regarding team rotations, knowledge
sharing and in general TCO of the technical solutions. Therefore, a definition of architecture
archetypes makes sense and should be considered.

• DDD Reference Collection by Michael Plöd
• Starter modeling kit for DDD by the ddd-crew

strategic domain driven design

Data-driven functional decomposition
Overview
This pattern is useful in case we start with designing an application architecture based on a given
business solution design. These solution designs are often still based on use case descriptions and a
functional data model represented by entities.
The idea is to decompose the client and the server application into subsystems, where each
subsystem represents a grouping of the overall required functionality.
Since most of the time, the data model is the predominant discriminator used in identifications of
subsystems, we named this approach data-driven decomposition.
Identification of server side subsystems
