1. Identify “the server” as one subsystem.
2. Ideally, a grouping of the business logic into something similar to subsystems should already
have been done in the business solution phase. If so, use this grouping as a starting point for the
subsystems. If not, discuss the business logic with the business analyst to identify the first draft
subsystems.
3. Assign each entity to a subsystem. If it cannot be meaningfully assigned to an existing
subsystem, define a new subsystem.
4. Assign each use case and application function to a subsystem. If it cannot be meaningfully
assigned to an existing subsystem, define a new subsystem. Although a use case / application
function may in the end be implemented by several components, there should be one clear
subsystem that serves as its “anchor” or entry point.
5. Evaluate the borders between subsystems – identify all entities whose assignment is disputable.
Check if an additional subsystem introduced to contain them would be a better assignment for
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 106
other entities or use cases. If so, consider the introduction of the additional subsystem.
6. Evaluate each subsystem, if a further logical subdivision would be possible. If so, consider doing
so (considering the size of the resulting subsystems). Continue with step 3 for each new (finer)
subsystem.
7. Evaluate the dependencies between subsystems using business processes that span subsystems.
If you find strong coupling (a lot of dependencies) between two subsystems, consider
combination of the two or restructuring (is there a common and strongly coupled part that
might be an own subsystem?). .Evaluate each subsystem by trying to find a simple and short
description that describes the complete competence of that subsystem. If you end up with a list
of things independent from each other, check if you can find a better structure making clear
descriptions possible.
8. In the end, the finest subsystems (leafs) should be of comparable size (as determined by the
amount of entities and amount of use cases/application functions they contain). A subsystem
should usually consist of several components. The resulting structure should as a guideline not
contain more than five layers. As always, exceptions, when well motivated and explained, are
permitted.
Identification of client subsystems
Dialogs
Restrict to the
considered
subsystem
Client Build Define logical groups Subsystem
Subsystem logical groups as subsystems model
Further refinement of a subsystem needed
Identify “the client” as one subsystem.
1. Look at all dialogs contained in this subsystem, and identify logical groups within this
subsystem (e.g. administration dialogs, master data entry dialogs, user dialogs,…)
2. Use each logical group as the next level finer subsystem.
3. Continue with step 2 until no finer logical groups can be found.
Contrary to the server decomposition, this can lead to groups/subsystems of different size.
NB: The distinction of “the client” as a high-level subsystem serves to strengthen de-coupling of the
client from the server. It is not strictly necessary (client-server-spanning subsystems would also be
possible), but experience shows that coupling between client and server should be low. Defining
“client” and “server” as distinct high-level subsystems, together with the rules for integration of
coupling areas, automatically result in lower coupling between client and server.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 107
Classification of subsystems
In Strategic DDD, a classification for subdomains is presented. This classification is also applicable
for subsystems in the data-driven approach and should be considered. It allows to define and apply
different micro architecture patterns per classification if useful.
Macro Architecture Patterns
Macro Architecture
The Macro Architecture defines the cornerstones for structuring the whole system and sets
design decisions affecting all or at least most building blocks of the solution. In particular a
Macro Architecture determines the overall architecture style of the system and guiding
principles, however leaving room for individual design options. Where there is freedom to
choose between different design options, it is the field of micro architecture.
In order to enable a flexible architecture, which solves problems only where they

occur, try to minimize the macro architecture decisions to those which are really
needed and useful.
Coming from the functional decomposition, the architecture style is one of the first design decisions
to be made. In this section we collect the architectural styles as patterns. It is the responsibility of
an architect to pick and - even more - combine those patterns to design a macro architecture fitting
the specific needs in a customer setup. The needs are most dominantly defined by to the functional
structure, non-functional requirements and project priorities.
Patterns
• Modular monolith: building a well-structured, maintainable system as one deployable unit
for multiple functional building blocks
• Microservices: as a counterpart, instead building a distributed system where potentially each
functional building block can be a single deployment unit
• Self-contained Systems: similar to Microservices but extending the aspect also to the
frontend promoting a vertical architecture with loose dependencies between the verticals.
• Event-driven Architecture: as a pattern for the integration of the building blocks
• Cloud-native Architectures: leveraging the benefits of a cloud platform to fulfill quality
requirements such as scalability, efficiency and reliability
Guidelines for Microservices vs. Modular monolith
For designing the macro architecture, we provide the following ATAM matrix as an indicator
what requirements are better or less supported by which architecture style. Of course, a
combination of styles is also possible.

 concrete context and can only serve as an orientation. Do not simply use it as-is
without consideration. It is also only a first draft to demonstrate the idea and will
be refined in the future.

Microservice enforce stricter physical boundaries between building

Microservices can provide better scalability enabling better efficiency
in time behavior, but maybe not in resource utilization. However,
there is no a network layer in between building blocks which still
need integration and which can cause latency issues.
Modular monolith pattern
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 109
Overview
In a modular monolith, multiple (if not all) functional building blocks derived from functional
decomposition are implemented in one application, i.e. one unit of deployment and release. The
difference to a monolith is that modules with explicit dependencies put a structure in the monolith,
so it is only monolithic with respect to its deployment/release capabilities. In contrast, a monolith
would not have any inherent structure making it difficult to maintain and manage. From our point
of view the modules should be derived from the building blocks of the functional decomposition on
the one hand and cross/cutting technical modules on the other hand.
Modular Monolith
Application Module
Application Module
Application Module
Application Module
Technical Module Technical Module
Figure 18. Modular monolith
At Capgemini, the component has established itself as a suitable building block for the design of a
modular monolith. We then differentiate between A-components and T-components.[1]
If Strategic DDD has been applied during functional decomposition, an A-component typically
represents one bounded context, but this is a design decision to be made.
The important point for a modular monolith is that it needs to explicitly define how a component is
represented in the code. This depends on the concrete technology stack applied:
• Some programming languages allow the definition of modules or components others do not or
only insufficiently (e.g. Java, Go or typescript modules).
• In some technology stacks the build system can be a choice for defining modules as atomic build
units (e.g. Maven projects).
• Depending on the size of the modular monolith, conventions for namespaces can also be a
choice (e.g. package names).
Without such a representation of a component in the code, the architecture of the

modular monolith will have no "physical" relevance to the developers and will
quickly degrade to useless paperwork.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 110
Guidelines for modular monolith design
Definition for components of a modular monolith
In this section, we intend to outline the properties of components and propose an initial definition
of a component. The term “component” has been defined in many different ways in the literature.
In Capgemini, the definition given by [Sie04] has become established:
Definition: Component
A component is defined on the basis of the following properties:
1. It provides its functions to other components in the form of interfaces.
2. It uses the interfaces of other components - to do this, it must be configured.
3. Its implementation is replaceable (principle of information hiding).
4. It bundles functionality with a high cohesion.
5. It may contain other components (composition).
6. It is a meaningful unit for the organization of teams.
Below you will find more details about each of the six properties:
A component provides its functions to other components in the form of user or system
interfaces
In most cases, a building block that does not make its functions available to callers is
purposeless. This is particularly true in the case of components. Interfaces comply with the
principle of information hiding since a component only discloses that information via its
interface that is required by the caller in order to call the required function of the component.
Every component possesses one or more interfaces and consequently fulfills the “design-by-
contract” principle.
A component uses interfaces belonging to other components
The component imports the interfaces of the components whose functions it wants to use. Since
the principle of information hiding hides the implementation of the interface from the calling
component, this must be configured. During this configuration, the component is informed of
the concrete implementation that provides the function described via the interface.
The implementation of a component can be replaced
If an implementation is replaceable then this means that the users of the provided interfaces are
not affected by its replacement. They simply rely on the fulfillment of the interface contract,
something which must be ensured by all correct implementations. In modular monoliths, the
replaceability of components is important in that it enables components to be developed and
tested independently of one another. Of course, this requires automated integration tests to be in
place ensuring that the expected functionality is provided (s. Test Automation)
The component bundles functionality with a high cohesion
The functions implemented and provided by the component should have a high cohesion
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 111
between each other. This means that the functions highly depend on each other and changes
will most likely affect another function as well. In order to keep changes local and hence more
maintainable, the component should be the boundary for functionality with a high cohesion.
Note, that this characteristic is a given when reusing the building blocks after the functional
decomposition for the definition of components.
A component may contain other components (composition)
The implementation of a component can be broken down into further sub-components
encapsulated behind the component facade. The composition of other components to form a
larger component makes the component concept scaleable.
A component is a meaningful unit for the organization of teams.
This is one of the most important reasons for using components. It reflects the architectural
principles of the separation of concerns, the minimization of dependencies, information hiding
and data sovereignty. At the same time, we can note that this property raises the question of
what “meaningful” means. A high level guideline for this can be that a component should
require a team of 5-10 members to be realized.
One important aspect of the above definition, which is inherent in it, is that the components in a
modular monolith explicitly define dependencies between them via interfaces. This dependency
graph should be managed explicitly and should be acyclic following the principle of minimization
of dependencies.
Rules for the design of interfaces and operations
The following rules consolidate our best practices for designing interfaces of components. They
require an understanding of Interface design, so please read this article as well if you have not
already.
Use value-oriented interfaces for components
Only use data types, value objects or transfer objects as parameter types in value-oriented
component interfaces. As defined in the link above, these are non-modifiable in the sense that any
changes to them does not affect the provider. Note that the technical implementation in the
programming language is a different concept, i.e. in Java references are passed to objects, however
these objects should be decoupled from the source data model.
References to entity types must never be passed to the caller in value-oriented component
interfaces, otherwise changes to them would affect the providing component. Reference-oriented
interfaces thus are closely coupled and value-oriented interfaces are more loosely coupled.
However, application components should be loosely coupled.
This rule promotes the loose coupling between application components and therefore adheres to
the principles of the separation of concerns and the minimizing of dependencies.
Design interface operations to be business-related
The operations in components should be business-related, i.e. the specification should refer only to
the business logic and not indicate anything about the implementation.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 112
Implementation comprises the employed programming languages, the internal software
architecture of the component, the physical data model, and the technical infrastructure such as
databases, operating systems and hardware platforms. The rule reduces the adaptation effort
required for a calling component if modifications are made to the implementation of the called
component.
Design interface operations to be suitably coarse-grained
A small number of calls that do a lot are better than a large number of calls that only achieve the
required effect when taken together. We refer to these all-inclusive operations as coarse-grained.
One important aspect is choosing the appropriate level of abstraction for an operation. An
operation such as FindCustomerDateofBirthById(int id) is too fine-grained. An operation such as
FindAllCustomerDataById(int id) may be too coarse-grained if extensive data exists for a customer
and the user really only wants to know the date of birth.
A suitable abstraction could take the form of the operation FindCustomerDataById(int id,
CustomerStructure RequiredData). The user can define the scope of the search result in the second
parameter: date of birth only, all the customer data or even the entire contact history.
On the other side, be aware that coarse-grained interface can tend to grow over time. In case of
missing information, the decision might be made to simply add additional attributes to the
signature. The ratio for this is often that it concerns only two or three attributes which are missing.
This decision will then be repeated next time again, and so on until you end up with too large
interfaces.\
Instead try to detect disjunct usage patterns and consider creating different interfaces as different
views on the component.
Design interface operations to be context-free
The operations in a component should possess minimum knowledge of the context in which they
are called (session, transaction, batch/online) and also make as few assumptions as possible about
the call context.
There are different types of call context:
Session context
As far as possible, operations should not have any knowledge of users or sessions and not
require any knowledge of the call context. They should only implement logical functions. If
possible in terms of the business logic, the authorization check should be performed outside of
the operation. The operations should however define via some form of configurations what roles
or groups should have access.
Transaction context
Operations should make no assumptions concerning their transaction context.
Batch/online operation
Ideally, it should be possible to use operations both in batch and online mode without any
assumptions being made about the mode. However, this aim often conflicts with performance
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 113
requirements.
The aim of obtaining freedom from context is to decouple the calling and called components. It
increases the re-usability of operations. In case assumptions on the calling context cannot be
avoided, they need to be clearly documented in the interface contract.
Relation to other architecture patterns
Modular monoliths and strategic DDD
Since DDD has become popular with the advent of the Microservice architecture, it is often
misunderstood that Strategic DDD makes only sense for such distributed systems. However, it can
as well be applied to design a modular monolith.
Each bounded context could then be implemented by one A-component (s. above). This fits well
with the component definition, since a bounded context:
• serves as means to support the organization of development by separating teams even more
strongly then the definition suggests (e.g. via the ubiquitous language)
• bundles the functionality with a high cohesion based on the same model
The context map as the outcome of strategic DDD would then represent the explicit definition of
dependencies between components mentioned above. This essentially maps the strategic design
patterns to the following for a modular monolith:
• a shared kernel would be represented by an own component developed by multiple teams.
However from our experience, since this means no clear responsibility is assigned and
therefore we do not advise to follow that pattern. An alteration, closer to the Consumer-supplier
pattern could be that there is one responsible team and other teams can request
implementation changes (e.g. in the form of pull requests).
• Consumer-supplier means that the interface definition is owned by the team of the consuming
component (Note: that this it can still be defined either as a required or a provided interface!)
• Conformist means that the interface definition is owned by the providing team and is taken over
as is by the consuming component coupling it to the model provided
• Open-host service means that the interface is defined explicitly by the providing team and might
expose a different model than in the original bounded context component. The providing team
will then take care to adapt and transform the model to the published host service interface.
• An anti-corruption layer is nothing else then an explicit adapter component designed in
between the consuming and providing component decoupling both from each other. That way
both components can stick to their own model without having to fear that the respective other
model leaks into their own corrupting it. The explicit component established a dedicated loose
coupling between both.
• Separate ways is not an option since components must interact in a modular monolith
Note however, that DDD is quite vague with respect to how the dependencies should be represented
by interfaces and implementing artifacts. Our focus on provided interfaces as part of a component
definition and our rules for designing them, makes this more explicit and gives a clear guidance.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 114
Please see also the article on Interface design for more details. It depends on the concrete micro-
architecture of the A-components, how interfaces are provided in each component.
Modular monoliths with tactical DDD
In case A-components are build following the tactical DDD pattern, we propose to apply the
modular monolith pattern in the following way:
• Interfaces provided by a component are either the application services or the domain events
• Consequently, a component should also only depend on application services or domain events
of other components
• Application service interface should accept only Value Object parameters and only return those
as well.
Modular monoliths on the client
When people talk about creating components in application systems, they are probably primarily
thinking of the subdivision of the functionality of the backend application into components. The
component-oriented approach has so far usually focused on the backend, even though the client
can have the same or even higher level of technical complexity. In some projects a fat client is
required with a lot of business logic implemented in it and if so the necessary architecture for such
clients should not be underestimated. This results in the creation of complex monolithic clients
which can be difficult to extend, adapt and maintain.
To avoid this type of monolithic client and its consequences, the client must have a modular
structure in the same way as the backend by using a component-based approach. A core problem
here is the subdivision of the user interface. How is it possible to split up a coherent, complex user
interface into components which are only aware of one another via interfaces? One answer to this
question consists of the “visual embedding” of dialog components.
The figure below indicates the subdivision of a vehicle configurator application into dialog
components. Although the vehicle configurator is used only as a theoretical example here, it would
be implemented in this form.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 115
Figure 19. Subdivision of the vehicle configurator into individual dialog components that are visually
embedded in one another
Please note, that components on the client have however an important differentiating
characteristic in that they can be instantiated multiple times and each instance needs to be
managed. For more information on this architecture pattern, please see the Modular Client
Architecture).
Benefits and Risks of modular monoliths compared to distributed systems
Benefits
Simplicity (supporting Maintainability)
Overall a modular monolith is simpler to manage, operate and maintain then a distributed
system. This is kind of the summary of several of the benefits listed in a little more detail below.
Transactional consistency (supporting Maintainability)
Delivering multiple functional building blocks in one application allows you to manage
transaction on a facade of the backend. Transactional safety can those be guaranteed more
easily across those contained functional building blocks taking out a lot of complexity required
for managing eventual consistency.
Operability (supporting Usability)
Operating one application instead of a group of applications is easier to manage. You have no
network in between which your are required to monitoring and also the tracing of the
processing flow triggered by one interaction with the system is much easier to accomplish.
Efficiency
Calls between different components are inter-process communication and as fast as they can be.
In a distributed system this is different due to the network latency and overhead. It is much
simpler to orchestrate process flow across components in a modular monolith then it is across
different services.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 116
Refactorbility (supporting Maintainability)
Finding the right cut of functional building blocks is often difficult and the first try is seldom the
last. Refactoring within one code base is simpler and easier to manage than across multiple
applications or services. You can just use the board features of your favorite IDE to do it and you
can make disruptive changes across multiple components in one go more easily.
Risks
Maintainability
Modular monoliths tend to grow over time since additional functionality is added one after the
other. The team refrains from separating functionality because there is less experience in
developing a distributed system. Therefore, special care needs to be taken, that the functional
decomposition is thorough and the different components are clearly decoupled from each other.
A clear signal that this is no longer is the case are ripple effects and features often requiring
changes across the components. Hence, you need a more sophisticated form of automated
architecture QA to ensure that the architecture rules are adhered to and the structural erosion is
minimized.
Scalability
Delivered in one application means that only the one application can be deployed multiple times
mostly also with only one data store as the persistence. Depending on the data store used, this
can introduce bottlenecks and also makes scaling the backend more expensive, since you can
only deploy the complete application multiple times.
Releasability
Releasing the application requires more management aligning the work of the different teams,
so that no unfinished work is being released. This is due to the fact, that usually within a
modular monolith the teams trust the component interfaces they use. This in turn often means
that there is no explicit error handling in place, so intensive QA is need instead to ensure the
integration is working.
Technical changeability
A modular monolith is built on top of one tech stack (the T-architecture) and every component is
forced to use it, so there is no freedom of choice. Changes to this stack, also for updates of library
versions, requires all functionality to be tested and validated, even though the change might
only be needed for one component.
Further reading
• [Sie04] Moderne Software Architekturen, Johannes Siederleben, 2004
• Microservice Trade-offs: nice summary of pros and cons for microservices from Martin Fowler,
which can be of course reversed for modular monoliths
• Monolith-first approach: also a post by Martin Folwer advocating to start with a modular
monolith even for microservices
• Modular monliths: good talk from Simon Browne about why and how to build a modular
monolith
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 117
Microservices Architecture
In short, the microservice architectural style is an approach to developing a
single application as a suite of small services, each running in its own
process and communicating with lightweight mechanisms, often an HTTP
resource API. These services are built around business capabilities and
independently deployable by fully automated deployment machinery. There
is a bare minimum of centralized management of these services, which may
be written in different programming languages and use different data
storage technologies.
— James Lewis and Martin Fowler (2014)
Overview
The Microservices architecture pattern is an evolution of the concept of distributed systems too a
much more fine-granular build block level. Based on the outcome of the functional decomposition,
the decision could be for instance made to implement each functional building block in an own
deployable unit, i.e. an own possibly small system. Microservices offer lightweight communication
interfaces between each other and are integrated over these.
Microservice
Microservice Microservice
Microservice
Microservice
Microservice
The pattern was established as an alternative to a monolithic or even modular monolithic
approach, where all functionality of a domain or subdomain was implemented in one system. It
offers the following benefits:
Independence, Independence, Independence
This is the most important benefit in regards to multiple dimensions.
• Organization: Changes can be implemented and released independently by dedicated teams
for each Microservice faster and more efficiently (if designed correctly)
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 118
• Technology: Each service can have its own technology stack most suited for its purposes
• Operations: since each service is operated on its own, the overall system can be become
more stable, when one service runs into problems (again: if designed correctly)
Scalability
Each service can scale on its own allowing a much more fine-tuned scaling depending on the
concrete workload. Note that this does not fall under independence though it might seem so,
because a service can depend on other services being called, which requires them to scale for a
high workload as well.
However, it also comes with disadvantages to be considered:
Increased complexity
While each microservice on its own might be simpler to understand, the overall system with an
integration over the network is not. Especially during runtime the observability is more difficult
to achieve across process boundaries.
Dependency management
Dependencies between services cannot be traced via the IDE as in a modular monolith, but must
be maintained or tracked in an own solution.
Reusability
Reusing functionality across different microservices is a challenge. Providing those as a central
service can introduce a bottleneck. On the other hand shared modules counterfeit the
independence of microservices to some degree and need to be managed nonetheless. Last but
not least, code replication is an option but also not ideal.
Much has been already published about Microservices in articles, blogs and books and you can find
several of the main references in the links below. This article focuses on the applicability of this
pattern in terms of its pre-requisites. It then also gives some guideline of what you should take care
of when applying the pattern.
Applicability of Microservices
Even though Microservices are by now an established pattern, the inherent complexity of this
pattern and how to handled is still often underestimated. Mostly, this is due to a misunderstanding
of the pre-requisites for applying this pattern:
Functional decomposition
A functionally well decomposed domain is one of the main pre-requisites for a Microservice
architecture. Since communication now takes place across the network, tight coupling between
building blocks tends to be become not only a maintainability problem as before, but even a
performance problems due to the introduced latencies for each call being made. Therefore, care
has to be taken, that the building blocks are as independent of each other as possible and only
have a loose coupling between them. On the other hand, they should have an inherent high
cohesion, so that changes to the functionality can be made locally to only one service most of the
time. Typical signals for a poor design are change requests requiring a synchronized release of
multiple Microservices.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 119
Fitting requirements
This seems like a no-brainer, but needs to be emphasized nonetheless since it has been ignored
in the industry by some companies. Microservices can only be a solution to a requirement, but
never a requirement itself. The requirements should point to one of the benefits above as a need
for the system, e.g.:
• A system might have a special functional building block where a graph database would be a
perfect fit to the requirements, but for the other building blocks a relational database would
be better (Technological Independence)
• A system might process in one functional building block sensor data events which are
pushed in real-time with a high load. Separating out the import and processing functionality
(even though it is part of the functional building block!) might make sense to scale it
independently from the remaining functionality, which then only acts on the aggregated
results of the processing. This is as well an example for applying the Command-Query-
Responsibility-Segregation Pattern (CQRS).
• A system might contain a functional building block with a high uncertainty of what the best
functionality to be implemented will be. Separating this into an own service which is
released frequently to get feedback and to adopt might be a good choice.
Infrastructure-as-a-Service
In a microservice potentially every functional building block will require its own infrastructure
if developed as an own deployment unit. Therefore the minimum requirement is to have
Infrastructure-as-a-Service in place in order to be able to automatically set up new
environments without high overhead. Normally, a PaaS or CaaS should be available for all
microservices, which comes with even more benefits in reducing the effort required for cross-
cutting technical and infrastructure services.
Guidelines for Microservice architectures
One of the biggest drawbacks of Microservices is the high complexity of the overall solution
inherent to any distributed system which needs to be handled. Whereas each Microservice is by
itself easier to handle, the E2E-integration is the harder part and depends also on the coupling
between the services and the quality of the functional decomposition.
Therefore when designing a Microservice Application consider the following guidelines:
Apply best practices for distributed systems
Distributed systems have been around already before microservices and some of the lessons
learned are:
• Design interfaces as business use cases and not too fine granular in order to reduce number
of required service calls and thus the risk of performance problems
• Operations should be idempotent, i.e. if called repeatedly the redundant executions do not
change any state anymore
• Use state of the art protocols for the service interfaces
• Consider asynchronous vs. synchronous integration of services. Asynchronous
communication over messages introduces a loose coupling and is often more fault tolerant if
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 120
applicable.
• Be aware of the CAP theorem. In a distributed system the choice has to be made between
Availability and Consistency. This can lead to eventual consistency, where the data is
inconsistent between microservices which can lead to hard-to-analyze errors. At a bare
minimum make this trade-off transparent and discuss the real requirements
• A good collection of patterns for distributed systems can be found on Martin Fowler’s site
Conway’s Law
In order to achieve organizational independence the organization must match the cut of
microservices. That means the organizational architecture should be isomorphic to the
technological architecture. This follows from Conway’s Law (s.quote below). However, of course
the system can still be designed in a different way and the organization driven to comply with
this new structure (Inverse Conway Maneuver). This requires however either a culture where a
grass-root movement can succeed or management commitment.
Any organization that designs a system (defined broadly) will produce a
design whose structure is a copy of the organization’s communication
structure.
— Melvin E. Conway
High degree of test automation and quality
The real benefit of microservices with regard to independent development and releases can only
be gained, if new deployments do not disrupt the business due to errors caused. This on the
other hand requires a high degree of test automation and high quality of the tests themselves (s.
also Test Automation).
Design for resilience
You can only achieve real organizational independence, if microservice can continue to work
even if the ones they are depending on are not. To this end typical resilience patterns should be
applied such as Bulkhead, Circuit Breaker, etc.

• Building Microservices by Sam Newman: foundational book on how to design fine-granular
microservice systems
• Microservices Primer: Free E-Book from Eberhard Wolff giving a short introduction

experience blog post showing the intricacies of the pattern

• CAP Twelve Years Later: good recapitulation by the author of the original CAP theorem going
much more into the depth of the underpinnings of the theorem
• PACELC Theorem: extension of the CAP theorem for a trade-off in the non-partitioned case of a
distributed system
Cloud native architectures
Introduction
Designing, developing and operating software for the cloud introduces new architecture trade-offs
and challenges. In this article we shed some light on:
• the terms around cloud architecture
• what changes in software architecture if architected for the cloud
• important principles affecting cloud architectures
What is cloud native architecture?
Based on the definition for cloud native from the [CNCF18] we define cloud native architecture as
follows:
Definition: Cloud Native Architecture
Software architecture for solutions leveraging cloud native technologies in order to
typically fulfill the following quality requirements:
• scalability
• modular and loosely coupled
• resilient
• operable
• automated
This allows "to make high-impact changes frequently and predictably with minimal toil".
For a cloud native solution we still see two principle architecture options opening a first trade-off
decision to be made. The basic question is to what degree you want to depend on services provided
by a specific cloud provider entering into a vendor lock-in. This leads to the following definitions:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 122
Definition: Cloud Specific Architecture
Variant of a cloud native architecture making deliberately high use of cloud provider specific
services without any abstraction for decoupling. This allows leveraging the full potential of
the platform and avoids overhead for abstraction layers.
Examples of such an architecture would be leveraging a cloud specific data storage using only
the native SDKs for that service without any abstraction in the application code or a FaaS
architecture without taking care to properly abstract the logic from the technical function
context.
Contrary to that approach we can define:
Definition: Cloud Agnostic Architecture
Variant of a cloud native architecture introducing abstractions to the all services of the cloud
provider allowing the software to be redeployed with minimal effort to another cloud.
This limits the usage of this services to services that are standards and not vendor specific or
that are behaving equally on the major platforms. Most cloud agnostic systems are using
Kubernetes as method of abstraction from the underlying system because it is available on all
major cloud platforms as a managed service.
It should be noted that this is not an either/or decision to be made, but rather opens a range of
possible trade-off decisions with above definitions pointing out the two ends of the range.
Combining both approaches opens a huge potential to come up with a best-of-breed solution for
each aspect of the software. One common approach for instance could be to apply a cloud agnostic
architecture to the core application logic, while using cloud native solutions for specialized use
cases such as data stream processing. The following ATAM matrix will try to outline the
requirements to be considered in order to place your solution within this range.
Requirement Cloud specific Cloud agnostic Remark
Scalability / + 0 Cloud specific services often are focussed on one
Performance dedicated task which they perform as efficiently
efficiency as possibly. In cloud agnostic architectures this
is mostly not achievable without some effort.
Reliability/Secu + 0 Cloud specific services are managed by the cloud
rity provider with published SLAs and clear
responsibilities. For cloud agnostic solutions a
dedicated effort is required to ensure these non-
functional requirements
Portability — + As stated above the vendor lock-in is the major
drawback of cloud specific architectures
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 123
Requirement Cloud specific Cloud agnostic Remark
Maintainability 0 0 It needs to be highlighted that from a
maintainability perspective both approaches
require architecture work to ensure this non-
functional requirement and it depends on that
work how maintainable a software is. However,
it should be noted that abstractions in the cloud
agnostic architecture can add complexity which
needs to be managed. This is from our point of
view however not a major drawback.
Time-2-market + 0 One can see an advantage for cloud specific
services in case no agnostic platform has been
established. In case such a platform exists, this
advantage is however from our point-of-view
negligible. Another benefit are of course very
specific services for AI/IoT purposes which
might be faster and easier to integrate with a
cloud specific architecture, than with an
agnostic one with its abstractions.
Operability + - Cloud specific services are operated by the cloud
provider and require less effort. For cloud
agnostic platforms, own dedicated operations
are required even though there are automated
solutions available as well (e.g. Kubernetes
Operator pattern). In addition, the decoupling
adds additional complexity to be managed and
operated.
In addition, the term cloud ready architecture is often found in articles and marketing material. For
differentiating this term to cloud native architectures, we define a cloud ready architecture as
follows:
Definition: Cloud Ready Architecture
Software architecture which allows the software to be deployed and operated on a cloud
provider with minimal or limited modifications while still leveraging at least some of the
benefits of the cloud (elasticity, on-demand service, automation, etc.).
This means that the software system is not relying on local or on-premises resources and that
it was built in a way that it fits to the concepts of a cloud platform. There is no list of
properties a software system must have to be called cloud ready, but most cloud ready
systems are consisting of small decoupled containerized services.
Relation to cloud service models
The following figure illustrates the different cloud service models and highlights those of
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 124
importance to cloud native.
Application Frontend
SaaS
Events + Function API
("Software-as-a-Service")
FaaS Platform API
("Function-as-a-Service")
PaaS
Container + Container Manager
("Platform-as-a-Service")
CaaS
("Container-as-a-Service") OS
IaaS
("Infrastructure-as-a-Service")
For cloud native only CaaS, PaaS and FaaS are of importance. IaaS rather reflects the traditional
deployment models and simply put provides a VM in the cloud with access to (and responsibility
for!) the operating system.
CaaS offers the possibility to operate cloud native architectures based on containers on a container
manager. It requires a team for managing the container manager and does not offer out-of-the-box
technical building blocks such as data stores or monitoring stacks. The development teams needs to
handle all infrastructure concerns on container level. CaaS as such is therefore a good choice and
starting base for cloud agnostic architectures based on open source container managers. It offers
also the full flexibility to run whatever you want in the container manager.
PaaS builds on top of CaaS and can be defined in different ways. One possibility is to define PaaS as
an extension of CaaS, where technical infrastructure services are provided which can be easily
used when deploying a container-based application. Examples for these services would be data
stores, message brokers, etc. For instance, these could be defined as custom resources in
Kubernetes and then instantiated and used in a deployment (via the Operator pattern). An
alternative definition of PaaS goes further in that PaaS provides an abstraction to CaaS. Developers
can purely implement the application and deploy a compatible resulting build artifact via the
platform API (often again abstracted from via a CLI). The platform then "magically" takes care of
wrapping the application artifact (JAR, WAR, etc.) into a container and scaling this container behind
a load balancer. As such PaaS tends more towards the cloud specific architecture side, although
there are also open source alternatives available, which can arguably be considered as cloud
agnostic.
FaaS finally is very similar to PaaS (some argue say it is even the same). The major difference is the
scalability especially with regards to the granularity. Whereas applications in CaaS and PaaS are
typically running with some minimum and maximum instances handling a whole workload of
requests, functions can be scaled down to zero and up to whatever is needed to respond to
incoming events. Events are hence the bases for triggering functions and thus scaling them on a
very granular level. Hence, function implementations are single-threaded (one request per function
instance), whereas container apps are typically able to handle multiple requests at once in multiple
threads. Some FaaS platforms are very specific to a cloud provider, hence such applications are
typically designed as a very cloud specific architecture. However, even for FaaS there are open
source alternatives available as an additional abstraction layer on top of the container manager.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 125
On a final note, Software-as-a-service is not related to cloud native architectures since it is not
really an an architecture pattern, but rather a business model. Of course, in case one is building a
SaaS solution itself, it will be most probably be build on a cloud native architecture.
Impact of cloud on the software architecture
In the following section we describe the influence of cloud to the different architecture views
defined by the arc42 standard. The building block view will thereby be split in a technical view and
a functional view (as promoted by the SEAGuide elsewhere as well). In the following sections we
will analyze the effects of cloud on the different views to build a complete picture of what changes
in the realm of software architecture.
Impact on the functional building block view
Typically we define the application architecture (A-architecture) in the functional building block
view. The A-architecture tries to handle the complexity of the business by functional decomposition
into functional building blocks with dedicated, unique responsibilities. In our point of view, the A-
architecture remains unchanged in a Cloud environment. It remains equally important, but the
way the functionality is cut into different building blocks follows the same well-established
patterns and principles.
We do see however a risk, that due to the new technical possibilities of the cloud,
the work being done on the A-architecture might be neglected. Often the focus is
 rather on the other architecture views with shiny Cloud-icon-based diagrams
demonstrating the technical capabilities. How this is then used in the
implementation of functionality is however not trivial, but here the actual work
begins.
You might argue that with cloud the functional view has to be split into even smaller components
(aka microservices). But microservices is at first a technical concept, which is not 1:1 reflected into
the functional architecture. For sure there is an inter-relationship between those two. If you need to
distribute your technical implementation into several decoupled services, your functional
architecture has to support this approach by defining corresponding component (cuts). Another
aspect is that cloud does not mean necessarily microservices, but we assume that for a system with
a ”cloud architecture” you built some kind of distributed systems, so you need different business
components you “can” distribute.
Impact on technical building block view
The technical architecture (T-architecture) complements the application architecture. As defined in
the functional decomposition it defines:
• a blueprint on how functional building blocks are technically implemented and how each
building block should be structured
• technical components such as those for Monitoring, Logging, Security and other cross-cutting
concerns
Below you will see how cloud architecture will impact those definitions or not.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 126
Blueprint definition for building blocks
In Cloud environments the functional building blocks still need a technical representation.
When looking at existing cloud architecture blueprints, e.g. from the hyperscalers, you might
argue that these define the technical architecture. But this is not fully true. If it comes to bespoke
software you still need to design and describe how the system should work. You may adopt
existing patterns for that e.g. hexagonal architecture, event-driven-architecture and so on. The
cloud may give you a broader technology choice, but the design work remains the same.
Crosscutting technical concerns
The cross-cutting concerns on the other hand are already well taken care of in the cloud
providers service portfolio. Logging for example is usually achieved by writing logs to std:out
and a collector of the used service collects those logs and presents them in an own Logging
Service. Similar for telemetry data and security features, cloud providers typically offer services
for a lot of cross cutting concerns that integrate well with runtime services in the cloud.
Hence, for the T-architecture the decision needs to be made how cloud agnostic or specific the
solution should be (s. above, especially the ATAM matrix). This needs to be done on a case-by-case
basis for all cross-cutting concerns. For some concerns there might be open-source standards
available, so the portability of using a cloud service is not really endangered. In other cases, the
cloud specific services might be very hard to provision on an agnostic platform in time or might be
very costly in terms of operating and securing them. The T-architecture should therefore keep a
decision log for each of these options.
If services are used from the cloud provider, the T-architecture building blocks are starting to be
part of the technical infrastructure and hence shifting from the technical building block view to the
deployment view. The shift is heavier the more cloud specific services are being used. Contrary the
more agnostic the cloud architecture is, the more relevant the T-architecture remains.
Example 3. T-Architecture in the cloud
• In an extreme scenario for CRUD applications, the API gateway of some cloud providers
could be configured, so that API calls are directly relayed to a database service. In such
cases the T-architecture would still need to define this approach and especially clarify that
the A-architecture mainly consists of the REST API definition.
• In a more agnostic case the T-architecture could define a service mesh with sidecar
containers to be used, based on certain products which are leveraged for this purpose. It
would still need to define the blueprint for how the main container is structured.
Impact on the deployment view
As mentioned in the previous chapter the deployment view is growing, because more and more
concerns are shifting to existing cloud services that are part of the infrastructure. One striking
impact of cloud architecture is that the technical infrastructure nowadays typically is also
implemented in code (Infrastructure as Code, IaC). In the pre-cloud area, only the other
architecture views were really implemented.
But not only the number of topics is increasing here, also the frequency of changes drastically
increases. In traditional environments dedicated teams took care of the infrastructure. New servers
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 127
and products had a long time until they were introduced into that infrastructure. With a growing
DevOps mindset shift across the teams the deployment view is as quickly evolving as the rest of the
software. This leads to the same problems in the deployment view as with the rest of our system: it
can erode and should therefore be put under quality control and frequent review. IaC is one tool to
put the infrastructure under version control, allowing reviews on changes and use it to define
quality goals and generate an accurate deployment view from it. But IaC is also introducing a new
area architecture must take care of. Therefore the important of the deployment architecture view is
gaining much more importance for asserting maintainability and other quality requirements.
Like the traditional code of an application IaC needs versioning, testing and a maintainable
structure (micro architecture):
• versioning is already covered because one of the main objectives of IaC is to have the possibility
to version infrastructure
• designing a maintainable structure for IaC, the established patterns of software architecture
typically apply as well[2]
• testing is the most challenging aspect, since IaC actually trigger physical resources to be created
in the cloud and hence require a fully different kind of sandbox model than traditional tests
Another major impact on the deployment view and the corresponding architecture work is cost
management. With cloud computing and the consumption based pricing model, the costs of
architecture decisions becomes immediately transparent and needs to be considered as well as
monitored. Sizing was always part of the deployment view, however in the past it was often based
on a worst case scenarios solved by provisioning bigger infrastructure buffering usage bursts. For
cloud native deployments, the scaling and adoption must be much more automated and requires
much more fine-grained decision making. This can also have effects again on the T-architecture,
when costs effect the choice of cloud specific services for cross-cutting technical services.
In conclusion, the changes in the deployment view reflect the impact on the involved teams with
regards to DevOps.
Operations can no longer work independently on their own, but instead must move closer to
development. Infrastructure is no longer managed purely by governance or manual activities, but
instead becomes software defined. This creates the need for operation teams to incorporate
software engineering techniques in how the implement infrastructure management via code.
For the development teams on the other hand it means, that they need to learn classical operation
requirements and how they should be supported by the architecture. This becomes more important
the more you go into a pure DevOps approach a la You build it, you run it.
Impact on the runtime view
The runtime view typically reflects the collaboration of the building blocks for the major use cases
of the solution. This will be the case for cloud architectures as well, however with some building
blocks being out-of-the-box cloud services. We do not see however any major impact on this view. It
rather builds on the building block views.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 128
Principles of cloud native architectures
Cloud native architectures should support and assert the 12 factors for cloud native applications
([TFA]). These have been extended ([BTFA]) to include three additional principles. They are mostly
already well-established practices in our projects.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 129
Fifteen factors for cloud native applications (s. [Azure])
Factor Explanation
1 - Code Base A single code base for each microservice,
stored in its own repository. Tracked with
version control, it can deploy to multiple
environments (QA, Staging, Production).
2 - Dependencies Each microservice isolates and packages its
own dependencies, embracing changes
without impacting the entire system.
3 - Configurations Configuration information is moved out of the
microservice and externalized through a
configuration management tool outside of the
code. The same deployment can propagate
across environments with the correct
configuration applied.
4 - Backing Services Ancillary resources (data stores, caches,
message brokers) should be exposed via an
addressable URL. Doing so decouples the
resource from the application, enabling it to
be interchangeable.
5 - Build, Release, Run Each release must enforce a strict separation
across the build, release, and run stages. Each
should be tagged with a unique ID and
support the ability to roll back. Modern CI/CD
systems help fulfill this principle.
6 - Processes Each microservice should execute in its own
process, isolated from other running services.
Externalize required state to a backing service
such as a distributed cache or data store.
7 - Port Binding Each microservice should be self-contained
with its interfaces and functionality exposed
on its own port. Doing so provides isolation
from other microservices.
8 - Concurrency When capacity needs to increase, scale out
services horizontally across multiple identical
processes (copies) as opposed to scaling-up a
single large instance on the most powerful
machine available. Develop the application to
be concurrent making scaling out in cloud
environments seamless.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 130
Factor Explanation
9 - Disposability Service instances should be disposable. Favor
fast startup to increase scalability
opportunities and graceful shutdowns to leave
the system in a correct state. §Docker
containers along with an orchestrator
inherently satisfy this requirement.
10 - Dev/Prod Parity Keep environments across the application
lifecycle as similar as possible, avoiding costly
shortcuts. Here, the adoption of containers
can greatly contribute by promoting the same
execution environment.
11 - Logging Treat logs generated by microservices as
event streams. Process them with an event
aggregator. Propagate log data to data-
mining/log management tools like Azure
Monitor or Splunk and eventually to long-
term archival.
12 - Admin Processes Run administrative/management tasks, such
as data cleanup or computing analytics, as
one-off processes. Use independent tools to
invoke these tasks from the production
environment, but separately from the
application.
13 - API First Make everything a service. Assume your code
will be consumed by a front-end client,
gateway, or another service.
14 - Telemetry On a workstation, you have deep visibility
into your application and its behavior. In the
cloud, you don’t. Make sure your design
includes the collection of monitoring, domain-
specific, and health/system data.
15 - Authentication/ Authorization Implement identity from the start. Consider
RBAC (role-based access control) features
available in public clouds.
There are some higher level principles which you should always consider when designing cloud
native applications.
Automation everywhere
From CI/CD to monitoring to scaling, all aspects of the cloud native solution should be automated
wherever possible. Otherwise the major benefits of a cloud such as elasticity and on-demand
computing are not leveraged to the full extent and incur manual cost overhead.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 131
In-depth security
Security plays a much more prominent role and you should take care that each building block of
your solution is protected in and by itself not relying on any trust to other building blocks. Thus
your solution becomes more secure and resilient to attacks.
Constant change
The cloud is always evolving and so constant monitoring with subsequent adaption is required
to leverage new possibilities but also react to incompatible changes. This is another major
reason for the Automation everywhere principle.
A good source of cloud native architectures are of course the three hyperscalers ([AWS], [Azure],
[Google]). All hyperscalers in addition advocate an architecture framework based on the same
pillars:
• Cost optimization
• Operational excellence
• Security
• Performance optimization
• Reliability
Of these five pillars, the latter three are well known and established practices in our projects.
However, the cloud also offers new solution options to be considered and applied for those non-
functional requirements. Typically, for our customers a dedicated landing zone is established which
takes care of some of these cross-cutting concerns (e.g. [AWSLZ]). Such landing zones can for
instance be provided by CIS.
The first two pillars however now have much more impact on our architecture work as already
elaborated on above.

Event-driven Architecture
Event-driven Architecture models applications as a set of connected building blocks reacting
to a flow of events. Events are captured in an event channel and are available to all building
blocks.
Overview
Event-driven Architecture models applications as a set of connected building blocks reacting to a
flow of events. Events represent an action of interest for the application that has happened.
Building blocks listen to events and react appropriately - possibly creating new events resulting
into an event chain. Occurred events are captured in event channels (aka event streams). Building
blocks can take over event producer or event consumer roles or even both roles at same time but
for different channels.
«AppBuildingBlock»

c « o A n p s p u B m u e il r d / in p g r B od lo u c c k e » r pr e o v d e u n c t e <<Event Channel>> event
Figure 20. Event-driven architecture example
Event
An event represents an action of interest for the application that has happened. This could be either
an external event occurring outside the application or an internal event. Events consist of:
Time points
It is best practice that events contain at least two time points. One for point in time when the
event has occurred and one point in time when the event has been captured in an event
channel.
Event data
Event data on action or status change is captured either as structured data complying to a data
schema or as unstructured data, typically as a set of name-value pairs. It is called keyed in case it
references one concrete information object by (business) key. Event data is either self-contained,
that means it carries all information consumers require, or it contains references to actual data
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 133
only. We strongly recommend using self-contained event data. It relieves consumers from
requesting details from a second building block and fosters loose coupling.
Context data
Events also contain context data in order to identify related events e.g. in case of event chaining.
Context data might be a correlation, user or application building block id.
Bear in mind that messages and events are conceptually not the same - although
 often used synonymously. Messages have a sender and one or multiple known
receivers whereas events just happen (and have an owner). Messaging systems
might be used to forward event notifications within an event-driven architecture.
Event Channel
Event channels capture events and make them available to consumers. There are options for
implementing event channels:
Publish/subscribe
Implementing publish/subscribe concept requires event consumers to subscribe to an event
channel. Consumers are notified immediately on capture of a new event in that channel after it
has been published by an event producer. Publish/subscribe is quite flexible. When consumers
subscribe to a channel as a group, events can be consumed by one group member only (first
consumer wins). This results into a load balancing group. If many building blocks subscribe
individually they are all able to consume all published events.
Event streaming
Event streaming keeps all captured events in a channel like an append-only log. Consumers read
events from the channel remembering the last event read. Last read event gets updated each
time consumer reads more events. Consumers are able to read all events from a channel for a
second time if needed.
An event channel persist events as immutable entries of an append-only log. Conceptually, events
are not removed from channels. Practically, there needs to be some channel data retention policy
defined. Retention policy might come along with tertiary memory backups.
Consuming Events
Typically three variants of event processing are distinguished:
Simple event processing
For unrelated events processing takes place immediately. This variant is called simple or discrete
event processing.
Complex event processing
Complex events are defined as a pattern of many independent events occurring (or not
occurring) in a given time interval. Complex event processing cannot take place immediately
after occurrence of a single event. A consuming building block needs to wait until all events of a
complex event pattern were received. Reaction to a complex event needs to be abandoned by
consumer if not all events required by complex event definition have occurred within a given
time interval.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 134
Event stream processing
Event stream processing is applied to a stream of related events. In event stream processing
consumers react on each event individually. This might be counting the number of occurred
events or incrementally updating an average value.
Micro-batch processing
Micro-batch processing is a stream processing variant where consumer a starts processing only
after collecting a fixed number of events.
Applicability
Event-driven architecture is a good idea when you need it
Decoupling
Event-driven architecture decouples producer from consumer building blocks. Producers just
capture events in a channel not knowing which building block will consume them or if they are
consumed at all. Vice versa, consumers do not know which building block has created an event.
This makes it easier to change implementation on both sides.
Elasticity
Adding new or removing running building block instances as reaction to request load changes
has no impact on building blocks on the other side of the event channel thanks to decoupling.
Robustness
Application robustness increases as building block failure can be compensated by replacing a
failed instance by a newly created one. New instance is able to consume events from channel in
order to continue service of failed instance.
But it comes with a price tag
Application complexity
Decoupling of building blocks makes it harder to understand and monitor an application’s
information flow. This adds complexity to development, operations and error root cause
analysis procedure.
Infrastructure complexity
An event channel is an additional infrastructure element that needs to be implemented,
operated and monitored.
Take special care of
Complex event chains
Application behavior becomes hard to understand and monitor if reaction to one event results
in a chain of consecutive events. It is recommended to double-check if chained events are not
actually side-effects of the original event in case the event chain is becoming complex.
Loops in event chains
Event chains must not contain cycles in order to ensure termination of event processing.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 135
No self-contained event data
Watch out for events not containing all data required by consumer. For most simple case event
data is just containing the ID of affected information object, but no actual data. Consumer needs
to request details from producer creating a tight coupling between them.
Remaining consumer-agnostic
Putting the right amount of information into an event is easier said than done. Putting too much
information in events might overburden some consumers with lots of unnecessary information.
Putting too little information in events either requires consumers to come back to the producer
(see above). Also each new consumer could result in an event schema change to include new
information.
Event schema evolution
Changes of the event data schema might cause issues consuming events. Events are assumed to
be immutable and must not be migrated to a higher schema version. Complexity of consumer
increases as it needs to cope with multiple schema versions; possibly requiring event data
migration.
Event channel qualities
There are usage scenarios where qualities like no event loss, no duplicate events or no re-
ordering of event ordering is essential for application correctness.
Usage scenarios
The event-driven architecture is a general purpose pattern that can be applied in many scenarios
each having specific requirements. The following examples provide some assistance to understand
when to use event-driven architecture.
Business Applications
Domain events are used when designing business applications using an event-driven
architecture. A domain event is used to capture an external action that can trigger a state change
in your application. Events captured in channel are sensitive to duplicates, events loss or re-
ordering of original sequence occurrence. The creation of a customer order is a typical (initial)
event for a web shop. It triggers picking order’s execution at the warehouse as well as the
preparation of delivery order, shipping order and customer invoice. Goods are picked and
moved to the outbound logistics creating a second event. This event triggers delivery order’s
execution at the outbound logistics. Goods are packaged, package gets labelled and placed in the
logistics hub’s outbound area creating a third event. This event triggers the sending of a shipping
order to the carrier as well as the customer notification on forthcoming delivery and invoice. In
addition, a debt claim on the customer is created in the financial accounting system. Customer
pays invoice and money is transferred to the web shop company’s bank account creating a
fourth event. This event triggers settlement of customer debt claim and closing of customer
order.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 136

Internet of Things (IoT) Applications
Sensor events are used when designing control and analytical IoT applications using an event-
driven architecture. Sensor network environments typically generate time-series of events at
high (arrival) rates. Event processing is either expected to happen in real-time for controlling
applications or in near-real time for analytic applications. Machine’s operational anomaly
detection, e.g. for detecting vibration anomalies, is an example for an analytic IoT application.
This means that implementation of event channels has to deal with high event capture rates and
event processing to be done with low latency. Event loss, duplicate events or re-ordered events
typically do not affect application correctness if not becoming significant. The meaning of
significant depends on actual correctness requirements.
User Activity Monitoring Applications
User action events are used when designing analytics for an e-commerce application using an
event-driven architecture. A recommendation engine is an example of such an analytical
application. Although a single user is causing action events at low (arrival) rate, it is the total
number of users at a time that requires event channels to cope with high event capture rates.
Event processing are expected to be carried out in near-real time as user’s site visit is expected
to last a few minutes. Event loss, duplicate events or re-ordered events typically do not affect
application’s use if not becoming significant.
The event-driven architecture pattern is independent of other macro-architecture patterns.
Therefore, we will describe how event-driven architecture can be combined with microservice or
modular monolith pattern and discuss serverless applications being event-driven by definition.
Event-based Microservices
Event-driven architecture can be applied to a microservice architecture. It is reasonable to
decouple each microservice by placing an event channel in-between. An event-based coupling is
especially recommended if microservices reside in different business domains.
Event-based Modular Monolith
Event-driven architecture can also be applied to a modular monolith. It is reasonable to
decouple its components by placing an event channel in-between. Especially if occurrence of one
event requires more than one component to react on it. A lean event channel solution is
recommended in this case, maybe using an in-memory approach. Applying event-driven
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 137
architecture is no obvious choice for a modular monolith architecture.
Event-driven Serverless Application
Serverless or Function-as-a-Service applications are inherently based on an event-driven
architecture. The events can be incoming messages from a queuing service, an HTTP call on an
API gateway, an alert in a monitoring services and a whole set of further possible event sources.
The events are passed to functions implemented on the serverless platform (AWS, Google,
knative, OpenFaaS, …), which then implement the logic on these events, possibly creating further
events yet again handled by other functions.
Further Information
• Martin Fowler on Events: Unavoidable reference to Martin Fowler’s article collection on event
architecture
• Introduction to Event-driven Architecture: Good introduction to topic within decent coverage
• [Bel 20] A. Bellemore, Building Event-driven Microservices, O’Reilly 2020: Decent introduction to
event topics with details on event stream processing
• [McPa 84] St. McMenamin, J. Palmer, Essential Systems Analysis, Yourdon Press 1984: Presented
event partitioning concept is basis for what is now called event-driven architecture. Discusses
good and bad event design in detail. Excellent German translation available thanks to Peter
Hruschka
• [ACL 18] T. Akidau, S. Chernyak, R.Lax, Streaming Systems, O’Reilly 2018: Details on
implementing event channel qualities
• AWS Compute Blog on Lambda + Events: AWS recommendation of an event-driven architecture
based on lambda
• Google on event-driven functions: the pendant to the AWS article
Generative AI
Introduction
In the training Generative AI Fundamentals on NEXT, the following definition is given:
Definition: Generative AI
Generative AI is a type of machine learning that can learn the patterns and properties in a
dataset so deeply that it can generate creative new variants inspired by the original. The
content can be any type of data — text, images, audio, video, source code, JSON, SQL, 3D
objects, molecules, and many more.
It is important to know when to use generative AI and when not to use it. A "classic"
implementation will create deterministic results, it will be faster, it will consume less resources.
Generative AI should be used for use cases where the result is not deterministic, e.g., when the
result is creative or when the result is based on a lot of data which is not available in a structured
way.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 138
An enhancement of Generative AI is Agentic AI. In this Capgemini Whitepaper, the following
definition for agents is given:
Definition: Agent
An agent is any entity that works on behalf of another entity, working to accomplish high-
level objectives often using specialist capabilities. Agents have a degree of autonomy and
authority to take actions that modify their world.
CCA point of view: The ability of modification is no strict criteria for acting as agent.
The term AI agent is recently used to describe software-based agents that leverage LLMs to
autonomously execute specific tasks in a more sophisticated - and so to speak, intelligent - manner.
In this article, the terms agent and AI agent are thus synonyms. Within an agentic AI system, several
specific agents are combined to solve more complex tasks in a collaborative way.
The promise of AI agents is:
• Workflow Automation: AI agents can assist users and clients by automating complex
workflows that involve reasoning and decision-making - capabilities that go beyond what
traditional chatbots or rule-based systems can offer.
• New and Enhanced Use Cases: By leveraging the power of LLMs, AI agents can enable and
improve use cases that were previously unattainable and achieving outcomes that surpass
human capabilities. For example, they can monitor quality aspects or security aspects, make
decisions, suggest actions, or even autonomously execute them.
• Multi-Agent Collaboration: These agents can be composed into systems where multiple
specialized agents interact and collaborate, achieving even more complex tasks.
This article covers both "standard" GenAI and agentic GenAI solutions, from an architecture view.
Context view
A robust, standard GenAI-enabled application architecture without agents typically consists of
several core components:
• the business application itself (frontend/backend), including a Generative AI (GenAI) adapter,
• a large language model (LLM),
• a retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) database (optional),
These components work together to deliver intelligent, context-aware responses to user inputs. For
standard GenAI use cases, the flow is based on a simple chat interaction. The user creates a chat
message as input, the GenAI adapter retrieves domain-/company-specific knowledge via RAG (if
needed) and then contacts the LLM to generate the answer. The answer is then sent back to the
user.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 139

When using agents, the architecture differs slightly. In addition to the above building blocks, the
following components are used:
• Model Context Protocol (MCP) servers to invoke tools in agentic use cases (see further below),
• MCP client(s) inside the application, to call the MCP server(s),
• Agent-to-Agent (A2A) server(s) to discover other agents outside of the own application (remote
agents)
• an A2A client inside the application to interact with other agents outside of the application via
the A2A protocol.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 140
Definition: MCP vs. A2A
Both MCP and A2A tempt to standardize the agentic ecosystem. For both protocols, servers
and clients are used in agentic environments. They are complementary to each other:
• MCP is used to standardize the interface from a single agent towards the tool the agent
can use.
• A2A is used to standardize the communication towards other remote agents to fulfill the
user task.
• A2A is not used within the same application. The communication between agents inside
the application is handled by the agentic frameworks.
A detailed comparison can be found at the A2A website.
A single AI agent typically interacts with users through a chat interface powered by a large
language model (LLM). It receives input related to its designated task and returns either
intermediate or final results. To complete its tasks, the agent interacts with the LLM and other
agents (inside or outside the application), often making multiple calls throughout different stages.
The overall workflow is orchestrated within or across multiple AI agents, forming a multi-agent AI
application. There exist a number of design patterns for combining agents to form a multi-agent
application, see LangGraph: Multi-Agent Workflows or the Capgemini training course Mastering
Agentic Design Patterns on that topic.
User interaction takes place through a natural language chat interface, enabling intuitive and
conversational engagement. In enterprise environments, the agent must access enterprise data and
interact with tools and APIs to perform relevant actions.
