Application including a Generative AI adapter
The business application (a type of application that we build usually in our projects) serves as the
entry point for user interactions and orchestrates the flow of data and commands. The application
itself can be a monolith or a distributed microservice landscape. In the context of this article, this
does not make a difference. The application communicates with the GenAI adapter.
The GenAI adapter is a conceptual building block within or outside the application. The tasks of the
GenAI adapter are to integrate with the rest of the GenAI application on the one side and to call the
LLM on the other side. General cross-cutting concepts such as monitoring and error handling as
well as Quality Assurance apply to this kind of adapter, but are not explicitly mentioned here.
LLM
The Large Language Model (LLM) is the component responsible for the generation of content via
generative AI. This component is not part of the application. The details of the ML algorithms the
LLM was trained by are not of relevance for the solution architect. As a solution architect, it is
however necessary to drive the decision for the to-be used model(s).
Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG)
Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG) is essential when the LLM needs access to external or
proprietary knowledge. RAG is used when the original LLM knowledge is not enough to generate
adequate answers, e.g. if domain- or client-specific knowledge is needed.
It is important to know that the RAG content is not part of the LLM. An application with a GenAI
adapter first calls the RAG database with the user input via a vector search, and then adds this
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 142
result to the context of the original prompt before sending it to the LLM for refinement and answer
generation.
For details on RAG, see the section RAG integration below.
MCP components
To facilitate interaction with a wide range of third-party tools, the Model Context Protocol (MCP)
has been introduced. MCP provides a standardized mechanism for agents to communicate with
tools (acting as MCP servers) and perform actions. MCP is relevant for agentic AI use cases.
MCP is a protocol that works within a client-server architecture. Via MCP, information on tools can
be given in a standard way as context to an LLM, and the LLM can give answers including
suggestions for tool calls, which then can be actually executed by the agent. This enhances
portability and maintainability by eliminating the need for proprietary APIs when integrating new
tools or switching between vendors. MCP can also be used to connect with internal business
applications or to integrate and communicate with other agents (specific other agents as tools). You
can learn more about MCP here.
It is worth mentioning again that the LLM itself does not execute tools or system calls. This is the
responsibility of the MCP server in the application environment (or the application itself if no MCP
components exist).
A2A components
Agent-to-Agent (A2A) interaction becomes more dominant when building semi-autonomous
application landscapes. A2A is a protocol invented by Google to standardize the interaction
between agents within and outside the application. A2A works technically on the basis of JSON-RPC
and HTTPS. The agent-to-agent prompts are part of the JSON structure. A detailed documentation of
A2A can be found here
A2A works in a client-server architecture as well. The GenAI adapter acts as an A2A client. An A2A
server is taking the role of an agent discovery service that provides the client with connection and
authentication information for other agents. These agents can be pre-built by another solution, or
even another vendor. While these agents can technically run on the same machine as the A2A
client, the protocol used is always HTTPS, allowing remote communication as well.
Business application considerations
The application itself should provide a comparable user experience to what the users know from
public GenAI tools like ChatGPT. The user interface is either being provided by the business
application’s UI component, or the microfrontend of the GenAI adapter. Though UX design is not
part of this guide, just a few points to consider from a functional point of view:
• The user should be able to see the chat response streamed in (not getting a bunch of text at
once).
• Example prompts for the most important use cases help the users to start going.
• In agentic use cases, it should be visible that a tool was used for the reply - and which.
• If a modifying action (create, update, delete) is to be executed by a tool, the user should be asked
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 143
for permission before the execution (Human-in-the-loop principle).
• For RAG answers, a reference to the document helps to verify the correctness of the answer.
• Depending on the use case, a feedback mechanism on the answer may be desired - either for the
model directly, or as a request towards an operations team (e.g. via email).
GenAI adapter considerations
Programming language and frameworks
Usually, LLM-facing components like the GenAI adapter are written in Python due to the maturity
of this programming language for any AI/ML/GenAI tasks (Java frameworks may miss some
important features). The programming language decision leads to the decision of decoupling
between business application and GenAI adapter.
• A decoupling into a dedicated microservice is recommended when the programming languages
from business application and GenAI adapter differ (e.g. using Java for the business application
and Python for the GenAI adapter). This is especially the case in brownfield projects and
existing solutions that need GenAI enhancement after the initial implementation.
• On the other hand, if the application itself is already written in Python, an encapsulation within
the application via packages and modularization may be enough to maintain simplicity.
Langchain is the most common framework to integrate LLMs and RAG. It is recommended to
adhere to existing frameworks and standards instead of trying to reinvent the wheel and trying to
build an own solution.
The decision not to use Python for the GenAI adapter should be evaluated
carefully. Thy Python ecosystem (and Langchain in particular) has very high
 maturity for GenAI use cases. Even the Java SDKs of Langchain and other
frameworks are missing important features that are present in the Python
frameworks already, e.g. integration to some LLMs, debugging, agent types, state
management and others.
For agentic AI frameworks, see here.
Interface design
The interface from application towards an LLM, a RAG database or an MCP server is encapsulated
by frameworks and does not need to be defined from scratch. They are therefore not further
considered here in detail. As an architect, it is usually not necessary to integrate the low-level APIs
of LLMs.
If the GenAI adapter is encapsulated in a dedicated microservice, the interface between GenAI
adapter and the rest of the GenAI application needs special attention. The usage of an
asynchronous interface is recommended to enable the streaming capability for the user. This leads
to the usage of protocols like gRPC (with protocol buffers) or Python frameworks like FastAPI.
gRPC has a better performance and type safety than usual REST interfaces.
General cross-cutting concepts and microservice principles apply for the interface design too.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 144
Usage Control, Quotas, Rate Limiting, FinOps and Cost Management
Usage control oversees the frequency of interactions with a specific model, preventing excessive
usage beyond predefined limits. This is particularly relevant for resource-intensive models such as
image generation models or large language models.
The limits need to be implemented or configured on the application side and are therefore the
responsibility of the application design. These limits can be externalized for configuration or be
handled as hardcoded values. For details see this guide
Choosing the correct model is one way to control the costs. See Model selection for more details. It is
important to revisit on this topic frequently as the LLM models and their cost structure evolve.
Cost management on the other side sets hard limits to the resulting costs.
System Prompt Design and Guardrails
While prompt engineering itself gets less and less important as the models evolve, the system
prompt is still a crucial factor for the application’s behavior. The system prompt is the prompt that
is applied to all user prompts, no matter the input. The system prompt is implemented in the GenAI
adapter.
The system prompt should follow a structured text, describing
• the role of the AI or agent,
• its capabilities (e.g. tools),
• the tone of communication,
• the way of approaching a query (step-by-step),
• example queries and answer formats.
It is best to use a system prompt generator (that itself is based on GenAI) to create a good system
prompt. You can use online resources and GenAI tools like Microsoft Copilot to generate a good
system prompt. The general rules for prompt engineering also apply (general tips can be found
here).
Depending on the use case, the system prompt configuration can also be externalized to the users
(e.g. functional admins). This needs to be done via the custom implementation in the application.
But even in that case, a generic, non-editable system prompt should be added in addition to ensure
the usage of guardrails and basic behavior.
Guardrails constitute a set of safety controls designed to monitor and guide a user’s interaction
with a generative model. These programmable, rule-based systems act as intermediaries between
users and generative models, ensuring that the GenAI application operates within defined
organizational principles. The primary goal of guardrails is to enforce the input to and the output of
a generative model to adhere to specific formats or contexts, e.g. the syntactic correctness of a JSON
response.
Guardrails and content safety help to implement the guidelines from the Capgemini guidelines for
responsible AI. They are implemented using two different approaches: On the one side, the prompt
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 145
contains instructions to the generative model to follow certain guardrails. On the other side, the
GenAI Adapter checks the output of the generative model if it also follows the guardrails.
RAG integration
Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) is an architecture that enhances GenAI models by
combining them with external knowledge sources.
RAG is technology-agnostic. While one can implement a static list of content (e.g. a dictionary)
within the application directly to use for retrieval, in our contexts we usually need more and bigger
data. This is why the basis of RAG is usually a vector database, containing documents and other
artifacts.
A typical approach in the case of unstructured information is semantic search and vector
embeddings. This approach consists of two parts: ingestion and retrieval.
The ingestion part reads the information, stored in documents or other artifacts, and chunks it into
smaller parts. It then has these chunks translated into vectors representing the semantics of the
chunk, typically by using an LLM. These vectors are called embeddings, and the LLM used to
calculate them is called embedding model. The embedding model can be different to the model that
is used to create answers of user queries later. Then, the ingestion part saves the chunks together
with their vector embeddings and some metadata into a vector database. Alternatively, other types
of databases could be used such as graph databases or classical SQL databases.
Ingestion is usually not part of the development of GenAI-applications itself, but is
handled more centrally, likely by a different team. RAG ingestion is a process that
occurs either one-time or frequently, depending on the content. It is time-wise

decoupled from the retrieval, which is a real-time query. RAG ingestion provides
data that can be relevant for multiple use cases and applications. The
responsibility for RAG ingestion is therefore usually not at the feature team that
implements the GenAI application.
The retrieval part, for each prompt, calculates an embedding vector using the same embedding
model as the ingestion part. Then it searches the vector database for chunks close to this
embedding vector using a distance metric, e.g., cosine similarity or dot product. It adds these
selected chunks to the prompt as additional knowledge for the generative model. The retrieval logic
is part of the GenAI adapter.
The aspects to design when designing and integrating a RAG database are
• Chunking Strategy: Use overlapping windows to preserve context across chunks.
• Metadata Tagging: Store source, timestamp, and topic to improve filtering and traceability.
• Hybrid Search: Combine keyword and vector search for better precision.
• Caching: Cache frequent queries to reduce latency and cost.
• Feedback Loop: Use user feedback to refine retrieval and update the vector store.
One guide for RAG best practices and database proposals can be found here.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 146
Memory handling
Memory concepts in GenAI applications help the application learn from the user’s behavior.
• During the conversation, the GenAI adapter can store context in short-term memory of the LLM
or agent.
• For continuity across sessions, such as maintaining a history of previous results or
personalizing the experience, long-term memory is used.
Short-term memory is implemented by attaching the message history to each new message that is
sent towards the LLM. It is the responsibility of the application to ensure that these messages get
appended. Some frameworks support in that process by configuration parameters. Appending is a
simple string concatenation (where technically each string represents a JSON object of the previous
conversation) to the prompt text.
Long-term memory relies on vector databases, similar to RAG. NoSQL databases can also be used,
though this results in more custom handling to retrieve the memory for the next conversation.
Special topics for agentic use cases
Frameworks
Several frameworks support the creation and programming of AI agents, including:
• LangGraph - an extension of LangChain programming framework for multi-agent systems
(multi-step workflows)
• OpenAI’s Swarm - a multi-agent orchestration framework
• n8n - a powerful workflow automation platform that supports AI agents (based on LangChain),
fair-code licensing
• CrewAI - an open source Python based AI agent framework (independent of LangChain), crewAI
also offers a visual agent builder tool
• Microsoft’s AutoGen & Magentic AI - open-source framework (AutoGen) and a workflow
automation tool (Magentic AI) on top of the framework
• Google’s ADK - framework for developing and deploying AI agents, optimized for the Gemini
LLM
• IBM’s watsonx.ai & watsonx Orchestrate - a development environment for agents and an
orchestration tool which allows to work on agents with a no-code approach
• AutoGPT - started early as an agent to solve general tasks on top of GPT, but now evolves to a
platform for developing AI agents supporting a number of built-in LLMs
Currently, there is no clear market leader. These frameworks are not compatible with each other.
Langgraph is part of the Langchain ecosystem and can be integrated into existing "standard" GenAI
use cases.
The application design differs a lot between these frameworks. It is therefore important to choose
the correct framework based on
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 147
• its capabilities (e.g. supported LLMs, tools),
• the support model,
• the license model,
• the existing landscape,
• the knowledge within the team.
Agent scope
Similar to microservices, the agents and their scope need to be defined in a multi-agent
environment. The design of the agent ecosystem is done declarative within the frameworks by
• defining roles and system prompts for each of the agents,
• linking tools, LLMs and memory to each of the agents,
• defining the workflow
Some agentic frameworks like CrewAI support sequential as well as hierarchical workflow
executions.
• The former leads in a modelled, streamlined process with only one direction. If the process
itself is known, the sequential flow is the simpler way to achieve the goal.
• The latter introduces a manager agent that orchestrates the work of other agents. If the
requirements from the user are unclear, or the use case is unstructured, a manager agent
makes sense to break down the work and distribute it to the correct agents.
To simplify the development of personal assistants and intelligent agents, specialized tools provide:
• Predefined AI agents as reusable components (libraries)
• Graphical workflow modeling languages for orchestration
The more complex and generic an agent is modelled, the higher the risk of hallucination or
unexpected results. It is recommended to slice the agents as small as possible, with clear context
and roles for their execution. Consider the same design principles as in Functional Decomposition.
MCP
When using MCP, the GenAI adapter acts as the host for one or multiple MCP clients. The host
orchestrates the process flow between user input, tool execution and LLM in its application logic.
The MCP client is responsible to connect to the MCP server(s) that have access to the tools.
The MCP server is responsible for declaring the tools and resources, their arguments, and their
execution logic. You can build your own MCP server or deploy a predefined one (an example list
can be found here).
From a topology perspective:
• One MCP client instance always connects to exactly one MCP server.
• An application (MCP host) can host multiple MCP clients, each connecting to one MCP server.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 148
• One MCP server can fulfill the needs of multiple MCP clients.
• There can be multiple MCP servers within an application (each of them holding one or multiple
tool/resource definitions). It is not necessary to bundle all tool and resource definitions in only
one server. While this may be confusing at first, it makes sense in terms of the reusability of
existing MCP servers, scaling capabilities and fault tolerance.
A2A
When interacting with remote agents, the A2A protocol should be used. The GenAI adapter then
acts as the A2A client, communicating with the A2A server to retrieve information about other
remote agents. This includes their URI and authentication schemes.
There is one A2A client inside the own application, individual own agents can all share the same
client. Though depending on the agent flow, it can make sense to introduce one A2A client per
remote agent called. There is no one-size-fits-all approach. It is generally recommended to use the
simplest approach here to avoid complexity in the network communication.
A tutorial on how to build and integrate an A2A client can be found here. The main points to
consider when building a flow with A2A are:
• Definition and alignment on the task state model of the requested agent, e.g. its possibility to
send updates on a request asynchronously via SSE or push notification,
• Implementation of retries and circuit breakers to avoid deadlocks when interacting with remote
agents,
• Integration of the own application with the given identity provider to authenticate against other
remote agents, e.g. via OpenID Connect.
As of July 2025, not all popular agentic frameworks support A2A natively yet.

When remote agents are used, the architect possibly needs to take care of the A2A
client definition (see above) when designing the GenAI adapter.
Retries and limits
Agents act semi-autonomous. It is not possible to control the flow of an agent task execution. This
can result in many interactions between the agents and their respective prompts. The tokens that
are needed for these interactions add up fast and incur costs. The more agents exist, the more
tokens are used. A manager agent (hierarchical workflow) incurs more tokens due to the necessary
coordination. Clear retry limits (as defined in the respective frameworks) need to be applied to
abort the execution after a given amount of failed executions. A limit of 5 executions is usually
enough. This also increases the fault tolerance of the execution itself by introducing an error
condition.
Limitations of AI Agents
AI agents inherit the core risks of large language models (LLMs), including concerns around
security, safety, reliability, scalability, and efficiency.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 149
These risks become even more significant when an AI agent moves beyond being
read-only or generative and gains the ability to actively create, update, or delete

business data through connected tools. Then quality assurance becomes even
more important. In many cases, this can mean that you need a human expert in
the loop who can check and decide on intermediate results of an agent.
In addition, AI agents introduce new challenges:
• Limited Portability: Switching to a different LLM often requires reworking internal prompts
and workflows.
• Immaturity: Agentic systems are still in early development stages, with evolving standards and
tooling.
• High Maintenance costs: Frequent updates to models and tools demand ongoing testing and
adaptation.
The field is rapidly advancing, and significant changes to the agent ecosystem are expected as
research and development continue.
LLM considerations
Model selection
As the landscape is steadily evolving, we do not provide recommendations here. Use resources like
this model comparison to get an up-to-date overview over the current models and their capabilities.
Different aspects affect the decision for the correct model(s) for a use case:
• The capability of the model itself (amount of parameters, multi-modality, reasoning, tool
calling…)
• The hosting model (cloud or on-premise) and the security protection requirements
• The balance between value and cost (models with higher capabilities usually cost more per
token)
• The ability to partner with a LLM provider to access expert knowledge and potential cost
benefits
The standard approach is to use a pure LLM-text model, with capabilities for tool calling. The use
case requirements need to be checked if additional and specific features like image generation are
needed, as this also affects the model selection.
Temperature
The temperature of a model indicates its creativity and predictability. The value is a float scale
between 0 and 1.
The value that is adequate is always dependent on the use case:
• For use cases where knowledge retrieval, a somehow deterministic and specific output is
needed, a low value between 0.0 and 0.2 is preferred. This is the case for most business
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 150
applications.
• For highly diverse, imaginative and create results to generate content, a value of 0.8 or 0.9 is
recommended.
• A mixture of these two extremes leads to a temperature in the middle of the scale, e.g. 0.5.
The temperature can be configured in the frameworks as a parameter. Depending on the user
requirements, this configuration can also be externalized (this needs to be implemented
individually).
Testing
See Engineering Practices from Martin Fowler. Depending on the possible threats and the resulting
testing goals, testing for bias in the result generated can be one part of the testing strategy.

Micro-architecture Patterns
In this article, we collect all patterns applicable on the micro-architecture level. Based on the
functional decomposition and the choice of appropriate macro-architecture, each functional
building block (component, microservice, bounded context, etc.) requires consideration of design
patterns specific to the chosen macro-architecture. In this case we are talking about architecture on
the micro-level in contrast to the macro-architecture of a complete application.
Pattern classification
Our architectures in the projects often take the form of frontend-backend systems with an
optional batches for background processing. These applications are typically based on a
technical infrastructure which consists of at least two parts. In this original sense, we distinguish
between a frontend part which runs on the users' machines and therefore has to be distributed,
and a server part which runs centrally on a host or host cluster and uses central resources. The
frontend uses the services provided by the backend in order to obtain access to central data and
functionality. The batch processing can then be part of the same backend or run separately from
it triggering the backend by calling its provided services. The storage and persistence of data is
often split from the backend into a third part since dedicated, specialized database solutions are
used for handling the data. Hence, we also organize the collection of micro-architecture patterns
accordingly to these building blocks.
User
Frontend
Backend Batch
Persistence
Figure 22. Typical three-tier architecture in our projects

Batch patterns
Migrated

This section has been migrated from Architecture Guide with some minor, but no
major rework.
In our projects not all processing is triggered by a user interaction with the software. Oftentimes
background processing is necessary as well e.g. for generating reports, statistics, housekeeping, etc.
In those cases, a dedicated batch implementation is needed to handle these requirements. In the
following the basic pattern of batches are described and what patterns batch frameworks should
support for handling the cross-cutting requirements. Based on this, more advanced patterns with
regards to error handling and performance are described.
Basic patterns
Definition: Batch
As a batch we identify a closed (=complete) functional unit of mass processing of data, which
runs under system supervision.
A batch has the following qualities:
• It runs without user interaction (asynchronous to the online processing). However, its
execution possibly can be triggered by a user.
• It processes several common business transactions in a volume of input data and
preferably uses available services for this processing.
• It normally summarizes several business transactions in a technical transaction (due to
optimization reasons). Several such technical transactions can be processed by a single
batch execution.
• It can be understood as one single (increased) business transaction (“Batch is successful or
not”).
Batch identifies for us the programmatic implementation (also the batch program or the
batch component/building block).
The following is to be noted for classification:
• Asynchronous processing is not same as batch processing. Even message processing (JMS, MDB,
MQS…) is asynchronous processing, but normally limited to one single technical transaction per
message received (and usually even only one single business transaction).
• Mass processing of data is not the same as batch. Even online transactions can manipulate mass
data (several business transactions), and if required this can be split into several technical
transactions (several server calls from the client, or multiple transactions managed on the
server).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 153
The following Figure 23 explains the context of transactions in a batch:
Business Tx (whole batch)
Batch Control
getAllX():X[] doIt(X1) doIt(X2) doIt(X3)
Application Logic
Business Tx0 Business Tx1 Business Tx2Business Tx3
Technical Tx0 Technical Tx1
Figure 23. Transactions in a batch
In this example, it is differentiated between the batch-control logic (batch control) and the
application logic. Blocks of business transactions are collated into technical transactions. Existing
application logic for the single business transactions is re-used as far as possible.
The programmatic implementation “Batch” can be executed. The concrete execution instance we
then call a job. A job has the following qualities:
• It supports all necessary (even functional) parameters for the execution of batch.
• It has a status (mostly managed by technical basis component) (e.g. yet to start, started,
complete, cancelled, irrelevant…).
• It has results (e.g. return code, error and other notifications, number of processed data records
or functional objects…).
• It runs under system supervision and can be diagnosed via monitoring functions.
• Specific persistent data and configuration data are part of this job (e.g. respective batch logic
interface, next starting job…) as well as functional usage data (e.g. input parameter, data to be
processed…).
In the simplest case, a job corresponds to a program run or a process.
It makes sense to implement the functional logic of batches in dedicated building blocks which
reuse the existing interfaces of other building blocks. In this building block, the composition of
these interfaces in a functional batch process is done. We do extract the technical aspects as far as
possible as cross-cutting tasks.
In our projects the batch building block typically works closely together with a batch framework:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 154
Batch Framework
Job Control Batch Control
Batch Interface
Batch Building Block
Other building blocks
Figure 24. Overview of batch building block and batch framework
• The batch framework calls the batch building block and controls the execution.
• The functional execution control of a batch is implemented in the corresponding batch building
block.
• While performing the functionality of the batch, the batch building block relies on other
building blocks. Thus, it uses the already available services (single record-oriented) or batch
specific services (multiple record-oriented) of these components.
• In exceptional cases, the batch building block can also include the complete functional logic of
the batch - mainly for migrations, initial filling and other one-time batches, which are defined
for one-time use.
If necessary, the batch building block can optionally call the batch framework - e.g. for starting
another job (logical interface Job Control Service), or to obtain the data volume to be processed next
(logical interface Batch Control Service). The details depend on the concrete batch framework. A
good batch framework will avoid this inverse linkage wherever possible.
The batch framework implements the cross-cutting tasks:
• The Job Control handles the execution of the jobs (also batch process instances).
• The Batch Control handles the execution of a batch for a single job.
The exact tasks of job control and batch control are explained in the latter sections.
For most purposes, batch building blocks are exclusively called by the batch

framework and not by other application components, especially where their batch
processing methods are concerned.
Batch logic can be structured as per hierarchy:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 155
• First of all we have the batch as per our definition.
• Often it makes sense to model partial functional processes as closed steps of batch processing (s.
definition below).
• Often sequential execution of several batches is necessary depending on each other (standard
“batch night” job chains). It is a process of several batches, running in succession (sometimes
also in parallel).
The definition of a batch step is given below:
Definition: Batch step
As batch step we identify a processing step of a batch which is separately implemented. Batch
steps have the following qualities:
• Batch steps are part of the batch (belong to the batch), and are orchestrated by the batch.
• Batch steps are functionally classified and implement an independent part of a batch use
case.
• Batch steps can depend sequentially on each other or can be executed in parallel.
• Batch steps have a life cycle of type initialize-process-cleanup.
• Batch steps process a volume of input data, divided in blocks of one or several data
records.
The exact design of the interface of the batch step is mostly specified by batch framework.
The framework should then also control its life cycle.
In batch frameworks, the batch steps are also often mentioned as Job Step. We

adhere here to the differentiation between “batch” as executable program logic
and “job” as execution instance.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 156
Batch Building Block
Batch step 1
Batch step 3

Batch
Control

Figure 25. Example Batch Steps
In order to simplify the finding of batch building blocks in the construction, we formulate the
following rules:
Implement the batch logic of exactly one batch only in exactly one batch logic component.
This follows from the single responsibility or separation of concerns principle.

Consider splitting the batch in a sequence of batches if this is not the case and also
consider the granularity of the batch so not too many functional tasks are assigned
to it.
Example 4. Batch sequence
In a planning system, the creation of a plan is the task of the batch “plan creation” and the
provision to external systems with the plan data is a task of batch “plan provision”. If the
creation of the plan and the subsequent initial provision to external systems are necessary for
new plan data, the latter can be done by a batch “initial plan provision”, which is started after
successful ending of the batch “plan creation”. Both batches can again consist of several batch
steps. The batch “initial plan provision” can be implemented by the same building block as the
batch “plan provision”, since it will contain similar if not the same logic.
Example 5. Batch group
In a planning system, several types of plans are created for each batch; each plan type includes
specific data types, which are created separately in batch steps. The process of different plan
creation batches also differ in this data type specific batch steps, however always has the
identical rough structure as well as reusable batch steps for several plan types (which do not
depend on the data type). This group of different plan creation batches was implemented in
one batch building block.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 157
Not every batch framework supports the formation of batch steps with its batch control. A similar
sub-division of functional process logic can be provided alone by the job control, if this supports a
conditional chain of jobs.
Batch framework patterns
A reliable, well-performing and simple to implement batch processing requires technical base
services, which are consolidated in the aspects job control and batch control. Ideally, several of
these tasks are provided by a batch framework (e.g. Spring Batch), and if required along with a job
control component (e.g. Quartz Scheduler). However, some of aspects might need to be
implemented by a separate, project specific batch framework or they must be performed in the
batch building blocks.
Consider the selection of suitable frameworks for job control and batch control
In general, as much as possible of the necessary aspects of job control and batch
control are performed by a framework. However, not all aspects might be required

in every project. In order to control the resulting complexity, one should consider
that the used batch framework can be controlled in a simple fashion and covers
the necessary aspects. Uncovered aspects should be implemented, if necessary, by
individual additions.
Not all of the job control and batch control tasks listed in the following two sections must be
relevant in every project context. Rather, they describe the function of an idealized batch
framework, which might not be completely covered by any single product available on the market.
Job control
The job control ensures the management and execution of process instances of batches. Thus, the
individual batch is the elementary unit, and the job control does not care about the internal sub-
division of the execution logic of the batch.
Following tasks are part of job control:
Job Definition
The Job Definition takes care of the static configuration of batch instances, that is, the parts that
can be held fixed for multiple concrete batch runs.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 158
These can include:
• The batch service to be executed
• Fixed parameters for the batch run
• Mutual exclusions and limitation of parallelism of batches
• Definition of batch sequences (“if batch A ends, batch B starts”)
• Classification of the job by resource consumption classes (CPU/run-time, storage
consumption, …)
• Fixed or repeating (according to some pattern) execution times
Job Scheduling and Control
The job scheduling ensures the selection of jobs for execution.
This includes:
• Explicit submission of individual jobs for execution (e.g. from online transactions, or
during system start-up)
• Event-driven submission of individual jobs for execution (e.g. by messaging event, creation
of files, achieving an instance in time, …)
• Submission of individual jobs or job sequences (“first A, then B” chains)
• Management of jobs that should be executed at fixed times or repeatedly
• Management of all jobs which are executable and already executing (queuing)
• Selection of next jobs to be started from the executable jobs by considering job sequence,
predicted resource consumption and priorities
• Checking depending on the current situation as to whether a job can be now executed and
can be started or whether its start must be postponed for some time (this must be done in
cooperation with the functional logic of the batch building block)
• Start, restart, pause and cancellation of jobs
• Prevention of execution of jobs (set irrelevant)
• Saving of parameter values of the batch to be used while executing the job as well as the
batch result (return code, error, messages)
• Distribution of jobs to several servers (horizontal load balancing on job level)
Job State Management
The job state management tracks the state of jobs (executable, is executed, successfully
completed, ended with error, …) and the time when most of the important states were achieved
(at least: submission, start of execution, end of execution). Tracking the time is especially
essential for error diagnosis.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 159
Job Monitoring
The job monitoring allows access to information about jobs.
• Information about all jobs depending on the state, triggered user, start or end time or other
criteria
• Information about the resource consumption (required or actual) of separate jobs
(consumption of CPU time - if supported by operation system - or run time - storage
consumption, allocated disc space…)
• Information about the progress of separate jobs (this requires a relevant support by the
batch control)
There are different commercial and open source products for the job control, whose set of included
tasks is very different. In a given project context, it must be decided on the basis of requirements as
to which of these products are used. It results then as to which tasks must be added by individual
development (either by a custom framework or in the batch building blocks itself).
Example 6. Quartz Scheduler
The Quartz Scheduler mainly supports the job scheduling and control tasks with a simple job
state management. It offers only a rudimentary support for a job monitoring. Several tasks of
job definition are not included (e.g. batch sequence, exclusions). These must be added by
separate development - in case of batch sequence (e.g. in a simplest case where a batch itself
triggers the next job sequence during completion via the Quartz Scheduler.
Batch control
The batch control ensures the internal execution of a batch. This mainly includes the distribution of
this execution to business transactions and technical transactions, the division of the batch in
different batch steps, and the execution of the batch steps in the correct order.
Following tasks should be supported by a batch control:
Batch Definition
The batch definition ensures the configuration of a batch run and can either be stored or
dynamically defined at execution time.
This includes:
• Predicted resource consumption (CPU, memory, …)
• Division of the total batch process in several batch steps
• Definition of mutual dependencies and exclusions of batch steps
Batch Lifecycle Control
The batch lifecycle control ensures the full lifecycle of a batch.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 160
This includes:
• Check, whether processing has to be done at all
• If supported by the batch framework or job control, check whether the execution can be
done now or must be postponed (e.g. due to functional blocks or semaphores)
• Initialization of the batch, specific for start and restart This mainly includes the
initialization of batch control
• Cleaning up after completion of the batch
The batch lifecycle control does not manage any state - this is either included in

the job state management (state of batch job or all batches) or in the batch process
control (state of batch step).
Batch Execution Control
The batch execution control ensures the execution control of the overall batch as well as of the
batch steps.
This can include:
• Selection of steps to be executed as by the dependencies and exclusions according to the
batch definition
• Parallel execution of steps within the limits of available resources (especially allocation to
several threads and server), considering the level of parallelism required by the batch if
necessary and the limitations resulting from the dependencies and exclusions of steps
• Execution of individual steps, including their lifecycle (initialization-processing-cleanup)
• Control of the execution loop of a step including transaction control (technical
transactions)
• Define as to how most of the input data to be processed are processed in a transaction
(commit rate, block size)
• Retry of steps for occurring errors (for the complete processed data block)
• Logging of occurred error and results in technical or functional logs
• Management of state of step if required including management of checkpoint
• Restart of cancelled steps while restarting the total batch run
• Cancellation of the current batch steps for cancellation of the batch
Example 7. Spring Batch
Spring Batch supports the individual batch („Job“) as well as batch steps („Job Step“), which can
be reduced to separate process elements („Tasklet“).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 161
Error handling patterns
If a functional or technical error occurs during an online transaction, the user gets an error
message and then can (after correction of inputs or correction of error cause) once again simply
trigger the action. If an error occurs during batch processing, this is clearly not so simple. The batch
control must respond to the error and has the following options:
Cancellation of batch processing
The transaction in which the error occurred is rolled back and the batch run is ended. All not yet
processed data blocks remain unprocessed. This is by far the most easy to implement behavior
on the side of the batch framework.
 In particular SQL databases are optimized for successful transactions. Rollbacks of
big transactions can introduce huge performance problems.
Retry
It is tried to process the incorrect transactions once again. This makes sense only for problems
which in most of the cases can be corrected by itself (e.g. DB locks).
Skip the error
The transaction in which the error occurred is rolled back and the incorrect data block is
skipped. The batch processing continues with the next to be processed data block. In the case of
a commit rate > 1, the individual commit rate can be changed, whereby only the incorrect data
record must be skipped. In cases of error, a data block or data record is not processed for each
error case.
The error situation must then be identified by a user or system operations: it must be recognized
that an error occurs at all, and if possible the error cause must be identified. This is mainly ensured
by the job monitoring. In each case the data which is not processed remains (at least the data
record causing the error). Normally this must also be processed after the correction of the error
cause - mostly by a new run of the cancelled batch with the still to be processed input data.
In order to ensure that a new batch run can process missing data records, it is necessary that they
can be easily identified by the processing logic. Following scenarios exist for this:
Checkpoint logic
The processing progress is logged with each transaction. This happens by saving the unique key
of the last successfully processed input data record as a check point in the database. If the batch
aborts or is cancelled, the check point defines to what extent the processing was successful. In
case of restarting a batch, the check point is read and the input data is processed further at this
point of processing. The persistence of the check point should be managed by the batch
framework. Normally, it does not make sense to implement this in the application if the batch
framework does not support any checkpoint. In this case, rather one of the other scenarios for
rerun should be selected. During rerun of a batch, the batch run is also started from scratch
(with the same input parameters). During restart the batch starts exactly at the check point with
the processing. The check point logic must not always be the first choice - the functional
implementation necessary for the creation and evaluation of check point data can be costly and
prone to failure (e.g. database queries must support repeated iterations) and a simple rerun
usually will be enough for many functional requirements.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 162
Input data staging area
The input data to be processed must be available in a logical database area, in which they are
reserved only for the processing by a batch and in which they were copied beforehand - we
name such an area as staging area. After the successful processing of a data record this is
marked by the batch as “successfully processed” or deleted from the staging area. The staging
area always includes the data yet to be processed (as “successfully processed” marked data
records can be easily filtered out from the selection for the next run). If the batch is stopped by
an error, a restart (rerun) of the batch would be enough after the correction of the error cause.
The batch then continues the processing with the first not processed data record from the
staging area. In order to simplify the error analysis, it is advisable to mark the processed data
only as “successfully processed”. They can then be used for analysis if there are any issues with
the batch results. However, a concept for removal of no longer needed input data must then be
then implemented for the staging area.
Deletion of old result before running
The processing always begins in a blank result space - e.g. by deleting all possible results from
the database. The batch then always simply generates the result data records. If the batch aborts
due to an error, a simple restart (rerun) of batch will be sufficient (the already successfully
processed input data records are processed once again before occurring of the error). The
deletion of data can also be implemented as a simple operation that is performed only in case of
error correction.
Idempotent batch logic
The batch logic is designed in such a way that the result is not changed, if the batch simply
processes the same input data once more as it was done for the first time (i.e. processing the
same input element multiple times has the same result as processing it only once). If the batch
aborts due to an error, a simple restart (rerun) of the batch will be sufficient (whereby certainly
already successful processed input data are newly processed).
Performance patterns
On account of the high data volumes to be processed, batches are often performance-critical.
Unfortunately, in batch processing, a high performance cannot be achieved by simple, retroactive
optimization measures. High performance requirements must be considered already while
constructing the batch building blocks and sometimes event the other building blocks called by
them. Therefore, some aspects are elaborated below, which are to be considered during the
construction of batch building blocks.
Parallelization
With special high performance requirements, the parallelization of batch processing is to be
considered. In the following, we focus on the parallelization of functions for frequent, similar data.
Two aspects are to be differentiated: logical parallelization (classification of batch logic in parallel
executable units) and physical parallelization (i.e. the actual parallel execution). The logical
parallelization is the pre-condition for the physical parallelization.
The logical parallelization can be done on two levels:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 163
Parallel processing of the batch in several jobs
The data volume to be processed is distributed in blocks to several instances (jobs) of the same
batch. These instances can be then executed by the job control.
Parallel processing of batch steps of a batch (within a job)
The batch itself distributes the data volumes to be processed in blocks to several instances of the
same batch step. These are executed in parallel by the batch control.
A parallel processing on job-level is possible with each batch framework, if the execution of several
jobs is supported at the same time. Whether a parallel processing of batch steps within a single job
is possible, depends on the used batch framework.
The prerequisite for the logical parallelization is that the processing of data blocks can be done
mostly independent from each other. In particular, this means that the data modified in jobs or
batch steps running in parallel should be disjoint if possible. The logical parallelization should
already be considered during the design.
The physical parallelization concerns the distribution of execution to several threads, processors or
processor cores and servers. Normally it does not have any impact on the design of the batch
building block. Not every batch framework does support all of these physical distributions.
Block size
Sizing the blocks of the data to be processed in a single technical transaction must consider
performance aspects with respect to technical limits:
• On the one hand the efficiency of the database access is increased (especially for write access)
with increasing block size as the ratio of latency to other access times is reduced (with a batch-
enabled database interface).
• On the other hand there are several technical restrictions, which prevent arbitrary increase in
block size. This includes e.g. transaction timeout settings, database limits for the length of an IN-
List (for queries) and number of write accesses(log-size) possible in a transaction. Usually these
limits are product specific and some of them also can be configured in the product settings.
Collaboration with persistence service
While using the persistence service, several aspects are to be considered, which have an impact on
the design of the services used from other building blocks:
Cache-initialization
If the persistence service has a caching mechanism built in, this can be used for optimization, if the
batch logic iterates over the data volume via single record operations of the business logic
interfaces. With cache-initialization, the cache of the persistence service is initialized at the start of
the batch processing with the entity objects required later. This must be done with as few as
possible database accesses via the persistence service, e.g. by setting the respective multiple record
read requests.
If a cache-initialization is used, it should be done when initializing a batch step. This means that the
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 164
respective owning building blocks must provide suitable read- or initialization functions in their
business logic interfaces.
Batch access to the database
The number of accesses to the database should be reduced if possible for high performance. Apart
from caches, which are used mainly for optimization of read accesses, database drivers and
implementations of the persistence service offer different abstractions in order to reduce the
number of database accesses while writing the data (e.g. JDBC Batching, statement reordering for
Hibernate, …). The objective of these abstractions is to collect (writing) database accesses while
using the single record operations in the business logic interfaces. Since they are an
implementation secret of the used technical building blocks, these abstractions can fail
spontaneously as a result of even minor changes of the implemented business logic.
If this abstraction by the persistence service fails, it can be necessary to implement the business
logic in dedicated batch operations, so that multiple record accesses can be accordingly optimized.
Query language
Most persistence services offer a query language abstracted from database products for
formulation of database queries (e.g. Java Persistence Query Language, Hibernate Query Language,
…). It must be clear that maximum performance of queries cannot be always achieved, since the
implementation of the persistence service cannot always translate an abstract database query into
an optimal native database query (e.g. SQL), especially if product specific optimizations require a
completely different formulation of the database query. At least as an option, native database
queries must be considered for maximum batch performance.
Frontend Patterns
The frontend layer or tier describes the part of an application which implements the following
tasks:
• Creation and display of the visual representation
• Data display and provision of actions in the visual representation
• Acquisition and (syntactic) validation of user input
• Control of the visual representation (dialog navigation)
None of the application’s business data is stored persistently permanently in the presentation layer
since this is the task of the backend.
The figure below illustrates how the frontend’s tasks can be distributed at the browser and web
server in the case of web applications. Therefore when we talk about frontends in connection with
web applications, the term does not simply refer to the part running in the browser.

independently of the deployment architecture.

Modular Client Architecture
Overview
In our business applications, the client easily gets underestimated. Sometimes the client is more
complex to develop and design than the server. While the server architecture is nowadays easily to
agree as common sense, for clients this is not as obvious and stable especially as it typically
depends on the client framework used. Finding a concrete architecture applicable for all clients
may therefore be difficult to accomplish.
This document tries to define on an abstract level, an architecture which is supposed to be a mental
image and frame for orientation regarding the evaluation and appliance of different client
frameworks. As such it defines terms and concepts required to be provided for in any framework
and thus gives a common ground of understanding for those acquainted with the architecture. This
allows better comparison between the various frameworks out there, each having their own terms
for essentially the same concepts. It also means that for each framework we need to explicitly map
how it implements the concepts defined in this document.
The architecture proposed herein is neither new nor was it developed from scratch. Instead it is the
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 166
gathered and consolidated knowledge and best practices of various projects.
Goal of a Modular Client Architecture
The goal of the client architecture is to support the non-functional requirements for the client, i.e.
mostly maintainability, scalability, efficiency and portability. As such it provides a component-
oriented architecture following the same principles listed already in the Layered architecture
article. Furthermore it ensures a homogeneity regarding how different concrete UI technologies are
being applied in the projects, solving the common requirements in the same way.
Architecture Views
As for the server we distinguish between the business and the technical architecture. Where the
business architecture is different from project to project and relates to the concrete design of dialog
components given concrete requirements, the technical architecture can be applied to multiple
projects.
The focus of this document is to provide a technical architecture on the client on a very abstract
level defining required layers and components. How the architecture is implemented has to be
defined for each UI technology.
The technical infrastructure architecture is out of scope for this document and although it needs to
be considered, the concepts of the architecture should work across multiple deployment
architectures, i.e. native or web clients.
High level architecture view
The following gives a complete overview of the modular client architecture. It will be built up
incrementally in the following sections.
Figure 27. Complete Client Architecture Overview
On the highest level of abstraction we see the need to differentiate between dialog components and
their container they are managed in, as well as the access to the application server being the
backend for the client. This section gives a summary of these components and how they relate to
each other. Detailed architectures for each component will be supplied in subsequent sections.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 167
Figure 28. Overview of Client Architecture
Dialog Component
A dialog component is a logical, self-contained part of the user interface. It accepts user input and
actions and controls communication with the user. Dialog components use the services provided by
the dialog container in order to execute the business logic. They are self-contained, i.e. they possess
their own user interface together with the associated logic, data and states.
• Dialog components can be composed of other dialog components forming a hierarchy
• Dialog components can interact with each other. This includes communication of a parent to its
children, but also between components independent of each other regarding the hierarchy.
Dialog Container
Dialog components need to be managed in their life-cycle and how they can be coupled to each
other. The dialog container is responsible for this along with the following:
• Bootstrapping the client application and environment
◦ Configuration of the client
◦ Initialization of the application server access component
• Dialog Component Management
◦ Controlling the life-cycle
◦ Controlling the dialog flow
◦ Providing means of interaction between the dialogs
◦ Providing application server access
◦ Providing services to the dialog components
(e.g. printing, caching, data storage)
• Shutdown of the application
Application Server Access
Dialogs will require a backend application server in order to execute their business logic. The
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 168
backend exposes business oriented interfaces. These should also be present on the client backed by
a proxy handling the concrete call of the server over the network. This component provides the set
of interfaces as well as the proxy. Nowadays it is quite common to generate an API client based on
its specification in the concrete UI framework being used.
Dialog Container Architecture
The dialog container can be further structured into the following components with their respective
tasks described in own sections:
Figure 29. Dialog Container Architecture
Application
The application component represents the overall client in our architecture. It is responsible for
bootstrapping all other components and connecting them with each other. As such it initializes the
components below and provides an environment for them to work in.
Configuration Management
The configuration management manages the configuration of the client, so the client can be
deployed in different environments. This includes configuration of the concrete application server
to be called or any other environment-specific property.
Dialog Management
The Dialog Management component provides the means to define, create and destroy dialog
components. It therefore offers basic life-cycle capabilities for a component. In addition it also
allows composition of dialog components in a hierarchy. The life-cycle is then managed along the
hierarchy, meaning when creating/destroying a parent dialog, this affects all child components,
which are created/destroyed as well.
Service Registry
Apart from dialog components, a client application also consists of services offered to these. A
service can thereby encompass among others:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 169
• Access to the application server
• Access to the dialog container functions for managing dialogs or accessing the configuration
• Dialog independent client functionality such as Printing, Caching, Logging, Encapsulated
business logic such as tax calculation
• Dialog component interaction
The service registry offers the possibility to define, register and lookup these services. Note that
these services could be dependent on the dialog hierarchy, meaning different child instances could
obtain different instances / implementations of a service via the service registry, depending on
which service implementations are registered by the parents.
Services should be defined as interfaces allowing for different implementations and thus loose
coupling.
Dialog Component Architecture
A dialog component has to support all or a subset of the following tasks:
1. Displaying the user interface incl. internationalization
2. Displaying business data incl. changes made to the data due to user interactions and localization
of the data
3. Accepting user input including possible conversion from e.g. entered Text to an Integer
4. Displaying the dialog state
5. Validation of user input
6. Managing the business data incl. business logic altering it due to user interactions
7. Execution of user interactions
8. Managing the state of the dialog (e.g. Edit vs. View)
9. Calling the application server in the course of user interactions
Following the principle of separation of concerns, we further structure a dialog component in an
own architecture allowing us the distribute responsibility for these tasks along the defined
components:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 170
Figure 30. Overview of dialog component architecture
Presentation Layer
The presentation layer generates and displays the user interface, accepts user input and user
actions and binds these to the dialog core layer (Tasks 1-5). The tasks of the presentation layer fall
into two categories:
• Provision of the visual representation (View component)
The presentation layer generates and displays the user interface and accepts user input and
user actions. The logical processing of the data, actions and states is performed in the dialog
core layer. The data and user interface are displayed in localized and internationalized form.
• Binding of the visual representation to the dialog core layer
The presentation layer itself does not contain any dialog logic. The data or actions entered by
the user are then processed in the dialog core layer. There are three aspects to the binding to
the dialog core layer. We refer to “data binding”, “state binding” and “action binding”.
Syntactical and (to a certain extent) semantic validations are performed during data binding
(e.g. cross-field plausibility checks). Furthermore, the formatted, localized data in the
presentation layer is converted into the presentation-independent, neutral data in the dialog
core layer (parsing) and vice versa (formatting).
Dialog Core Layer
The dialog core layer contains the business logic, the control logic, and the logical state of the
dialog. It therefore covers tasks 5-9:
• Maintenance of the logical dialog state and the logical data
The dialog core layer maintains the logical dialog state and the logical data in a form which is
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 171
independent of the presentation. The states of the presentation (e.g. individual widgets) must
not be maintained in the dialog core layer, e.g. the view state could lead to multiple
presentation states disabling all editable widgets on the view.
• Implementation of the dialog and dialog control logic
The component parts in the dialog core layer implement the client specific business logic and
the dialog control logic. This includes, for example, the manipulation of dialog data and dialog
states as well as the opening and closing of dialogs.
• Communication with the application server
The dialog core layer calls the interfaces of the application server via the application server
access component services.
The dialog core layer should not depend on the presentation layer enforcing a strict layering and
thus minimizing dependencies.
Interactions between dialog components
Dialog components can interact in the following ways:
Figure 31. Dialog Interactions
• Embedding of dialog components
As already said dialog components can be hierarchically composed. This composition works by
embedding on dialog component within the other. Apart from the life-cycle managed by the
dialog container, the embedding needs to cope for the visual embedding of the presentation and
core layer.
◦ Embedding dialog presentation
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 172
The parent dialog needs to either integrate the embedded dialog in its layout or open it in an
own model window.
◦ Embedding dialog core
The parent dialog needs to be able to access the embedded instance of its children. This
allows initializing and changing their data and states. On the other hand the children might
require context information offered by the parent dialog by registering services in the
hierarchical service registry.
• Dialog flow
Apart from the embedding of dialog components representing a tight coupling, dialogs can
interact with each other by passing the control of the UI, i.e. switching from one dialog to
another.
When interacting, dialog components should interact only between the same or lower layers, i.e.
the dialog core should not access the presentation layer of another dialog component.
Appendix
Notes about Quasar Client
The Quasar client architecture as the consolidated knowledge of our C&CA projects is the major
source for the above drafted architecture. However, the above is a much simplified and more agile
version thereof:
• Quasar Client tried to abstract from the concrete UI library being used, so it could decouple the
business from the technical logic of a dialog. The presentation layer should be the only one
knowing the concrete UI framework used. This level of abstraction was dropped in this
architecture, although it might of course still make sense in some projects. For fast-moving agile
projects in the web, however, introducing such a level of abstraction takes effort with little
gained benefits. With frameworks like Angular we would even introduce one additional
seemingly artificial and redundant layer, since it already separates the dialog core from its
presentation.
• In the past and in the days of Struts, JSF, etc. the concept of session handling was important for
the client since part of the client was sitting on a server with a session relating it to its remote
counterpart on the users PC. Quasar Client catered for this need, by very prominently
differentiating between session and application in the root of the dialog component hierarchy.
However, in the current days of SPA applications and the lowered importance of servers-side
web clients, this prominent differentiation was dropped. When still needed, the referenced
documents will provide in more detail how to tailor the respective architecture to this end.
Backend Patterns
The backend is the part of an application which implements the following tasks:
• Processing and persistence of the application’s business data
• Implementation of the business logic
• Access to central resources
• Call of neighboring applications and third-party systems (possibly by means of suitable
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 173
middleware)
• Creation of central analyses and reports
When designing the architecture of a backend you have to make the following two major design
decisions:
• How to distribute and structure technical tasks such as transaction handling, integration with
other systems etc.
• How to design and model your business logic specific to your application
There are different options for both concerns addressed in the following pages:
• Technical patterns for the backend
• Business model patterns for the backend
Our current recommendation regarding the combination of both concerns is the
following:
• Use hexagonal architecture for addressing technical concerns, it should be our
 new standard
• For complex and business-critical subdomains, we propose to apply tactical
DDD
• For other use cases, or where the applicability of tactical DDD is not given we
recommend the use-case driven approach
Technical Backend Patterns
The technical architecture for the backend defines the high level structure of the backend code and
distributes the responsibilities for technical concerns. In order to better compare the different
patterns we will list the typical concerns here for later reference:
1. Implementation of the business logic: where is business logic implemented in the backend
and how can it be decomposed into smaller parts (s. application patterns for the backend)
2. Orchestration of business logic building blocks into higher services
3. Exposing functionality to other processes to the frontend and other 3rd party systems
4. Mapping of transfer objects to entity types and vice versa
5. Syntactic validation: checking the correctness of the incoming data from other systems or the
frontend
6. Semantic validation: checking the data and actions against the business rules of the domain
and application
7. Authentication and authorization: verifying the identity of the user and that they have the
corresponding rights with regards to the action as well as the data the action is executed upon
8. Transaction management
9. Data management: persistence and retrieval of data in a suitable data store
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 174
10. Integration of other 3rd party systems
11. Monitoring and logging (incl. performance metrics)
12. Error handling safety net
For the technical architecture we see most prominently two major patterns:
• Hexagonal architecture
• Layered architecture
 Currently, we recommend to adopt the hexagonal architecture in our projects (for
the underlying rationale please see below).
Comparison of Technical Backend Patterns and Recommendations
The hexagonal architecture is on first sight very similar to a typical layered architecture as
proposed e.g. by devonfw for some time or by the original DDD book:
• both have a dedicated place where to put the business logic of a system (logic layer / application
core)
• both provide abstractions towards the clients of the backend as well as to the persistence
solution
On a detailed level however, there are important differences, which make a hexagonal architecture
in our point of view the more business focussed and the more versatile option.
The figure below shows the same artifacts modeled in a hexagonal architecture as well as a layered
architecture.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 175
External External
System System
Services Primary/Driving Adapters Secondary/Driven Adapters
REST GraphQL Batch GraphQL WSDL
Controller Controller Controller Controller Adapter
Business Logic (Application Core)
WSDL REST
REST
Adapter Adapter
Controller REST
Adapter
Application Core
Use Case Use Case Use Case Use Case Use Case
Persistence
Batch
DB-Adapter
Controller
DB-Adapter
DB
Figure 32. Layered Architecture vs. Hexagonal Architecture
When you compare both figures you can see the above mentioned differences and resulting
benefits of the hexagonal architecture summarized below.
Cleaner separation of concerns
The interfaces of the persistence layer are defined in and by the application core thus advocating
a more clean dependency inversion principle. This is important to really put the focus on the
business or domain logic and not take the risk of a rather data-driven approach, where the data
model in the database influences (if not corrupts) the application core (s. warning below). This
risk is quite high and we see that in some of our projects, where the design starts with
identifying where to put the data of a feature and only afterwards making somehow use of that
data in the use cases. The emergent risk is that the data model tends to grow and is no longer
cleanly segregated.
More versatile integrations
Instead of decoupling the business logic just from a service/facade layer and a persistence layer,
the hexagonal architecture is much more open to whole range of different adapters. After all
you could consider an external system, where you get and update data also as some form of
persistence. Instead of the rather selective decoupling in the layered architecture, the hexagonal
architecture just differentiates between incoming and outgoing technology adapters. This not
only concerns API calls to other systems but also the integration of other crosscutting services
such as mailing, monitoring, etc. Such integrations don’t find a good place in the layered
architecture, since it either requires a new or cross-cutting layer or a logical dependency
upwards towards an interface in the service layer contradicting the layering principle.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 176
We have seen many cases in the past where the layered approach leads to a very
data-centric design driven by the data model of the database. The design then
starts by looking where the new data should be stored and adding it often to the
existing major entities because it seems to be the simplest solution. Over time this
can result in big entities and a bad separation of concerns. Use case
implementations then have to handle big entities, where they oftentimes only
need a small part of. This again leads to confusion and cognitive overload of the
development team. In addition, the application logic heavily depends on the

persistence layer and can sometimes not be tested independently from it.
Therefore you should be either mitigate this risk when applying the layered
architecture, or consider adopting the hexagonal pattern. By clearly separating the
application core from the technical concerns, it will highlight the focus of the
business logic and prevent functionality creep into the core, where it does not
belong and hence prevent bloated entities. Instead you will see more clearly when
a separated module or bounded context makes more sense to build additional
features instead of trying to force them into one and the same context.
For a more detailed comparison see the ATAM matrix below.
