Layered Architecture
The layered architecture divides the backend into layers based on the architectural principle
"arrange into layers". Each layer handles dedicated technical concerns described in the overview
below. Typically, a layered architecture has three separate layers, but there can be differences in
regard to the distribution of tasks among these layers. There are also cases of more than three
layers, but from our experience we would discourage such an approach, because the separation of
concerns becomes to fine granular and hard to manage.
Layer Overview
Layers bundle software with dedicated concerns and a similar technical structure, e.g. mapping
between entities and data transfer objects, use cases, or repositories. The layers are always
arranged in clear sequence, usually depicted vertically from top to bottom. A layer always makes its
functionality available to the layers above it, but can itself only access the layers located below it.
The layering described here is a layering with respect to static and dynamic dependencies as
described in the architectural principle "arrange into layers". Layers are therefore imported and
called from the top down. In the case of a strict layered architecture, there is the additional
restriction that a layer may only depend upon the layer located directly below it.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 178
The below figure shows the two pre-dominant layered architectures in C&CA. The left is the more
current approach as advocated by devonfw and present in projects based on this framework (s.
devonfw Layered Architecture). It is a more simple approach assigning most concerns to the logic
layer. The right approach is based on the one from our old Quasar methodology and old
architecture guide. It is based on an explicit facade-layer design moving some of these concerns to
that layer in order to divide the complexity across these layers.
Simplified Layered Architecture (devonfw) Facade-based Layered Architecture (Quasar)
Frontend Frontend
Backend Service A Backend Service A
11 12 11 12
Service Layer Batch Layer Facade Layer Batch Layer
Backend
3 Service B 2 3 4 5 8 2 8
Logic Layer Logic Layer
1 2 4 5 6 7 8 10 1 6 7 10
9 Data Access Layer 9 Data Access Layer
Backend
Service C
Database/Schema A Database/Schema A
Technical 1 Implementation of the business logic 4 Mapping of transfer objects 7 Authentication & authorization 10 Integration of 3rd party systems
concerns:
2 Orchestration of business logic 5 Syntactic validation 8 Transaction management 11 Monitoring and logging
3 Exposing functionality to other processes 6 Semantic validation 9 Data access management 12 Error Handling
inter-process or inter-tier dependency intra-process dependency betweeen logical layers
Figure 33. Layered Architecture
Simplified Layered Architecture (devonfw)
The simplified layered architecture (left option) divides the backend into the following layers:
Service layer
Exposes access to the backend logic via a technical protocol suitable for inter process
communication (REST, GraphQL, etc.). In the literature, this is sometimes called "remote facade".
Batch layer
Implements the batch jobs to trigger batch-processes (e.g. mass imports).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 179
Logic layer
The logic layer defines (value-oriented) interfaces (s. interface Design) exposing only transfer
objects. Interfaces are designed for different consumers (other components, other systems and
other clients). The implementation in the logic layer is then the business logic of the component.
This also includes the logical adaptation of data and its preparation when accessing third-party
systems. The artifacts of the logic layer work with the entity types of the data access layer and
provide transport objects to higher layers. They are not bound to any single entity but can work
with multiple entities via the data access layer.
Please note that there are multiple ways how the logic layer can be structured and designed (s.
Application Architecture patterns for the backend) For devonfw normally the Use-case driven
approach is taken.
Additionally, the tasks listed as bubbles in the figure above are performed in the logic layer (s. list of
concerns of the backend).
Data access layer
In the data access layer, the entity types are mapped to the database by means of a technical
persistence service. Individual database queries are also defined here. The data access layer
manages the lifecycle of the entity objects (generally: create, read, update, delete).
Facade-based layered architecture (Quasar)
Alternatively to the service layer described above, there is a different layered architecture possible
(and also done in our projects prior to the devonfw era). In this option, the service layer was called
facade layer with an additional hidden service access layer for exposing the defined interfaces via a
technical protocol. This facade layer resembles the pattern "Service Layer" as described in Patterns
of Enterprise Application Architecture. The logic layer would in this alternative directly return
reference-based entity types instead of DTOs and DTS. Instead, the facade layer was responsible to
map the entity types to transfer structures and also take over some of the other concerns (s. figure
above). Hence, the facade layer was providing the value-oriented interfaces to different clients and
implemented additionally some semantic validations in collaboration with the business logic layer.
Benefits of this alternative can be the following
• The service layer described above sometimes seems to be nothing more than a slim
boilerplate repetition of the logic layer. As such it can seem rather artificial, which
developers would question.
• As a facade, value-oriented interfaces for other components can be designed explicitly in this
abstraction layer, while reusing the same business logic artifacts. In the simplified version
often the same transfer structures are simply reused and extended for various purposes. A
dedicated layer for this concern at least advocates more thought on the interface design.
• The logic layer in the simple approach has to address most of the concerns of the backend,
not really following the divide & conquer approach
However, in practice the facade layer often can turn out to be quite complex and the differentiation
of what validation should be implemented in the facade or logic layer can get confusing. In
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 180
addition, the explicit decoupling by provisioning different interfaces for different components and
contexts can also be done in the logic layer in the simplified version. It just requires dedicated care
to do so. Introducing a dedicated layer does however not remove the need for this explicit design
and immediately enforces a better design.
Our default recommendation is to apply the simplified layered architecture as

described above following the KISS principle. The approach is also now more
widely known due to devonfw and therefore eases team onboarding.
Layered Architecture for a modular monolith
For an application structured as a Modular monolith, the layered architecture can be applied in
each component derived from the functional decomposition. Each component should be physically
represented in the code (e.g. via a namespace or a build unit). Below this structuring element, each
component is then subdivided into layers.
From our point of view, a pure layer-only architecture makes sense only in very
 small applications. It should always be combined with the vertical component
architecture, even if you start with only one component since it adds only near-to-
none overhead.
When applying both architecture patterns together a structure as shown below can be the result.
The Layered Component Architecture focuses on the backend service A that consists of two
components. The components are divided into the layers described above. In addition, cross-cutting
concerns are implemented in technical components, not directly assigned to one particular layer,
since they can affect or be used by multiple of those.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 181

Basic communication rules across layers
The following rules apply to the communications performed by a component artifact:
• It is possible to call component artifacts of the same layer within the same application
component
• It is possible to call component artifacts of the lower-level layers within the same application
component
Communication rules for component artifacts in the Service layer
The component artifacts in the service layer may communicate with
• Value-oriented interfaces in the logic layer of other application components
• Component artifacts in the logic layer of the same component
• Component artifacts in the data access layer of the same component
Communication rules for component artifacts in the logic layer
The component artifacts in the logic layer may communicate with
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 182
• Value-oriented interfaces in the logic layer of other application components
• Component artifacts in the logic layer of the same component
• Component artifacts in the data access layer of the same component
Communication rules for component artifacts in the data access layer
The component artifacts in the data access layer may only communicate with the technical
persistence service
Of particular importance is the fact that component artifacts in the data access layer may not
communicate with the interfaces of other component artifacts.
There is one exception to the rule which is introduced for reporting requirements. In such cases,
queries may need to be constructed over a larger graph of entities then those contained in the
reporting component. Therefore, for read-only access such a component might access the persisted
data of other components directly.
Components for a facade-based layered architecture
For a facade-based layered architecture the figure looks similar, but due to the enhanced
responsibility of the facade layer, it also takes care of defining value-oriented interfaces to other
components for loose coupling (s. figure).

In addition, as can be seen from the picture, the facade-based approach allows to differentiate
between different levels of coupling. A component can be closely coupled to another component by
directly invoking its reference-based entity interfaces. Of course, this comes with a whole series of
drawbacks, but might be useful in exceptional cases.
Beware of the risks of sharing entities between components. This introduces a high

degree of coupling, the risk of leaking business logic to the other component and
endangers locality of changes among others.
Hexagonal Architecture
(a.k.a. Ports-and-Adapters)
Overview
In a hexagonal architecture, the application logic is isolated within a core building block, separating
it from the surrounding context. The context, in turn, is divided into two parts:
• a primary context (also called driving or incoming) and
• a secondary context (also called driven or outgoing).
The difference between both contexts is that the primary one calls the application core and the
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 184
secondary one is called by the application core. The primary context calls the application core
through interfaces defined and implemented by the application core. The secondary context is
called by the application core through interfaces defined by the application core and implemented
by the secondary context. See section Provided and Required Interfaces.
Thus, the application holds the sovereignty of all interface definitions (the ports). For incoming
ports it also provides an implementation, whereas for outgoing ports it expects an implementation
in the secondary context. For this reason, the adapters are also called driving and driven adapters.
Incoming / primary adapters are driving the application because they trigger an action of the
application being performed, while outgoing / Secondary adapters are being driven from the
application to do something.
The focus on the application core is one of the main benefits of this pattern, since it allows
designing, developing and testing the business logic isolated from any technical considerations. An
excellent more detailed summary can be found in the link listed below from Herberto Graca
[graca2017].
Primary/Driving Adapters Secondary/Driven Adapters
GraphQL
Controller External
System
REST
Controller WSDL
Adapter
Application Core
Place your application logic
here strictly separating it
from the surrounding
technology-bound context
REST
Adapter
External
Batch
System
Controller
DB-Adapter
DB
Figure 36. Adapters and interfaces in a hexagonal architecture
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 185
Ports-and-adapters
The original name for this kind of architecture was port-and-adapters originating from Alistair
Cockburn. The ports are the interfaces provided or required by the application core and are
typically programming language specific artifacts (e.g. Java interfaces). The adapters are either
incoming or outgoing adapters connecting the application core to the context. The incoming
adapters expose a technology- and caller-specific interface and implement it by calling the ports
provided by the application core. See section Interfaces Grouped by Utilization. The outgoing
adapters on the other hand implement the ports defined by the application core by
delegating/adapting the interface calls to external systems. These outgoing adapters are responsible
for translating the core’s domain model to the model of the external system called. They also use a
specific technology to connect to the external system. The application core consists of the domain
entities and logic including the ports which connect the application core to the adapters.
As the name of the architecture readily suggests, it puts a lot of emphasis on the separation of the
business logic from everything else. Extensive descriptions of strategies to achieve this goal in
regard to persistence can be found in the persistence adapters chapter.
The Core
The core focuses on providing a business solution for the problem to be solved with the help of the
application. It is independent of the surrounding contexts and uses ports to abstract from it.
The concrete structure of the core is another design decision to be made and different options exist
(s. Application architecture patterns for the backend).
Ports
Ports are interfaces defined by the core. They are either:
• called by the core and should be implemented by an according outgoing adapter
• or implemented by the core and called by an incoming adapter.
Ports should be agnostic of any knowledge belonging to the corresponding adapter, e.g., the domain
of the external system or the technology used. One big advantage of the hexagonal architecture is
that the adapters can be changed without changing the core and therefore, without touching the
business logic.
This is especially important with regard to the emerging architecture. E.g., a service that needs to
store some data could provide a port called DataPersistencePort. In an early stage, writing JSON to a
file as storage is enough because it’s not yet decided how it is stored best. A very simple adapter is
built for this reason. Later this adapter is replaced by an adapter using the right technology (s.
[JITJEA] sample 4). By keeping the port agnostic to uncertain details (in the example the storage
technology, and not naming it FileWriterPort) the core does not need to be touched at all.
Slicing ports
There are varying opinions on how to slice ports, ranging from just one driving and one driven port
described as an option in [cockburn2005] to having a separate driving port for each use case and a
driven port for every single function realized by a driven adapter (e.g. for every database
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 186
operation) advocated for in [Hombergs21].
The former approach was already judged as not optimal by the author itself, which becomes
obvious at the latest when needing to divide a subsystem into components (see modular monolith
pattern). The latter one carries the risk of producing an unmanageable number of implementation
artifacts when dealing with rich business logic.
As usual, the most fitting approach for a given subsystem lies somewhere between those extremes.
There are, however, several questions to which the answers may proof helpful when deciding on
guidelines in a project:
• If the business logic is subject to frequent changes, having single use case driving ports allows
for multiple implementations of the same use case to be available at the same time, for example
for backwards compatibility.
• If the external systems are subject to frequent changes or the subsystem uses different
technologies to implement the same type of driven adapters (e.g. JPA and JDBC for data access),
having single function driven ports makes those truly technology-agnostic and decoupled,
following the single responsibility principle (see Separation of concerns).
Testing the core
The core contains the business logic and as explained in this article should be independent of
specific technologies or surroundings. Hence, unit or integration tests are used to test the business
logic in the core. The adapters can be easily mocked away using the port interfaces.
Adapters
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 187

Figure 37. Adapter overview
Adapters connect the application core to the surrounding context. They can have, for example, the
following tasks:
• Implement a specific protocol to connect to the context (e.g. REST, JDBC, MQTT, …).
• Maintain a data model that is necessary to communicate with the context.
• Translate the domain model from the core to that model or vice versa.
• Handle protocol-specific errors.
• Implement resilience patterns. E.g., the rate limiter pattern in the incoming adapter or timeout
and retries in the outgoing adapters.
• Log the interaction with the surrounding context.
• Handle transactions.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 188
Incoming adapters
Incoming Adapter Core
adapter <<interface>>
use case
implementation incoming port
adapter
mapper domain model
specific model
Figure 38. Incoming adapter
Incoming adapters trigger the application’s business logic. Usually, they react to the surrounding
context and execute the application. This might be a web-based request from another service or a
user interface. It could be an event, but also a scheduler firing. The incoming adapters then execute
the corresponding incoming port of the application core. If necessary, the adapter is responsible for
creating the data structures expected by the port.
Outgoing adapters
Core Outgoing Adapter
<<interface>> adapter
use case
outgoing port implementation
domain model mapper adapter specific model
Figure 39. Outgoing adapter
Outgoing adapters allow the core to actively interact with the surrounding context. This includes
reading or writing data from or to a persistence service, sending events or web requests.
Errors that happen in the outgoing adapters can be either business related like a missing resource
or pure technical like a service that is not available. Business related errors always need to be
resolved by the business logic and handling is therefore implemented in the application core.
Technical issues are usually handled by a defined strategy. If the strategy requires a certain
business logic to solve the error, the implementation belongs into the core. Only a pure technical
solution is located in the outgoing adapter.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 189
Example 8. Resilience patterns in outgoing adapters
In case a service is not reachable, there exist multiple resilience patterns. To clarify the point
here, the retry and fallback patterns are elaborated. A retry is a pure technical solution, where
the service is called again after a certain time, probably with a maximum number of retries
and an exponentially increasing, jittered waiting time. This is a typical example of a pure
technical solution that can be implemented in the outgoing adapter.
Another option is to use fallback values that are used when the service is not available. Those
require knowledge of the business case and should be located in the business implementation
as part of the core.
Especially in regard to adapters that abstract a persistence service, there are different
considerations to take into account. Those are described in detail in the persistence adapters article.
Example 9. An Example of the difference between incoming and outgoing adapters
Incoming adapters are the active part of the application. They trigger the business core on
some kind of event. This event is independent of the protocol. It could be a scheduler checking
on the database if a certain data set is available. In case this happens, the core’s business logic
is triggered.
It then could be that the same function calls an outgoing adapter to store some other
information in the exact same database. But this action is passive, meaning it’s triggered by the
core.
Testing adapters
As adapters usually do not contain any complex logic, they can be tested implicitly by the
integration tests of the application as a whole. In case of adapters contain more complexity, they
should and can be tested by an own unit test mocking away the external dependencies. Complexity
could mean a complex mapping, some orchestration of external calls, etc.
Depending on the type of adapter and the used technology or frameworks, the testing techniques
vary:
• For incoming adapters, usually there needs to be a trigger, e.g., a scheduler firing or a web
request. Web requests usually need a running application, and for schedulers you do not want
to wait until the actual trigger time. In both cases, it is wise to select frameworks that come with
a dedicated support for testing.
• For outbound adapters, the challenges lie more in the external context and how to interact with
it within the testing setup. There are multiple solutions available for such challenges:
◦ For some technologies, there are solutions by the used framework to connect to the context.
◦ Some tools come with dedicated testing support by providing test stubs.
◦ Containers can be used to easily ramp up the needed context in a containerized
environment.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 190
Flow of dependencies
Similar to how the direction of static dependencies is always downwards in a layered architecture,
in hexagonal or onion architecture, the static dependencies point inwards: The adapters import the
ports defined by the core, there is no static dependency from the application core to the adapters.
This principle makes sure that the decoupling of the application core from its contexts mentioned
in the overview is fulfilled.
Primary/Driving Adapters Secondary/Driven Adapters
Application Core
Figure 40. Static dependency directions in a hexagonal architecture
On the one side, for the driving adapters of an application this is realized naturally and equivalent
to a layered architecture. On the other side, for the driven adapters, the dependency inversion
principle is applied: The adapters import ports from the core but are called by the core, i.e., the
static dependencies point inwards, the dynamic dependencies outwards. The benefits of this
approach in comparison to the layered architecture are described in the section Comparison of
Technical Backend Patterns and Recommendations.
Of course, this direction of static dependency flow implies a fully technology-agnostic application
core, which presents challenges touched upon in the following section.
Application of the Software Categories principle
The main focus of this pattern is a principle we are well familiar with and which has been part of
our architecture mindset for a long time: the separation of different knowledge areas by software
categories and in particular the separation of business from technology logic.
In a hexagonal architecture, the application core should be as technology-agnostic as possible and
only implement application or business logic. This allows you to focus clearly on this concern only
and enables a design of a structure which best reflects the business model.
Of course, this opens a trade-off question of what is considered technology specific and what is part
of the standard technology. The 0-software category from Quasar tries to define exactly such a
neutral software, which can be used in A-software as well. It postulates that 0-software should be
globally available, unchanging extensions of the development environment (such as undo/redo
mechanism, a virtual time, etc.).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 191
However, there are already differing opinions on whether a library such as Spring should be
considered as 0-software. In the advent of new DI frameworks such as Quarkus, it does not
necessarily fit the criteria of unchanging and hinders a migration to such a framework if the
application core is wired via Spring configurations. Therefore, one interpretation of this paradigm
is to only use the vanilla runtime environment available in the application core without any
dependencies to other libraries. This requires, however, a heavy decoupling and can at times seem
artificial. Similar trade-off decisions from the Java ecosystem would need to be made, e.g., for JPA,
Lombok or Project Reactor.
In the end, as always, you should base your trade-off decision on the NFRs of your project. Simple
put the question on the table: is a portability across different DI containers or database a NFR or
not? Each project should define what is 0-software, document that decision properly and enforce
the adherence to it by using ArchUnit or jQAssistant.
Applicability
The hexagonal architecture in combination with DDD has gained a lot of traction in the past years.
In our projects in the past, we typically rather applied the layered architecture from devonfw.
However, even though similar, the hexagonal architecture offers in our point more benefits and
should definitely be considered as the preferred option in new projects. For a detailed comparison,
see section Comparison of Technical Backend Patterns and Recommendations. Before applying a
hexagonal architecture, consider the following:
• a full separation of the application core from technical concerns will also add complexity to
understanding the overall solution
• therefore, consider if you will really benefit from the pattern:
◦ is there a real need to have different adapters or is the application only consisting of a
service and persistence port anyway?[3]
◦ is the application logic rich enough, so it makes actually sense to fully separate it from any
technical concerns?[4]
• is your team used to the layered approach and would this impose too much of a change without
actually requiring the benefits (s. above considerations)?
In case you adopt the pattern for your project:
• define namespace conventions and communicate them to the team (we are working on a
proposal for this by the way)
• document the approach and provide guidance for team members if needed
Hexagonal Architecture for a Modular Monolith
The hexagonal architecture can also be applied in the context of a modular monolith. In a modular
monolith, the application is decomposed into components, which are in turn derived from - or the
same as - the functional building blocks after performing a functional decomposition. Each
component must, however, be represented in the structure/organization of the code as well. In
combination with the hexagonal architecture pattern, each component can be represented by a
hexagon.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 192

Internal Call
Figure 41. Hexagonal Component Architecture Overview
As an alternative approach, the core could be divided into business components. We do not
recommend this alternative, because in and outgoing adapters would be independent of the
business components. Leading to adapters, that are possibly connected to more than one business
component, which would lead to an increased complexity when extracting a component into a
separate service.
Business components should be loosely coupled, so the dependencies between them must be
strictly managed. Therefore, it is highly recommended to hide communication details in outgoing
and incoming ports. As shown in the overview of a hexagonal component architecture, there are
two kinds of communications between internal components: synchronous and asynchronous calls.
Every communication across components should be via an outgoing adapter on

the calling component and an incoming adapter on the called component. This
way a clear separation of components is guaranteed.
Synchronous calls within an application usually means that one component calls procedures of
another component directly. For this communication, an outgoing adapter should be created in
component A calling methods of an incoming adapter of component B. This way there is a clear
interface in component B that can be called and separating both components. Furthermore, this
implementation makes it easier to carve out components into independent services when needed.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 193
It might feel cumbersome to use this way of communication, because the use cases
of component A could just call use cases of component B directly. In this scenario,
component A would be coupled to the core of component B including B’s model.
Hence, changes to component B’s core would immediately lead to changes in the
core of component of A and A could have access to more functionality than

intended.
To decouple A from B, component A defines a port in it’s core, based on the data
that is needed. An outgoing adapter in A would than map this to the functionality
of B. This functionality is exposed via the incoming adapter of B with defined
DTOs.
For asynchronous communication, the same applies. When using an external system for
asynchronous communication, like a queuing provider, incoming and outgoing adapters should be
created and the communication should be handled as if it was coming from or to an external
service. For application internal event buses, again incoming and outgoing adapters should be
created. This creates two independent components communicating via an explicitly defined
interface. This allows an easier extraction of components into single services in the future.
Hexagonal architecture in Quasar?
On a side note, the Quasar reference architecture (s.below) again resembles a bit the
hexagonal architecture. It emphasizes an application core, which provides interfaces to and
depends on interfaces from other systems. It also propagates the idea of a clear A/T-
separation between the different building blocks.
Figure 42. Quasar Reference Architecture
However, as you can see in the figure above, it still does not match the hexagonal
architecture fully, since the core does not define all interfaces itself (s. persistence or
authorization).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 194

Business model backend patterns
The backend encapsulates the business logic in order to provide access to its functionality not only
for the UI, but also for other systems. The business logic respresents the core value of the
application for an information systems. As such, it needs to be maintainable, i.e. understandable,
analyzeable and changeable in order to be able to evolve efficiently. Different patterns have
emerged over time to model the business logic to this purpose.
We present two major patterns here as a dichotomy of two principle options:
• Tactical Domain Driven Design aims to create a rich model closely reflecting the domain and
formalizing the ubiquitous language
• Use Case Driven Design takes a simpler approach in encapsulating cohesive functionality in one
artifact, the use case
Both patterns should be applied on the level of a component of a modular
 monolith) or for a single, small microservice of an application. In case of a
modular monolith, the patterns can even both be applied for different components
of a system if feasible.
From our point of view both patterns have their valid applications depending on a trade-off
decision to be made. The most fundamental difference is that of the domain moddel and how it is
reflected. The basic choice is between a rich or an anemic domain model.
Anemic vs Rich Domain Models
"In a rich domain model, as much of the domain logic as possible is implemented within the
entities at the core of the application. The entities provide methods to change state and only
allow changes that are valid according to the business rules. […] In an “anemic” domain
model, the entities themselves are very thin. They usually only provide fields to hold."
[hombergs2019]
Both ways have some benefits and drawbacks. In object oriented languages interacting with objects
and business models resides in a very versatile objects that use the business language. [Vernon13]
states that the ubiquitous language should be a pattern to capture the concepts and terms of a
specific business domain in the software model itself.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 195
On the other hand the domain model is available for the adapters and hence business logic might
leak into the adapters. Furthermore, the design of a good software model that adheres to the
business model is a complex task. Keeping the business logic in the use cases as sequential flows
might be easier.
In regards of when and how the domain model can be combined with ORM frameworks take a look
into the persistence adapters article.
Based on this the following ATAM matrix can be derived to give an orientation of the advantages
and drawbacks of both approaches.

Tactical Domain Driven Design
Overview
Domain Driven Design can be roughly split into a strategic and tactical design level. As part of the
Strategic DDD a domain is decomposed into subdomains and each subdomain into bounded
contexts. The tactical design level is designated for further designing a single bounded context. It
consists of a series of building blocks proposed initially by Eric Evans in order to define the model
of a bounded context. These building blocks result in certain expected artifacts with distinct
responsibilities. As such the tactical DDD is a blueprint on how to structure the business logic of an
application in these building blocks. It has the clear focus of putting the domain into focus and
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 197
favors a rich domain model over an anemic one.
For detailed information on the building blocks we refer to the DDD Reference which contains
short summaries of each. It also contains the following overview which can serve as an orientation:
Figure 43. DDD Overview
In this guide we will focus on the following aspects:
• The applicability of tactical DDD in a project (What are the prerequisites for this to work?)
• Our point of view on the building blocks and some guidance on how to apply them
• Relation to other approaches for backend design and possible migration paths
Applicability
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 198
Ubiquitous language and domain model
As can be seen from the overview above both the tactical design and the strategic design have as a
common element the ubiquitous language. It serves as a cross-cutting ambition underlying domain
driven design in general. The goal is to establish and build a common language expressed as a
domain model shared between business and IT.
However, given that for a complex system across multiple organizational units this
will become to complex, DDD only thrives to establish this goal on the tactical level

of a bounded context. On strategic level between bounded contexts, the ubiquitous
language instead serves as a demarcation line between bounded contexts where
concepts and words mean different things.
Although we see this as a worthy goal in order to maximize the business value out of the software
to be developed, it requires a culture on customer side which is willing to accept this goal as well.
Especially since both sides are expected to contribute to the ubiquitous language. Oftentimes our
customers are however seeing themselves as clients requesting a service and are not willing to
invest their own time to adapt to this approach and dive into modeling details.
Without business support willing to build the language and model together with the development
team, the alternative would be to let the developers build their own model. This in turn leads to
major benefits of DDD being thwarted:
• The implemented model will not reflect the business perspective.
• Change requests will require a high amount of refactoring for some time until developers have
gained enough domain knowledge.
• Requirements can no longer easily be traced to the code implementing them, an implicit
mapping from business requests to the code is required
• In the end there might even remain certain parts in the model requiring mapping from the
terms the business uses to the artifact names in the model.
• This is because some business concepts and terms are likely not defined accurately enough and
should not be used 1:1 in the model. Normally the feedback loop from the development team to
the business would lead to clarification and sharpening of the underlying concepts.
• Consequently, changes in the domain could not easily be evaluated with regards to the impact
they have on the model and implementation.
Likewise, the development team also needs to be interested in the domain they are developing
software for and willing to learn these concepts. This is also not always a given and would lead to
even more of the above listed problems. It also means that when applying tactical DDD with an
inexperienced team, care has to be taken to manage this aspect.
Complexity and differentiation
DDD on a tactical level requires a lot of initial investment into communication and subsequent
design of the model. This can require more initial effort than other approaches to software
architecture. The investment is therefore especially worthwhile in those subdomains with a high
complexity and which allow a customer to differentiate itself from competitors. The high
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 199
complexity leads to a rich domain model, the differentiation is the argument for a valid business
case with a positive ROI. In other subdomains with less complexity or no real business benefit, the
tactical DDD approach might not be worthwhile and should carefully be considered. Im summary,
tactical DDD should be especially considered in core subdomains (s. Strategic DDD). Be aware
however of the risk, that an originally thought supporting/generic subdomain can turn out to be a
core subdomain!
Seniority of the development team
As mentioned in the previous section, DDD requires investment into a good design of the domain
model. This in turn requires developers with some experience or the opportunity and room for
development. For some customers this might be a mismatch to their expectation of a consultancy
company to be able to directly deliver value. The available team mix should therefore be taken into
account and might lead towards a decision of a more industrialized approach.
Guidance for applying DDD in your project
Preamble
Although the books by Eric Evans and Vaughn Vernon (blue and red book) provide a
comprehensive explanation of the building blocks, the implementation still offers a lot of freedom
and requires trade off decisions to be made:
• What should go into an application service and what should be placed into a domain service?
• What should be placed into a domain service and what should be part of a an aggregate?
• What is the fitting technical architecture for tactical DDD? Is it the layered architecture, or
rather the hexagonal one?
• Should there be restrictions on the allowed dependencies between artifacts?
• …?
This guide does not intend to provide the silver bullet to answer all these questions. We do think
however that in a concrete project, clear guidance on these questions should be given. From our
point of view we do not see much benefit in allowing more freedom than is necessary, since this
unnecessarily complicates the maintenance of the software. We would rather advise to follow the
architecture principle of consistency instead. Otherwise, team members will be confused by the
different design decisions being made.
We provide here an example of such a guidance on how the tactical design should be applied. Since
each project context is different, deviations from this guidance should be considered and rather a
commitment within a team reached.
Example Guidance for applying DDD in practice
From our point of view the following recommendations can make sense:
Technical Architecture
As outlined in the applicability (s. Complexity and differentiation), DDD on a tactical level makes
mostly sense in core domains with high complexity and the willingness to invest more effort. In
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 200
those cases it also makes sense to go all the way and properly separate this valuable business
logic completely from technical aspects. Therefore we to consider applying the hexagonal
architecture pattern in those cases. This kind of architecture will properly separate a rich
business-logic core implementation from technical aspects, which would be the case here.[5]
Services
Consequently when a hexagonal architecture is applied, from our point of view, application
services act as an input port artifact and are part of the infrastructure layer around the domain
core. Domain services on the other hand should only be used, when they are orchestrating logic
across multiple aggregates and they are used by different application services. Otherwise and as
a starting point, this orchestration can be directly implemented in the application service.
Aggregates
Aggregates are often underestimated and should be a central building block for further
modularization of a bounded context. They should be understood as a collection of consistent
rules (minimum one, otherwise don’t create an aggregate). They hide their entities (incl. the root
entities) and for achieving loose coupling only exchange value objects. Thus, they act as a facade
or guard to a set of entities ensuring the rules are preserved. As a rule of thumb, aggregates
typically manage 1-3 entities. They should also be completely autonomous, i.e. after they are
created from a factory or builder, they don’t require any access to for instance repositories. A
good method of aligning aggregates with the business understanding achieving locality of
changes is design-level event storming (s. this video for more details and a concrete example)
Dependencies
We advise to only allow the dependencies and associations shown in the figure below in order to
slightly enforce a homogenous design and prevent cyclic dependencies. The core design choices
lie in the modeling of aggregates, entities and value objects anyway and you still have the
complete freedom to do so. The more technical building blocks however do not require this
freedom in design (e.g. it does not make sense to allow value objects to use repositories). In
addition, these rules are derived from the the clear separation of technical and domain logic
driven by the hexagonal architecture.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 201
Infrastructure
uses

«InputPort» «OutputPort»

Figure 44. Technical DDD blueprint based on a hexagonal architecture
Conventions
We would advise to establish clear naming, namespace and packaging conventions. Although
there are different flavors for doing so, there is more value in following one pattern, than in the
freedom of having multiple ways of doing things, without clear benefits.
On a side note: DDD in functional architecture
Complex domains can also be implemented in a functional architecture. This follows a
completely different, non-object-oriented approach based on functions and immutable
records only as well as more complex concepts like monads. Since we usually do not apply
functional architectures in our projects, we only reference here to the book Domain modeling
made functional linked below for further information.
Migrating to tactical DDD
In case you have a system, which has degraded in terms of maintainability (e.g. due to an overally
generic domain model), it is possible to refactor your domain logic towards DDD. This can be done
incrementally also within an already existing system (s. paper by Eric Evans listed below). The idea
is to create a bubble context within the existing architecture applying a proper hexagonal
architecture with the infrastructure layer being implemented within the frame of the existing
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 202
application. This allows later migration of the domain core into an autonomous context.
Links
• DDD Reference by Eric Evans: contains a link to a free PDF which contains explanations for all
the patterns in the book incl. the tactical ones.
• Anemic Domain Model by Martin Fowler: His view on anemic domain models being an anti-
pattern. In the experience from our projects we where able to nonetheless provide complex
functional systems successfully based on such anemic domain models.
• DDD in legacy systems by Eric Evans: different strategies for applying DDD in systems following
a different approach
• Domain Modeling Made Functional by Scott Wlaschin
• DDD implemented with Spring: repository from Michael Plöd with an example implementation
in Spring applying also some of the guidance from above

• Copy of the DDD Reference book
• DDD Resources Miro Board Useful links to books, blogs, resources, DDD communities provided
by INNOQ in one of their trainings with allowance to share within the companies of the
participants.
• DDD Training in MyLearning
Use Case Driven Design
The use-case driven design follows a rather straightforward approach by structuring your
application logic in different use cases, which orchestrate the required steps and actions. Between
the use cases dependencies can exist if needed.
The figure below shows the structure of such an application component applying the hexagonal
technical architecture. The figure would look similar for the layered architecture. The important bit
to focus here is the structure of the core explained below.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 203
Call
Dependency
Internal Call
Application
Input Adapters
Incoming Adapter
calls

Incoming Ports

Domain Use Cases Services
Core
<<interface>>
Outgoing Ports
depends on
through DI
implements
Output Adapters Outgoing Adapter
Domain
The domain contains the entities of the business domain which is reflected by a simple data model.
The entities should only contain if at all only business logic based on the data of their own fields
and the data provided by methods of their directly associated classes. This is a simple appliance of
the Law of demeter (speak only to your next friends) and enforces a minimization of dependencies.
As an alternative, the rule can be to prohibit any business logic in order to prevent dependency
creep.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 204
The design of the entity model is one of the major design decisions to be made and
as such can pose a risk for the future maintainability of the application. You should
 take care to create a sound, business oriented data model clearly separating
segregated functionality. Often we see in our projects the tendency to add for
change requests fields to new entities where they seem to best fit instead of
reconsidering and possibly refactoring the entity model.
Use cases
Use cases are the main entry point for the core logic of the application. They validate the given
input against business rules, fetch and send data via ports (or a data access object in a layered
architecture) and implement the logic using the data. For the implementation they can make use
and orchestrate services explained in the next chapter.
The organization of the business logic in different use-cases is the other
fundamental design decision to be made in this approach apart from the domain
model. It allows to modularize the component or bounded context further into

cohesive parts which should be maintained together. This follows the principle of
low coupling and high cohesion. Best practice is to align this with the business and
structure it as they would group it. That way changes to their requirements will be
ideally localized to the respective use cases.
Use-cases can be well compared to Aggregates in domain-driven design and design-level event
storming could be viable option to determine the cut of use-cases same as for aggregates.
Services
Services provide a reusable part of the application’s business logic that is used by multiple use
cases. They can be also used to extract a coherent part of the business logic, which also enables
independent testing of that logic. Services are an option to further structure the application, but
they’re not required.
Persistence Patterns
We collect patterns here which enable the persistent storage of data and the retrieval thereof as
well as the integration within the backend micro-architecture.
In the future we plan to provide additional articles on:
• SQL database
• NoSQL databases
Persistence Adapters
Overview
Persistence adapter is a mediator between domain logic and storage technology. The primary
purpose of implementing persistence adapters is to hide all technical details of storage technology
and expose only the necessary models and interactions via an interface.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 205
inbound outbound
flow
domain port adapter
dependency
inbound outbound
Figure 45. Conceptual view of persistence adapter
In this guideline, we present the following aspects:
• implementation techniques for relational and non-SQL storages,
• notes on using of automatic ORM tools,
• change detection techniques,
• transaction handling.
Challenges
Certain characteristics make persistent adapters more challenging to implement than other kinds
of adapters.
Persistence adapters are always driven (see Figure 46), that is, the domain logic must be aware of
them, because business logic code is an initiator of the communication here.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 206
driving adapters driven adapters
s
t
b o
e r
w a
g
e
flow of control
core
flow of control
s t n o t s
r
e a
v g
e e
Figure 46. Driving and driven adapters in hexagonal architecture
Business (domain) logic is therefore exposed to all implementation details of persistence adapters
unless a port (an interface) is introduced to separate both worlds, as shown on Overview. Port
declaration becomes a part of domain logic as well as the data model it uses to transfer data
between the logic and persistence.
It is not that uncommon that the data model of the port reflects the data model of the adapter:
either it involved two nearly identical data structures or, in extreme case, the same data structure
is shared by both modules (that is, the business logic and the persistence adapter). In both cases,
domain logic and persistence code become semantically tangled, meaning a change in one of them
may lead to changes in the other. This semantic dependency between modules is exactly what
architects and developers should avoid.
It is noteworthy that persistence mechanisms are very different in their implementation details,
therefore, some adapters can be very easy to implement and some can be challenging. Document
databases are very efficient in modeling and persisting complex data structures but can be very
tedious to use in reporting, complex migrations and complex querying. Relational databases are
making reporting and querying trivial, but persisting complex data structures just by using
standard SQL queries is cumbersome.
Because of that, the choice of persistence technology may not be transparent to the system and
especially to the ports thus business logic. It makes technology-agnostic systems difficult to design,
and sometimes not even possible.
Let’s look at these challenges in details and then, consider solutions as described in [Solutions].
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 207
Data structure mapping
Good software architecture promotes strict concept separations. That is, adapter implementation is
considered a technical aspect thus should be separated from domain logic, which is more business
related and generally more abstract. This leads to the tendency of having two data models and
utilizing a kind of mapping code to transfer data between them.
It happens that these models are nearly identical and mapping code gets immediately replaced by
ready to use libraries such as MapStruct. It is noteworthy that in this case, there is very little value
added in maintaining these two families of data models at the price of greater code base and
overall software complexity. This topic is described in details in Model separation.
Change tracking
For data-oriented systems it is not that uncommon to have situation as depicted on Figure 47. The
use case loads an entity via load method of the storage port, the content is somehow modified by
the use case and then the changes should be persisted via save method of the storage port. In case
our data model is very complex and/or large such save implementation may be non-trivial and
some kind of change detection mechanism may be helpful.
domain logic
load
modify
storage
save
Figure 47. Flow of data in typical entity modification use case
Object Relational impedance mismatch
As for 2022, still the most common method of persisting data in enterprise systems is to use
relational databases. Interoperability between relational database and object-oriented
programming language is frequently implemented by using object-relational mapping (ORM in
short).
ORM is taking advantage of certain similarities between relational and object models. However,
there is no full compatibility between relational and object-oriented worlds, and this fact is known
as Object-Relational impedance mismatch. The impedance mismatch can be characterized by the
following problems:
• object to table mapping problems (i.e. inheritance and associations - concepts that are unknown
to relational model),
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 208
• the schema ownership problem (whether the DBA owns the schema or application developers),
• the dual schema problem (the definition is both in database and in ORM configuration, and in
most of the cases it is not feasible to derive one from another [6]),
• the data retrieval mechanism concerns (led to development of HQL, JPQL, Query DSL etc.),
• the partial object problem and nullability of object properties (not always all properties need to
be fetched),
• load time paradox (leading to SELECT problem),
• entity identity issues.
All problems are thoroughly described in [Neward06].
On top of this, the mandatory change detection mechanism implemented in some of the ORMs (in
particular in Hibernate) causes unexpected, implicit updates leading to hard to spot errors in some
cases.
The impedance mismatch implies serious consequences for the software developed with ORM:
• maintainability is affected,
• unavoidable performance problems,
• necessary optimizations leading to tight binding with underlying technology (Hibernate, SQL).
These problems are typical for classical architectural styles (such as layered), but they will affect
hexagonal architectures if ORM-based domain model "leaks" to the business domain.
How to avoid problems connected with impedance mismatch is described in Separate models.
There are also mitigation techniques that can be applied, and as a result the ORM-based domain
model can be still used, see: Using ORMs.
Performance
Performance challenges when implementing storage related adapters can be divided into few
categories:
1. Using single persistence technique for all use cases.
2. Using a common data model for all use cases.
3. Exposing an ORM-based data model to the business domain (implied from impedance
mismatch, see: Object Relational impedance mismatch).
Hexagonal architecture is actively promoting approaches with separating data models thus using
dedicated structures, optimized for certain use cases. This topic is explained in Model separation
but also in [Hombergs21].
Model separation
Separate models
Separating persistence and domain models is a technique that:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 209
• breaks technology dependencies and promotes zero-dependency architecture [7],
• increases maintainability by reducing the number of inter-module dependencies,
• solves most if not all of object-relational impedance mismatch related issues in case of ORM-
based adapter implementation.
There are, however, downsides of full model separation:
1. more code needs to be written and maintained for both models,
2. mapping code between two models needs to be written and maintained,
3. change detection mechanism of automated ORMs such as Hibernate is effectively useless in
some cases.
Code overhead related to points 1 and 2 can be mitigated with additional tools such as code
generators (e.g. Mapstruct). These tools however work best if both models are nearly identical, as
shown on Figure 48.
Save booking
Confirm booking Save booking port
JPA adapter
use case
@Entity
Booking
mapper BookingRecord
Figure 48. Example of full models separation
Change detection (point 3) is an important problem that becomes significant if data being persisted
is becoming complex. As full mapping de-facto detaches an entity from tracked record, the ORM
looses possibility to use its own synchronization code efficiently. Merging a record after being
mapped back from the entity (aggregate) very often results in extensive "delete all and insert again"
scenario rather than just a few updates and inserts depending on the actual delta. Therefore, it
should be considered a mistake to blindly use full model separation technique in every possible
case. Especially for such situations where record and entity are fairly identical, please consider
shared model approach described in Shared model.
So, when exactly should we use model separation?
Let’s consider following example shown on Figure 49 and compare it with Figure 48. Both are
basically depicting the same system. The only difference is that in case of Figure 49 two bounded
contexts have been identified. We have two distinct data models per context defined, but in this
case both are mapped into the very same JPA entity. In such a case, separate models approach is a
natural choice.
Our recommendation is to use separate models only if models after separation aren’t identical.

Separate models with change events
Separation with change events is a modification of full separation described in Separate models
where entity registers changes in a form of list of domain events. Change events allow efficiently
applying changes while still retaining full model separation. This implementation requires that all
modification use cases are identified and implemented as events (event is here a modification
command in command pattern).
It is important to clarify at this moment, that usually we do not declare separate

event per each property. What are the events and which properties are involved in
each of the events should be derived from a modification use case analysis.

Example 10. Booking example
Let’s consider Booking example as shown on Figure 50. The Booking (BookingRecord) has
following properties: description, creationDate, bookingDate, status and list of positions (not
visualized on the diagram).
The following use cases have been identified:
• CreateBooking that creates booking in status DRAFT. During creation creationDate and
bookingDate are set and cannot be modified.
• UpdateDescription allows to update textual description of the draft booking. Description can
be modified many times as long as Booking is DRAFT.
• AddPosition allows to add position to the booking as long it is in DRAFT status.
• RemovePosition allows to remove position from the booking as long it is in DRAFT status.
• Approve changes status of the booking to APPROVED.
In result we will have 5 types of business events relevant for change management of Booking
entity. UpdateDescription would take textual content of the description. AddPosition would most
likely take plenty of attributes describing such position. RemovePosition would require just that
many attributes that are necessary to identify a position (key value). Approve would not require
any attributes, in result we just know that status of the booking needs to be changed.
"Change events" based approach is applicable when:
1. Full model separation is required by any reason.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 212
2. Entity/aggregate is complex and efficient change detection is required.
3. Underlying storage technology is a relational database. This approach can be used with any
ORM solution.
It is noteworthy that this approach is relatively complex, because we basically recreate change
detection mechanism of the ORM. Therefore, this approach should be chosen with care. One reason
to apply this approach is when storage technology is based on event sourcing. Another reason
would be a complex data structure / aggregate that is not directly mappable onto relational tables.
Shared model
Sharing persistence and domain model is a technique that promotes reusing data structures used in
both adapter and domain implementation.
Save booking
Confirm booking Save booking port
JPA adapter
use case
@Entity
Booking
Figure 51. Example of a shared ORM-based model
The shared model approach can be used with any form of ORM. In this case, the entity model
mapped by an ORM can be used directly in the domain logic. The shared model approach has
several disadvantages:
1. It can only be used when the domain model is identical or very similar to the persistence model.
2. It does not protect us from ORM design flaws as implied by object relational impedance
mismatch.
One may conclude that by making the domain model always identical to the
persistence model (that is, to the relational database definition) it is possible to
 skip mapping and use the shared model everywhere. This is basically a trap,
because this way we lose an opportunity to tailor our domain models to the
bounded contexts and corresponding use cases. In result, we produce code that
soon becomes unmaintainable because of its complexity.
If the shared model is applicable, it has the following advantages:
1. No code redundancy.
2. No need of using mappers.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 213
The shared model approach is a natural choice when using non-object oriented persistence
protocol, for instance, plain JDBC. The adapter is responsible for mapping result sets into domain
objects, and there is no need to declare any intermediate set of structures.
Shared models can be effectively used with all kinds of no-SQL databases, especially document
oriented databases. Document databases usually allow storing even complex data models
(aggregates) in single collections. This eliminates the needs of costly change tracking because we
always store the whole aggregate. Naturally, any mappers from document databases are free of OR
impedance, by definition.
Hybrid approach
Shared and separate model approaches can be applied together in a single system. That is,
depending on the subdomain and even a domain entity, one or another approach may be
applicable.
Heuristics and recommendations
Which approach should be taken is a very complex decision. In order to provide aid on this topic,
let’s define a few terms.
Kind of data
What kind of data model are we dealing with:
• simple meaning that we have simple entities consisting of any number of simple attributes,
• complex meaning we have entities (aggregates) that contain nested structures, collections,
sets etc.
Kind of a system
Each system or even subsystem can be categorized as follows:
• shallow system basically has 1:1 correlation between what we see at user interface or REST
API and how data are organized in databases. This category is very common for CRUD
systems.
• deep system means that the structure of the user interfaces (forms, tables, etc) and API are
decoupled from how data are stored in the system. This category is common for DDD derived
systems with separated bounded contexts.
Given mentioned differentiation (which is anyway simplified), the choice of natural model
separation strategy is uneasy. Let’s look at Figure 52. Two most natural paths would be to pick
shared model strategy for simple data and separated for complex data.
But shallow system can be constructed for both simple and complex data and both cases are perfectly
okay. It is because CRUD systems can be also implemented for fairly complex entities and aggregates.
We can also imagine that system using only simple data can benefit from separated strategy,
because we could have multiple use cases, each of them can use a subset of attributes.
Implementing such a system as deep and using full model separation helps in reducing semantic
tangling and can increase system quality.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 214
Kind of data Simple Complex
partial
m odels CR
m
U D
pl
w
ex
i t
d
h ata
co
Kind of a system Shallow Deep

Model strategy Shared Separated
Figure 52. What model strategy should be chosen?
Finally, consider a dashed arrow between shallow system and separated model. It is noteworthy,
that most of the system in early development (and design) phase, especially these realized under
“agile” regime, will appear to be shallow and CRUD-like. Some of them (if not most) will evolve to
more complex as time progresses - either new requirements will show up or initial analysis wasn’t
accurate. In case of such high uncertainty, it may be beneficial to use separated model strategy even
if the system appears simple and shallow.
Note that there is no connection between deep system and shared strategy, as this transition is
excluded by definition.
Please use the presented diagram as a guide in your own decision process. You, as
 an architect, are the best person to judge what are the characteristics of your
system, how it would possibly evolve over time and how certain are your
assumptions.
Using ORMs
In the case of relational databases, it is important to determine what kind of ORM technique we
use. In principle, we have two categories:
• automated ORMs such as JPA implementation accompanied with lazy loading and change
detection,
• simple SQL mappers such as JOOQ, JDBI, R2DBC that usually cleanly separate load use cases
from update use cases and do not provide change detection [8].
As it has been already mentioned in Object Relational impedance mismatch there is a significant
mismatch between relational and object worlds. It is in fact big enough to imply certain flaws in
every automated ORM implementation. This chapter describes methods of avoiding or reducing
these flaws which is essential if shared model approach (see Shared model) is used.
For a typical automated ORM such as Hibernate, the following flaws implied by Object Relational
impedance mismatch can be identified:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 215
partial
For big entities, it can be a substantial overhead to transfer data for all properties where only a
few are needed.
load time
Using lazy loading for associations is usually recommended, therefore we can easily get
LazyLoadingException or SELECT performance problem.
hot entity
Managed entities that are left in persistence context and used as a shared model are still a
subject of change detection thus can be unintentionally modified and implicitly persisted.
Avoiding load time problems
Some of ORMs support mapping of associations (that is the "relations" between objects). Because
objects with associations can easily form a graph that will span across all tables in relational
databases, usually there is a possibility to select a loading time scenario for all associations. These
scenarios usually are:
eager loading
Association is instantiated almost immediately after a parent object is instantiated.
lazy loading
Association is instantiated lazily, i.e., when association is first used by the business code.
For performance reasons, a lazy loading scenario is preferred by default. The problem with lazy
loading has been already identified in Object Relational impedance mismatch and can be
characterized in the following way:
1. The moment when we access the association that is not yet initialized requires that database
connection is still active. If it is not active, a runtime exception occurs.
2. The ORM is unable to predict how the code is going to traverse graph of objects, quite often this
leads to SELECT problem.
Both scenarios are effectively turning ORM into something called "leaking
 abstraction" - that is, the coder cannot treat ORM as a real abstraction and ignore
underlying storage technology because it either results in runtime errors or
performance problems.
Projections
Projection is an auxiliary mechanism provided by some ORMs that allows to "project" a result of an
arbitrary SQL query directly into the data structure bypassing entity mapping. Projection may solve
most of the ORM related flaws of our application as long as a read model is concerned. That is,
projections can only be used for read use cases and implementing any data updating use case with
just projection is not possible
Constructor expressions (CE) is an example of projection mechanism provided by JPA. Constructor
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 216
expressions allow to avoid partial flaw at the cost of declaring dedicated data structures (classes)
per read use case. Such a structure can contain a partial entity (just some attributes of the full set)
or even any tuple (as a result of SQL JOIN).
Unfortunately, CE only some times can be used as shared model, because by definition CE cannot
contain associations that would be initialized by the query. Therefore, if a complex domain model
needs to be built from results of CE, it will be a de-facto full mapping scenario, because a bunch of
objects from CEs need to be mapped into a complex, deep domain model.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 217
Example 11. Partial object for bookings
Let’s consider a booking database table that is normally mapped into Booking entity using JPA.
In an use case a list of approved bookings for today with exact start time should be presented
to the user. Of course the Booking entity can be used to deliver this data, but here user is not
interested in description, creation date and probably most of other attributes: all user needs is
id of the booking and startTime. Even status is not needed, because user knows, that use case
produces only approved bookings.
domain persistence adapter database
<<table>>
Booking
@Entity
Booking attributes
id id
description description
status JPA status
date date
startAt startAt
... ...
JPQL/CE
ApprovedBookingsForToday
SELECT NEW ApprovedBookingsForToday (b.id, b.startAt)
attributes
FROM Booking b
id WHERE status='APPROVED' AND startDay=today()
startAt
...
Therefore, it is efficient to declare additional data structure called ApprovedBookingsForToday
which is a projection onto Booking entity. This data structure can be easily populated by
relevant data with constructor expression.
The ApprovedBookingsForToday is not an entity in JPA sense - it is not tracked for changes thus no
changes will ever be persisted, thus it is free from hot entity flaw. It is also free from other
flaws because it contains only the necessary attributes and contains no elements that are lazily
loaded. In fact the ApprovedBookingsForToday class can be implemented as immutable.
Using simple SQL mappers
Simple SQL mappers are ORMs that do not implement the following features:
1. change tracking [9],
2. lazy loading of any kind.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 218
Simple SQL mappers are free of lazy loading and hot entity flaws. Because, for simple SQL
mappers there is no restriction on how many data transfer objects are mapped onto single database
table, each DTO can be considered as a projection. With this consideration, we also assume that
simple SQL mappers are free of partial flaw.
A serious disadvantage of simple mappers when compared with fully automated ORMs is that
always read use cases require separate mapping than write (insert/update) use cases, which results
in coding overhead. This problem can be mitigated by active record pattern (see [Hayes18]), if only
given SQL mapper supports it.
With this in mind, simple SQL mappers can be recommended only if at least one condition is met:
1. Persistence ports use separate read and write models, i.e., when CQRS approach is used.
2. Implemented component, module or subdomain is not CRUD-like (models are different from
underlying relational table structures).
For further recommendations related to SQL mappers refer Notable Tools.
Transaction handling
Transactions are a mechanism to ensure consistency of the system. We distinguish business
transactions and system transactions. Business transactions are operations that must be
consistently performed from the business process point of view. System transactions are used to
realize consistent data modification against system resources such as databases, message queues,
and so on.
Each transaction, no matter whether it is a business or system one, has a boundary. All activities
performed within the boundary are therefore part of a given transaction and contribute to the
consistent modification of the system state.
System transaction boundaries
Within ports and adapters architecture, there are three possibilities on how the system transaction
boundary is defined.
If system transaction boundary is set on outbound adapter level (see Figure 53), it means that
operations performed by adapter are expected to be consistent. In this scenario, the system
transaction demarcation mechanism can be fully isolated from domain logic.
inbound outbound
adapter port domain port adapter
inbound outbound
system transaction boundary
Figure 53. Transaction boundary set on outbound adapter
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 219
If system transaction boundary is set on domain level, it can wrap the whole use case within single
transaction (see Figure 54). This, however, requires that all persistence adapters can contribute to
the same system transaction; thus they must be technologically consistent with respect to the
system transaction handling mechanism.
In this scenario, the transaction demarcation mechanism is exposed to the domain logic.
inbound outbound
adapter port domain port adapter
inbound outbound
system transaction boundary
Figure 54. Transaction boundary set on application core
The system transaction boundary can span over multiple use cases. In that case, it can be set on
inbound adapter level (that is, the web controller, the event listener or batch process). This scenario
is depicted on Figure 55. Under certain conditions, the domain logic can remain isolated from
transaction details, but inbound and outbound adapters become tangled with persistence technical
details.
inbound outbound
adapter port domain port adapter
inbound outbound
system transaction boundary
Figure 55. Transaction boundary set on inbound adapter
Business transaction boundaries
Boundaries of business and system transactions may be identical. This is the case when the system
utilizes only a single persistence mechanism, i.e., it connects a single relational database to
implement complete persistence. Therefore, all considerations from previous chapter remain
effective.
The boundary of a business transaction may be however more general than system transaction. It is
possible and desirable when:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 220
• system utilizes more than one persistence mechanism that cannot be unified with respect to
transaction handling,
• business transaction spans over multiple use cases that involves more than one inbound
communication (that is i.e. via multiple HTTP requests / API calls).
More general business transactions require specific handling methods that include:
• distributed transactions (i.e. realized with two phase commit mechanism),
• various locking mechanisms including optimistic locking.
Sometimes it is even practical to give up on transactional consistency and introduce eventual
consistency instead. All these mechanisms cannot be transparent to the domain logic.

Glossary
automated ORM
A tool that manages mapping between objects (in sense of object oriented programming
paradigm) and database relations (tables in sense of RDBMS) in a way, that state of persistent
object (hot entity) is automatically synchronized with the storage.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Building Block Patterns | 221
data access API
A software stack-specific interface for accessing databases that is also low level. An examples of
data access API are JDBC for Java and ADO.NET for .NET.
data transfer object
Also known as DTO is an object, in the OOP sense, that serves as a data carrier and do not react
on any events (does not implement any business relevant methods).
deep system
A software system in which the storage structure cannot be seen simply by looking at its
interfaces (UIs, APIs).
hot entity
Hot entity is a persistent object managed by automated ORM, that is, all changes to the state of
such object are implicitly synchronized with the store by the ORM tooling.
partial object
An object (in the OOP sense) that cannot be fully initialized at given moment (that is, some of its
property values will remain undefined or empty).
persistence adapter
An implementation of outbound port that interact with any kind of persistent storage.
simple SQL mapper
A tool that manages mapping between objects and database relations in a way, that state of
persistent object must be explicitly synchronized with database by developer (there are no hot
entities in simple SQL mappers).
shallow system
A software system in which storage structure is reflected by its interfaces (UIs, APIs).
