[1] Note that organizations like iSAQB more abstractly speak of building blocks. However, especially in this context we find
component better fitting. Components convey a more coarse-grained building block than classes and are therefore suitable for the
design of large systems. We stay with the term component throughout this document. But keep in mind, that this unit of
composition may be called by other terms in other organizations.
[2] Please note that declarative approaches for IaC can impose challenges for establishing a maintainable structure. Those
approaches often are missing clear interface constructs, which would be required to formalize dependencies between modules.
[3] Especially in the context of microservices, you might just have a simple REST endpoint, a bit of logic and some output on a
message queue or a data store.
[4] You can base the answer to this question by classifying your system as either being a deep or a shallow system. Deep system
means that you cannot see database structure from application interface (either UI or REST API or both). Shallow system means
you clearly see database structure from the interface. The hexagonal approach makes more sense the deeper your system is.
[5] Likewise, in case of only simple business logic the hexagonal approach would add too much unnecessary complexity and effort.
[6] Hibernate supports DDL auto-generation mechanism to derive DB schema out of the ORM configuration, but it has very small
use because it does not support data model migration cases. Therefore, additional mechanism such as Liquibase or Flyway is often
applied, which duplicates the definition again.
[7] Zero dependency architecture forbids any technology-related dependencies in hexagonal domain core.
[8] Active records of JOOQ can play similar role to change detection but still, as an explicit mechanism it is by definition very
different from automated ORMs.
[9] Some SQL mappers such as JOOQ or LINQ do, however, implement sort of active record pattern, which compensate lack of
change tracking in that cases.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Deployment patterns | 223
Data Architecture Patterns
This section addresses architecture patterns applicable to data-driven and artificial intelligence
based solutions. This is the core domain of our global business line Insights and Data (I&D).
Classical data-driven solutions use approaches such as
• data warehouse,
• data lake,
• data fabric,
• data product, and
• data mesh.
For details about data mesh see e.g.
• https://martinfowler.com/articles/data-monolith-to-mesh.html
• https://martinfowler.com/articles/data-mesh-principles.html
• https://medium.com/@neil.avery_99/on-the-alignment-of-data-mesh-and-the-hexagonal-
architecture-using-kafka-03716245eb35
Regarding artificial intelligence-based solutions, we divide them into two different types: those
using rule-based models and those using trained models. Rule-based models use a set of hard-coded
rules to make decisions. Trained models are trained using machine learning (ML) algorithms and
therefore are also called ML models. There are four primary machine learning algorithms
[ML_Models]:
• Supervised learning
• Unsupervised learning
• Semi-supervised learning
• Reinforcement learning
Deep learning (DL) is a further evolved subset of unsupervised learning. And a large language
model (LLM), the type of model used for generative AI, is a deep learning model trained on
enormous amounts of text data [AI_Models]. Choosing the right model for a given use case is the
expertise of a data scientist.
The word "model" in the context of artificial intelligence has a different meaning
than in the context of data modeling: A data model is a visual representation
showing the different types of data with their attributes and relations. An ML

model is software functionality that can be executed similar to an algorithm but
without the clear predictability and traceability of a classical algorithm. The
parameters of the ML model, called weights, are adapted to the model’s task using
training data.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Data Architecture Patterns | 224
Bibliography
▪ [ML_Models] Coursera: Machine Learning Models
▪ [AI_Models] The Ultimate Guide to Understanding and Using AI Models
▪ AI-Based Modeling: Techniques, Applications and Research Issues Towards Automation,
Intelligent and Smart Systems
Event sourcing
Overview
Event sourcing is an architectural pattern that fundamentally changes how we think about storing
and retrieving data. Instead of keeping the current state of the data in our domain, updating and
deleting it when necessary, we store the sequence of immutable events that have happened over
time.
Events
An event represents a change in the state of the system. In an event-sourced system, state
changes are stored as events in the order they occur. Each event is a fact that happened in the
past and cannot be updated or deleted. This immutability of events is a key principle in event
sourcing. Often, more detailed, internal events are distinguished from less granular, external
events used to communicate with other systems.
Strict immutability can be in contradiction to requirements imposed on us by the

need to evolve event schemas as well as handling Personally Identifiable
Information (PII). Please refer to the corresponding sections below.
DDD
Typically, event-sourced events are modeled after a business domain, often in the context of
Domain-Driven Design. However, this is not a necessary requirement for applying the pattern.
Event Stream
An event stream consists of a sequence of ordered events and usually corresponds to a single
aggregate or process.
Event Store
The event store is the storage mechanism where event streams are persisted. It offers read and
append operations on event streams and acts as the source of truth in an event-sourced system.
Event Schema
Event stores usually do not contain an explicit event schema. The event schema is therefore
implicit by default, and should be made explicit be applying a schema definition through
formats such as XML, JSON, Avro or DSLs. This can then be combined with explicit (de-
)serialization to make the schema explicit.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Bibliography | 225
Command Query Responsibility Segregation (CQRS)
CQRS is a pattern that segregates the operations that mutate data (Commands) from the ones
that read data (Queries). This separation provides a high level of architectural freedom and is a
key aspect of an event-sourced system.
Projection
Projections are the way to create read models in sourcing. A projection reads and/or listens to
the events in the event store and updates the read model accordingly. Read models can be based
on single or multiple event streams and can take different forms, such as different database
types or search indexes.
Snapshots
Snapshots are a technique used to optimize the reconstruction of the current state. Instead of
replaying all events from the beginning of time, you can take a snapshot of the current state and
then apply only the events that occurred after the snapshot. This technique is often applied
together with deleting events which are older than the snapshot to save storage, although this
combination is not necessary. Snapshots can be used as a standalone technique as well, using
them to support quicker state reconstruction while at the same time retaining older events.
While snapshots can aid in event storage requirements and event replaying speed,
erasing the previous history of events waives one of the major benefits of the

whole architectural pattern. Additionally, snapshots might become a subject of
Schema Evolution, causing additional effort. They should therefore only be used
after conducting a careful risk-benefit analysis.
Benefits
Event sourcing offers several compelling benefits:
Information Retention
Unlike in classical CRUD based system, event sourcing allows not only to keep the current state
of information, but the complete and traceable history of how this state was achieved. This
allows for easy handling of business use cases which might not have been present at the initial
design phase of the system. In contrast, when applying a traditional, non-event sourced
approach, it’s necessary to first extend the data model and start gathering new data.
Audit Logging
Event sourcing provides automatic audit logging. Every change to the system’s state is recorded
in the event log, providing a detailed history of actions.
Complete Reversibility
Any action, and therefore any mistake, can be reversed because of the complete history exists.
Debugging
By replaying the events, bugs can be reproduced and fixed more easily.
Temporal Query
The state of the system can be determined for any previous point in time.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Event sourcing | 226
Challenges
Despite its benefits, event sourcing is not without its challenges:
Schema Evolution
Over time, business logic changes, which leads to changes in the structure of the events. This
requires evolution of the event schemas, which can be non-trivial to implement.
Upcasting
This technique introduces upcaster functions, which events are passing through before being
passed to projectors. These upcasters modify the events to adhere to a new schema. When a
schema is changed multiple times, several upcasters have to be chained, which can have impact
on projection building- and therefore read-performance of the system.
Weak schema
One possibility is to describe events using a "weak" schema instead of a detailed one. This allows
for changes to the event structure, while forcing the projections to be able to deal with different
manifestations of such events.
Updating events
It is indeed possible to soften the concept of event immutability and simply update existing
events on schema changes. Of course, this affects some advantages of event sourcing described
earlier.
Copy and transform
This technique is similar to updating events, but instead of doing so in place, a new event stream
is created for each event stream requiring changes to adhere to a new schema. The schema
related changes are applied in this new event store, preserving the immutability of events.
PII & GDPR
The regulations imposed by GDPR conflict with the concept of immutability in event sourcing. PII
must not be stored forever and must be deleted on demand. There are several approaches to solve
this situation.
Data segregation
One common pattern is to store PII in a separate (event) store. On read of the main event, PII is
supplemented from the specialized storage. When PII must be deleted, it can be replaced by
default values.
Crypto-trashing
When applying crypto-trashing, PII is stored inside events in an encrypted fashion. Instead of
deleting the PII on request, the encryption key is destroyed, therefore making it impossible to
read the PII. IMPORTANT: The legal status of crypto-trashing is not clear in some countries.
There is concern whether this technique can be interpreted as fulfilling the requirements
imposed by the GDPRs Article 17. In addition, future attacks using quantum computing are a
possible threat.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Event sourcing | 227
Updating events
As discussed in the Schema Evolution section, it is indeed possible to soften the concept of event
immutability and simply update existing events on demand, deleting any information necessary.
Of course, this affects some advantages of event sourcing described earlier.
Tech Stacks
Event sourcing can be applied without the usage of specific technology, but there are several
popular stacks for implementing the pattern:
Axon Framework
Axon offers a Java-based framework that provides comprehensive building blocks for CQRS and
event sourcing as well as an event store implementation.
EventStoreDB
EventStoreDB is a database built specifically for event sourcing.
Marten
Marten is a .NET and PostgreSQL based database built specifically for event sourcing.
Wolkenkit
Wolkenkit is a Node.js based CQRS and event sourcing framework, supporting JavaScript and
TypeScript.
Applicability
Event-sourced systems are used in production across many industries and scales. When the aspects
described under the Benefits section are of high importance, event sourcing might be a good
pattern to employ.
To do so successfully, an in-depth understanding of the system’s domain is required, as otherwise
big and frequent schema changes might induce a lot of developmental effort.
Generative AI
Introduction
In the training Generative AI Fundamentals on NEXT, the following definition is given:
Definition: Generative AI
Generative AI is a type of machine learning that can learn the patterns and properties in a
dataset so deeply that it can generate creative new variants inspired by the original. The
content can be any type of data — text, images, audio, video, source code, JSON, SQL, 3D
objects, molecules, and many more.
It is important to know when to use generative AI and when not to use it. A "classic"
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 228
implementation will create deterministic results, it will be faster, it will consume less resources.
Generative AI should be used for use cases where the result is not deterministic, e.g., when the
result is creative or when the result is based on a lot of data which is not available in a structured
way.
An enhancement of Generative AI is Agentic AI. In this Capgemini Whitepaper, the following
definition for agents is given:
Definition: Agent
An agent is any entity that works on behalf of another entity, working to accomplish high-
level objectives often using specialist capabilities. Agents have a degree of autonomy and
authority to take actions that modify their world.
CCA point of view: The ability of modification is no strict criteria for acting as agent.
The term AI agent is recently used to describe software-based agents that leverage LLMs to
autonomously execute specific tasks in a more sophisticated - and so to speak, intelligent - manner.
In this article, the terms agent and AI agent are thus synonyms. Within an agentic AI system, several
specific agents are combined to solve more complex tasks in a collaborative way.
The promise of AI agents is:
• Workflow Automation: AI agents can assist users and clients by automating complex
workflows that involve reasoning and decision-making - capabilities that go beyond what
traditional chatbots or rule-based systems can offer.
• New and Enhanced Use Cases: By leveraging the power of LLMs, AI agents can enable and
improve use cases that were previously unattainable and achieving outcomes that surpass
human capabilities. For example, they can monitor quality aspects or security aspects, make
decisions, suggest actions, or even autonomously execute them.
• Multi-Agent Collaboration: These agents can be composed into systems where multiple
specialized agents interact and collaborate, achieving even more complex tasks.
This article covers both "standard" GenAI and agentic GenAI solutions, from an architecture view.
Context view
A robust, standard GenAI-enabled application architecture without agents typically consists of
several core components:
• the business application itself (frontend/backend), including a Generative AI (GenAI) adapter,
• a large language model (LLM),
• a retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) database (optional),
These components work together to deliver intelligent, context-aware responses to user inputs. For
standard GenAI use cases, the flow is based on a simple chat interaction. The user creates a chat
message as input, the GenAI adapter retrieves domain-/company-specific knowledge via RAG (if
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 229
needed) and then contacts the LLM to generate the answer. The answer is then sent back to the
user.
Non-agentic GenAI use case

When using agents, the architecture differs slightly. In addition to the above building blocks, the
following components are used:
• Model Context Protocol (MCP) servers to invoke tools in agentic use cases (see further below),
• MCP client(s) inside the application, to call the MCP server(s),
• Agent-to-Agent (A2A) server(s) to discover other agents outside of the own application (remote
agents)
• an A2A client inside the application to interact with other agents outside of the application via
the A2A protocol.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 230
Definition: MCP vs. A2A
Both MCP and A2A tempt to standardize the agentic ecosystem. For both protocols, servers
and clients are used in agentic environments. They are complementary to each other:
• MCP is used to standardize the interface from a single agent towards the tool the agent
can use.
• A2A is used to standardize the communication towards other remote agents to fulfill the
user task.
• A2A is not used within the same application. The communication between agents inside
the application is handled by the agentic frameworks.
A detailed comparison can be found at the A2A website.
A single AI agent typically interacts with users through a chat interface powered by a large
language model (LLM). It receives input related to its designated task and returns either
intermediate or final results. To complete its tasks, the agent interacts with the LLM and other
agents (inside or outside the application), often making multiple calls throughout different stages.
The overall workflow is orchestrated within or across multiple AI agents, forming a multi-agent AI
application. There exist a number of design patterns for combining agents to form a multi-agent
application, see LangGraph: Multi-Agent Workflows or the Capgemini training course Mastering
Agentic Design Patterns on that topic.
User interaction takes place through a natural language chat interface, enabling intuitive and
conversational engagement. In enterprise environments, the agent must access enterprise data and
interact with tools and APIs to perform relevant actions.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 231

Application including a Generative AI adapter
The business application (a type of application that we build usually in our projects) serves as the
entry point for user interactions and orchestrates the flow of data and commands. The application
itself can be a monolith or a distributed microservice landscape. In the context of this article, this
does not make a difference. The application communicates with the GenAI adapter.
The GenAI adapter is a conceptual building block within or outside the application. The tasks of the
GenAI adapter are to integrate with the rest of the GenAI application on the one side and to call the
LLM on the other side. General cross-cutting concepts such as monitoring and error handling as
well as Quality Assurance apply to this kind of adapter, but are not explicitly mentioned here.
LLM
The Large Language Model (LLM) is the component responsible for the generation of content via
generative AI. This component is not part of the application. The details of the ML algorithms the
LLM was trained by are not of relevance for the solution architect. As a solution architect, it is
however necessary to drive the decision for the to-be used model(s).
Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG)
Retrieval Augmented Generation (RAG) is essential when the LLM needs access to external or
proprietary knowledge. RAG is used when the original LLM knowledge is not enough to generate
adequate answers, e.g. if domain- or client-specific knowledge is needed.
It is important to know that the RAG content is not part of the LLM. An application with a GenAI
adapter first calls the RAG database with the user input via a vector search, and then adds this
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 232
result to the context of the original prompt before sending it to the LLM for refinement and answer
generation.
For details on RAG, see the section RAG integration below.
MCP components
To facilitate interaction with a wide range of third-party tools, the Model Context Protocol (MCP)
has been introduced. MCP provides a standardized mechanism for agents to communicate with
tools (acting as MCP servers) and perform actions. MCP is relevant for agentic AI use cases.
MCP is a protocol that works within a client-server architecture. Via MCP, information on tools can
be given in a standard way as context to an LLM, and the LLM can give answers including
suggestions for tool calls, which then can be actually executed by the agent. This enhances
portability and maintainability by eliminating the need for proprietary APIs when integrating new
tools or switching between vendors. MCP can also be used to connect with internal business
applications or to integrate and communicate with other agents (specific other agents as tools). You
can learn more about MCP here.
It is worth mentioning again that the LLM itself does not execute tools or system calls. This is the
responsibility of the MCP server in the application environment (or the application itself if no MCP
components exist).
A2A components
Agent-to-Agent (A2A) interaction becomes more dominant when building semi-autonomous
application landscapes. A2A is a protocol invented by Google to standardize the interaction
between agents within and outside the application. A2A works technically on the basis of JSON-RPC
and HTTPS. The agent-to-agent prompts are part of the JSON structure. A detailed documentation of
A2A can be found here
A2A works in a client-server architecture as well. The GenAI adapter acts as an A2A client. An A2A
server is taking the role of an agent discovery service that provides the client with connection and
authentication information for other agents. These agents can be pre-built by another solution, or
even another vendor. While these agents can technically run on the same machine as the A2A
client, the protocol used is always HTTPS, allowing remote communication as well.
Business application considerations
The application itself should provide a comparable user experience to what the users know from
public GenAI tools like ChatGPT. The user interface is either being provided by the business
application’s UI component, or the microfrontend of the GenAI adapter. Though UX design is not
part of this guide, just a few points to consider from a functional point of view:
• The user should be able to see the chat response streamed in (not getting a bunch of text at
once).
• Example prompts for the most important use cases help the users to start going.
• In agentic use cases, it should be visible that a tool was used for the reply - and which.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 233
• If a modifying action (create, update, delete) is to be executed by a tool, the user should be asked
for permission before the execution (Human-in-the-loop principle).
• For RAG answers, a reference to the document helps to verify the correctness of the answer.
• Depending on the use case, a feedback mechanism on the answer may be desired - either for the
model directly, or as a request towards an operations team (e.g. via email).
GenAI adapter considerations
Programming language and frameworks
Usually, LLM-facing components like the GenAI adapter are written in Python due to the maturity
of this programming language for any AI/ML/GenAI tasks (Java frameworks may miss some
important features). The programming language decision leads to the decision of decoupling
between business application and GenAI adapter.
• A decoupling into a dedicated microservice is recommended when the programming languages
from business application and GenAI adapter differ (e.g. using Java for the business application
and Python for the GenAI adapter). This is especially the case in brownfield projects and
existing solutions that need GenAI enhancement after the initial implementation.
• On the other hand, if the application itself is already written in Python, an encapsulation within
the application via packages and modularization may be enough to maintain simplicity.
Langchain is the most common framework to integrate LLMs and RAG. It is recommended to
adhere to existing frameworks and standards instead of trying to reinvent the wheel and trying to
build an own solution.
The decision not to use Python for the GenAI adapter should be evaluated
carefully. Thy Python ecosystem (and Langchain in particular) has very high
 maturity for GenAI use cases. Even the Java SDKs of Langchain and other
frameworks are missing important features that are present in the Python
frameworks already, e.g. integration to some LLMs, debugging, agent types, state
management and others.
For agentic AI frameworks, see here.
Interface design
The interface from application towards an LLM, a RAG database or an MCP server is encapsulated
by frameworks and does not need to be defined from scratch. They are therefore not further
considered here in detail. As an architect, it is usually not necessary to integrate the low-level APIs
of LLMs.
If the GenAI adapter is encapsulated in a dedicated microservice, the interface between GenAI
adapter and the rest of the GenAI application needs special attention. The usage of an
asynchronous interface is recommended to enable the streaming capability for the user. This leads
to the usage of protocols like gRPC (with protocol buffers) or Python frameworks like FastAPI.
gRPC has a better performance and type safety than usual REST interfaces.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 234
General cross-cutting concepts and microservice principles apply for the interface design too.
Usage Control, Quotas, Rate Limiting, FinOps and Cost Management
Usage control oversees the frequency of interactions with a specific model, preventing excessive
usage beyond predefined limits. This is particularly relevant for resource-intensive models such as
image generation models or large language models.
The limits need to be implemented or configured on the application side and are therefore the
responsibility of the application design. These limits can be externalized for configuration or be
handled as hardcoded values. For details see this guide
Choosing the correct model is one way to control the costs. See Model selection for more details. It is
important to revisit on this topic frequently as the LLM models and their cost structure evolve.
Cost management on the other side sets hard limits to the resulting costs.
System Prompt Design and Guardrails
While prompt engineering itself gets less and less important as the models evolve, the system
prompt is still a crucial factor for the application’s behavior. The system prompt is the prompt that
is applied to all user prompts, no matter the input. The system prompt is implemented in the GenAI
adapter.
The system prompt should follow a structured text, describing
• the role of the AI or agent,
• its capabilities (e.g. tools),
• the tone of communication,
• the way of approaching a query (step-by-step),
• example queries and answer formats.
It is best to use a system prompt generator (that itself is based on GenAI) to create a good system
prompt. You can use online resources and GenAI tools like Microsoft Copilot to generate a good
system prompt. The general rules for prompt engineering also apply (general tips can be found
here).
Depending on the use case, the system prompt configuration can also be externalized to the users
(e.g. functional admins). This needs to be done via the custom implementation in the application.
But even in that case, a generic, non-editable system prompt should be added in addition to ensure
the usage of guardrails and basic behavior.
Guardrails constitute a set of safety controls designed to monitor and guide a user’s interaction
with a generative model. These programmable, rule-based systems act as intermediaries between
users and generative models, ensuring that the GenAI application operates within defined
organizational principles. The primary goal of guardrails is to enforce the input to and the output of
a generative model to adhere to specific formats or contexts, e.g. the syntactic correctness of a JSON
response.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 235
Guardrails and content safety help to implement the guidelines from the Capgemini guidelines for
responsible AI. They are implemented using two different approaches: On the one side, the prompt
contains instructions to the generative model to follow certain guardrails. On the other side, the
GenAI Adapter checks the output of the generative model if it also follows the guardrails.
RAG integration
Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) is an architecture that enhances GenAI models by
combining them with external knowledge sources.
RAG is technology-agnostic. While one can implement a static list of content (e.g. a dictionary)
within the application directly to use for retrieval, in our contexts we usually need more and bigger
data. This is why the basis of RAG is usually a vector database, containing documents and other
artifacts.
A typical approach in the case of unstructured information is semantic search and vector
embeddings. This approach consists of two parts: ingestion and retrieval.
The ingestion part reads the information, stored in documents or other artifacts, and chunks it into
smaller parts. It then has these chunks translated into vectors representing the semantics of the
chunk, typically by using an LLM. These vectors are called embeddings, and the LLM used to
calculate them is called embedding model. The embedding model can be different to the model that
is used to create answers of user queries later. Then, the ingestion part saves the chunks together
with their vector embeddings and some metadata into a vector database. Alternatively, other types
of databases could be used such as graph databases or classical SQL databases.
Ingestion is usually not part of the development of GenAI-applications itself, but is
handled more centrally, likely by a different team. RAG ingestion is a process that
occurs either one-time or frequently, depending on the content. It is time-wise

decoupled from the retrieval, which is a real-time query. RAG ingestion provides
data that can be relevant for multiple use cases and applications. The
responsibility for RAG ingestion is therefore usually not at the feature team that
implements the GenAI application.
The retrieval part, for each prompt, calculates an embedding vector using the same embedding
model as the ingestion part. Then it searches the vector database for chunks close to this
embedding vector using a distance metric, e.g., cosine similarity or dot product. It adds these
selected chunks to the prompt as additional knowledge for the generative model. The retrieval logic
is part of the GenAI adapter.
The aspects to design when designing and integrating a RAG database are
• Chunking Strategy: Use overlapping windows to preserve context across chunks.
• Metadata Tagging: Store source, timestamp, and topic to improve filtering and traceability.
• Hybrid Search: Combine keyword and vector search for better precision.
• Caching: Cache frequent queries to reduce latency and cost.
• Feedback Loop: Use user feedback to refine retrieval and update the vector store.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 236
One guide for RAG best practices and database proposals can be found here.
Memory handling
Memory concepts in GenAI applications help the application learn from the user’s behavior.
• During the conversation, the GenAI adapter can store context in short-term memory of the LLM
or agent.
• For continuity across sessions, such as maintaining a history of previous results or
personalizing the experience, long-term memory is used.
Short-term memory is implemented by attaching the message history to each new message that is
sent towards the LLM. It is the responsibility of the application to ensure that these messages get
appended. Some frameworks support in that process by configuration parameters. Appending is a
simple string concatenation (where technically each string represents a JSON object of the previous
conversation) to the prompt text.
Long-term memory relies on vector databases, similar to RAG. NoSQL databases can also be used,
though this results in more custom handling to retrieve the memory for the next conversation.
Special topics for agentic use cases
Frameworks
Several frameworks support the creation and programming of AI agents, including:
• LangGraph - an extension of LangChain programming framework for multi-agent systems
(multi-step workflows)
• OpenAI’s Swarm - a multi-agent orchestration framework
• n8n - a powerful workflow automation platform that supports AI agents (based on LangChain),
fair-code licensing
• CrewAI - an open source Python based AI agent framework (independent of LangChain), crewAI
also offers a visual agent builder tool
• Microsoft’s AutoGen & Magentic AI - open-source framework (AutoGen) and a workflow
automation tool (Magentic AI) on top of the framework
• Google’s ADK - framework for developing and deploying AI agents, optimized for the Gemini
LLM
• IBM’s watsonx.ai & watsonx Orchestrate - a development environment for agents and an
orchestration tool which allows to work on agents with a no-code approach
• AutoGPT - started early as an agent to solve general tasks on top of GPT, but now evolves to a
platform for developing AI agents supporting a number of built-in LLMs
Currently, there is no clear market leader. These frameworks are not compatible with each other.
Langgraph is part of the Langchain ecosystem and can be integrated into existing "standard" GenAI
use cases.
The application design differs a lot between these frameworks. It is therefore important to choose
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 237
the correct framework based on
• its capabilities (e.g. supported LLMs, tools),
• the support model,
• the license model,
• the existing landscape,
• the knowledge within the team.
Agent scope
Similar to microservices, the agents and their scope need to be defined in a multi-agent
environment. The design of the agent ecosystem is done declarative within the frameworks by
• defining roles and system prompts for each of the agents,
• linking tools, LLMs and memory to each of the agents,
• defining the workflow
Some agentic frameworks like CrewAI support sequential as well as hierarchical workflow
executions.
• The former leads in a modelled, streamlined process with only one direction. If the process
itself is known, the sequential flow is the simpler way to achieve the goal.
• The latter introduces a manager agent that orchestrates the work of other agents. If the
requirements from the user are unclear, or the use case is unstructured, a manager agent
makes sense to break down the work and distribute it to the correct agents.
To simplify the development of personal assistants and intelligent agents, specialized tools provide:
• Predefined AI agents as reusable components (libraries)
• Graphical workflow modeling languages for orchestration
The more complex and generic an agent is modelled, the higher the risk of hallucination or
unexpected results. It is recommended to slice the agents as small as possible, with clear context
and roles for their execution. Consider the same design principles as in Functional Decomposition.
MCP
When using MCP, the GenAI adapter acts as the host for one or multiple MCP clients. The host
orchestrates the process flow between user input, tool execution and LLM in its application logic.
The MCP client is responsible to connect to the MCP server(s) that have access to the tools.
The MCP server is responsible for declaring the tools and resources, their arguments, and their
execution logic. You can build your own MCP server or deploy a predefined one (an example list
can be found here).
From a topology perspective:
• One MCP client instance always connects to exactly one MCP server.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 238
• An application (MCP host) can host multiple MCP clients, each connecting to one MCP server.
• One MCP server can fulfill the needs of multiple MCP clients.
• There can be multiple MCP servers within an application (each of them holding one or multiple
tool/resource definitions). It is not necessary to bundle all tool and resource definitions in only
one server. While this may be confusing at first, it makes sense in terms of the reusability of
existing MCP servers, scaling capabilities and fault tolerance.
A2A
When interacting with remote agents, the A2A protocol should be used. The GenAI adapter then
acts as the A2A client, communicating with the A2A server to retrieve information about other
remote agents. This includes their URI and authentication schemes.
There is one A2A client inside the own application, individual own agents can all share the same
client. Though depending on the agent flow, it can make sense to introduce one A2A client per
remote agent called. There is no one-size-fits-all approach. It is generally recommended to use the
simplest approach here to avoid complexity in the network communication.
A tutorial on how to build and integrate an A2A client can be found here. The main points to
consider when building a flow with A2A are:
• Definition and alignment on the task state model of the requested agent, e.g. its possibility to
send updates on a request asynchronously via SSE or push notification,
• Implementation of retries and circuit breakers to avoid deadlocks when interacting with remote
agents,
• Integration of the own application with the given identity provider to authenticate against other
remote agents, e.g. via OpenID Connect.
As of July 2025, not all popular agentic frameworks support A2A natively yet.

When remote agents are used, the architect possibly needs to take care of the A2A
client definition (see above) when designing the GenAI adapter.
Retries and limits
Agents act semi-autonomous. It is not possible to control the flow of an agent task execution. This
can result in many interactions between the agents and their respective prompts. The tokens that
are needed for these interactions add up fast and incur costs. The more agents exist, the more
tokens are used. A manager agent (hierarchical workflow) incurs more tokens due to the necessary
coordination. Clear retry limits (as defined in the respective frameworks) need to be applied to
abort the execution after a given amount of failed executions. A limit of 5 executions is usually
enough. This also increases the fault tolerance of the execution itself by introducing an error
condition.
Limitations of AI Agents
AI agents inherit the core risks of large language models (LLMs), including concerns around
security, safety, reliability, scalability, and efficiency.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 239
These risks become even more significant when an AI agent moves beyond being
read-only or generative and gains the ability to actively create, update, or delete

business data through connected tools. Then quality assurance becomes even
more important. In many cases, this can mean that you need a human expert in
the loop who can check and decide on intermediate results of an agent.
In addition, AI agents introduce new challenges:
• Limited Portability: Switching to a different LLM often requires reworking internal prompts
and workflows.
• Immaturity: Agentic systems are still in early development stages, with evolving standards and
tooling.
• High Maintenance costs: Frequent updates to models and tools demand ongoing testing and
adaptation.
The field is rapidly advancing, and significant changes to the agent ecosystem are expected as
research and development continue.
LLM considerations
Model selection
As the landscape is steadily evolving, we do not provide recommendations here. Use resources like
this model comparison to get an up-to-date overview over the current models and their capabilities.
Different aspects affect the decision for the correct model(s) for a use case:
• The capability of the model itself (amount of parameters, multi-modality, reasoning, tool
calling…)
• The hosting model (cloud or on-premise) and the security protection requirements
• The balance between value and cost (models with higher capabilities usually cost more per
token)
• The ability to partner with a LLM provider to access expert knowledge and potential cost
benefits
The standard approach is to use a pure LLM-text model, with capabilities for tool calling. The use
case requirements need to be checked if additional and specific features like image generation are
needed, as this also affects the model selection.
Temperature
The temperature of a model indicates its creativity and predictability. The value is a float scale
between 0 and 1.
The value that is adequate is always dependent on the use case:
• For use cases where knowledge retrieval, a somehow deterministic and specific output is
needed, a low value between 0.0 and 0.2 is preferred. This is the case for most business
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Generative AI | 240
applications.
• For highly diverse, imaginative and create results to generate content, a value of 0.8 or 0.9 is
recommended.
• A mixture of these two extremes leads to a temperature in the middle of the scale, e.g. 0.5.
The temperature can be configured in the frameworks as a parameter. Depending on the user
requirements, this configuration can also be externalized (this needs to be implemented
individually).
Testing
See Engineering Practices from Martin Fowler. Depending on the possible threats and the resulting
testing goals, testing for bias in the result generated can be one part of the testing strategy.

Integration Architecture Patterns
This chapter aims to provide an overview of various integration architecture patterns. These
patterns are fundamental to understanding how different systems can communicate and interact
with each other in a seamless and efficient manner. We will explore the principles, advantages, and
potential drawbacks of each pattern. Understanding these patterns and their appropriate use can
greatly enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of system integration.
Parts of this chapter are based on the book Enterprise Integration Patterns by Gregor Hohpe and
Bobby Woolf (2003), which provides a comprehensive overview of integration patterns and their
application in real-world scenarios. The book is now more than 20 years old but explains many
fundamental concepts that are still valid and worth knowing, although some examples given in the
book may appear outdated. The introduction and chapter 2 "Integration Styles" can be found in the
Sample Pages of that book. A complete list of patterns discussed in the book can also be found
online.
There is also an L&D training "Integration Foundations - Integration School Unit #1" and a
corresponding Integration Foundations learning path.
Capgemini’s offer in the field of integration is called Business APIs & Apps Integration.
This chapter of the SeaGuide is organized as follows: The section on Integration Styles introduces
the most basic patterns and architectural styles for integration. Fundamental Challenges and
Patterns dives deeper into some integration challenges and their solution that are more-or-less
independent of the specific integration style or technology. The section on API Management
introduces the concept of an API, i.e. the "interface of a software system" at the granularity of
systems integration. Finally, some Integration Technologies are presented to give some do’s and
don’ts for practical integration projects.
Integration Styles
Integration styles are the most basic patterns used in integration and define the basic
characteristics that an architect has to deal with when designing an integration solution. They are
not 100% orthogonal and share some common aspects like the need for addressing the
communication partner.
Messaging
Messaging Integration is an integration style where systems communicate via message passing, i.e.
the operation of sending and receiving a single message is the key concept of all architectural
design.
Other integration styles like Remote Procedure Invocation are technically built on top of messaging
mechanisms. However, they restrict these mechanisms by allowing only specific message exchange
patterns and/or topologies or by implementing higher logic by defining specific messages. Using
these restrictions, they create their own key concepts, e.g. the Request/Response message cycle of
Remote Procedure Invocation. By doing so, they mask some of the intricacies of messaging.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Styles | 243
If you want to use the entire spectrum of possibilities in integration or want to understand the
limitations and implications "under the hood" of other integration styles, you have to understand
Message Integration and all the patterns that help to tame its complexity.
Messaging architectures can be categorized according to the following criteria:
• Synchronicity
◦ synchronous: Sender and receiver are required to be active/available at the same time.
Synchronous messaging has the advantage that you do not need a complex messaging
infrastructure. Instead, a simple messaging library within each participating node will
suffice as long as all questions of addressing are dealt with elsewehere (e.g. in a static
configuration or by using an address resolution service). Also, synchronous messaging has
the potential of very high throughput. The disadvantage is obviously that a sender may
block or all messages will be lost if the receiver is not available. Also the problem of
congestion must be considered.
◦ asynchronous: The sender and receiver are not required to be available/active at the same
time. Instead, the messages are sent via a messaging middleware that persists the messages
until the receiver becomes available. Addressing and congestion control are also handled by
the middleware as well as support for various topologies.
• Conversation pattern
◦ Fire-and-Forget: The sender sends a message and does not expect a response. This is the
most basic and "natural" conversation pattern.
◦ Request-Response: The sender sends a message and expects a response. Request and
response are message categories, and each response message is semantically linked to one
request message and vice versa. In contrast to the Remote Procedure Invocation integration
style, the messages are still "visible" in terms of the integration design. The architect must
still think of what can/should happen when the request is already sent but before the
response is received — or a timeout occurs.
◦ other conversation patterns are possible like polling, or a ping-pong-like conversation
with a "right to send the next message" alternating between the participants, or a message-
acknowledge pattern where each message with a business logic meaning is answered with a
technical acknowledge message that is used only to avoid message loss. See the section in
the EiP book for more.
• Message intent
◦ Command: The message is a command to perform an action.
◦ Event: The message is an event that has occurred.
◦ Document: The message is a document that contains data. Note that the Document message
intent is not a command, although the sender may be said to "command the receiver to
learn" about the information contained in the document: The receiver will use this
information in some way that is usually unknown to the sender. The Document message
intent is also not an Event, although the document creation and sending may be triggered by
a business event: It is quite common that Document messages are sent on timer events or on
demand.
• Topology
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Styles | 244
◦ Point-to-point: a message is sent from one sender to one receiver.
◦ Publish-subscribe: a message is sent from one sender to multiple receivers.
◦ Message bus: a message is sent from multiple senders to multiple receivers.
Topologies
In this section, we will compare three common messaging topologies: Point-to-point, publish-
subscribe, and message bus. These topologies denote the logical relationship between senders and
receivers and influence the degree of coupling between them.
Point-to-Point
Point-to-point messaging involves a direct (logical) communication link between a single sender
and a single receiver — the sender "knows" the receiver. This knowledge may have to be quite
specific if the sender has to provide e.g. a DNS name in order to address the receiver. Or the
knowledge can be more abstract if the sender uses some kind of "role" to address the receiver and
an address resolution service transparently maps the role to a network address.
In any case, the sender can be sure that there is one specific receiver that will receive and process
the message. This results in a (logically) tighter coupling between sender and receiver compared to
other topologies: If the support of a business process as implemented in the sender system requires
the message to be sent to the receiver, then the sender cannot fully function without the
receiver — both systems mutually need each other. If the business process support in the sender
doesn’t require the message, i.e. the message is necessary for the business processes supported by
the receiver only, then the sender contains sending functionality that is not required by the
sender’s business logic, unnecessarily coupling the sender to the receiver.
Publish-Subscribe
In the publish-subscribe topology, a single message from a publisher can be received by multiple
subscribers. The publisher sends messages to a "topic", i.e., an abstract address of a group of
receivers, and all subscribers to that topic receive the message. This implies that the sender always
addresses an abstract group of receivers and has no knowledge who the individual receivers are.
The sender may not even be sure that there are any receivers at all!
Some messaging middleware products allow for receivers to receive messages that were sent
before the receiver subscribed to the topic. This requires the middleware to persist the messages
and deliver them when a new receiver subscribes to a topic. The same applies if a receiver detaches
itself temporarily from the middleware and re-subscribes when it comes up again. To avoid
message duplication, this requires the use of an identification scheme for the participating nodes,
so that the middleware can recognize a receiver that already has consumed some messages in the
past. Also, messages usually have to be deleted from the persistent storage after some time in order
to limit storage capacity needs. This limits the applicability of this feature and has to be taken into
account when designing the integration architecture.
Some variations to this topology are:
• Hierarchical Topics: The name space of topics is hierarchically structured, so that groups of
receivers can be subdivided into subgroups. A subscriber to the topic "businessevents" will also
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Styles | 245
receive messages that are sent to the topic "businessevents.sales", while a subscriber to
"businessevents.production" will not.
• Persistent Receiver: The middleware guarantees the delivery of messages that were sent
during an intermediate downtime of a receiver. However, a receiver never receives a message
that was sent before it subscribed for the first time. This allows for immediate message deletion
when all known receivers have received their messages.
Message Bus
The term Message Bus is not sharply defined but sometimes used for a messaging topology where
each participant may send a message at any point in time, and each other node will receive the
message if that node is online. This means that there is explicitly no guarantee with respect to
delivery. Usually, there is also no guarantee with respect to the sequence of messages as they are
delivered.
Note that the term Message Bus is also used to describe a distributed architecture for messaging
middleware software products where there are replicated software components that implement
the messaging system in a highly scalable way supporting high availability.
Event-Driven Architecture
This section is a summary of two articles by RedHat and Hazelcast.
Event-Driven Architecture (EDA) is a software architecture paradigm that promotes the production,
detection, consumption of, and reaction to events. It is closely related to the Integration Style of
Messaging with asynchronous fire-and-forget messages with an event intent in a publish-subscribe
topology.
An event can be defined as a significant change in state. For example, when a consumer purchases
a car, that’s an event in the system.
In EDA, events drive the flow of the application and impact the behavior of the software. This is in
contrast to traditional request-driven architectures. In an event-driven system, a producer sends an
event without knowing what service or services will receive the event.
This is a key principle of EDA, the production of an event is separated from the consumption of that
event. A car dealer’s system, for example, will simply publish an event that a car has been sold. Any
downstream systems, such as the department of motor vehicles or an insurance company, can
subscribe to these events and react accordingly.
EDA allows for loose coupling of services which can simplify development and allow for flexibility.
It’s particularly useful in real-time analytics and for applications that need to react to changes in
real-time.
However, EDA also comes with its own set of challenges. Ensuring that all events are processed in
the correct order, dealing with "poison" messages that can’t be processed correctly, and managing
transactions that span multiple services can be complex in an EDA.
For details see the macro architecture pattern Event-Driven Architecture.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Styles | 246
File Transfer
File Transfer was widely used in the past because file systems and scripting of file manipulation
and FTP was readily available in any operating system. Also, storing messages as files in directories
allows for manual inspection and — if necessary — manipulation of the integration flow.
Today this integration style is still sometimes used for EDI (Electronic Data Interchange) where data
is exchanged between systems belonging to different organizations or companies.
File Transfer has some characteristics that limit its use. This is why it is not recommended for a
newly built integration.
• Lack of atomicity and transactionality: Writing and reading of files is not an atomic
operation. You have to add renaming and/or moving operations to your file access procedure to
ensure that files are not processed before they have been fully written. File access is also not
transactional. If you need exactly-once-semantics, you have to do additional housekeeping in a
database which opens the risk of inconsistencies between the file system’s content and the
metadata.
• Lack of control: The ability to manipulate files, e.g., deleting unwanted messages or moving
archived messages back to some inbox directory, creates the risk of misuse. While you may be
able to log what the File Transfer infrastructure does, you cannot constrain or log what the
operator does to the files.
• Lack of metadata support: Any metadata that describes the file or its messages contained
therein has to be encoded into the file’s content or into its filename. This is cumbersome and
adds extra complexity to the access procedures.
• Throughput limitations for small messages: File systems and transfer protocols like FTP tend
to perform poorly if used with a large number of small files. Therefore, File Transfer is most
often used with bulk files that contain a large number of semantically independent messages
each. This adds extra complexity to the error handling because you have to keep track of
unprocessed parts of a file and handle these snippets in a special way, e.g., copy them to a new
file of unprocessed messages.
• Latency limitations: Usually, the arrival of a new file can only be detected by polling the file
system. This adds extra latency to the process and/or extra load to the file system if the polling is
done too frequently.
Sometimes, integrations are built on some messaging platform that offers high throughput for large
messages. In this case, the architect may decide to accumulate semantically independent logical
messages into one technical bulk message. This approach will have to deal with the same problems
in error handling as a traditional File Transfer integration. Some learnings from experience with
File Transfer may be valuable.
Remote Procedure Invocation (RPI)
RPI allows one system (the client) to execute a procedure in another system (the server).
Usually, RPI middleware and/or RPI libraries enforce a completely synchronous transfer of control
from the client to the server and back, i.e., the client is blocked until the underlying response
message is received from the server. This mimics the programming paradigm of a local procedure
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Styles | 247
call and is well suited if the client has to wait for the completion of some business logic in the server
anyway. This could be the retrieval of some crucial information or the completion of a transaction
on the server’s state that is necessary for the client’s progression in its own business process.
In order to successfully design an integration solution with RPI, the architect must understand the
drawbacks and limitations:
• Performance penalty: A call to a remote procedure is slower than a local call by several
orders of magnitude. See Latency Trends and Numbers Every Computer Engineer Should Know
for details. Hiding the remote call behind an abstraction that unifies local and remote calls,
making RPI transparent to the developer is a guarantee for disaster. RPI calls must be limited to
the unavoidable minimum in order to meet non-trivial performance requirements.
• Coupled availability: Since the client cannot operate without the server, the availability of the
client’s functionality is decreased by the limited availability of the server.
• Uncertainty in case of a time-out: Unlike in local calls, the client’s calling thread may return
with a time-out error. The error handling within the client must deal with all the following
situations without knowing which one is actually true:
◦ The call may never have reached the server, e.g., because the sever is not available.
◦ The call on the server side may have been aborted without an error message due to a
catastrophic failure.
◦ The call may have come to a successful end, but the response message got lost.
◦ The call is still running and will end in the future — successfully, with an error message or
with a catastrophic failure.
Especially the last point — the correct handling of time-outs — makes the use of RPI much more
complicated than local calls. A remotely invoked procedure must not be (blindly) retried if it is not
idempotent. Even if there is a retry mechanism, it is possible to have repeated time-outs. At some
point, the client must give up in a graceful way — still not knowing whether the call, maybe, has
been executed successfully.
In rarely seen asynchronous RPI frameworks, the calling thread is not blocked but immediately
returns a future or promise object. This opens the possibility to proceed with useful work on the
client side while the server executes its task asynchronously. The feature is especially important to
improve over-all performance if there are only a few threads of activity on the client side.
However, it requires that there is useful work to be done after calling the RPI procedure.
Shared Database
Databases can be used by more than one client system to read and write data. These operations are
transactional and persistent — which comes in handy when handling messages. Therefore, they
have been widely used for integration purposes in the past.
There is a spectrum of ways how to share information using a shared database:
• Shared data model: A straight forward but naive way of integration is to share operational
database tables. Each participating system reads and perhaps even writes from/to these tables
at will. This leads to an extremely close coupling because the operational tables are structured
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Styles | 248
along the lines of the technical data model — exposing it to the outside world and undermining
data ownership.
• Interface views: The system that "owns" the data provides read access to selected parts of the
system’s data by creating one or more views on the database that other systems can read from.
This database view takes the role of an interface including an interface contract with respect to
the data model of the view and timeliness of the data. The contract includes obligations to the
clients in terms of maximum database load induced by their read operations. This kind of
integration offers the advantage of a generic "self-service" data supply for the reading client
systems. Interface views must be crafted carefully in order to keep the separation of concerns:
The view’s data model must be designed strictly along the lines of the business data model and
should include only those fields that are actually needed. This will prevent client systems from
building up unnecessary dependencies. The main technical disadvantages are:
◦ Access to the database is possible only by using a database access protocol that is usually not
allowed across network segment boundaries. To overcome this limitation, an additional
(generic) GraphQL-layer can be built on top of the view.
◦ The performance risk for the "owner" system imposed by the read operations to the view.
Since the operational data and the view is hosted by the same RDBMS, there is a close
performance coupling.
◦ The integration will break or at least create high maintenance costs when the system that
"owns" the data changes its database technology. If the new technology, e.g., a key-value-
store, does not offer views, a table in an additional relational "interface database" must be
artificially filled by the data owning system in order to fulfil the interface contract.
• Message tables: Similar to a file system in File Transfer, a readily available database can be
used as messaging middleware. In this case, a database table is created that can hold messages
and their metadata, imitating the functionality that is offered by proper messaging middleware
products. Senders write new rows into the table, receivers read and delete these rows. While
this kind of integration can be found in some legacy systems, it should be avoided for new
integrations. Instead, use a proper middleware product that comes with cross cutting support
functionality for IT operations.
Given the disadvantages and risks discussed in this section, the use of the Shared Database
integration style — in the Interface view flavour — is recommended only in specific scenarios where
flexibility and performance of client read access is key and the risks can be mitigated.
Fundamental Challenges and Patterns
In the realm of integration architecture, there are several fundamental challenges and patterns that
we often encounter. These challenges can range from handling out-of-sequence messages and
ensuring idempotency to managing compensating transactions and dealing with dead letter
channels. This section aims to provide an overview of these fundamental challenges and patterns.
Understanding these challenges and patterns is crucial for designing robust and reliable integration
architectures.
Idempotency
This section is inspired by a blog post by Jonathan Oliver.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Fundamental Challenges and Patterns | 249
Idempotency refers to the property of certain operations in mathematics and computer science,
where these operations can be applied multiple times without changing the result beyond the
initial application.
Idempotency can be achieved in various ways in software systems. One common approach is to
assign a unique identifier to each request. When the server receives a request, it first checks if it
has already processed a request with the same identifier. If it has, it simply returns the result of the
previous operation instead of performing a new one. This requires to persistently store request
identifiers and results at least for a certain retention time.
Another approach is to design operations to be inherently idempotent. For example, setting a
variable to a certain value or deleting an item from a database can be done multiple times without
changing the outcome.
In systems that follow the rules of an <a
href="./integration_styles.pdf#<em>event_driven_architecture">Event-driven Architecture
(EDA)</a> idempotent interactions are key: Sending and receiving an event multiple times must not
change the behavior of the over-all system. In general, this requires that the receiver can
_identify</em> the event, so that the receiver can distinguish between receiving a single event
multiple times from receiving multiple, but similar events. Identification can be supported by a
technical event ID that the receiver uses to deduplicate identical event
messages&#8201;&#8212;&#8201;which is equivalent to the identification of duplicate requests
mentioned above. As an alternative, the EDA-system can be built based on an event broker
implementation such as Apache Kafka that offers the <em>idempotent writes</em> feature. In case
the producer or event broker fails during the write, this ensures that a duplicate of that event is not
created upon retry, eliminating the need for deduplication in the receiver. Note that this approach
will work correctly only if all non-Kafka messages and events at the edge of the over-all system are
properly deduplicated!
So, implementing idempotency can be challenging and requires careful design. It’s important to
consider the potential impact on system performance and to handle edge cases correctly. There are
some key questions that must be answered to come to an adequate implementation of an
idempotent operation:
• "Does the operation change the internal state?" and if yes, "will another execution of the
operation change the state in a way that is not intended"? Adding another 10€ to a bank account
is an example of an unintended state change, while turning on a light again is probably not.
This is true even if the light had been turned off by another command message in the time
between the first execution of the "on"-operation and the second. Note that the result, i.e., the
light being on, must be considered correct since it could also be the consequence of the non-
deterministic sequence of command message receiption.
• "Does the operation trigger other external operations and are these other operations
idempotent?" Triggering a non-idempotent external operation usually yields an operation non-
idempotent even if all local state changes can safely be executed multiple times.
• "Does the response depend on the time of execution?" and if yes, "does the caller depend on a
response that is identical to the response given after the first execution?" In the latter case, the
operation should not be thought of as "do X" but as "do X at time Y" and at best be also explicitly
named that way. Note that "time" may be a rather coarse concept as an ordered sequence of
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Fundamental Challenges and Patterns | 250
version numbers. An implementation of "do X at time Y" could be implemented using
historization. Operations that retroactively change state probably will be harder to implement.
Compensating Transactions
This section is inspired by an article from Microsoft Learn.
Compensating Transactions help to undo the work performed by a series of steps, which together
define an eventual consistency operation. This pattern can be useful in situations where each step
is contingent on the success of the previous step.
In a distributed system, achieving consistency across multiple services can be challenging.
Traditional ACID transactions which provide strong consistency may not be feasible or desirable in
a distributed setting due to the associated performance and availability trade-offs. Instead,
compensating transactions offer a way to achieve eventual consistency in a distributed system. For
a comparison of ACID and BASE transactions, see ACID vs. BASE Transactions. In this pattern, each
operation that changes the system’s state is accompanied by a compensating operation that can
undo the changes if necessary. Instead of rolling back an uncommitted ACID transaction, the system
is "rolled forward" to a state that is equivalent (at least in a business logic way) to the state before
the original operation. The pattern becomes complicated if other state changes in the meantime
impede the compensation or if the compensation fails for some technical reason. In this case,
human interaction is almost always necessary.
Designing and implementing compensating transactions can be complex, especially when
operations have side effects. Also, compensating transactions may not always be possible or
practical, depending on the nature of the operations and the system’s requirements.
Compensating transactions are a key part of the SAGA pattern. The coordination of the
compensating transactions as part of a SAGA can be implemented in two ways: as choreography or
as orchestration.
Out-of-Sequence Message Reception
This section is inspired by the section on the resequencer pattern in the EiP book.
Out-of-Sequence Message Reception refers to the scenario where messages are received in a
different order than they were sent. This can occur in distributed systems due to network latency,
message rerouting, or concurrent processing.
The Resequencer is a messaging pattern that can be used to deal with out-of-sequence message
reception. It is a component that receives a stream of related but out-of-sequence messages and
releases them to the next component in their correct order.
The Resequencer uses correlation identifiers to determine the sequence of the messages. It holds
onto a message until it has received all preceding messages. Once the Resequencer has all the
messages in the correct order, it can release them to the next component.
This pattern can be implemented in a stateful or stateless manner. In a stateful Resequencer, the
component retains the message sequence state information between the messages. In a stateless
Resequencer, each message contains enough information to reconstruct the sequence.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Fundamental Challenges and Patterns | 251
The Resequencer pattern can help ensure data consistency and correctness in distributed systems.
However, it also introduces additional complexity and can impact system performance. Therefore,
it should be used judiciously and only when necessary.
Dead Letter Channel
The Dead Letter Channel is a pattern used in messaging systems to handle messages that cannot be
delivered or processed. It acts as a system’s safety net for messages that meet certain failure
conditions.
In a typical messaging system, a message is sent from a producer to a consumer. However, there
can be situations where the message cannot be processed. This could be due to errors in the
message, temporary issues with the consumer, or other unforeseen problems.
When such a situation occurs, instead of discarding the message or continuously retrying delivery,
the message is sent to a Dead Letter Channel. This is a specific queue designed to store these failed
messages.
The Dead Letter Channel allows developers to isolate and inspect the failed messages. This can help
in understanding the issues leading to the failure and in debugging the system.
It’s important to monitor the Dead Letter Channel and set up alerts for when messages arrive there.
This is because a message arriving in the Dead Letter Channel typically signifies an issue with the
system that needs to be addressed.
However, the Dead Letter Channel should not be used as a mechanism to handle business errors or
to control regular system flow. It is specifically designed for system-level errors and issues that
prevent a message from being delivered or processed.
API Management
API Management is the process of creating, publishing, and monitoring APIs in a secure and
scalable way. It involves defining the API’s structure, security, and usage policies, as well as
monitoring its performance and usage.
What is an API?
This section is inspired by a video by Mulesoft and an article by RedHat.
An API, or Application Programming Interface, is a set of rules and protocols for building and
interacting with a software application. APIs define the methods and data formats that a software
component should use to interact with another software component, allowing them to
communicate and share data. They act as a contract, specifying the operations that can be
performed, the input that is required, and the output that will be returned. The API contract also
includes cross-cutting aspects and functionality like quality of service and security.
APIs can be categorized into different types based on their functionality and use cases. These
include Web APIs, Operating System APIs, Database APIs, and many others.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini API Management | 252
Web APIs, also known as HTTP APIs, allow communication between different systems over the
internet using the HTTP protocol for transport and basic protocol functionality like error signaling.
They are commonly used to enable the interaction between client-side web applications and server-
side resources. But they can be used for server-to-server communication within an organization’s
network just as well.
Operating System APIs provide a way for applications to interact with the underlying operating
system. They provide functions and services that an application can use to perform low-level tasks
like accessing the file system or managing memory.
Database APIs allow applications to interact with a database. They provide functions for
performing operations like creating, reading, updating, and deleting data in a database.
While Operating System APIs and Database APIs are APIs in the true sense of the meaning also, they
are not part of the integration context of this chapter and will not be discussed here any further.
APIs are a fundamental part of modern software development, enabling the creation of complex,
interconnected systems. They allow developers to leverage existing services and components,
reducing the amount of code they need to write while maintaining a loose coupling of the software
components.
However, designing and implementing APIs can be challenging. It’s important to consider factors
like security, performance, and ease of use when designing an API. Proper documentation is also
crucial to ensure that developers can understand and use the API effectively.
Integration Technologies
This section will provide an overview of some technologies that can be used to implement the
integration patterns discussed in this chapter. These technologies reside at a basic level while
integration platforms or even API Management Products utilize these technologies and add
additional functionality for development, IT operations and management of integration solutions.
SOAP
SOAP (formerly known as Simple Object Access Protocol) is a protocol for exchanging structured
information in the implementation of web services. We highly recommend not using SOAP for
new projects, as it is a 25 years old legacy technology. The authors of the EiP book "jokingly
postulated that SOAP be renamed to Complex Remote Access Protocol" (p. 373).
Motivation and Design Goal for the SOAP Protocol
As SOAP started out as an object access protocol, the main design goal was to encapsulate and
exchange Remote Procedure Calls (RPC) using the extensibility and flexibility of XML, resulting in
three main characteristics:
• extensibility (security and WS-Addressing are among the extensions)
• neutrality (SOAP can operate over any protocol such as HTTP, SMTP, TCP, UDP)
• independence (SOAP allows for any programming model)
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Technologies | 253
As an example of what a SOAP-based architecture can do, an application can send a SOAP request
to a server that has web services enabled — such as a real-estate price database — with the
parameters for a search. The server then returns a SOAP response (an XML-formatted document)
with the resulting data, e.g., prices, location, features. Since the generated data comes in a
standardized machine-parsable format, the requesting application can then integrate it directly.
The term RPC must be interpreted in a broader sense here than a strict synchronous invocation of a
remote object’s method together with a communication of the invocation’s result back to the caller:
SOAP was always designed to support various Message Exchange Patterns, including partial
processing of a task by an intermediary along a path from the originator of a message to the final
destination. Therefore, SOAP is a messaging protocol allowing (via extensions) e.g., to specify how
secondary messages shall be routed.
For further reading, see Wikipedia on SOAP, W3C SOAP Main Page, W3C SOAP 1.2 Page, and
Wikipedia on SOAP Message Exchange Patterns.
Common Problems with SOAP
Marshalling and Unmarshalling
As with the gap between object-oriented programming and relational databases, also called the
object-relational impedance mismatch, there is a similar mismatch between the details of the
object-oriented programming model of the specific programming language used, and the features
and limitations of object representation in XML and SOAP. The most prominent problems are:
• empty arrays
• restrictions on the allowed set of values
• legal/illegal identifiers
• XML-namespaces
• cyclic object clusters (when trying to stay WS-I conformant)
• overloading and polymorphism
There are some wide-spread technologies and standards that can be used to automate the
procedure of serialization and de-serialization of objects in the course of SOAP message processing.
Most prominently, these are JAX-RPC (now deprecated) and JAX-WS in the Java world. Some
projects even use JAXB in order to marshall/unmarshall SOAP messages on an element level,
spreading dependencies on the SOAP message format all over the code. The (direct) use of JAXB is
therefore strongly discouraged.
While these tools allow for a fast development, their use often sacrifices the idea of contract-first
and thus impose a risk if interoperability across the boundary of the Java world is required.
Interoperability
While interoperability across technological borders was the key motivation for the development of
SOAP and its related specifications, the reality of available implementations — and their
incompatibilities — create a substantial risk when used naively. This is especially true for the less
often used extensions ("WS-* specifications") and is a result of gaps and unclear language in the
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Technologies | 254
specification documents.
For this reason, the Web Services Interoperability Organization (WS-I, now part of OASIS) has
defined Profiles of WS-* specifications that restrict the use of features and clarify open issues from
the standards documents. However, even the Basic Profile for web services interoperability exists
in four different versions, not to mention the profiles for security, attachment, SOAP binding, etc.
Being "WS-I conformant" therefore does not automatically mean to be "interoperable"!
Efficiency / Performance
Since SOAP messages are usually serialized as plain XML, SOAP is not very efficient in terms of
network performance. This can be optimized by choosing a more efficient serialization and
