binding, if need be. Other optimization options can be applied to some of the usual steps of XML
and SOAP message processing, e.g. the schema validation step before the actual processing may
simply be omitted, shifting the responsibility to error detection during the actual processing. All
these measures, however, require extra design-effort in the integration architecture and also create
additional effort for the definition of the integration contract.
The more relevant performance risk may even lie somewhere else: SOAP as a basic building block
is accompanied by a large number of additional specifications that offer features and properties
that may degrade performance if used based on thoughtless architecture decisions. Standards like
WS-Coordination, WS-AtomicTransaction, WS-ReliableMessaging and others introduce additional
message round-trips and XML-message processing cycles at a protocol layer where it is difficult to
achieve the performance that is usually necessary for features like these. If e.g., atomicity together
with a non-trivial performance is required, it must be validated if other technologies than SOAP
(e.g. the Shared Database integration style, possibly using a distributed database system) should be
utilized.
Complexity
The evolvement of SOAP and SOAP-related standards as developed by consortia of independent and
sometimes competing organizations has led to a large number of specifications, documents,
implementations and best practices that is almost impossible to overlook. Some of these
developments got stuck in a draft status but are nonetheless used as the basis for implementation.
Newer and enhanced versions of other specifications never get adopted, leaving implementation
projects with the well-known problems of the superseded older versions.
Today, after more than two decades of SOAP-based web services, work on standardization has
almost completely seized. But that doesn’t mean all questions have been answered and the original
goals have been achieved.
If SOAP is an option or even a requirement in an integration project of today, the architect should
keep the following rules in mind:
• If interoperability across technological boundaries is relevant, follow a strict contract-first
approach when using tools like JAX-WS. At best, use specific XML- and WSDL-Tooling to create
the contract before you write a single line of integration code.
• Plan for extensive testing, even if you restrict yourself to WS-I compliant implementations.
Concentrate on edge cases and "try to break the system" by defining relevant test cases.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Technologies | 255
• Restrict the use of WS-* specifications to the absolute minimum and to those specifications that
are widely in use.
• Keep an eye on performance and plan for several optimization cycles throughout the course of
the project.
For more details see SOAP Learning Material.
REST
This section contains a brief introduction to REST by providing a summary of an article by RedHat.
For a more detailed introduction to REST see the the REST fundamentals course on pluralsight.
REST, or Representational State Transfer, is an architectural style used for web development. It
leverages standard HTTP protocols and methods, making it a straightforward choice for web-based
APIs.
In a RESTful system, resources, which can be anything that can be named and represented, are
identified by URLs. Clients interact with these resources using standard HTTP methods, such as GET,
POST, PUT, and DELETE.
REST is stateless, meaning each request from a client to a server must contain all the information
needed to understand and process the request. The server does not store any context between
requests, which can simplify the server design.
REST APIs return data in a standard format, typically JSON or XML, which makes it easy for clients
to parse and use the data.
In comparison to SOAP, REST is generally considered to be simpler and more efficient.
Using REST as a base technology for integration comes with some drawbacks and risks:
• Statelessness: REST APIs are stateless, meaning each request from a client to a server must
contain all the information needed to understand and process the request. This can lead to
increased complexity in managing client state and can make the client heavier. In this case, the
author of the REST API should provide client-side libraries for commonly used programming
languages like Java and C#. These libraries should hide the extra complexity behind an API on
the programming language level, e.g., by handling the client-side state.
• Over-fetching and Under-fetching: REST APIs can sometimes return more data than necessary
(overfetching) or require multiple requests to get all the necessary data (underfetching). This
will lead to inefficiencies and increased latency. This problem can be handled by the definition
and use of additional parameters on the API that specify the amount of requested data. If this
approach is not successful, think of using other technologies like GraphQL.
• Security Concerns: REST does not have built-in security features like SOAP. However, HTTP and
thus REST APIs can be used with well-established security features like TLS (i.e. HTTPS).
• Lack of Standardization: REST APIs can suffer from inconsistencies in naming conventions
and data formats, which can complicate integration efforts. The extensive use of standards like
OpenAPI and JSON Schema as well as good documentation can mitigate this risk.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Technologies | 256
Best Practices for error handling in REST API Design
This section is a summary of a Baeldung article. For a more detailed introduction to the design of
RESTful web APIs, see fundamentals training on pluralsight. For more general considerations in
interface design, see Interface Design and System Interfaces.
When designing APIs, it’s important to consider how errors will be handled. Proper error handling
can make an API more reliable and easier to use.
One best practice — or even part of the REST standard — is to use HTTP status codes to indicate the
result of an API request. Each status code has a specific meaning, and clients can use these codes to
understand how their request was processed. Especially the distinction between 4xx errors and 5xx
errors is important: While 5xx errors are usually transient and can be handled by the client by re-
retrying the call, a 4xx error indicates some kind of malformed request. Resending a malformed
request will never result in a successful execution.
Another best practice is to provide meaningful error messages. These messages should be clear and
concise, and they should provide enough information for the client to understand what went
wrong. For APIs that are publicly exposed, however, the amount of technical detail must be
restricted. This is necessary to avoid the leaking of information that attackers can use to break into
the system. Error messages should therefore contain information only on the data that the caller
provided, and the data that the caller is allowed to retrieve anyway (e.g. her own bookings), and on
inconsistencies / mismatches or rule violations that lead to the error condition.
It’s also important to be consistent in how errors are returned. This can make it easier for clients to
handle errors and can improve the overall usability of the API.
In addition to these practices, it’s also recommended to log errors on the server side. This can help
with debugging and can provide valuable information about how the API is being used.
Finally, it’s important to handle exceptions properly. Uncaught exceptions can lead to unexpected
behavior and can make an API less reliable, even if the uncaught exception is finally caught by the
server’s REST API framework and mapped to an HTTP 500 error message: The client may retry the
request over and over again, even if the root cause of the exception was a malformed request
parameter.
Swagger/OpenAPI
Swagger, now known as the OpenAPI Specification (OAS), is a specification for machine-readable
interface files for describing, producing, consuming, and visualizing RESTful web services (REST
APIs). It provides a standardized, language-agnostic way to describe RESTful APIs, making them
easier to build, document, and consume.
For details, see the OpenAPI Guide, especially the following sections:
• What is OpenAPI
• Basic Structure
• Paths and Operations
• Describing Parameters
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Technologies | 257
• Describing Responses
• Using $ref
Using OAS as a means of API design is highly recommended in order to foster the API-first
development methodology.
GraphQL
GraphQL is a query language for APIs and a runtime for executing those queries with your existing
data. It provides an efficient and powerful alternative to REST and offers significant advantages for
some use cases. GraphQL allows clients to define the structure of the responses they require. This
means that servers can return exactly what the client needs, avoiding over-fetching or under-
fetching of data. In GraphQL, you model your business domain as a graph by defining types and
fields on those types, then providing functions for each field on each type.
One of the main benefits of GraphQL is its ability to aggregate responses across multiple sources
with a single query, and without over-fetching or under-fetching of data.
GraphQL queries are hierarchical and easy to understand. They mirror the shape of the response,
making them more intuitive to write and understand.
GraphQL also provides strong type checking. This means that the API’s shape is defined by the
server, and errors can be caught at compile time rather than run time.
However, like any technology, GraphQL is not a one-size-fits-all solution. It’s important to consider
the specific needs and constraints of your project when deciding whether to use GraphQL.
For further details see the GraphQL course on pluralsight.
JMS
Java Message Service (JMS) is a Java API that allows applications to create, send, receive, and read
messages in a loosely coupled, reliable, and asynchronous way. JMS is a part of the Java Platform,
Enterprise Edition (Java EE), and was designed to enable communication between different
components of a distributed application. JMS supports two models for messaging: Point-to-Point
(PTP) and Publish/Subscribe (Pub/Sub).
In the PTP model, the sender sends messages to a specific queue, and a single receiver consumes
the message from the queue. This model ensures that the message will be delivered to and
consumed by one consumer only.
JMS also supports a variant of the point-to-point topology where multiple receivers "listen" to the
queue but each message is delivered to only one randomly picked one of them. With this topology,
JMS implements a simple, efficient and easy-to-use mechanism for load distribution and higher
availability.
In the Pub/Sub model, messages are sent to a topic. Multiple subscribers can subscribe to that topic
and receive all messages sent to it. This model is useful when a message needs to be broadcast to
multiple receivers.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Technologies | 258
JMS messages are composed of three parts: a header, properties, and a body. The header contains
fields used for routing and identification, the properties section is used for custom meta data fields,
and the body contains the actual data.
JMS provides reliable and asynchronous message delivery, which is crucial for distributed systems.
It also supports distributed transactions and can be integrated with Java Transaction API (JTA) to
participate in distributed transactions.
However, JMS is not a protocol but an API, meaning that it does not define how messages are
transmitted over the network. The actual implementation is provided by a JMS provider, which can
be a standalone messaging server or a part of an application server.
In conclusion, JMS is a powerful tool for building distributed applications that require reliable,
asynchronous, and loosely coupled communication.
For further details see the Pluralsight course on JMS.
Apache Kafka
Apache Kafka is a distributed event streaming platform used for building real-time data pipelines
and streaming applications. It is designed to handle high-throughput, fault-tolerant, and scalable
event streaming.
While Kafka is popular and well-suited in some scenarios, it also has its drawbacks:
• Limited Transactional Scope: Kafka’s transactional guarantees are limited to Kafka itself. This
means that if your application involves multiple systems (e.g., databases, other messaging
systems), ensuring end-to-end transactional integrity can be challenging.
• Data Ordering: Ensuring strict message ordering can be difficult, particularly when dealing
with multiple partitions. This can complicate the development of applications that rely on
ordered data processing.
• Learning Curve: Kafka has a steep learning curve. Understanding its core concepts like
brokers, clusters, partitions, and topics can take time and extensive training.
• Complexity and Operational Overhead: Setting up and maintaining Kafka clusters, including
monitoring, scaling, and ensuring high availability, is complex and can add significant
operational overhead. It requires a good understanding of distributed systems and can be
challenging for teams without prior experience.
• Storage Costs: Kafka retains data for a configurable amount of time, which can lead to high
storage costs, especially if large volumes of data are involved.
• Latency: While Kafka is designed for low latency, achieving this can be challenging in practice,
especially under a heavy load or with complex configurations.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Integration Technologies | 259
Cross-Cutting Concepts
Cross-cutting concepts are those patterns and solutions which often span multiple building blocks
and possibly even different architectural views. Therefore, they do not fit in the description of one
view and are collected here.
Errors
Migrated

This section has been migrated from Architecture Guide with some minor, but no
major rework.
There is no such thing as error-free software and not even any genuinely error-free environment
(hardware, operating system, users). Anyone who develops software has to deal with the issue of
errors. Often a very large (too large!) portion of a software system is devoted to situations which
are considered to be “not normal”, “unusual” or “undesirable”.
Overview
Despite this, error handing is often a weak point in development projects. This may have the
following consequences.
• Errors remain unnoticed.
• Systems are inconsistent after errors occur.
• Errors are (unintentionally) logged multiple times.
• The information about errors that have occurred is often insufficient to locate their causes.
• Error handing overloads the code and makes it difficult to understand.
• Exception classes make the system unclear and create superfluous dependencies.
To avoid the above problems and design code that is as clear as possible, we systematically address
the issue of errors and develop an end-to-end error concept. The following sections present clear
definitions, meaningful conventions and notes to form the basis on which project-specific error
concepts should be constructed.
Errors, error situations and responses to errors
Figure 56. Errors, error situations and error reactions
Errors may occur for various reasons, for example due to a hardware defect, a faulty
implementation or because a plausibility check detects a discrepancy. In all cases, the error
situation is detected during the execution of an operation in the provider and is returned as an
error to the consumer which is able to react to it.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 260
Definition: Error situation
An error situation occurs if it is detected in the program that something that does not
correspond to the desired behavior. The error situation is either detected explicitly at a point
in the code by checking a condition or is recognized by the runtime environment.
Example 12. Two examples on error detection
• Detection of the error situation by the program logic by means of a plausibility check
(“Amount may not be negative…”)
• Detection of the error situation by the runtime environment (NullPointerException)
The error situation results in the error which we define as follows:
Definition: Error
With an error, the provider indicates to the consumer that an error situation is present so
that the consumer is then able to react to this (seeXXXXX Error reaction). The error contains
information about the underlying error situation. Depending on the programming model,
errors are implemented as return codes or exceptions. Errors are generated either by the
program logic or by the runtime system once an error situation has been recognized.
The consumer receives errors when calling an operation and is therefore able to react to it in a
suitable way. We define this as an error reaction.
Definition: Error reaction
We use the term “error reaction” to designate the interface operation consumer code that
reacts to an indicated error.
During the error reaction, the error may be output at suitable locations or displayed to the user.
This is performed by means of a suitable error presentation which we define as follows:
Definition: Error presentation
We use the term “error presentation” to refer to the display of an error to the user by means
of a dialog (error dialog or dialog extended by error messages) or output in a log for the
specialist department, operation staff or development department.
In the following sections, we examine errors, error situations and error reactions more precisely
and also consider the issue of error presentation.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 261
Errors as components of interfaces
Errors play an important role in the design of interfaces: The provider uses errors to indicate the
error situations which can occur to the consumer. The consumer can then react to the errors in a
suitable way.
Figure 57. Error reaction to specified and unspecified errors
Before we can discuss errors further, we need to classify them: the crucial criterion here is whether
or not the error has been specified in the interface. Consequently, we define specified and
unspecified errors as follows:
Definition: Specified Error
An error that forms part of the interface contract is referred to as a specified error. It must
therefore also be described in the interface definition to that the consumers of the interface
can react to it without having to make assumptions about the implementation of the
interface.
Providers and consumers must therefore take account of all the specified errors – the provider
must return them in the specified situation and the consumer must react to them, preferably by
handling them. As a result, in the interface specification, it is necessary to clarify what information
has to be passed by means of errors in the interface. These statements apply to purely logical as
well as to technical interfaces – depending on the level of abstraction at which the specification is
positioned. It is not possible, however, to specify the occurrence of all the possible errors in
interfaces. This is because many errors – primarily programming errors – depend solely on the
implementation and are not part of the interface contract. Consequently, we define not only
specified but also unspecified errors:
Definition: Unspecified Error
An error that does not form part of the interface contract is referred to as an unspecified
error. It may therefore also not be described in the interface definition. In general,
unspecified errors cannot be listed in full and are not considered by the interface. They are
not handled by the consumer.
Unspecified errors are dependent on the implementation, for example External application not
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 262
available, Impossible to access database, Not enough RAM etc.
Since unspecified errors are not part of the interface and consumers of the interface only program
against the interface contract, we can formulate the following simple but effective rule:
 Unspecified Errors
The consumer of an interface operation must never react to an unspecified error.
Example: A consumer of a bank transfer component must react to specified errors (e.g. account
overdrawn) but never to unspecified errors (e.g. ConnectionFailure).
Mapping of errors in the programming language
Since they are components of interfaces, specified errors can of course also be found in the
programming of interfaces. We distinguish between errors and exceptions:
Definition: Exception
The term “exception” refers to the construct of the same name in programming languages.
Exceptions can be used to implement errors. Unlike return codes, exceptions must be trapped
explicitly as otherwise they terminate the application.
And we also define:
Definition: Returncode
A returncode is another concept in programming languages which is primarily used for the
implementation of errors in procedural languages.
In object-oriented languages, unspecified errors are always signaled by means of exceptions.
However, unspecified errors must never be implemented as checked exceptions – otherwise, they
would automatically become part of the interface and, by definition, would be specified errors!

Never use checked exceptions to implement unspecified errors.
In contrast, it is sensible to use checked exceptions to map specified errors.
 Use checked exceptions to implement specified errors, if the programming
language supports checked exceptions.
Otherwise the following applies, in particular in the case of languages which do not support
checked exceptions:

Document specified errors properly in the interface code.
For example, in C# , specified errors are always indicated in the operation’s XML documentation.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 263
In programming languages which do not have an exception concept, unspecified errors are
implemented using returncodes. The employed returncodes must be clearly differentiated from all
the specified returncodes. Disjunctive number or name ranges are usually employed to do this:
 Use disjunctive number or name range when implementing specified and
unspecified errors with returncodes.
Object-oriented languages usually possess a series of predefined exception classes which cover the
typical error situations. You should reuse these for unspecified errors in order to obtain a
consistent categorization of the exceptions and avoid having to generate unnecessary exception
classes:
 When implementing unspecified errors, use the (unchecked) exception classes
available in the programming environment.
Java examples:
IllegalArgumentException
for parameters which violate the precondition
IllegalStateException for inconsistencies/discrepancies in the internal state of a
class/component
NET examples:
InvalidOperationException if a call of a method is invalid due to the current state of
the object
ArgumentOutOfRangeException if the value of an argument does not lie in the range of
values which has been defined as permissible by the called method.
ArgumentNullException if a NULL reference (Nothing in Visual Basic) is passed to a
method for which this is not a valid argument
In the case of specified errors, it is advisable to define a project-specific exception basic class:
Use a basic class for the implementation of specified errors to: * Support the
 localization of the error on display * Provide for additional possible attributes such
as the logical error number in order to make it possible to find reference
information in the documentation
The application’s specified errors must then be derived from this class. In general, however, a
project-specific exception hierarchy should be kept as flat and small as possible. Separate
subclasses should only be recommended if they can be handled sensibly.

Keep project-specific exception hierarchies as flat and small as possible
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 264
Error situation
There are many different reasons that may cause an error to be signaled. We next consider typical
error situations and provide some comments on how to deal with them.
Table 1. Error situations, their detection and the resulting error category
Error situation Detection Error category
Call parameter outside of Detected by assertion Example: Unspecified error
interface specification Example: Assertion concerning an
Invalid argument when passing argument’s preconditions
a negative number to a root
function
Error during processing. Detected by the logical Specified error
Example: The account was application code. Example:
overdrawn during a “Bank Balance<0
transfer” operation
System error / missing Detected by runtime Unspecified error
resources. Examples: Not environment
enough RAM, not enough disk
space
Every interface is a contract between the provider and the consumer. The execution of operations
is often dependent on preconditions. The consumer must ensure that these preconditions are
fulfilled before calling the operation since it must otherwise accept the possibility of receiving an
unspecified error from the provider.
However, within the framework of defensive programming and in order to avoid jeopardizing data
consistency, the provider must not assume that the preconditions are met but instead has to check
this itself. We formulate this requirement in terms of a rule:
Check an operation’s preconditions as early as possible.
When implementing an operation, it is necessary to check the preconditions for
the operation as early as possible (i.e. at the start of implementation). For example,
the value ranges for arguments should be asserted in this way.

The following exceptions apply: The checking of certain preconditions would
require a disproportionate amount of effort (in terms of development, testing,
runtime). If the precondition is not checked because of this then the fact that the
operation will react in an undefined way if the precondition is not fulfilled must
be mentioned in the interface.
Error reaction
The consumer of an interface operation can only react to specified errors.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 265
Distinguish between the basic error reactions: “Terminate”, “Repeat” and “Neutralize”.
The following basic pattern applies to the reactions of consumers to interface
operation errors:
1. Termination of the current operation, no specific handling. The error may be
 extended by context information and may be signaled via a separate error
(more coarse-grained) or it may be passed on directly to its consumer.
2. Repetition of the operation (e.g. after waiting for an appropriate time).
3. Neutralization of the error, i.e..: display the error ( Error presentation) and
continue current processing in an alternative way. In exceptional cases, errors
can even be ignored and formal processing can be continued.
If the operation is repeated, it is necessary to make sure that the repetition has no side effects. If the
operation itself is terminated then the following principle which simplifies troubleshooting during
development applies:
Do not hide or lose the original error as the error reaction if the current operation itself
is terminated.

If an exception concept is used, this means that exceptions should be chained if
possible. Please note: At system boundaries, chained exceptions must be converted
into text since the chained exceptions are unknown to the consumer.
The following also applies to the implementation of the error reaction:
At the consumer, implement the error reaction in the immediate proximity of the
interface call.

However, when doing so, you should make sure that the implemented logical
sequence is still easy to recognize and is not destroyed by the implementation of
the error reaction.
Logical errors can usually only be handled by the user. They should therefore be reported to the
user or calling component together with all the necessary (logical) context information. Operation
staff cannot do anything about this type of error and should therefore not be “flooded” with them,
i.e. these errors should not end up in a protocol that is to be analyzed by Operations. For this
reason, we also fundamentally and specifically reject the automatic logging of all exceptions. This
results in the next rule:
Do not log any specified errors in the error reactions of application components.
Exceptions:

• If logging is explicitly required in such cases.
• In the case of repetition or neutralization, it is often purposeful to log the error
in order to obtain information about the program behavior.
If a specified error can never be handled by the consumer then it is possible to react to it at two
different levels. Either revise the design of the interface or forward the error:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 266
If a specified error cannot be handled, forward it.
If the error matches an error specified in the current interface then it can be

forwarded as a specified error in the interface.
Otherwise, it must be converted into an unspecified error.
Designing error-handling in interfaces
The consumers of an interface operation must react to specified errors but must not take account of
unspecified errors. Whether or not an interface can be used by a consumer depends largely on the
way the interface is designed. We now consider the issue of errors as a part of interface design. If
we consider the call of an interface operation, the consumer may obtain three fundamentally
different results:
1. The consumer receives a normal result that is specified in the interface. It can continue its
processing as normal.
2. The consumer receives an error that is specified in the interface. The consumer reacts to the
error. It may react by terminating, repeating or neutralizing the operation.
3. The consumer receives an error that is not specified in the interface. Since the consumer cannot
react to it, its own processing is terminated with the same unspecified error.
The figure below presents these three possibilities for the result of a call of an interface operation:
Figure 58. Error reaction to specified and unspecified errors
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 267
Instead of reacting as consumer to the errors specified by the interface, it is also possible, within
the framework of application design, to create interfaces that are “more suitable” for the consumer.
This is particularly true in the case of special interfaces belonging to a subsystem’s application
components.
Example 13. Handling errors in interfaces
Let us consider the interface operation:
Order[] readOrders(Parameter selection) throws OrderNotFoundException;
This operation returns a list of orders (Order) corresponding to the passed parameters. If no
orders are found then an OrderNotFoundException is returned to the consumer as a specified
error. This specification requires all consumers of the operation readOrders() to handle the
specified error OrderNotFoundException.
If this occurs with a large number of consumers and all the consumers react by neutralizing
the error, thus continuing normal (!) operation, then the interface has not been optimally
designed. It would be just as good to do without the exception and instead return an empty set
to the consumer. As a result, normal processing would continue at the consumer without any
exception processing and the consumer’s code would be simplified.
We define the approach presented in the example as a rule:
Use a normal result rather than a specified error in the interface operation…
if consumers always react by neutralizing the specified error and thus continue
their normal processing.

Figure 59. Specification of the behavior of an interface
If we employ this rule in the interface readOrders(), then the specified error
OrderNotFoundException is converted into a normal result (the empty set). If, in contrast, we
assume that all the consumers of the operation readOrders() would have to terminate their
processing if they received an empty set as the result of the operation then we may retain the
original specification of the operation with a specified error:
Order[] readOrders(Parameter selection) throws OrderNotFoundException;
This results in the opposite conclusion to the one formulated in the previous rule and represents an
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 268
additional rule:
Use a specified error rather than a normal result in the interface operation
if all consumers always(!) react to this process by terminating their own
processing.

Please note: Such cases are rather rare and are only mentioned here for the sake of completeness.
Let us consider a vehicle configurator interface which possesses the methods:
Configuration createConfiguration();
Configuration changeConfiguration(Configuration c,Code[] ncodes, Code[] rcodes);
The vehicle configurator is used to create and modify valid vehicle configurations
(Configuration).
The method createConfiguration() creates an initial valid vehicle configuration for the process.
The method changeConfiguration() modifies this through the choice of new equipment (Code)
or by removing existing equipment and then returns a valid vehicle configuration. If the
consumer uses the interface as intended then it can make sure that it always only passes valid
configurations to the method changeConfiguration().
It therefore makes perfect sense for the implementation of the method changeConfiguration()
to react with an unspecified error if an invalid configuration is passed (parameter c). This is
especially true because the consumer cannot react meaningfully – it cannot correct the faulty
configuration.
The clarification provided by this example results in the following rule:
Use a an unspecified rather than a specified error in the interface operation…
if all consumers always react to an unspecified error by terminating their own
processing.

Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 269
Example 14. Example
Let us again consider a vehicle configurator interface which possesses the methods:
Configuration createConfiguration();
Configuration changeConfiguration(Configuration c,Code[] ncodes, Code[] rcodes);
If an additional consumer of the interface now receives vehicle configurations from an
external system, e.g. from a configuration user interface for end customers on the Internet,
then the consumer can no longer be sure that it is passing a valid configuration to the method
changeConfiguration() (for example, the configurators may have different data stocks). In this
case, it is better to specify an error InvalidConfigurationException in the interface, i.e.:
Configuration changeConfiguration(Configuration c,Code[] ncodes, Code[] rcodes)
throws InvalidConfigurationException;
This results in the opposite rule to the previous one:
Use a a specified rather than an unspecified error in the interface operation…
…if the consumers cannot fulfill the preconditions for the call of the operation but
can react meaningfully to a specified error.

Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 270
Example 15. Example
Another solution is possible for the vehicle configurator interface: Since checking the validity
of a passed configuration is a very time-consuming task, consumers which receive a possibly
invalid configuration may check this using an additional operation, e.g. with:
boolean checkValidConfiguration(Configuration c);
Calling this method does not throw an error – the (logical) result of the method is precisely to
test whether the passed configuration is valid or not. Using this method, the operation
changeConfiguration() is again able to react with an unspecified error if an invalid
configuration is passed. However, it is important to note that there must be no change to the
assessment of the validity of the configuration between the call of checkValidConfiguration()
and changeConfiguration(). For example, a change in the configurator’s data stock could mean
that a configuration that was only recently valid suddenly becomes invalid! In this case, the
interface variant described in the rule should be preferred.
We again formulate this explanation in terms of a rule:
Use an additional checking method in the interface in the case of an unspecified error in
an interface operation…
…if some consumers are unable to fulfill the preconditions for the call of the
operation and the checking conditions remain unchanged across the calls of the
 two operations.
Error reaction in the technical infrastructure
The consumers of an operation must not react to unspecified errors. So what happens to these
unspecified errors? They are logged or neutralized by the technical infrastructure in a way that is
transparent to the consumer. This is performed in the technical infrastructure’s security facades.
Definition: Security facade
The security facade encapsulates a part of the application whose state it knows and monitors
in that it is able to restore or reset it. Security facades must trap and forward calls in this part
of the application and are therefore able to react to the errors that occur. Security facades are
usually a part of the technical infrastructure. Security facades are typically employed for
individual application components, subsystems or for technical services (in particular for the
server access service in the case of accesses to application core interfaces).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 271
One important reaction for which the security facade is responsible is the reaction to errors that
have to be interpreted as disasters:
Definition: Disaster handling
The term disaster handling refers to the reaction of a security facade in the technical
infrastructure to errors which occur when a method is called via the security facade. Disaster
handling is usually performed at (sub)system boundaries. During disaster handling * the
monitored state is reset * used resources are released again * the error is logged if necessary
* the error is forwarded to the caller
Example 16. Example
Let us consider the security facade of the server access service used for accesses to the
components of a stateless application core. The state of the application core components is
monitored by the technical transactions of the O/R mapper.
As a result, the state can be reset by rolling back the technical transaction in the event of an
incorrect call. Unspecified errors (in the component interface) are converted into specified
errors (in the technical infrastructure, e.g. ServerError).
In such cases, the unspecified error is supplied in text form together with all the information –
including chained errors. In addition, the unspecified errors are recorded at this point.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 272
Figure 60. Example of a call between two components via the ServerAccessService and its security facade
Error presentation
The subject of error presentation does not just consist of the output of errors in dialogs but also,
and most importantly, the recording of errors by the components of the technical infrastructure.
Displaying errors in a dialog or business logic log
When displaying errors in a dialog or the business logic log, it is necessary to clarify issues which
relate to the client as a whole.
 Clarify the system’s behavior in the event of errors and their representation in
dialogs or the business logic log as early as possible.
Recording errors for Operation and Development
Unspecified errors are logged centrally by means of the components in the technical infrastructure.
Here, we differentiate between the recording of tracing and logging information:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 273
Definition: Tracing
Tracing refers to the system recording of program activities in a file or database in order to
permit the detailed tracking of past program flows or parts thereof. Tracing is only intended
to provide information for Development.
It provides information, for example, about the operations that have been performed, the
code sections and branches that were processed and the values assigned to the employed
variables. Tracing data tends to become very extensive very quickly.
In contrast to tracing, logging is intended for a number of different target groups:
Definition:Logging
Logging refers to the recording of program activities in “logs”, i.e. in files or in a database, in
order to be able to track specific actions in the program flow. The technical logs intended for
Operation record system states and errors, while business logic logs tend to record logical
actions (in this case, we also refer to audit trails or special logs). Log information may be of
use to a variety of target groups – Operation and Development often use the same logs.
Both tracing and logging record program activities. Although the content and form differ, the
technical implementation often does not. In addition, it makes sense to assign the output in trace
and log files to categories in order to make it possible to define the relevance of the output
information.
Examples of common categories: Debug, Info, Warn, Error, Fatal.
Meaningful information in log files provided to developers for troubleshooting purposes includes,
for example:
• Error message text – logical and technical
• Start and end of operations together with additional location information if required (tracing)
• Contents of important parameters and variables if required (tracing)
• System messages – including all warnings and information
Experience shows that the requirements of Operation staff in terms of logging are often taken into
account only very late, very imprecisely, or not at all. The discrepancies this causes then escalate,
often at a very inconvenient time – just before or during acceptance or even shortly after the start
of productive operation…
To prevent this, we formulate:
 Clarify logging requirements with Operation at an early stage and in sufficient
detail.
When doing this, it is necessary to clarify the following and other concepts:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Errors | 274
• Unique static error identification To permit the use of reference documentation such as help
systems or manuals, component-specific or cross-component numbers.
• Unique dynamic error identification (GUID: Global Unique Identifier) To create a unique link
between an observed error reported by Operation (possibly also displayed to the user in a
dialog!) and the relevant locations in possibly different error logs.
Here again, the following applies: Do not create additional dependencies between the components
via error classes and GUID. A random value may be sufficient instead of a generated sequential
number (local to the component).
Monitoring
Monitoring is a very important topic, especially for distributed applications. This article covers
multiple aspects of monitoring, e.g. infrastructure view, performance monitoring and health
checks.
Overview
For monitoring a complex application landscape it is crucial to have an exact overview which
applications are up and running and which are not and why. On a high level view we strongly
suggest to separate the application to be monitored from the monitoring system itself. Therefore,
your application should concentrate on providing app specific data for the monitoring. Aspects
such as aggregation, visualization, search, alerting, etc. should be addressed outside of your app by
a monitoring system product. There are many products providing such a monitoring system like
checkmk, icinga, SkyWalking, etc. Before choosing a product you should check if a monitoring
solution is already in place.
As monitoring covers a lot of different aspects we separate the following types of monitoring and
according data:
Log monitoring
is about collecting and monitoring the logs of all apps and containers in your IT landscape. It is
suitable for events such as an HTTP request with its URL, resulting status code and duration in
milliseconds. Your monitoring may not react to such data in realtime. Instead it may take a delay
of one or a few seconds.
Infrastructure monitoring
is about monitoring the (hardware) infrastructure with measures like usage of CPU, memory,
disc-space, etc. This is a pure operational task and your app should have nothing to do with this.
In other words it is a waste if your app tries to monitor these aspects as existing products can do
this much better and your app might probably only see virtual machines and is unable to see the
"real" infrastructure.
Health check
is about providing internal data about the current health of your app. Typically you provide
sensors with health status per component or interface to neighbor service (database
connectivity, etc.).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Monitoring | 275
Application Performance Monitoring
is about measuring performance and tracing down performance issues.
Health check
The idea of a health check is to provide monitoring data about the current health status of your
application. This allows to integrate this specific data into the monitoring system used for your IT
landscape. In order to keep the monitoring simple and easy to integrate consider using the
following best practices:
• Use simple and established platform- and language-independent protocols such as ReST.
• Consider using recent standards such as openTelemetry.
• Consider to drop access-control for your monitoring interfaces and for security prevent
external access to it in your infrastructure (loadbalancers or gateways). Monitoring is only for
usage within an IT landscape internally. It does not make sense for externals and end-users to
access your app for reading monitoring data from a random node decided by a loadbalancer.
Further, external access can easily lead to sensitive data exposure.
• Consider to define different end-points per usage-scenario. So if you want the loadbalancer to
ask your app monitoring for availability of each node then create a separate service URL that
only provides OK or anything else for failure (NOK, 404, 500, timeout). Do not mix this with a
health-check that needs more detailed information.
• Also do not forget about basic features such as providing the name and the release version of
your application.
• Be careful to automate decisions based on monitoring and health checks. It easily turns out to
be stupid if you automatically restart your pod or container because of some monitoring
indicator. In the worst case a failure of a central component will cause your health-check to
report down for all apps and as a result all your containers will be restarted frequently. Instead
of curing problems such decisions will cause much more harm and trouble.
• Avoid causing reasonable load with your monitoring and health-check itself. In many cases it is
better to use log-monitoring or to collect monitoring data from use-cases that happen in your
app anyway. If you create dummy read and write requests in your monitoring implementation
you will easily turn it into a DOS-attack.
Log monitoring
With trends towards IT landscapes with many but much smaller apps the classical approach to
write log-files to the disc and let operators read those via SSH became entirely obsolete. Nowadays
we have up to hundreds or even thousands of apps that themselves are clustered into multiple
nodes. Therefore you should establish a centralized log monitoring system in the environment and
let all your nodes log directly into that system. The process of feeding the log monitoring system
with the log data is called log shipping. This approach gives the following benefits:
• all log information available in one place
• full-text search across all log files
• ability to automatically trigger alerts from specific log patterns
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Monitoring | 276
• ability to do data-mining on logs and visualize in dashboards
Options for log-monitoring
Typical products for such a log monitoring system incl. log shippers are:
• ELK-Stack
• Graylog
• Splunk
API for log-monitoring
The "API" for logging to a log monitoring system for your app is pretty simple:
• Write your logs to standard out
• Use JSON as log format to simplify the processing of log data in the log monitoring system (e.g.
indexation, field extraction, …)
Then the container infrastructure can automatically collect your logs from standard out and
directly feed those into the log monitoring system. As a result, your app does not need to know
anything about your log monitoring system and logging becomes most simple. Further, if you do
not write log files anymore, you might not need to write any other files and therefore may not even
need write permissions on the filesystem of your container. In such case an attacker who may find
a vulnerability in your app will have less attack surface in case he can not write any file.
Observability in distributed systems
Distributed systems need to be observable. This means that you need to have a holistic view on
whats going on in the systems. To analyze problems in distributed systems can be become
cumbersome because you need to be able to follow calls through multiple services (tracing). We
strongly advice you to pay attention to observability when designing a distributed systems. This
should not be implemented by yourself into your services. There are out-of-the-box solutions
available which you are advised to use.
OpenTelemetry
To follow a request through multiple systems, standards like OpenTelemetry introduce Spans which
allow you to correlate related calls and logs through the system. These spans are also included in
log messages, making it simple to collect all the log statements for a given request.
There is a broad support to implement observable systems for many languages. Many of these
could be found via the OpenTelemetry registry. For instance, some providers such as Jaegers have
even deprecated their client agents in favor of OpenTelemetry.
Service Meshes
Service-meshes manage all inter-service-communication in distributed systems. So when designing
your application platform carefully consider using a service mesh, especially if you have high
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Monitoring | 277
requirements in monitoring your distributed systems, e.g. because of high availability
requirements.
Reliability
Definition
Reliability is defined as the:
degree to which a system, product or component performs specified
functions under specified conditions for a specified period of time.
— ISO/IEC 25010:2011(en)
Reliability measures the "failure rate" of a system. Thereby reliability describes how consistently
and compliant with its requirements the system operates over time.
In contrast, availability describes the ability of a system
to be in a state to perform a required function under given conditions at a
given instant of time or over a given time interval, assuming that the
required external resources are provided
— ISO/IEC 2382-14:1997(en)
This constitutes the percentage of time the system serves its purpose. The systems availability can
be derived from its mean time to failure (MTTF) and mean time to recovery (MTTR):
TTR TTF TTR
Time
Incident Incident Incident
determined Resolved determined
Figure 61. Relation of time-to-recovery and time-to-failure
Another important term when it comes to building "reliable" systems is "resilience". Resilience is
defined as the system’s:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 278
ability to absorb and adapt in a changing environment
— ISO 22300:2021(en)
A fragile system can only tolerate a very limited level of stress before it breaks, whereas a more
resilient system is able to cope with a higher level of stress. A system achieves this elevated stress-
resistance or fault-tolerance by adjusting its way of operating to the new situation. There is a broad
spectrum of what constitutes "stress", ranging from modifications in the underlying environment,
changes in the system itself, different load patterns to faults of any kind. Therefore, a system is
more resilient if it adapts its way of operating when facing unknown conditions or faults.
Why reliability matters
For a software system to create value, it needs to run reliably in the production environment. It
must not only be available on-demand during normal circumstances but also meet its agreed upon
standards when facing real-world situations with abnormal conditions. Especially if the systems
serve a business-critical purpose, major outages or impairments can cause substantial damage to
the clients.
In the modern world of constantly growing, highly complex distributed systems with an
accelerating need for changes, a system is confronted with constantly varying situations. With this
resulting advancing likelihood of failure, ensuring the overall business continuity becomes a major
challenge. To ensure the business continuity a high availability is required, which in turn can be
achieved by maximizing the underlying reliability and resilience.
Principles to increase reliability
Embrace failures
The obvious strategy to increase a software system’s availability relies on increasing the MTTF,
meaning trying to avoid failures as much as possible. But this approach assumes that it is possible
to know the relevant conditions under which the system runs, including all failure situations in
advance. While this might be the case in theory, most modern software applications are distributed
systems. With increasing size and distribution of such systems, the number of unique failure
scenarios grows exponentially. Even trying to enumerate a majority of failure situations in advance
would incur tremendous upfront design costs. This directly conflicts with the ambition to increase
agility and reduce the time-to-market. Based on real-world experience, the industry acknowledged
in recent years that failure is inevitable.[10]
As a result, the overall rising complexity requires more efforts to maintain an acceptable level of
the system’s reliability. This is why efforts to improve reliability should also account for the second
factor in the formula by reducing the MTTR. This implies that failures need to be resolved quickly.
By actively handling incidents and exploiting them to maximize learning, failures become a
valuable source for improving the overall system’s reliability. For this reason, a mindset emerged in
the last decade: Do not try to avoid failures. Embrace them. The following sections explain the
principles underpinning this mindset.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 279
3 Ways of DevOps
The "3 ways of DevOps" is a valuable model to derive basic principles for improving the overall
reliability of a software system [kim2012].
1st way: from dev to ops
3rd way: continuous
DEV OPS
improvement
2nd way: from ops to dev
Figure 62. 3 Ways of DevOps
With the main goal of improving the flow from "Dev" to "Ops", the first way focuses on bringing
changes to production quickly and without sacrificing quality by shipping defect artifacts. Besides
increasing the MTTF, mastering this first way is also crucial for a speedy resolution of incidents in
production, hence reducing the MTTR.
As the next step to further improve reliability, the second way of DevOps comes into picture. The
primary target is to close the "loop" by leveraging feedback from operations and incorporating it
into the development. Observing how a distributed system behaves in critical situations under real
load and the learnings that can be concluded from incidents are key sources highlighting areas in
which the reliability can be improved.
As the final step, the third way of DevOps emphasizes that any effort to advance reliability is not a
one-time activity but requires continuous efforts. Hence, this is about establishing continuous
rituals in the daily work to ensure that reliability does not deteriorate slowly and unnoticed. For
example, taking every incident seriously by analyzing it thoroughly and not just "when there is
time" or constantly optimizing alerts with high noise-to-signal ratio. Another best practice is to
implement periodic training exercises for reliability-related scenarios.
Site Reliability Hierarchy
Based on the high-level principles from the "3 ways of DevOps", the Site Reliability Hierarchy
evolved by Google Site Reliability Engineers (SRE) and offers a more concrete approach to improve
reliability. Google realized that successfully operating a service entails a range of activities that can
be put into a hierarchy, where methods at the bottom are the most basic requirements and higher
levels aim at taking active control over the system’s reliability rather than reactively responding to
incidents [beyer2016site].
Thus, this hierarchy can be used as a blueprint to assess the maturity of a system with respect to
reliability as well as to derive a step-by-step approach on how to address shortcomings.
The section below summarizes the Site Reliability Hierarchy.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 280
Product
Development
Capacity Planning
Testing and Release Procedures
Postmortem & Root Cause Analysis
Incident Management
Monitoring
Figure 63. Site Reliability Hierarchy
Monitoring
The most basic requirement to successfully operate a service is monitoring. To begin with, a broad,
minimally-invasive instrumentation of the software and its environment is required for detecting
problems as well as driving any improvement based on real-world data. This typically includes data
that is emitted by the software applications like logs, metrics, and traces. For more details, see
Monitoring.
Correlate and integrate monitoring data
Being able to quickly identify related logs, metrics, and traces that are caused by the same request
speeds up troubleshooting. Therefore, monitoring data that is provided by the software application
should be properly labeled:
• static labels to indicate which (business) service, component, class or method produces the data
• unique ID for every single request (only logs and traces)
Besides the application-level monitoring data, it is also advisable to integrate "events" that were
caused by or changed the infrastructure into a single "monitoring hub". The reasoning here is that,
typically, most unplanned outages are caused by ill-planned changes, and a significant part of the
MTTR is used to determine what was changed [behr2005visible]. So, tracking "events" like
deployments, configuration changes or any batch runs, and displaying them alongside the other
application, middleware, and infrastructure monitoring data can help to accelerate the incident
resolution.
Define and monitor SLI/SLO
Managing a service reliably without knowing which behavior is required or how to measure this
behavior is unfeasible [beyer2016site]. Thus, its level of service needs to be defined, starting from
the business perspective. Whereas service level indicators (SLI) are a quantitative measure of a
specific level of service provided by the application, service level objectives (SLO) define the
threshold for a service level measured by an SLI [beyer2016site].
As an example, for a web application request latency and error rate could be meaningful SLIs,
whereas SLOs might be average request latency of 50ms, 95th percentile of all requests are
answered within 75ms, and sliding window average error rate of maximum 1%.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 281
Leveraging SLIs and SLOs provide several key benefits:
• expectations are set to users on how the service will perform
• meaningful and business-focused alerts can be easily derived since they are defined from a
business perspective
• lays the foundation for error-budgets to navigate the balance between "feature velocity" and
"service reliability"
• helps to avoid unnecessary complexity, which might be added to achieve unclear reliability
targets that are beyond the requirements from a business perspective
Incident response
An effective incident response based on a clearly defined framework is paramount to reduce the
disruptions caused by an incident and restore normal operations as soon as possible, i.e. reduce
MTTR. In general, the response to an incident can be divided in three phases: Detection, Resolution
and Follow-up.
Detection
For any incident response to start, the on-call team needs to be proactively notified, otherwise it
would be impossible to react before the end users suffer. This requires a proper monitoring
solution with business-focused and actionable alerts notifying the on-call team. Once a notification
is received the on-call team quickly assesses the situation, triages the impact, and decides whether
the current conditions qualify as an incident. There are two main reasons behind this explicit
decision:
• alerts might be just informative and do not require immediate attention, but some action in the
near future (e.g. certificate expires in the next 14 days)
• alerts might be false positives or "unmeaningful" and thus every occurrence should be tracked
so they can be eventually removed in case the signal-to-noise ratio over time is too low
This decision implies that are agreed-upon criteria established that can be used to judge whether a
situation qualifies as an incident. For example [beyer2016site]:
• Do you need to involve a second team in fixing the problem?
• Is the outage visible to customers?
• Is the issue unsolved even after an hour’s concentrated analysis?
Resolution
Key components
To successfully resolve an incident, the following key components must be established:
Psychological safety
The product and resolution teams needs to feel "safe" so that they can fully focus on the incident
without fear of retaliation or overburdening. Psychological safety is based on several pillars:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 282
• agreed-upon and well-established incident management framework providing guidelines on
how to steer the whole incident
• case-specific and detailed instruction on how to analyze common problems and resolve
standard procedures, ideally accelerated by automation
• continuous training under realistic circumstances to exercise and increase trust in the
incident procedures and tools
• blameless culture so that everybody focuses on identifying contributing factors without
blaming any individual or team. This is rooted on the basic assumption that everybody has
generally good intentions and wants to do the job as well as they can. Consequently, people
cannot be "fixed" but the systems, tools, and processes they are given should be subject to
improvement [beyer2016site],[dekker2007just].
Quick internal information flow
While working on an incident, new insights are gathered continuously by different team
members and actions must be coordinated. Thus, a shared communication channel should be
established connecting all stakeholders. This enables a fast and reliable flow of information and
serves as rudimentary documentation. In the course of an incident, further people can be pulled
into the channel and quickly get up to speed by reading the message log.
Professional external communication
The shared communication channel typically does not contain the end-users that are actually
affected, higher management, other departments like legal or authorities. Thus, it is important
for the incident resolution team to provide periodic status updates targeted for the audience.
This can prevent constant queries from impatient interested parties, which would disturb the
team’s concentration.
Clear roles
The resolution team needs to take care of several responsibilities besides actually stopping the
bleeding. Establishing clear roles with pre-defined tasks enables effective and efficient incident
response. A resolution team is typically comprised of three roles [beyer2016site]:
• Incident commander: leads the group through the incident by condensing all insights into a
high-level state of the incident, assigning responsibilities, and removing obstacles
• Operational work: this group of people actually analyzes the situation and exclusively makes
changes to the systems
• Communication: this role takes care of properly communicating the current state of the
incident to the outside world
Incident report
The incident report is the main "vehicle" to structure and advance through the incident. During
the resolution phase, the group documents new insights and all actions taken to analyze the
situation or modify the system. For every insight or action taken, the team should note the actual
description, the timestamp, and persist relevant details like log file excerpts, screenshots,
command outputs, ticket IDs, etc. By keeping a structured and complete log of the incident’s
timeline, the report:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 283
• enables distribution of information within and outside of the resolution group in a readable
format (in contrast to the shared communication channel, which just contains unstructured
messages)
• facilitates handovers
• preserves the knowledge for later follow-up analysis
Example incident report
Runbooks
A runbook, also called standard operations guide, is a detailed guide containing precise
instructions to analyze or resolve a commonly repeated task. Having proven runbooks in place
for many common scenarios helps the on-call team to resolve incidents faster and more safely.
To that end, any alert that is configured should contain a link to the alert-specific runbook in
addition to a reference to the general incident response framework. The collection of runbooks
represents the operational knowledge for a system and thus should be reviewed continuously.
Especially the list of error codes, failure message, exceptions, etc. allows the on-call team to
quickly find runbooks that potentially help them to resolve an incident with unknown cause.
A runbook is typically comprised of:
• architecture and service overview
• goal of the runbook and list of situations, errors, exceptions for which this runbook is applicable
• possible side effects
• actionable list of steps to be executed precisely documented with caveats
Bootstrapping
Once the situation is declared as an incident, the resolution phase is set into motion with the
bootstrapping steps, such as:
• assign the alert in the notification system to a person
• assign roles to persons
• create a shared communication channel and invite all relevant stakeholders
• create an incident report based on the template
• add the initial findings and actions taken to assess the situation to the report
• post the link to the incident report in the communication channel
• deduplicate alerts and different triggers (mails, tickets from end users etc.) that are assumed to
be caused by the same issue
By automating these steps the MTTR can be reduced.
Stopping the bleeding
With the basic incident response harness in place the resolution team can start working
[beyer2016site]:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 284
Prioritize
Stopping the bleeding should have the highest priority, besides trying to preserve evidence of the
contributing factors
Share
Distribute findings, suspicions, and planned next steps among the incident team
Document
Roughly note down in the incident report the findings (including screenshots, logs, exceptions)
and actions taken
Communicate
Inform all stakeholders promptly and in a manner appropriate to the target audience
Consider alternatives
Remind each other periodically to consider alternative paths of action and challenge whether
the path leads in the right direction
Handover
In case new personnel joins the resolution team ensure the current state is properly handed
over
Introspect & escalate
If a team member starts to feel panicky or overwhelmed solicit more support via incident
commander
Follow-up
Upon stopping the bleeding, restoring all affected services and removing "workarounds", the
resolution team should enhance and polish the incident report. The further steps are described in
the next chapter.
Postmortem & Root cause analysis
Whereas the incident response is primarily about reducing MTTR by trying to "stop the bleeding" as
soon as possible, this layer focuses on understanding what went wrong in the system so it can be
rectified in a sustainable manner. If done properly, this will result in improved MTTF when failures
can be avoided in the first place or at least their impact can be mitigated. In short, the main idea is
to leverage operations feedback from production to improve the reliability of the system. This idea
can be applied on incident-level and aggregated across all incidents.
Postmortem
Identify contributing factors
The postmortem concept can be applied on the incident level. A postmortem is based on the
incident report and amends the contributing factors that caused the incidents as well as the actions
that will be taken to prevent the incident from reoccurring. It is the responsibility of the incident
resolution team to identify the contributing factors and, together with all relevant stakeholders,
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 285
define follow-up actions. A prerequisite for a structured postmortem meeting is a well-prepared
incident report so that the timeline of the incident including observations and actions taken can be
reconstructed for everybody. This is why the resolution team, governed by incident commander,
should polish the incident report and bring it in a presentable format. After reviewing the incidents
timeline, the postmortem group identifies contributing factors. During this process, it is important
to cover a wide area of possible factors:
• start with the obvious technical failures or bugs in the system
• also cover the software development process, from requirements engineering to testing to
deployment
• do not forget the incident response actions themselves (e.g. was the issue detected by the
alerting? Was there a helpful runbook available for the team?)
Avoid blame and hindsight bias
The most critical rule in the postmortem process but especially in this phase is to avoid blame. Any
accusations on personal level, finger-pointing or naming of individuals must be interrupted by the
moderator immediately.
Also, the moderator needs to reduce the hindsight bias effect. The hindsight bias affects people’s
memory of past events because, after knowing the outcome, the consequences of actions seem to be
more predictable [allspaw2012]. As a result, individuals might get blamed for "not seeing that an
action will have these consequences" before executing it. Based on this distorted perception of the
incident timeline, it becomes likely for postmortem group to name "personal failure" as most
relevant contributing factor. To avoid blame and hindsight bias [pagerduty][allspaw2014]:
• try to understand how it made sense for someone to do what they did
• do not ask directly who or why someone took an action
• consider the perspective from every involved individual, not just the manager’s view
• do not name individuals directly
Derive action items
In the next step the postmortem group iterates over each contributing factor and derives action
items to remedy the issue. When documenting the action items in the incident report it is important
to:
• describe the factor properly
• specify a concrete, actionable, and realistic action
• document whether this issues attempts to prevent the factor or mitigate the impact
• assign an owner responsible for executing the action
• create a ticket with realistic priority in the product backlog
The following action items should be part of the template so that they are considered on every
incident:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 286
• Create or improve an existing alert so that team is notified about a prospective critical situation
• Create or enhance an existing runbook to reduce the MTTR on the next occurrence
• Add automated tests or rework existing ones to prevent the issue from reoccurring
Add lessons learned
In the final section of the report the postmortem group should focus on two more questions:
First, the team should identify the aspects that went well throughout the incident response process.
By looking for positive things, the mood of the postmortem groups should be elevated because
solely focusing on the deficiencies can be depressing.
The final question aims to raise the awareness in the team that the incident might have resulted in
worse consequences if there was no luck involved. This is why the team should answer the
question: Where did we get lucky? Thinking honestly about the question can also help to identify
further action items.
Review, publish and promote
Once the final incident report is prepared, it should be reviewed by peers and relevant
stakeholders. Once the review is completed, the report should be made available company-internal
and presented to interested or affected teams so that everybody can learn from it.
As a final step, the postmortem can also be shared with the customer and end users. Publishing the
postmortem is a critical step since it can build trust from the customer by openly disclosing the
lessons learned.
A challenge is to motivate the teams to prepare high-quality postmortems. Initiatives can be put
into practice that praise the authors by awarding them and increasing their visibility, e.g. giving an
award for the postmortem-of-the-month. Additionally, postmortems can also be used as a rich
catalog of scenarios for training exercises and to onboard new people by showing them what could
go wrong in the system.
Failure mode analysis
Having a detailed postmortem analysis of each incident at hand facilitates the failure mode analysis
(FMA) of a system. The failure mode analysis is a continuous process wherein all (recent) incidents
and near misses are analyzed in order to identify areas in the system that deplete reliability.
Grouping the incidents by symptoms can be the first step to generate basic insights. But the
challenge for an effective FMA is to categorize incidents by their contributing factors. This requires
a standardized but system-specific catalog of contributing factors that evolves over time. Assigning
items from the catalog to a specific incident can be added as task during the postmortem process.
Once the categorization by contributing factors is established the team can try to identify patterns
and define remediation actions.
Testing and release procedures
The bottom three layers of the Site Reliability Hierarchy leverage feedback from the production
workload to derive changes for better reliability. The logical next step is to implement those
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 287
changes amongst the functional requirements. But the first way of DevOps mandates that these
changes should be safeguarded by a testing strategy targeting reliability aspects and by release
procedures that ensure a safe rollout to production.
Production Readiness Review
With a Production Readiness Review (PRR) the team can evolve a mandatory checklist to be
followed before major changes [beyer2016site]. The checklist ensures especially the quality of the
change with respect to the non-functional requirements by mandating answers to questions like:
• Who is the service owner?
• What are the SLIs and SLOs?
• What is the high-level architecture including components and their interaction?
• Which APIs are exposed?
• What are the dependencies to external systems?
• Where is the service hosted?
• What are driving factors for the capacity planning?
• What is the critical path or core of the application?
• Which disaster scenarios are expected?
• What does the disaster recovery plan for each scenario look like?
• Has the disaster recovery plan been tested?
• What does the process look like to bring a change to production?
• What are relevant metrics besides SLIs/SLOs?
By answering the questions in written form, the overall documentation of the service is reviewed.
Reliability Testing
There are two major kinds of tests than can be executed automatically targeting the reliability of a
system.
The first kind examines the system’s behavior "under load" in form of load and stress tests. When
designing the test suites, different load patterns should be considered such as steady load increase
and especially sudden load spikes.
Secondly, reliability tests should cover all sorts of abnormal conditions which the system might face
in the production environment. Therefore, various aspects can be manipulated on the different
levels of the application stack to simulate a reliability scenario. For example,
Network layer:
• disable network connection completely
• slow network by adding latency
• limited bandwidth
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 288
• stop all data from getting through and close connection after timeout
• slice TCP data into small bits
• peer resets connection
Provider layer:
• disk out of space
• crash computing instances (e.g. VMs or pods in Kubernetes)
Application layer:
• concurrent transactions
• provoke special timing conditions (e.g. reorder messages by delaying one)
GameDay
GameDay is hands-on learning experience for the whole team in a safe environment to increase the
reliability of the system itself and the processes surrounding it. The main purpose surrounds three
core principles:
• Incidents are inevitable. Thus, proactively prepare the system and the people operating it.
• All reliability mechanisms (including disaster recovery and incident processes) must be
assumed broken until proven to be effective. Continuously.
• There is no shortcut to experience. Excellence requires practice.
In general, a single GameDay scenario targets a specific reliability aspect of a system. Therefore,
faults targeting that aspect are actively injected and the system reacts to them in its natural way.
Styles
There are different styles of orchestrating a GameDay as learning experience:
In the classic GameDay style, an expert injects faults into the system without the team knowing
details and the team handles the consequences as a regular incident. This style simulates an
incident as close as possible but also requires an expert preparing and safeguarding the scenario.
Consequently, in addition to the technical aspects of the system’s reaction to failure, a classic
GameDay focuses also on the incident process executed by the team.
Another form of a GameDay can be "collaborative disaster recovery" procedures. In such a
scenario, faults are injected to trigger a pre-defined disaster and the application recovery plans
executed by the team. An example scenario could be to restore a database from a backup and
replaying all missed messages
In the third style, the team collaboratively induces situations to observe together how the system
behaves exactly. This is suitable to start with GameDay in general or if there is no expert who could
supervise a classic GameDay.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 289
Goals
For the GameDay to have an effect on the system and the team, it is paramount to schedule such
scenarios periodically.
On the short term, conducting GameDays can help the team to establish and improve the incident
processes and train debugging in more realistic situations. A broad spectrum of scenarios can also
ensure that every team member has all required tools, settings, accounts, permissions, etc. to
effectively debug the system.
The medium-term goals of continuous GameDays mainly target proactive knowledge gaining and
sharing. During the GameDay and in the postmortem afterwards, the team will discuss intensively
about the system, from its high-level architecture to low-level implementation details. Also, if the
scenarios involve the different technical areas, GameDay can also improve the knowledge flow
across technical domains.
When GameDays are well established, they can also affect the team itself positively. First, the team
