can grow stronger together because they have ample interaction, learn how the teammates react
under stress, and especially have more "war stories" in common. Secondly, by practicing the
incident response processes, and thereby demonstrating their effectiveness, the team can build up
the psychological safety based on the feeling of being well "prepared" for real world scenarios.
Capacity planning
Always having enough "capacity" (e.g. compute, storage, network) is key to running a service
reliably but consequently overprovisioning can be costly. Thus, the SLI/SLO combined with the
expected quantity structure from the product owner and the signals from the productive workload
collected by the monitoring solution should be used to balance capacity properly. The feedback
from load and stress tests can be included as well.
Development
The development layer uses the previous stages as preparation to make significant changes by
designing the system with reliability as first-class citizen in mind. There is a plethora of patterns
available, ranging from macro architecture paradigms to micro architecture patterns.
Product
The final stage of the Site Reliability Hierarch emphasizes that a system’s reliability is also a
business concern. The requirements for reliability should be defined together with the product
owner and business department to achieve an optimal balance between costs and benefits.
Error budgets
Typically, there is a conflict between the feature-focused demands from business departments and
the desire for stability and a small velocity in operations. The conflict might be further fueled by an
information asymmetry between the two parties: The business together with development teams
usually have more insights in the efforts required to develop features whereas the operations team
typically avoids sharing the service’s reliability. This conflict can be addressed by implementing
error budgets [beyer2016site].
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Reliability | 290
The error budget offers an objective metric that reflects how unreliable the system is allowed to be.
The budget is directly derived from the SLI/SLO metrics compared to the actual values measured by
the monitoring. As long as there is enough error budget left, the team can release new features, but
when the limit is exhausted the focus instantly shifts to tasks aiming to improve reliability. Thus,
error budgets act as a control loop to steer the feature velocity [beyer2016site].
With the help of error budgets, the learnings that have been derived from the operations feedback
can be prioritized properly by the business department and product teams jointly. Because in the
end there is little value in merely filling the backlog with ideas to improve reliability without
actively releasing them into practice.

Historization
Overview
The ability to track changes and recover old versions of the data is essential for many business
information systems. This subchapter introduces some tools and approaches that can be used to
simplify this process.
The subchapter starts with a sketch of two basic historization approaches: auditable entities and
soft deletion. It then discusses the concepts of entity auditing and database-level data historization.
Finally, event sourcing is introduced as a potential alternative to the more data-centric approaches.
Less is More
Historization considerably boosts the complexity of the application and may, in some
circumstances, have non-negligible performance and maintenance implications. That is why we
strongly advise choosing the simplest approach that fits your business use case. When deciding on
the right approach, you can take the following general aspects into account:
Legal requirements
Legal requirements can extend as well as restrict the extent of historization.
Policies
What are the company’s security, data protection and backup policies?
Unified concept
All business processes or systems that use the same historized data should use the same
historization concept. Otherwise a transformation has to be explicitly specified.
User interface
Requirements concerning historization should keep the user interface in mind, not only the
abstract business logic.
Retrospective changes
Changing the past (e.g. recalculating past retirement benefits) is a complex and error prone-task;
we encourage you to avoid it when possible.
Auditable Entities and Soft Deletion
For some applications, it is sufficient to provide information only about the last state of an entity
instead of a complete protocol of all changes. This requirement can easily be fulfilled using the
concept of auditable entities. An auditable entity is a normal entity extended by some metadata
such as creation date, last modification date, related users, etc. This information is automatically
generated and maintained (e.g. using entity listeners and callback methods).
This approach reaches its limits the moment that we want to physically delete an entity from the
database. Once the entity is physically deleted, one cannot access its last state. A possible
workaround is to mark the data as deleted, instead of permanently deleting it. A common way to
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Historization | 292
implement this pattern, also known as soft deletion, is to introduce an additional database column
of type timestamp or boolean that stores the information on whether the entity has been deleted.
Most of the modern object-relational mappers (e.g. Hibernate, Django ORM, Entity Framework
Core) support the pattern in one way or another.
Entity Auditing
If you need a complete protocol of all changes, we recommend an approach called entity auditing.
Entity auditing is a way of accessing previous versions of entities and reconstructing object
networks that existed at a specific point in time. Depending on the technical and business
requirements, there are different ways of implementing entity auditing. Here we discuss two
common patterns that can be used to implement this kind of historization.
Table-based Entity Auditing
The basic idea of table-based entity auditing is simple: instead of changing entities, new versions
are created. The naive approach - to save historical and current entities in one table - is not
recommended, because it slows the access to current data. Furthermore, the option is given away to
historize only a subset of entity attributes. Thus, at least two separate tables for current and
historical data make sense. Because we also have to keep track of transactions, table-based entity
auditing uses three types of tables:
• An entity table stores the current data of an entity type.
• An audit table stores all historical data including a copy of the data of the current entities.
• The revision table stores transaction numbers and transaction times. This information is used to
reconstruct object networks.
The table-based entity auditing can be optimized either for write or read performance. Both
strategies differ only in the representation of the revision number interval in the revision table. We
suggest optimizing for read performance only if you have to significantly improve the retrieval
time of the historized entities. Furthermore, the number of write operations should be
proportionally negligible compared to the number of read operations. A good explanation of this
trade-off can be found in Chapter 4.15.5 of Capgemini Architecture Guidelines for Application
Design v2.0. For a practical example, refer to this post.
The table-based entity auditing is completely supported by the Hibernate core module Envers
(available for Java and .Net). The design pattern is not restricted to Hibernate and can be used with
other object-relational mappers. However, in this case, additional development effort could be
necessary.
As the name suggests, table-based entity auditing can be used only with traditional relational
databases. Please be aware that structural database changes should also be implemented for the
audit tables (e.g. data migrations).
Object-based Entity Auditing
If you don’t use a relational database or want to decouple the audit data from your transactional
data, we recommend an approach we call object-based entity auditing. Object-based entity auditing
is all about storing serialized snapshots of the entity (e.g. its JSON representation) linked with
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Historization | 293
auditing metadata such as version number and list of changed properties. The snapshots can be
stored, retrieved, and analyzed independently from the transactional data. Often, a dedicated (non-
relational) database is used for that.
The pattern is relatively simple and you can implement it from scratch. Nevertheless, it is a good
idea to use an established framework such as JaVers.
Event Sourcing
The approaches that have been presented here so far have one thing in common: they all consider
historization as a cross-cutting concern and not as an integral part of the architecture. Event
Sourcing is different. It defines a persistence strategy that bases on historization.
Event Sourcing models the application state as a stream of immutable events stored in an append-
only manner. Each event represents a change to the application state. In theory, the application
state can be recalculated to any given point in time using these events. In practice, this is not a very
efficient way of handling large streams of data. Most of the applications that implement Event
Sourcing do not recalculate the application state from the very beginning but create snapshots and
replay only the events that occurred after the creation of the snapshot.
A more detailed description of Event Sourcing would go beyond the scope of this subchapter. It is
good to know that if you use Event Sourcing in your project, you can probably get some kind of
historization almost for free.
Complex Historization
Our discussion so far has only focused on the point in time at which an object was created/changed
in the database or deleted from it. This timestamp is also known as transaction time. It lies by
definition always in the past and allows us to answer questions such as "What was the price of 64
GB RAM on 08.05.2021?". Yet, this approach is not sufficient for implementing business
requirements that explicitly model factual validity of business facts independently from their
technical representation. For that, we need to introduce two new concepts: valid time and bi-
temporal data.
Valid Time
Valid time (and respectively valid interval) is the point in time from which onwards a business fact
is true, e.g. a discount is only valid for a specific period of time. It can lie in the past, present, or
future. Valid time is defined explicitly based on business decisions and not implicitly by technical
transactions.
The valid time interval can be modelled
• using an explicit interval [validSince, validUntil]
• or implicitly by validity start date.
The explicit interval has a better read performance. If you have a write-heavy use cases, an implicit
interval can be a better choice. Since most business applications that implement valid time perform
read accesses much more frequently than write accesses, we recommend using the former option.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Historization | 294
Bi-temporal data
By using (one-dimensional) historization by valid time, we can maintain and query data for
arbitrary time intervals in the past or future. However, we can only see the current state of the
database and cannot answer questions such as: "What was told to a customer a year ago (on
11.09.2021), when she asked for the price of 64 GB RAM on 08.05.2022?".
Answering this question requires a two-dimensional historization. It combines (one-dimensional)
historization by transaction time and valid time. Since the data is examined along two different
timelines, it is called bi-temporal. The approaches and tools introduced so far are still applicable.
Valid/transaction time diagrams are a handy tool for visualizing bi-temporal data. For example, we
can easily answer the question raised at the beginning.
Transaction Time
01.09.2022
110.30 EUR
01.12.2021
94.32 EUR
105.95 EUR
01.01.2021
01.10.2021 01.04.2022 01.01.2025 Valid Time
The price told to a customer on 11.09.2021, when she asked for 64 GB of RAM on 08.05.2022 was
105.95 EUR.
Temporal Databases
In recent times, database vendors have drawn attention to historization. Databases with out-of-the-
box support for handling historical data (temporal databases) have even become part of the SQL
standard.
SQL:2011 specifies two features related to temporal data: system-versioned tables (historization by
transaction time) and application-time period tables (historization by valid time). Currently, most
Database Management Systems implement the standard in some way or another. Since the
temporal databases are still in their infancy, we recommend using them with caution.
Further Readings and References
• Capgemini Architecture Guidelines for Application Design v2.0, 4.15 Historization
• Auditing with JPA, Hibernate, and Spring Data JPA
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Historization | 295
• Soft Deletion Probably Isn’t worth It
• The best way to implement an audit log using Hibernate Envers
• JaVers vs. Envers Comparison
• Event Sourcing
• Temporal Database
Data Modeling
Shifted from Architecture Guidelines for Application Design
 This article was shifted from the old architecture guide without further rework.
The content and style might need some rework, but still reflects helpful and in
general correct knowledge.
The data model is the set of all the entity types in an application or subsystem together with their
relations (generalization/specialization and associations incl. compositions).
Overview
Data models should adhere to the following design rules:
• Correctness: The data model must map the application’s business logic correctly
• Simplicity: "Everything should be made as simple as possible, but no simpler" (Albert Einstein)
• Zero redundancy: Redundancies should be avoided both at type and instance level
• Extensibility: Changes to the business logic should cause the smallest possible number of
adaptations in the data model.
In this article, we describe data modeling rules which pursue these aims.
Avoid inheritance

Use inheritance (generalization/specialization) sparingly.
In concrete terms, this means:
• Only use inheritance in genuine “is a" relations
• Only use single inheritance
• Avoid excessive nesting of inheritance trees
• Wherever meaningfully possible, replace inheritance with an association
Reason: The sparing use of inheritance promotes simplicity, extensibility and freedom from
redundancy.
• Reason 1: "is a" relation: Implementation inheritance model since the modeling relates to the
logical level only.
• Reason 2: Single inheritance: Multiple inheritance increases complexity and dependencies
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Data Modeling | 296
• Reason 3: Nesting depth: Excessively deep nesting trees are usually complex
• Reason 4: Association: The following "is a" relations can sensibly be replaced by associations
("has" relations):
◦ A red-head is a person
◦ A woman is a person
◦ A customer is a person
In connection with single inheritance in particular, it is very important only to use the most
prominent "is a" relation for the inheritance hierarchy. “You only have one shot at single
inheritance – and it has to hit" (Johannes Siedersleben).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Data Modeling | 297
Example 17. Use Inheritance
Data for a personnel management should be modeled.
The following requirements apply:
• The company has customers, suppliers, and employees.
• All of these are identified by their name and address.
• An employee is either a worker or a manager
• Workers can be promoted to become managers
• Customers may also be suppliers
• In the future, other persons such as partners etc. or other career stages such as engineer
etc. may become relevant
First possible solution: with inheritance
The two figures below illustrate how the requirements are implemented through the extensive
use of generalization/specialization. The UML class diagram illustrates the data model (type
level). The UML object diagram illustrates an example (instance level).
Data Model Object Model
Person
- name: String
- adress: String
Smith:Customer Huber:Supplier
Extends Extends Extends
Customer Supplier Employee
Extends Extends Singh:Manager Smith:Worker
Worker Manager
Figure 64. Example of a data model using inheritance
The modeling is correct but has disadvantages. If a customer is simultaneously a worker
(Smith” in the example), then it is necessary to create two objects: Customer and Worker. In
both, the name and address are stored redundantly. If an employee is promoted from worker
to manager (“Singh” in the example), then a new Manager type object must be created, all the
data copied from the Worker object and the Worker object deleted. This is time-consuming.
The subsequent addition of new roles such as Partner or new career stages such as Engineer
requires the addition of new entity types in the data model.
Better solution: using association (or composition)
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Data Modeling | 298
Data Model Object Model
Customer:Role
Person Smith:Person
- name: String
Worker:Role
*
Role Singh:Person Manager:Role
-typ: enum{Customer, Supplier, Employee:Worker, Employee:Manager}
Huber:Person Supplier:Role
Figure 65. Example of a data model using composition
In this solution, we introduce an entity role which is described by means of an Enum type with
the values “Customer”, “Supplier”, “Employee:Worker” and “Employee:Manager”. This data
modeling solves all the problems of modeling with inheritance (generalization/specialization).
If a customer is simultaneously a worker (Smith"" in the example), then the name and address
are stored only in the Person object. The dual function is expressed by two Role objects:
Customer and Worker.
The promotion of the employee "Singh" from Worker to Manager is achieved by simply
changing the “type” attribute in the Role object from “Employee:Worker” to
“Employee:Manager”.
The introduction of further roles such as Partner or Employee:Engineer is achieved by simply
extending the Enum type.
Artificial Example
This example is artificial, as it assumes one data model for different aspects (e.g.

workforce management, supplier management, customer management etc.). In
modern architectures, these objects might of course belong to different building
blocks and therefore different data models.
Normalize data
Normalization is a concept for the avoidance of redundancies taken from database theory. The
theory distinguishes between different normal forms: 1st Normal Form (1NF), …, 5th Normal Form
(5NF), Boyce-Codd Normal Form (BCNF), etc.
Rule: Normalize the database as far as this makes sense in terms of the logic.
The third Normal Form (3NF) is usually sufficient. A formal analysis is not necessary – intuitive
normalization is sufficient. Reason: Normalization reduces redundancies at instance level.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Data Modeling | 299
Example 18. Normalization
The task is to create a data model for an MP3 archive system.
The following requirements apply:
• Songs are to be stored in the MP3 archive system.
• For each song, it is necessary to store the title, the singer and the name of the album
First possible solution: denormalized
The following UML class model illustrates a simple data model for the MP3 archive. The UML
object model illustrates an example at instance level.
Data Model Object Model
Don't worry about it:Song
Song
artist=Pharell Williams
- artist: String album=Fly or Die
- album: String
- title: String
Grace Kelly:Song Jump:Song
artist=Mika artist=Pharell Williams
album=Life in cartoon motion album=Fly or Die
The data modeling is extremely simple and possesses only one entity: Song. However,
redundancies occur at instance level. In the case of an album by a singer (in the example, “Fly
or Die” by “Pharell Williams”) which contains multiple titles, then the Singer and Album have
to be stored redundantly for each Song object (e.g. “Jump”). Changes to album-specific
attributes have to be incorporated in all of the Song objects.
Better solution: normalized
The following data model is normalized.
Data Model Object Model

Figure 66. Example of a normalized data model
Although the normalized data model is more complex, it solves the problems of redundancy.
Most importantly, the singer data can be maintained independently of the album data and
song data. The (logical) memory requirement is also reduced.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Data Modeling | 300
Avoid redundant cyclical associations
Associations between two or more entities may form cycles. Cyclical associations should not be
confused with cyclical dependencies at component level.
Avoid redundant cycles in the associations between entities. If possible in terms of

the logic, remove redundant cycles by omitting redundant associations. If cycles
are unavoidable for logical reasons, use constraints to exclude inconsistencies.
UML constraints are conditions which can be defined for UML elements such as associations.
Reason
Cyclical associations may indicate a redundancy at type level – i.e. a lack of normalization.
Cycles may lead to inconsistencies at instance level and therefore jeopardize the requirement for
correctness.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Data Modeling | 301
Example 19. Avoid redundant cyclical associations
The task is to create a data model for a simple order management system.
The following requirements apply:
• Customers may order individual products as order positions
• Multiple order positions form an order
• An order always refers to one customer
First possible solution: cyclical associations
The direct modeling of the three requirements results in the following data model:
Data Model Object Model
Smith:Customer Huber:Customer
Customer
1
1
orders orders orders
orders
* 1 *
*
Order OrderPosition
December:Order Book:OrderPosition
Figure 67. Example with cyclical association
Initially, the data model appears to be correctly modeled at the logical level. However, it
permits inconsistencies as can be seen at instance level in the example. The Customer "Smith"
places an Order in December which contains an OrderPosition for a book. However, the data
model does not prohibit an association “orders” with another Customer “Huber” – and this is
clearly incorrect. It would be necessary to add a constraint specifying that the Customer object.
This customer object would be referenced via the “orders” association between Customer and
OrderPosition is identical to the one that is referenced via the “orders” association between
Customer and Orders – only then would the data model be logically correct. The problem
results from a redundancy – a lack of normalization. The “orders” association between
Customer and OrderPosition is redundant with the composition and the “orders” association
between Customer and Order.
Better solution: a-cyclical associations
In the following data model, the cyclical association is resolved.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Data Modeling | 302

orders
orders
* 1
*
Order OrderPosition
December:Order Book:OrderPosition
Figure 68. Example with acyclical association
The omission of the association “orders” between Customer and OrderPosition solves the
above problem. The model makes it impossible for the order position ordered by the customer
"Smith" to be incorrectly assigned to another customer Huber. The assignment of the order
position to the customer is still possible via the composition and the association “orders”
between Customer and Order.
Resolve attributed m:n relations
 Resolve attributed m:n relations into (multiple) 1:n relations. Find logically
appropriate terms for the resulting new entities
Reason
The resolution of attributed m:n relations is not a technical operation (at database level). The
newly created entities are independent and possess logical attributes and are thus entities in
accordance with the definition. Furthermore, dialogs are often required for their maintenance.
Frequently, the specialist unit does not as yet have any term for the new entity. It is, however,
valuable to work with the specialist unit to find an appropriate term which, for example, can
subsequently be used as the title of the dialog.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Data Modeling | 303
Example 20. Resolving attributed n:m relations
The task is to design a Customer Relation Management (CRM) system for a tour operator.
The following requirements apply:
• Customers can book tours.
• A travel order may comprise multiple travelers
• It should be possible to record complaints
• Customers can be informed of new products by means of campaigns
• It should be possible to display a customer’s contact history in the call center.
First possible solution: m:n relations
The following data model uses m:n relations.

At first glance, the data model is correct and simple. However, the customer contact history is

Additional rules
Although the following rules should really be self-evident, they are sometimes ignored.

Only model logical aspects, never business ones.
Example 21. Lists are business construct, not a logical data model one
The data model does not contain any “List” entity or even “ChainedList” entity.

Limit yourself to what is needed for the system.
Example 22. Only model needed information
If it is necessary to model hotel facilities (pool, sauna, tennis etc.) for a tour catalog then only
the categories are relevant. It is irrelevant here that tennis is a type of sport, whether the pool
is next to the sauna etc.
 Avoid 1:1 relations. Resolve 1:1 relations by merging the two entities if this makes
sense at the logical level.
Resolving 1:1 relations simplifies the data model.
Example 23. Resolve 1:1 relations
It makes sense to combine the two entities “Order” and “Order Header” to form a single entity
“Order”. However, 1:1 relations can be useful, for example, in order to decouple general from
component-specific attributes when modeling master data.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Data Modeling | 305
Notable Tools
The choice of tools heavily depends on the need in the project. If you need to model conceptually
for alignment with customer and development, a drawing tool like Visio, Draw.IO or whiteboard
might be sufficient. If you need to model for generation (using UML), for sure you need to go for a
real modeling tool.
• Enterprise Architect as broadly used modeling tool

Interface Design
Overview
In any kind of architecture, interfaces play a vital role in clearly defining the building blocks with
assigned functional responsibilities and to manage the dependencies between them. A building
block resulting from software decomposition may have different tasks and use different styles.
Examples for building blocks in a modular monolith can be business, technical or technical
infrastructure components, either on backend or frontend. Examples in a distributed system might
be microservices. All have in common that they should be loosely coupled to other building blocks,
so together they form the overall application/system. This coupling usually happens using some
kind of interface. Interfaces should follow the principle of information hiding (s. Architecture
Principles) by distinguishing between the external view (what information of the building block is
visible to the outside) and the internal view (what should not be visible to the outside). This
separation is not merely conceptual — it is also implemented in the program code. Interfaces form
the external view of building blocks. They are therefore of key significance for the architecture,
because they determine the effort needed to use them and dependencies arising from the usage.
Bad interface design leads to unneeded stronger coupling and - by that - may impact
maintainability, but also performance.
There is also a section more focused on the design of system interfaces.
Definitions and Basics of Interface Design
We define interfaces as follows:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 306
Definition: Interface
An interface implements an interface contract To do this, an interface combines operations. It
is specified by:
• A unique name
• The set of associated interface operations
• An interface protocol involving the sequences and restrictions for calling the operations
Note: the fewer restrictions there are concerning operation calls (interface protocol), the better!
We also define:
Definition: Interface Operation
An operation describes a functionality. It is specified by:
Signature
name of the operation, parameters and return values together with their types, exceptions
Semantics
functional properties in the external view, e.g. conditions before and after processing
Non-functional properties
e.g. performance, availability, security, batch capability, transactional behavior, locking
behavior etc.
The figure below provides an overview of the elements forming an interface and how these can be
specified.
Interfaces, operations and their specification
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 307
Exporting and Importing Interfaces
Providers and Consumers
Splitting a solution into building blocks follows the principle of the separation of concerns. In this
case, the functionality is distributed to different building blocks which use one another. Here, one
building block assumes the role of provider: it exports functionality. The other building block
assumes the role of consumer: it imports functionality.
The figure below illustrates this in the form of a UML component diagram.
Provider and consumer
Ci Ce
Consumer Provider
(importing building block) (exporting building block)
Definition: Exporting building block / provider
A building block which exports interfaces acts as the provider of these interfaces.
Definition: Importing building block / consumer
A building block which imports interfaces exported by another building block acts as the
consumer of these interfaces.
The dependency (as referred to in UML) always points from the consumer (importing building
block) to the provider (exporting building block), indicating that the consumer depends on the
provider.. Naturally, any building block can both export and import functionality - i.e. assume the
role of both provider and consumer vis-à-vis various other building blocks.
Provided and Required Interfaces
Usually, building blocks export interfaces for the functionality which they implement. Consumer
building blocks import and call these interfaces. The figure below illustrates this in the form of a
UML component diagram.
Exported and imported interfaces
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 308
Provided
(implemented by the exporter)
Normal case
Exported S Ce
(defined at the Ce: Provider
external view of C) (exporting building block)
normal interface
Imported Ci S
(defined outside, Ci: Consumer
but used by C) (importing building block)
normal interface usage
Definition: Provided interface
A provided interface is defined and implemented by an exporting building block (provider). It
can be imported and called by an importing building block (consumer).
In special cases, a building block may export an interface which it does not implement itself but
which it requires the importing building block to implement (required interface). The best-known
example is the callback interface as used in the Observer Pattern [Gam96]: the Observable exports
an interface Observer which must be imported and implemented by the Observer.
Definition: Required interface
A required interface is defined but not implemented by an exporting building block
(provider). The importing building block (consumer) must implement the required interface.
Required interfaces are mostly used to prevent cyclical dependency relations. The figure below
compares the special case of required interfaces with the normal case of provided interfaces.
Provided and required interfaces
Provided Required
(implemented by the exporter) (implemented by the importer)

As mentioned above, the dependency (in the UML terminology) always points from the importing to
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 309
the exporting building block, irrespective of whether the interface is provided or required.
Specific and Common Interfaces
The consumers of interfaces are of particular relevance when designing a system with building
blocks. This is because there is little point providing interfaces for which there are no consumers.
We distinguish between two types of provider/consumer relation.
Specific Interfaces
Specific interfaces are interfaces via which a (small) defined group of consumers use the
functionality supplied by a building block. In such cases, the consumers are known to the provider
at the time of interface definition and often have a significant influence on the way the interface is
defined since the interface operations and the employed data structures are negotiated individually
between the user and the supplier.
Specific interfaces
Building Block B1 Building Block B1 Building Block B1
specific interfaces A
for each building block
Building Block A
Common Interfaces
Common interfaces are interfaces via which an undefined and potentially large group of
consumers use the functionality supplied by a building block. The consumers are not always known
to the provider at the time of the interface definition and therefore have no significant influence on
the definition. A building block which provides this type of interface will tend to assume a general
requirements profile or a small number of variants of this requirements profile and the interface
will be designed accordingly.
Common interfaces
Building Block B1 Building Block B1 Component B1
common interface A
Building Block A
When to Use Specific and Common Interfaces
Due to the larger number of consumers, common interfaces have a higher export coupling level
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 310
than specific interfaces. If appropriate measures are not taken, this may make it difficult to modify
parts of the system. We therefore formulate the following rule:
Distinguish at an early stage between common and specific interfaces
When defining an interface, specify whether it is a common or specific interface.
Try to define common interfaces wherever possible. Restrict the use of any specific
 interfaces which are nevertheless necessary. If over time it becomes clear that a
specific interface is relevant for a wide consumer group, define an additional,
corresponding common interface. Decouple specific interfaces from common
interfaces,, i.e. do not use the transfer objects of a specific interface in a common
interface.
The standard interface is a particularly extreme form of common interface:
Definition: Standard interface
A standard interface is an interface that has been specified by a standardization committee
and can be publicly accessed.
Examples include JDBC, RMI, as well as java.util.List and java.util.Serializable.
UML Notation
UML uses a circular notation for interfaces (sometimes also known as lollypop notation because of
its appearance). The broken line indicates a dependency which can also be used between
classes/building blocks and interfaces. As of UML version 2, a semicircular notification (known as
socket notation) will also be introduced for required interfaces. The UML specification does not
prescribe which notification is to be used in what situations.
We define the following convention:
UML socket notification only for required interfaces
 We only use the UML socket notation for required interfaces (special case). In the
normal case, i.e. provided interfaces, we use the lollypop notation at the export
side and the dependency arrow from the importing building block to the interface.
UML notation for interfaces
provided
Ci S Ce
interface
S required
Ci Ce
interface
Note: The socket notation is not adequately supported in the current version of the UML Enterprise
Architect tool.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 311
Ownership of Interfaces
Who do interfaces belong to? The exporting interface, the importing interface or neither of them?
The answer to this question has both technical and organizational consequences. At the technical
level, it is necessary to identify the namespace (e.g. Java Package) in which the interface is defined.
If project teams are created on a building block-oriented basis then it is necessary to clarify which
team is responsible for the design and implementation of the interface.
Interfaces belong to the exporting building block (provider)
Interfaces are always assigned to the exporting building block (provider). This
applies to both provided and required interfaces. They are defined in the
namespace of the exporting interface. The team that is responsible for the

exporting building block is also responsible for the design of the interface. The
team may possibly have to adhere to the instructions provided by central
architecture management. The only exceptions here are the standard interfaces.
These are used both by the exporting building block (provider) and the importing
building block (consumer).
The figure below illustrates the ownership of interfaces by building blocks on the basis of UML
packages.
Ownership of interfaces
provided
Ci S Ce
interface
S required
Ci Ce
interface
Practical Examples
The figure below illustrates common practical examples of interfaces and their utilization.
Practical examples
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 312
Ci: Consumer Ce: Provider
(importing building block) (exporting building block)
e.g. CustomerManagement
(a) Ci S Ce
provided interface
e.g. EDIFACT, defined by
standard setting body
(b) Ci S Ce

e.g. Observer callback
S
(c) Ci Ce
required interface
e.g. SAP Adapter

provided interface
required interface
Example (a) is the conventional case: a building block Ce provides an interface I (e.g.
CustomerManagement) which it implements itself. Ci uses the functionality of I.
Example (b) differs from example (a) in that a standard interface I (e.g. Java API for EDIFACT) is
used. This has been specified by a standardization committee. The provider Ce imports I and
implements the interface. The consumer Ci also imports I and uses the functionality of the
interface. The employed standard may be valid internationally or simply apply within a single
enterprise.
Example (c) illustrates a callback interface I (e.g. Observer). The provider Ce defines I in its
namespace but does not implement the interface. The consumer Ci has to implement I in order to
use the functionality of Ce.
Example (d) presents a pattern for the further decoupling of provider and consumer: the Adapter
Pattern. In this case, Ci does not use the interface I directly. Instead, Ci itself defines a required
interface I’. I’ contains the precise functionality required by Ci, e.g. a subset of the operations made
available by I, possibly with different names and data structures. An adapter imports both I and I’
and implements the conversion from I’ to I.
Designing Interfaces
How should interfaces and their operations be designed? We provide design guidelines for
architects in the form of rules.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 313
Interface Design as a Contract
Interfaces should be considered the contract offered to the potential consumer. As such it should
define what it is delivering and not how, thus hiding the implementation. It should clearly define
what parameters are required incl. any restrictions on the value range. Likewise it must be
described what the consumer can expect of the returned value.
Interfaces Grouped by Utilization
Interfaces bundle a building block’s operations. What criteria should be used when combining
operations to form interfaces?
Group together a building block’s operations into interfaces on the basis of their

utilization:
e.g. by user groups.
The most important user groups to distinguish are operative and administrative users. We examine
this in detail in the next section.
Like all rules, this rule must also be applied intelligently. Naturally, in the case of a large number of
user groups with slightly different utilization scenarios, there is no point using a large number of
slightly differing interfaces. This leads to an excessive number of interfaces which are time-
intensive in terms of their creation and maintenance.
Operative and Administrative Utilization
Building blocks can be used for both operative and administrative purposes.
Operative utilization of a building block
Operative utilization corresponds to the primary purpose of a building block. The operative
utilization of a building block always occurs during its operation. The operative utilization of a
shop system, for example, might consist of calls to the operations involved in the sale of goods.
Administrative utilization of a building block
The administrative utilization of a building block permits its correct operative utilization. It is
therefore of a supporting nature. Administrative utilization frequently occurs during the
commissioning of a building block but sometimes also during its operation. The administrative
utilization of a shop system, for example, might consist of calls to the operations involved in the
maintenance of parameters such as VAT rates.
We distinguish between operative and administrative utilization both in terms of the functional
and the technical requirements. The examples above relate to functional requirements. An example
of technical administration would be the definition of technical parameters such as database URLs,
driver building blocks or timeout values.
Examples of technical operative utilization would be the starting and stopping of individual process
instances of a building block by Operation employees and information about the state of an
instance (number of active sessions, memory load etc.). Different user groups use building blocks in
accordance with the utilization types described above. The table below provides an overview of the
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 314
utilization types and the corresponding user groups.
Logical Technical
Operative Users Operation staff
Administrative Business administrators Technical administrators
Thanks to this subdivision of utilization types, we can now formulate the above rule “interfaces
grouped by utilization” in a more concrete way.
Subdivide interfaces on the basis of their operative/administrative and logical/technical
utilization

Design a building block’s operations so that they can be unambiguously assigned
to the operative/administrative and logical/technical utilization types. Group
operations together in interfaces on the basis of these utilization types.
The figure below illustrates interfaces in accordance with the four utilization types using the
example of a shop building block.
Operative and administrative interfaces
used by customer portal
e.g. placeOrder()
Sales
(operative logical)
used by logical administrator used by operations
e.g. setVAT() e.g. start(), stop()

(administrative logical) (operative technical)

e.g. setDBURL()

(administrative technical)
Commands and Queries
We distinguish between two types of operations in a building block: commands and queries
Command
A command is an operation which is able to modify the state of a building block, i.e. which has a
side effect. One example of a command in a customer management system is the operation
createCustomer. If the call is successful, it creates a new customer object and consequently
modifies the data stock - the state of the building block.
Query
A query is an operation which never modifies the state of a building block, i.e. which has no side
effect. An example query in a customer management system is findCustomerById. If the call is
successful, it returns a customer object but does not modify the data stock or, therefore, the state
of the building block.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 315
Separation of commands and queries

Categorize all the operations in a building block as commands or queries and
document these in the interface specification.
It is important for the users of an operation to know whether or not it has any side effects. Hidden
side effects, that is to say commands masquerading as queries, should be avoided. A
findCustomerById operation which creates a new customer object if none is found for the specified
ID has been poorly designed!
Value and Reference-oriented Interfaces
Reference Parameters and Value Parameters
We distinguish between two types of parameters in operations: reference parameters and value
parameters
Calls using reference parameters (Call-by-reference)
Reference parameters are references to objects belonging to the calling (in the case of input
parameters) or the called party (in the case of return values) in an operation. When an operation
is called with reference parameters (call-by-reference), the referenced objects can be modified. If
referenced input parameters are modified during the execution of the operation then this is
immediately reflected in the caller. If referenced return parameters are modified during the
execution of the operation then this is immediately reflected in the called party. We provide an
example below.
Calls using value parameters (Call-by-value)
Value parameters are non-modifiable objects or copies of objects belonging to the calling (in the
case of input parameters) or the called party (in the case of return values) in an operation. When
an operation is called with value parameters (call-by-value), the passed objects can never be
modified. For example, if the operation findCustomerById is called the ID value can never be
modified in the caller if it is passed as a value parameter.
The difference between reference and value parameters is logical in nature. From this point of
view, what is important is whether or not changes in values can be reflected in the caller (in the
case of reference parameters) or the called party (in the case of reference return parameters). We
are not concerned with the technical implementation via the programming language.
In Java, for example, all objects are passed as reference parameters. Despite this, in the Java
technology, reference parameters are often logical value parameters, for example because these are
explicitly copied or because their modification is excluded by the operation.
 Value objects of Tactical DDD are a good example for defining immutable
parameters for operations on interfaces.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 316
Call-by-reference may violate the principle of data sovereignty
By-reference calls on building block interfaces may violate the principle of a building block’s
data sovereignty. Consider the following example:
Example of reference parameters
Customer
Client S
Management
A building block CustomerManagement possesses a query findCustomerById with a customer
number (Id) as input parameter and a Customer object as return reference (not value!).
The client building block is now able to obtain a reference to a concrete customer object and
then modify this, e.g. by changing the customer name. Due to the reference semantics, this
change is immediately reflected in the CustomerManagement building block. Its state is
modified even though no command such as modifyCustomer has been called! The client has,
so to speak, unauthorized access to the building block’s internal data. This violates the
principles of data sovereignty (s. Architecture Principles).
Classification of parameter types (reference/value)
 Categorize all parameters and returns for the operations in building block
interfaces as reference or value parameters. Document the category of the
parameters in the interface specification.
In JavaDoc, for example, the parameters can be identified by in (input parameter value), out (return
value) and inout (reference parameter).
Reference-oriented and Value-oriented Interfaces
We distinguish between:
Reference-oriented operation
A reference-oriented operation is an operation which contains reference parameters, i.e. which
is used by means of a call-by-reference.
and:
Value-oriented operation
A value-oriented operation is an operation which only contains value parameters, i.e. which is
used by means of a call-by-value.
This consequently leads us to define:
Reference-oriented interface
A reference-oriented interface is an interface which contains at least one reference-oriented
operation, i.e. an operation which is used by means of a call-by-reference.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 317
and:
Value-oriented interface
A value-oriented interface is an interface which only contains value-oriented operations, i.e.
operations which are used by means of a call-by-value.
Reference-oriented interfaces are closely coupled and value-oriented interfaces are more loosely
coupled. However, loose coupling may require additional effort for development and maintenance
(performance).
Interfaces and Errors
The following rule combines a number of important considerations relating to the design of
interfaces.
Errors in interfaces
The following applies to the design of interfaces with errors:
1. Errors resulting from the implementation and the associated technology are
never part of the interface - they are unspecified errors.

2. All business logic errors must be part of the interface - as specified errors.
3. Errors can be expressed in the interface in the form of return codes or by
means of exceptions. Exceptions should be preferred if the programming
language provides an exception concept.
4. Return values (normal result of the operation) should be preferred to specified
errors if possible and meaningful.
Example 24. Errors in interfaces
• null should usually be assigned a meaningful value, e.g. findCustomer returns null instead
of CustomerNotFoundException
• -1 as an error code in an int return value is not recommended, e.g. if createCustomer
usually returns a new Id then the exception CustomerNotValidException should be
preferred to the return value -1 if the customer data contains errors.
• Hybrid forms should be avoided, for example representing certain error situations by
means of return values and others by exceptions.
Using Interfaces
Programming Against Interfaces
Interfaces implement the principles of information hiding. The consumer of a functionality is only
informed of the interface, not the implementation. I.e. programming is performed against the
interface. See the figure below.
Programming against interfaces
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 318
Consumer Provider
Declaration and Configuration Definition of external view
... public interface I {
I i; public void m();
i = new C(); ...
... }
Usage Implementation (internal view)
public class C
...
implements I {
i.m();
public void m() {...}
...
}
The interface I forms the external view. The implementation C imports and implements I (the
dependency is depicted by means of an arrow). The consumer also imports I and declares a
variable i, which is typed for I (not for C!). It is only necessary to import the implementation C in a
configuration in which the implementation and interface are combined. The configuration can be
separated from the utilization - in this case, the user does not need to import C. Calls of the method
m are always performed against the interface I and never against the implementation C.
Programming against interfaces

Always program against interfaces if these are available. I.e. type variables with
reference to the interface and not the implementation and then use its operations.
Always use java.util.List (interface) instead of java.util.ArrayList (implementation) or
java.util.Map (interface) instead of java.util.HashMap (implementation).
Principle of Minimum Assumptions
Principle of minimum assumptions

If a number of alternative interfaces are available, always use the most general
interface which provides the required functionality.
Applying this rule minimizes dependencies between consumers and providers.
Use java.util.Collection (interface) instead of java.util.List (interface, derived from Collection) if
the sequence of the elements is not important. This increases the selection of implemented
classes (e.g. ArrayBlockingQueue, ArrayList, AttributeList, TreeSet, Vector, etc.).
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Interface Design | 319
Transactions
Design for ACID
A transaction is a unit of work: a related set of operations acting on a shared data stock which may
be distributed across multiple locations.
Overview
Transactions possess the ACID properties (Atomicity, Consistency, Isolation, Durability).
• Atomicity: Execute transactions in full or not at all.
• Consistency: Transition data from a consistent state to a new consistent state.
• Isolation: Transactions do not influence one another.
• Durability: The state remains permanently in the database.
There are several design options for transactions, incl. locking and data isolation, that can be used
to design transactions meeting the specific needs of the to be designed system. In the following
sections, you will find the different aspects of transaction design, along with their impact on
availability.
Transactions and transactional resources
Database Isolation Levels
The ability to isolate transactions is one of the ACID properties. In practice, a weaker form of the
ACID property is often required. As a result, database systems implement different isolation levels.
Complete isolation of concurrent transactions is rarely employed due to the high resource
requirements as well as availability and performance problems. The following isolation levels have
been defined for databases.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 320
Definition: Isolation levels (as per ISO standard)
The following isolation levels exist for the execution of concurrent transactions:
Read Uncommitted
There is no isolation and all the changes are visible immediately, even before they have
been committed.
Read Committed
Data is not visible until committed.
Repeatable Read
Identical queries always lead to the same result and all changes as of the start of the
transaction are hidden.
Serializable
Highest isolation level. All executed transactions are performed as if they were running
alone.
The following problems may occur in databases:
Lost Updates
Two transactions modify the same record in parallel. On termination of these transactions, only
the last change is accepted.
Dirty Read
The data of a transaction which has only modified parts of the data and thus still is "in
inconsistent state" is read.
Non-Repeatable Read
Identical read operations return different results at different times.
Phantom Read
During a transaction, the search criteria apply to a record which is then deleted or modified by a
concurrent transaction before the end of the current transaction.
The table below lists the isolation levels and describes which of the database problems they may
lead to.
Table 2. Isolation levels and associated problems
Isolation Level Lost Updates Dirty Read Non-Repeatable Phantom Read
Read Possible Possible Possible Possible
Uncommitted
Read Committed Possible Impossible Possible Possible
Repeatable Read Possible Impossible Impossible Possible
Serializable Impossible Impossible Impossible Impossible
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 321
The isolation level “Read Committed” is generally used for commercial databases. The consequence
is that the application has to perform concurrency checks.
Basic operations
Transactions are controlled via three basic operations:
• Begin transaction starts a new transaction.
• Commit transaction confirms the transaction and thus makes the change permanent.
• Rollback transaction undoes the transaction.
Definition: Transactional resource
Transactions are not only provided by databases but, for example, also by EJB containers or
external applications (message-oriented middleware). We thus use the abstract term
“transactional resources”. The current application is itself also a transactional resource if it
provides functionality which possesses the ACID property.
Distinction between business and system transactions
The transaction concept is used in a number of different places and is sometimes used to cover too
wide a range. Based on our experience, we found it helpful to separate transactions for design it
into business and system transactions.
Definition: Business vs. system transaction
Business transactions define a transaction from a process point of view, e.g. reading data,
changing it and saving it again as one business transaction. System transactions define a
transaction from a system resource point of view, e.g. data update on database, writing a
message to a queue.
The difference is important as it is task of system design to take care for both and to correctly
implement business transactions at the end.
Designing Business Transactions
The definition of a business transaction forms part of the business solution design. In this context,
DDD’s concept of an aggregate is very helpful. Frequently, however, business logic concepts are
designed from the “single user” perspective: Parallelism and transactions do not appear because
these are considered to be purely technical topics. If questions concerning business transaction
boundaries and business logic isolation are not considered and explicitly designed, an
implementation will not generally meet the expected requirements.
Typical questions on business transactions to be answered as part of business solution design are:
• Which invariants need to hold after each business transaction?
• What data needs to be consistent directly after an action is performed?
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 322
• Which data can be updated later/independently?
• What is the impact of inconsistent data, what is the cost it may produce?
• How many concurrent users are working on same data, how likely is a conflict?
• Which business transactions need to be serializable?
• Can we demand the user to redo an action in order to achieve better availability?
• What kind of concurrency control can we use for a business transaction?
Once the business transactions have been identified, they have to be implemented using system
transactions. It is therefore necessary to map between business and system transactions.
In the simplest case, the system transaction corresponds to the business transaction. However, user
interaction means that increasingly complex mappings are necessary.
Implementing business transactions using system transactions
As shown in the picture above, having a 1:1 mapping between business and system transactions is
ideal since this represents the simplest and most economical implementation.
Why do we therefore depart from this pattern?
There are a series of reasons for this:
• A web application with a very large number of users will generally make use of short system
transactions. An http request corresponds to a system transaction. However, the web dialog,
and therefore also the business transaction, consists of multiple screens and therefore of
multiple http requests. The business transaction is performed using a number of different
system transactions.
• For reasons of scalability and resource consumption, the isolation level selected for the business
transaction is often lower (Read Committed) than the required isolation level of the business
transaction (ACID).
• Another reason can be found in batch environments: In order to minimize the number of SQL
commands, multiple business transactions are combined in a single system transaction.
• Usage of different isolation levels
Performance, resource consumption, availability and the differing runtimes of business and system
transactions are therefore reasons for more complex transaction architectures.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 323
Example 25. Why do we need design for business transactions?
Below is a typical example in which the runtimes of business and system transactions differ.
The scenario comprises the change of a customer master record via a web application.
An employee wants to modify a customer master record. The application core provides two
functions: findCustomer() loads the data that may subsequently be modified. The function
saveCustomer() saves the new customer data. Because we have used short transactions for
better availability, we also have two system transactions in order to implement the business
transaction “Change customer master”.
If no special mechanisms are used to cover this business transaction, changes are made in the
database which the user is unable to track or which may even be inconsistent, e.g. due to
parallel modifications of the same object by a different user.
Concurrency Checks
As shown in the example above, the business and system transactions may differ and the
application must perform a corresponding concurrency check in this case. The following strategies
can be used to handle the concurrency of business transactions which extend across multiple
system transactions:
Definition: Pessimistic locking
Pessimistic locking implements a concurrency check through the explicit locking and
unlocking of entities. In this case, specific operations are either rejected or blocked.
This strategy makes sense if conflicts occur frequently or if the repetition of a business transaction
requires either involvement of system administrators, needs a lot of computing power or
considerable effort from a user. This strategy needs a business lock to be implemented.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 324
Definition: Optimistic locking
When optimistic locking is used, concurrency is permitted and a check is performed at the
end of the transaction to determine whether conflicts have occurred. If they have, then they
are eliminated by rolling back operations.
This strategy makes sense if conflicts occur only rarely and a rollback is justified when a conflict
occurs. Repeating the transaction is usually a feasible effort for the user. In most cases, this is the
recommended strategy.
Definition: No locking
When no locking is used, concurrency is not controlled, so concurrent users might overwrite
each other and produce inconsistent and wrong data, for example wrong account balances.
This is theoretical strategy, as it only makes sense if there are no concurrent users, which is usually
not the case in the systems we design. Therefore, this concept is only mentioned for completeness
but should not be used.
Distinction between business and system locks
In the same way as for transactions, it makes sense to distinguish between business and system
locks.
Definition:System lock
System locks are the locking mechanisms used for system transactional resources, e.g.
"SELECT FOR UPDATE" to lock a database row.
System locks only lock technical units (individual rows, messages or entities). There are only
pessimistic locks at this technical (system) level. Optimistic system locks must be handled, for
example, by an O/R mapper since the databases provide no optimistic system locks.
 Pessimistic system locks only apply within a system transaction. After the Commit,
the database is unlocked again.
Definition:Business lock
Business locks are the locking mechanisms used for logical units, e.g. lock of product master
data as well as its country specific price listed for common update. Business locks need
explicit design and implementation.
Business locks are usually coarse-grained and apply throughout the entire business transaction and
therefore across multiple system transactions. In the case of pessimistic locks, entire object
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 325
structures are locked via an entry point.
Implementing business transactions using locks
Business locks are used to avoid conflicts between concurrent business transactions. Business locks
can be designed using the locking mechanisms described above: optimistic, pessimistic or no
locking.
As described in Concurrency Checks, locking can be done with an optimistic or pessimistic
approach. The following two examples show the difference between optimistic and pessimistic
business locks.
Example 26. Optimistic business lock
In the case of an optimistic lock, when the business transaction ends, a check is performed to
make sure that no other transaction has made changes to the same object in the intervening
period (since the start of the business transaction). Only then is a commit performed or, failing
this, a rollback.
A standard implementation consists of the use of a version attribute which is incremented by 1
each time a change is made by a system transaction. The first system transcation of a business
transaction is typically a reading transaction. All further system transactions belonging to the
same business transaction check that the previously read or written version attribute has not
been changed in the meantime using “update where version = …” or "select where version = …
".
A similar check has to be incorporated in deleteCustomer().
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 326
Example 27. Pessimistic business lock
If you want to avoid two employees editing the same customer, use a pessimistic lock. After
identifying the customer (findCustomer()), attempt to lock it. This can be achieved by using a
locking attribute in the table.
When pessimistic business locks are used, it is necessary to make sure that the remaining locks
that are no longer used are cleaned up.
Lock granularity
Limit Locks to the needed data set

In general, locks should be used as local as possible. Data that is not needed for the
transaction should not be locked.
This rule leads to needed design considerations, what exactly the data scope to lock is. For details,
especially the concept of aggregates, see Designing Business Transactions.
Further considerations during design have to be made when there is dependent data, as it might
make sense to set locks not at individual object/row level for all of this data but at the level of more
coarse-grained logical units. In the given example, the invoice might consist of an invoice header
and invoice items, that are stored in two separate tables. In this case, you should only include a
locking attribute in the invoice header which then, however, also applies to all the invoice items. In
the case of an optimistic lock, it is also necessary to make sure that changes at the level of the
invoice items result in the version counter for the invoice header being incremented. If it is not
possible to identify any representative (such as the invoice header) for the unit that is to be locked
then it is possible to create a separate lock table which can then be used to manage the locks of any
required units.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 327
For any type of lock, define a suitable representative for the structure that is to be
 modified.
If there is no suitable representative then it will be necessary to introduce an
additional semaphore object.
Transaction control
Transaction control starts (begin) and ends (rollback or commit) system transactions. It is located
outside of the application core which is only permitted to communicate with the transactional
resources and is able to signal a rollback by means of exceptions.
A standard solution for transaction control is structured as follows:
• Every method in an application component interface forms a potential transaction bracket.
• A transaction control strategy is declared for each method:
◦ REQUIRES: If no active transaction exists as yet, the method forms a transaction bracket:
When the method is called, a transaction is started which is then terminated on the return
from the function.
◦ REQUIRES_NEW: The method always forms a transaction bracket. Any transaction that
existed previously is suspended during this period.
Many application servers provide this functionality. Otherwise it is necessary to construct it via
aspects (method interceptors) or move the functionality to the access layer.
Locate the control of the system transactions outside the application core
 Transactions should be controlled outside of the core application logic. Depending
on the micro architecture, this can be the service layer (layered architecture,
tactical DDD) or an incoming adapter port (hexagonal architecture).
Define the standard transaction behavior (REQUIRES). In exceptional cases, it is possible to
configure a different transaction behavior. In rare cases, it is needed to influence transaction
control via a technical transaction service (e.g. in the case of batches).
It is not possible to control system transactions via the client since it is not possible to ensure that
the client will close the open transaction again, for example an employee may shut down his or her
PC in the middle of processing.
 System transactions are managed exclusively by the server.
A client may not explicitly start and end a system transaction.
Caution: Since the client does not control any system transactions, each server call is executed in a
separate system transaction.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 328
Start and end points for business transactions.
You should only start and end business transactions via:

• the dialogs at the client
• the batch frame and
• inbound calls from external application interfaces
Rules for implementation
Pessimistic business transactions
Since pessimistic locks are controlled via the client’s dialog behavior, it is necessary to take account
of the disappearance of a client, for example in cases where the client is shut down in the middle of
dialog processing.
Pessimistic locks which are no longer required must be released.
If pessimistic locks which are no longer required, remain, then these must be
 released. The architect specifies whether the lock is released:
• by means of an automatic procedure or
• by permitting user interaction.
The system transactions that are used within the pessimistic business transaction can use the following
locking strategies:
• pessimistic: For setting and resetting the lock. All the other data in the structure can be
pessimistically locked although this is not obligatory.
• optimistic: For all other data belonging to this structure with the exception of the locking
element.
• no-lock: No version counter or timestamp is checked when changes are made.
 Consider the consequences of pessimistic locks for the component interfaces.
There are lock and unlock methods for each pessimistic locking granularity.
In particular in the case of parallel batch processing, pessimistic locks should, as far as possible, be
set using short system transactions in order to avoid unnecessary synchronization operations.

Set the lock using a system transaction which executes as quickly as possible.
When pessimistic locking is used, it is necessary to monitor the behavior of the database. If there
are too many locks in a table then lock escalation may occur and the database will then lock large
sections of the table or even the entire table. The sequence in which the locks are set is also of
crucial importance if the pessimistic approach is to be used for further data in the structure. The
use of differing sequences may lead to deadlock situations which are recognized by some databases
and are acknowledged through the termination of one of the participants' system transactions.
Otherwise, an administrator must intervene and resolve the deadlock manually.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 329
Optimistic business transactions

Implement optimistic transactions by means of version counters or timestamps.
Every time the optimistically locked objects are modified, the attribute used for the concurrency
check is modified. If the user is able to modify the data a number of times in a business transaction
then the interface must return the new version information in response to the corresponding calls.
This is necessary because of the need to check whether the data provided by the client is up to date
in every system transaction.
When using optimistic locking, add the new version information as a result to the data
 modification methods.
All the methods, that can be called multiple times during a business transaction,
always return the currently valid version information as their result.
Example 28. User repeatedly presses save button
The save method can be called more than once in the customer maintenance dialog.
The signature of the save method is as follows:
public Customer save(Customer customer);
The customer is passed together with the current version information as the result. The client
can then continue operation and even perform another save() call. A single method must be
used. Realization using 2 methods:
public void save(Customer customer);
and
public Customer read(Customerkey key);
is incorrect. Between the calls of the two methods, it is possible that the customer will be
modified by another user – even though this is very unlikely due to the short latency between
the two calls.
The system transactions that are used can use the following locking strategies:
• pessimistic: Possible but offers no advantages.
• optimistic: For all other data belonging to this structure.
• no-lock: Must not be used.
If an optimistic locking strategy is used then the application core must know of the modified
version counter immediately. This is necessary in order to be able to inform the client of the new
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 330
status. The result transfer objects are constructed before the system transaction is committed.
The version counter must be incremented immediately on the first change to an entity.
Please note: The version counter should only be modified once if a number of
changes are made simultaneously.
If used, the O/R mapper must increment the version counter on the first change.

This enables the facade layer to create transfer objects with the most recent status
of the version counter.
If the O/R mapper does not increment the version counters until the modified
objects are saved in the database by means of a Commit, then the entities' Setter
method must perform this function.
General rules
The creation of new objects is not subject to the locking concept.
Locks have no effect on "create" operations (adding data)
A create operation cannot be locked directly using either the optimistic or
pessimistic strategy since the object has just been created and is therefore as yet
 invisible to other objects. It is possible to prevent duplicate create operations
resulting from parallel processing by means of:
• database constraints or
• a semaphore object that is locked pessimistically

There are no tools helping you to find the correct answers, but using modeling tools and drawing
graphics is helpful to discuss impacts on consistency and availability (esp. UML sequence
diagrams):
• BA Toolbox, also helpful for Architects - check Cluster "Solution Architecture"

ACID vs. BASE
Given the fact, that critical systems always need to continue operation and to be tolerant against
network failures (partition tolerance), it is not possible to achieve system consistency and
availability at the same time, as described in the CAP Theorem. ACID and BASE are two different
approaches to (basically) resolve the conflict of availability and system consistency: While ACID
defines the properties that value "system consistency over availability", BASE as counterpart
defines the properties that value "availability over system consistency".
Data consistency vs. system consistency
The word "consistency" in "ACID" has a different meaning than the one in "CAP"

from the CAP Theorem: In "ACID", consistency means the internal consistency of
the data, while in "CAP", it means the state of the replication between different
parts of a distributed system.
Overview
For a long time, integrity of data was ultimate goal of software architecture, along with a lot of
projects aiming to integrate small systems to one large and integrated one. Data consistency was
key. As we learned, system consistency came at the cost of availability and maintainability, so we
start distributing data again with microservice architectures. Optimizing for availability now comes
at the cost of system consistency.
ACID or BASE - It depends on the needs
The question of system consistency vs. availability (or ACID vs. BASE) cannot be
answered in general, but must be answered in the light of a specific situation with

specific client needs. While going for ACID is a clear decision with guaranteed
properties, BASE offers a multitude of follow-up trade-off decisions to be made. A
BASE system typically offers different configuration options to control the degree
of system consistency and availability.
We need to understand the needs for a system, and most probably we will find nowadays areas that
should comply with ACID (e.g. one microservice handling data with a relational database), and
other areas that should comply with BASE (e.g. a microservice using noSQL database / business
processes spanning over several microservices).
In some environments, distributed transactions are not feasible. This is typically the case in
environments where the participants of the transaction cannot or should not be coupled tightly.
The transfer of money across banks is a good example for this case. It would not be wise to make
the success of the money transfer depend on the simultaneous availability of the sending and the
receiving banks' IT systems. So different banks' IT systems should be coupled loosely and therefore,
a distributed transaction is not possible in this case. Instead, extra auditing activities are put in
place to ensure data integrity. When a failure is detected, the partial state is corrected so that no
money gets lost.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transactions | 332
