Security
Overview
Security is a topic which has to be considered in many areas/parts of a software product and its
architecture. It has an influence on aspects of the software development life cycle such as design
and implementation.
This article will cover multiple aspects of security for applications and discuss standard security
design patterns, security threats and mitigation strategies against them.
What is application security?
Definition: Application security
"Application security […] includes all tasks that introduce a secure
software development life cycle to development teams. Its final goal is
to improve security practices and, through that, to find, fix, and
preferably prevent security issues within applications. It encompasses
the whole application life cycle from requirements analysis, design,
implementation, verification, as well as maintenance."
[wiki_appSec]
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Security | 333
Applicatio...
Application Cloud service
OS OS
Hypervisor Hypervisor
Hardware Hardware
Building Building
Text is not SVG - cannot display
Figure 71. Application security in different layers
In a traditional view on application security, it includes the security of the software product such as
(software) architecture design, security at code level, configuration of the application — this
includes the management of those as well. In a more DevOps oriented view of application security,
it also covers operations aspects such as the security of containers or infrastructure as code. Other
security aspects, such as security of the operating system and of the hypervisor environment in the
cloud, are usually not considered as part of application security.
Data protection vs. security
Data protection — such legal requirements as GDPR or HIPAA — and security are often seen as one
subject. But they address different aspects and should be treated differently. Data protection
describes — amongst other things — which data must be protected and what happens, from a
business point of view, if the data is not protected. Security, on the other hand, describes in terms of
data protection, how to protect the data on a more technical base — at the correct level according to
their data protection definition. This makes security to requirement for data protection — at least to
a certain level.
In addition to that, security is not only data protection. Availability is a security aspect as
well — which is usually not direct part of data protection.
Threat & risk based
Security should be used on a high-level basis as a threat (risk) mitigation framework. Security helps
to uncover threats (risks) with mitigation strategies to tackle them. Security should not be seen only
as quality gateways for a go or no-go decision. Threat mitigation strategies should always be
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Security | 334
balanced with the business needs.

Confidentiality
Text is not SVG - cannot display
Figure 72. CIA triad
Risks in security are usually categorized according to the goal (kind of information) to protect
in the CIA triad.
• Confidentiality — preserving authorized restrictions on
information access and disclosure, including means for protecting
personal privacy and proprietary information
• Integrity — guarding against improper information modification or
destruction and ensuring information non-repudiation and
authenticity
• Availability — ensuring timely and reliable access to and use of
information
[NIST_cia]
Threat & risk identification
There standards methods/concepts, frameworks and tools help you to identify potential threats and
risks — for different kind of projects and project models.
In the future, we will describe more methods.
Threat modelling
One concept to identify potential threats and risks is Threat modelling [OWASP_threatModel].
In the future, we will describe the security method of Threat Modeling in more detail.
Divide & conquer (mitigate risks)
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Security | 335
Definition: Security control
A safeguard or countermeasure prescribed for an information system
or an organization designed to protect the confidentiality, integrity, and
availability of its information and to meet a set of defined security
requirements.
[NIST_secControl]
Once risks have been identified, they should be addressed by mitigation strategies or
countermeasures. Such countermeasures — called security controls — are often used. They a
defined common standard to improve security. They can vary from high level, e.g., "you need to
execute penetration tests regularly" to low level, e.g., "how to implement database queries
securely". There are standard catalogues or bricks for security controls depending on the context of
the project.
To apply the appropriate level of security controls, the security threats must be identified for
different parts of the application. Every part of the application has different threats in the CIA triad
and requires different security controls. Therefore, the concept of Separation of concerns is a
requirement for appropriate risk/threat identification and applying correct security controls.

Common concepts & patterns
Authentication & authorization
Definition: Authentication
Verifying the identity of a user, process, or device, often as a
prerequisite to allowing access to resources in an information system.
[NIST_authentication]
Definition: Authorization
The right or a permission that is granted to a system entity to access a
system resource.
[NIST_authorization]
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Security | 336
In the future, we plan to describe authorization methods
• Central identity provider
• Single sign On
• Multifactor authentication
Secure Coding
Definition: Secure coding
Secure coding is the practice of developing computer software in such
a way that guards against the accidental introduction of security
vulnerabilities. Defects, bugs, and logic flaws are consistently the
primary cause of commonly exploited software vulnerabilities.
[wiki_secureCoding]
Secure coding should be part of every of our projects — at least to a certain level.
To verify secure coding, there are open standards defining security controls. You should apply such
security controls to your source code. An example standard is the OWASP Application Security
Verification Standard (https://owasp.org/www-project-application-security-verification-standard).
Automated security tests
A common pattern to enhance the security is to use automated security scans in the software
development life cycle. Such scans can uncover, for example
• issues in used 3rd party libraries, with Software Composition Analysis (SCA)
• security flaws in code, with Static Application Security Testing (SAST)
• security flaws in general, with Dynamic Application Security Testing (DAST)
If possible, use automated security scans within your project.
Zero Trust
Zero trust is a conceptual approach to computer systems security that differs from currently still
often used ones. The main idea is not to distinguish between internal (trusted) and external (not-
trusted) world/zones.
More detailed and in depth explenation of the concept can be found in a seperate article Zero Trust.
Zero Trust and (micro) services
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Security | 337
External vs. internal
Zero trust
Firewall Firewall
external internal external internal
Service Service
exte... exte...
Inte... Inte...
Not... trusted partially... partially...
Text is not SVG - cannot display
Figure 73. Internal vs. external world
Zero Trust has a (micro) service architecture. Without Zero Trust, often the internal and external
worlds are handled differently — internal world is trusted and external not. This means input from
a system/service of the internal world is trusted and the input is not validated in terms of security.
While the external world has no trust at all and input is fully validated in terms of security.
In Zero Trust, the internal as well as the external world is not (fully) trusted. The input of both must
be validated in terms of security. This means each microservice — or its interface, e.g. an API
gateway — regularly acts as a PEP and needs to verify the trust. The correct level of trust and the
appropriate validation/measures are defined by PDP.
Cryptography patterns
If any data or communication of the application requires encryption, common and known
algorithms/standards must be used. You must not create your own encryption algorithms. You
should — highly recommended — use well-know implementation in common libraries and follow best
practices.

Usually traffic between systems/microservices should be encrypted
There are two major kinds of cryptography methods — symmetric and asymmetric. The different
types of cryptography should be used for different purposes.
Definition: Symmetric cryptography
A cryptographic algorithm that uses the same secret key for its
operation and, if applicable, for reversing the effects of the operation
(e.g., an AES key for encryption and decryption).
[NIST_symmetricCrypto]
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Security | 338
Definition: Asymmetric cryptography
Cryptography that uses two separate keys to exchange data, one to
encrypt or digitally sign the data and one for decrypting the data or
verifying the digital signature. Also known as public key cryptography.
[NIST_asymmetricCrypto]
• Symmetric — Use in the context where the sender and the receiver can and do share a common
secret. The sender encrypts or signs with the same key as the receiver decrypts or verifies. For
example, encrypted traffic between systems or encrypted data in a database.
• Asymmetric — Use in the context where the sender and the receiver can’t and don’t share a
common secret. The sender uses the private key for encryption or signature, and the receiver
uses the public key for decryption or verification. For example, you want to ensure that a
session token was created by the correct system: The session token is signed with the private
key, and everybody can verify the session token via the public key.
• Mixed — Asymmetric encryption is usually slower than Symmetric encryption. A mixed usage
of both can be used to enhance performance. Use asymmetric first to exchange the common
secret, and then use symmetric afterward with the exchanged common secret. For example, this
is used in protocols such as Secure Socket Layer (SSL).
Secrets/credentials storage
In the future, we plan to describe common patterns of storing secrets.

In the future, we plan to describe what certificates are and how they can be used

Zero Trust
Zero trust is a conceptual approach to computer systems security that differs from currently still
often used ones. The predominant approach today is based on the concept of a security perimeter. A
security perimeter builds a wall around a computer system leaving a central but heavily guarded
entry point open. Passing the entry point makes untrusted identities becoming trusted ones, having
access to internal resources. Communication is not necessarily encrypted when not crossing
perimeter boundaries. Extending the security perimeter to include home office locations, mobile
devices, and cloud-based applications is complex and makes computer systems hard to maintain.
Contrary to the perimeter approach, the zero trust approach makes sure that all resources (devices,
network elements, applications or data) are accessed by trusted identities (human or machine) with
the necessary rights only. Following a zero trust approach also means encrypting all
communication.
What is the idea of zero trust?
Definition: Zero Trust System
A Zero Trust System is an integrated security platform that uses
contextual information from identity, security and IT Infrastructure,
and risk and analytics tools to inform and enable the dynamic
enforcement of security policies uniformly across the enterprise.
[GaCh21]
"… an integrated security platform …"
The zero trust systems' main purpose is to exercise access control uniformly on all resources of a
computer system (i.e. network elements, devices, applications or data) according to a given set of
security policies. Current computer systems employ multiple different security components to
implement access control for its resources, i.e., one for applications, a second for network
elements, and a third for data — if available at all. These different security components need to
be integrated in order to be able to uniformly enforce the access control according to given
security policies.
"… that uses contextual information from identity, security and IT Infrastructure …"
Zero trust systems make security policy decisions using both static information like user groups
and dynamic information on currently used network access or on currently used device
characteristics. Access to confidential data, e.g., might be granted to users using a device
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Security | 340
complying to required security patch level but might be rejected when using a device not yet
appropriately patched or running a deprecated operating system version.
"… and analytics tools to inform …"
Zero trust systems monitor and log network traffic metadata — extended by context data like
used device or requesting user. Analytic tools like security information and event management or
intrusion detection systems analyze network traffic logs and identify security-relevant events like
a non-identified device trying to access internal resources. Security events will be forwarded on
detection of, e.g., intrusion attempts or incoming requests for confidential data over an unsecure
communication channel.
"… and enable dynamic enforcement of security policies …"
Zero trust systems dynamically adapt access control rules according to security policies in
response to identity, device, network or system context changes. Access control rule adaption
requires a high degree of automation within the security platform in order to perform
efficiently.
"… uniformly across the enterprise"
Zero trust systems maintain a single set of security policies applicable to all resources of a
computer system. These security policies are the only source for creating resource’s access
control rules.
What are the building blocks of zero trust systems?
One essential component of zero trust systems is the policy enforcement point (PEP). PEPs in zero
trust systems exercise access control on one or more resources. They are typically control resources
like network elements, devices, applications or data. PEPs map security policy decisions onto access
control rules and propagate them to resources. Resources store access control rules using data
structures like access control lists, following a role-based access control approach, or as iptables
firewall rules. Concrete implementations of data structures and algorithms for access control vary
per resource. Therefore, PEPs provide a uniform interface to store security policy decisions with
resources.
Another essential component of a zero trust system is the policy decision point (PDP). It is
responsible for maintaining all security policies governing access to all resources of a computer
system. Secondly, it makes security policy decisions for given requests and distributes them to PEPs.
The PDP is conceptually a single, central component of a zero trust system. An actual
implementation is expected to be distributed, i.e., consisting of a set of interacting components. A
distributed implementation provides better efficiency and availability when adapting to high-load
and failure scenarios on the one hand. On the other hand, there is currently no solution available
meeting all requirements for a PDP in a zero trust system. For the time being, any reasonable PDP
solution is expected to be provided by a number of integrated tools.
Both PEP and PDP have been defined according to terminology for policy-based

management from the networking domain as defined by RFC 3198
(https://www.ietf.org/rfc/rfc3198.txt).
It is common to divide a zero trust system architecture into two layers, a control plane and a data
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Security | 341
plane. Network elements, devices, applications and data as well as their PEPs make up the data
plane. Incoming requests are checked for authorization by applying PEP’s access control rules.
Requests are either accepted and forwarded or rejected and returned. The control plane finds a
security policy matching an incoming request and distributes its security policy decision to PEPs in
case no matching access control rule could be found by a PEP for an incoming request.
Both data plane and control plane have been borrowed from the domain of data
communications. In data communications, network elements are making up the
data plane. Incoming data packets are forwarded to an outgoing link according to

network element’s forwarding table data. The control plane executes routing
protocols for an incoming packet and distributes calculated forwarding data into
the forwarding table of network elements in case no matching outgoing link could
be found by a network element for an incoming packet.

Figure 74. Sample zero trust system for web application
Above sample zero trust system consists of a web application accessed from a web client running
inside a browser on a mobile device. A firewall is placed in between the mobile device and the web
application which runs possibly in a cloud environment. There are three PEPs and a controlling,
central PDP. All three PEPs are implemented in a different way. Whereas the User Agent PEP is
installed as a browser plugin, the firewall contains an embedded Network Element PEP being an
integral part of the product itself. The Application PEP is a software process of its own that is placed
right in front of the application following a sidecar pattern.

Performance
Software performance is an umbrella term for how timely and efficient a system executes its tasks.
While a non-functional requirement, inefficient software has a negative effect on other non-
functional requirements like reliability, and can bring a system to a complete halt in the worst case,
robbing it of its ability to do any meaningful work. In some scenarios, these effects are cumulative:
for example, users who constantly try to access an overloaded website put even more pressure on
the system; queues back up during an outage; instances cannot restart since they require a
database connection during boot.
Example 29. Launch of healthcare.gov
On October 1st, 2013, healthcare.gov, the official website for selecting a healthcare provider in
the United States, launched in the morning. The site experienced over 2.8 million page visits,
which significantly slowed it down. Users were frequently prompted to try again later. This
surge in traffic was driven by a registration deadline of December 15th. [CBS2013]
Overview
Performance is filed in ISO 25010 under "performance efficiency", split further into "time
behavior", "resource utilization", and "capacity".
In this article we will focus on the three aspects capacity, throughput, and latency.
Capacity
describes the maximum load (transactions, data volume, …) the system can handle before
experiencing severe service degradation. In the example above, healthcare.gov reached its
maximum capacity and collapsed under the load.
Throughput
measures how much work can be processed in a given period of time. If the amount of work
arriving exceeds throughput, congestion occurs (see Little’s Law).
Latency
denotes the time spent between starting a single task and its completion. Real-time applications
are especially sensitive to latency. In a system with finite resources, latency typically correlates
with throughput, since more work can be done if the amount of time spent by each worker
decreases.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 343
Example 30. Ordering fast food
Latency
yticapaC
Throughput
Text is not SVG - cannot display
When ordering at a fast food restaurant using a terminal POS screen, latency is influenced by
how quickly the menus respond to user input and how fast payments are processed.
Throughput is primarily dictated by the number of terminals available, but it can also be
increased by reducing latency, allowing the next customer to use the terminal sooner. Capacity
is less straightforward in this context; while a fully occupied restaurant with queuing
customers indicates full capacity, many people will likely choose not to queue if the line is too
long, so maximum capacity can be reached much earlier.
Addressing performance issues during software development involves:
1. Research and document: Investigate the expected or actual numbers of items, requests, etc.,
that the system processes during normal operation. Document these findings and update them
regularly.
2. Consider worst-case scenarios: Envision the worst-case load, such as a batch being paused for
several days due to a bug. Assess the risk of this scenario to determine its importance.
3. Design your system with performance in mind: Evaluate different deployment, storage, and
messaging options to select the best architecture without adding unnecessary complexity.
Calculate the required resources using the measured throughput of a prototype.
4. Train developers: Ensure developers are aware of performance-critical paths in the business
logic. Review solutions for their time complexity and scalability. Provide computing resources
for developers to test their algorithms with large datasets and real-world performance.
5. Perform regular load tests: Conduct realistic load tests regularly. In waterfall-style projects
with infrequent releases, perform a load test before every major release to avoid unpleasant
surprises post-launch.
6. Monitor: Survey your application’s performance characteristics continuously and use alerts to
spot anomalies.
We will look at each step in the following sections.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 344
Why and when should you care about performance?
Unfortunately, performance is often an afterthought. The primary focus when implementing
requirements is on completeness and correctness, which is the right approach. However,
performance should receive as much attention as other non-functional requirements like reliability
and security. Kent Beck famously said, "Make it work, make it right, make it fast" [C2Wiki2014].
Once you have a working piece of software, evaluate its performance under both expected and
unexpected loads using load testing (Load testing). Don’t wait until it’s running in production.
Testing functionality with a handful of records is not the same as handling thousands of users
creating new entries simultaneously.
Performance issues can lead to:
• Disruption of functionality: The application is not just simply running slow, it does not work
at all anymore. Deadlocks, timeouts and resource consumption bring it to a halt.
• User experience degradation: Users expect services to work in the blink of an eye. According
to Google, a user (potential customer) is 32% more likely to close a page if a page takes three
seconds to load instead of one [ThinkWithGoogle]. This is not restricted to consumer-facing
applications. While users of enterprise software won’t close the tab when they have to wait
several seconds, the decrease in throughput will cost you money all the same.
• Increased operational cost: With today’s pay-as-you-go cloud pricing models, every CPU hour
incurs additional cost. In the past, it may have not been unusual to leave an on-premise server
running of which you didn’t know what it was actually doing. Nowadays however, it can be
worthwhile to invest person hours to be able to shut down one additional AWS instance. Plus,
with automatic provisioning of resources, an inefficient algorithm spinning out of control under
load can create a hefty bill. Not to speak of the environmental impact of useless CPU cycles.
You should already identify load scenarios during requirements engineering. Do not confuse agile
development with lack of planning; if you can predict how many users will access an application
concurrently, or how many records a batch has to process every night, then you should take
precautions and design your system to accommodate for that.
Some common load scenarios include:
Initial launch
During an initial launch, a system faces high stress as users flock to it, like in the healthcare.gov
example above. Whether it’s a government website for grant applications or an e-commerce
platform, the initial hype and the fear of missing out drive thousands of users to the system
within the first hours. Media coverage is at its peak during this time, and any errors can quickly
damage your client’s reputation.
However, an initial launch event isn’t limited to the system’s first deployment. Whenever a
major release goes live, the system’s performance characteristics may change. Therefore, load
tests should be conducted throughout the application’s lifetime, not just during the initial
launch.
Staged releases can help reduce the stress on the system and allow for rollbacks in case of bugs.
The update is not deployed to all instances or shipped to all users simultaneously, but one-at-a-
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 345
time over a longer period. Staged releases involve deploying updates to instances or users one at
a time over a longer period. This approach requires zero-downtime deployments and seamless
operation between different software versions, which may incur additional development costs.
Short-term demand by a planned event
While it’s impossible to predict all sudden surges in user demand, some events are foreseeable.
For example:
• Shopping sites experience heavy traffic during Black Friday sales
• Public transport apps see increased usage during major sporting events
• Booking websites crash when a famous pop star embarks on a world tour
Work closely together with domain experts to identify these events before they occur, both in
the initial design, and during operations.
To mitigate a sudden surge in operations, consider employing dynamic [horizontal scaling].
Sometimes you even control the demand, for example when sending out mass emails to users
prompting them to perform an action. Stagger these emails to smooth the load over a longer
period of time.
Virtual queueing
Despite meticulous planning and ample resource allocation, a system may still
reach full capacity and be unable to accommodate more users. An often-utilized,

albeit ironic, solution is to implement a virtual queue in front of the actual
website, requiring users to "draw a number." This approach employs a relatively
simple system to manage the load, alleviating pressure on the main application
until it can handle additional users again.
Estimating performance
As you can see, not taking performance into account when designing a system can lead to issues in
production. But how can you estimate the performance of your system before a single line of code
has been written?
In this section, we will have a look at ways to get a rough first estimate on how well a system might
scale with the number of tasks. These are in no way a replacement for actual measurements
obtained when running actual code. Instead, they should guide how you design the overall system,
and think about writing code. Anticipating issues early on is much cheaper than a big refactoring at
a later stage.
Your pessimistic estimates may make you think that you need a much more complicated design
than initially thought. However, before you set up a ten node Kafka cluster or replace all your
relational databases with NoSQL because you "heard they were faster", it is important to measure,
measure, measure. Build a small prototype and perform [load testing] early on to see if you need to
change the architecture.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 346
Little’s Law
Little’s Law from queuing theory is a simple but effective way to estimate the number of parallel
running tasks in a system. It states that the average number of customers L in a system equals to
the time spent in the system, W, times the average arrival rate λ:
For example, if a web server can process a single request in 500ms, and there are 100 requests
arriving per second, then on average, 50 requests need to be handled simultaneously. You can
divide L by the number of concurrent workers; if this exceeds 1, then more tasks arrive than can be
handled. If all work is queued and not discarded, the queue will grow infinitely.
The formula can also be used to calculate latency if throughput and queue size are known. If you
are able to observe the average number of items in the queue (L) and the rate at which tasks are
completed (λ), you can derive how long it takes for an item from queue insertion time to leaving the
system. [Sookocheff]
Big O notation
We should forget about small efficiencies, say about 97% of the time:
premature optimization is the root of all evil. Yet we should not pass up our
opportunities in that critical 3%.
— Donald A. Knuth, Structured Programming with go to Statements
When starting out with programming, it’s tempting to include what appear to be ‘micro-
optimizations.’ For instance, you might have heard that infix increment (++i) is faster than postfix
(i++) in C. Similarly, you might be inclined not to write a class in Java for an ISBN number because
you want to avoid additional heap allocation. Or perhaps you manually unroll a loop to save on
jump instructions.
The truth is that such examples are quite often myths, outdated knowledge, or optimized by the
compiler or runtime anyway. Even if they worked, they only reduce the number of instructions
executed by a tiny amount, at the cost of readability. Unless you’re writing low-level code or
operating on the scale of Netflix, you need not bother with these implementation details. There are
much more fruitful avenues to pursue.
Instead of splitting hairs and counting individual instructions, it is much more helpful to talk about
the time an algorithm takes as a function of its input size n. O Notation classifies algorithms in
terms of their space and time complexity. For example, an algorithm said to have O(n) time
complexity scales linearly with the size of its input. The notation is not meant to calculate the exact
time it takes, rather to compare two solutions with each other. An algorithm in the O(n2) class
(quadratic complexity) takes longer than one in O(n). This may be less evident for small n, but with
large batch sizes, having linear or cubic complexity can make a difference between a report taking
one hour or one hundred years to generate.
Time complexity is easily overlooked when invoking library functions. Consider the following
Python code:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 347
students = find_all_students() # a list of students
teachers = find_all_teachers() # a list of teachers
number_of_student_teachers = 0
for student in students:
if student in teachers: # This check is O(n)
number_of_student_teachers += 1
This snippet finds the number of university students who are also teachers. For each student, we
check if they are in the teacher list. However, checking whether an item is in a list is O(n) in most
programming languages, since in the worst case we traverse the entire list. Combined with the O(n)
complexity of the loop, this gives us O(n2) time complexity overall. For two lists with 10,000 entries
each, this results in 100 million comparisons! If we convert the list of teachers into a set instead, the
inclusion check becomes effectively constant (O(1)), and the overall complexity O(n).
These hidden complexities are more common than you might think. They usually don’t matter in
online transactional business logic, since only a handful of elements are retrieved. In batches or
growing tables, however, they can become a serious bottleneck. Raise your developers’ awareness
of time complexity so they write efficient algorithms from the start.
Measuring Performance
Once your application is in production, you should constantly monitor its performance
characteristics and set up alerts when it exceeds normal operation parameters.
The simplest way to gather data points is through logging. By logging both the start and end of a
task, you can calculate throughput by counting the number of finished tasks per time unit, and
latency by subtracting the start time from the end time. However, this can become tedious and may
require writing many manual pattern extractors.
A better solution is to emit data points in a standardized format like OpenTelemetry metrics.
Concepts such as a counter or gauge can be used to collect information like SQL query latency,
incoming request payload size, or the number of HTTP errors. These data points can then be stored
in a time-series database like Prometheus and visualized with tools like Grafana. Additional tools
may be available for your language ecosystem. For example, Java has the JDK Flight Recorder,
which provides statistics such as garbage collection pause times. The same monitoring tools should
also be enabled during load tests.
When tackling a performance issue, you may require more detailed information about what the
program is doing.
For investigating a memory issue, a memory dump can be taken and checked for unusually large
entities. Since this includes all passwords and personally identifiable data held in memory by the
application at that point in time, this approach may not be suitable for production environments. In
that case, it may still be possible to reproduce the issue in a test environment and take the snapshot
there.
For slow-running tasks, profiling allows you to see how much time is spent in each function. All
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 348
major programming languages provide tools for profiling, and there are two kinds of profiling
available:
• Sampling takes a snapshot of the execution context (stack) at regular intervals. By counting how
often a function is seen, you can estimate the time spent in each relative to the overall time. This
approach is non-invasive and can even be run in production with minimal overhead.
• Instrumentation modifies the application’s code to inject metric collectors. These can provide
high resolution and accurate information about how long each function call takes but incur
significant overhead compared to sampling.
Load testing
When testing an application for functional correctness, tests — whether automated or manual —
typically involve a single user and a small number of records. This approach is practical because
hiring 100 testers to access an application simultaneously or running hour-long unit tests on
millions of records for each commit would be costly. However, evaluating your application’s
performance under expected load is crucial. Even with a small number of concurrent users, a real
system will likely have a large number of database rows that are queried and processed.
Load testing simulates many virtual users accessing the application simultaneously, providing a
realistic view of how the system may perform in the real world. Various load test types exist (Types
of load tests), ranging from simulating normal operations to sudden bursts of interest.
Make it a habit to always include load tests in your project. Some greenfield

projects have a long development cycle before they see actual users. You don’t
want to be surprised by failing servers on launch day!
Performing load tests involves these steps:
1. Estimate the expected load. If the system under test already exists, you can obtain real-time
statistics with good monitoring. For greenfield projects, estimate based on experience and
numbers provided by the business. Avoid simply taking the average usage across a day or week,
as real users are more active during particular time zones or seasons. For example, don’t base
the number of expected users for a winter sportswear shop on data collected during the
summer.
Don’t assume a uniform distribution over all operations. Identify the different use cases in your
application and how frequently each will be accessed to get a realistic distribution.
2. Prepare realistic load. Use libraries like Faker to generate realistic, random data. Distribute
the load over time, for example by using a Gaussian distribution, instead of sending all requests
simultaneously.
3. Set up a load test environment. Deploy the system under test in a production-like
environment, including real versions of neighboring systems and production-grade versions of
products like databases. For example, if you scale to two instances in production, use two
during your load tests to test load balancing and concurrent access to shared resources.
Some cloud providers offer cheaper instances with slower response times, such as object
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 349
storage with high latency. You can use these for load testing, but don’t expect accurate results. If
your system can manage the load with a pessimistic setup, it will likely perform better in
production.
Ensure the environment that simulates the virtual users has ample resources. Generating load
stresses both your system and the load generator itself, potentially causing issues like running
out of network ports or process handles, and CPU stress from compression algorithms. Consider
deploying load generators in a swarm.
4. Decide what you want to measure and simulate, and write the test.
◦ How do you decide if requests were successful? Do you check for a 200 status code or verify
response correctness as well?
◦ Are you more interested in delay or throughput?
◦ Are you simulating a realistic user scenario running functions in sequence, or do you
randomly select operations?
◦ Do you invoke the API directly for faster simulation of more users, or simulate actual UI
usage to test your frontend’s rendering ability?
5. Interpret the results. Load test results can be visualized and queried in tools like Grafana.
When summarizing multiple data points like single-request latency measurements, the average
is not a very useful metric due to sensitivity to outliers. Instead, calculate the mean or 95th
percentile for a better picture of your system’s performance.
Also check your application’s logs for any errors logged.
In the next section, we will look at different load test types.
Ensuring performance across diverse client environments
This article focuses on server-side reporting and online transaction processing,
utilizing resources under your control. However, if you’re developing any kind of
user interface — whether it’s a single-page web application, desktop client, or
mobile app — you must also consider the performance on the client machine.

Local testing by developers, who typically use high-end machines with powerful
CPUs and ample RAM, may not be sufficient. These machines can run both
backend and frontend without latency. To reach a broader audience, it’s essential
to test on low-end machines and older operating systems as well. In the web
development world, Chrome Developer Tools allow you to simulate slower CPUs
and networks.
Types of load tests
When testing batches, such as report generation, load testing involves generating a large amount of
test data and executing the job to see how long it takes to process all items. For virtual users,
however, you can simulate various scenarios. [GrafanaLoadTest]
The load amount is usually ramped up slowly, starting with a few users and gradually increasing.
This approach provides more realistic results for systems that optimize frequently used paths over
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 350
time (like just-in-time compilation or database statement caching).
Average-load
Applies the expected load you calculated over a set period without increase or decrease after the
ramp-up period. This is the most important load test type and should always be performed.
sresU
Time
Spike
A spike test simulates a sudden surge of requests with minimal ramp-up time. This allows to test
the system’s behavior for events like the initial launch or event-induced demand discussed in
Why and when should you care about performance?.
sresU
Time
Soak
A soak test is an average-load test performed over a longer period (think hours or days). The
focus here is on the long-term durability of the system. It helps detect issues like queues filling
faster than they are consumed or slow-forming memory leaks.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 351
sresU
Time
Capacity
A capacity test increases the load gradually with no upper limit. Its goal is to find the maximum
capacity or breakpoint of the application, answering the question, "How many users could we
potentially serve?"
sresU
Time
Limited resources
To ensure operability during disasters, such as a cloud availability zone shutting down or a CPU
chip degrading, you may perform a load test with limited resources. For example, deploy only
one instance instead of two, or move it to a less performant host.
Notable tools
There are various load testing tools at your disposal. These can be deployed and run by you, but
there are also load-testing-as-a-service offerings. Major cloud providers like Azure, AWS, or GCP
provide ready-to-use solutions, templates, or guidelines.
When looking for a load test tool, you want to check it offers:
• Ramp-up and randomization
• Sessions so virtual users can stay logged in / remember data from a previous step
• Result checking and the ability to fail a scenario
• Various export formats, preferably with existing integration with monitoring tools
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 352
Apache JMeter
is a Java-based load test tool that allows to define scenarios both in code and in a visual editor. It
has proven to be reliable in Capgemini projects, although operating the UI is a bit fiddly. Load
generators can be run in an orchestrated cluster.
Gatling
Offers both open source and paid load-test-as-a-service solutions. Load tests can be written in
JavaScript or in JVM languages like Java.
Common performance pitfalls
Beyond the generally inefficient algorithms discussed in Big O notation, there are several common
performance pitfalls you might encounter in your project.
Inefficient database operations
SQL’s declarative nature makes it excellent for querying relational databases. Instead of specifying
how to obtain data, you describe the desired result, and the database selects the best execution plan
based on parameters like table size or available indices.
However, sometimes the chosen plan is inefficient. For example, outdated statistics might lead the
RDBMS to perform a cross join (joining every row of table A with every row of table B) instead of a
hash join (looking up rows in table B based on a hash). Query abstractions like the Java Persistence
Query Language (JPQL) can also hide the true complexity of a query. Additionally, concurrent
queries might contend for row or table locks, potentially resulting in deadlocks.
To mitigate these issues, use your RDBMS’s monitoring tools to be alerted about slow-running
queries and lock contention. Utilize the EXPLAIN feature to display the execution plan of a query and
its attributed cost..
Your toolbox for addressing these issues includes:
• Indices to speed up joins and lookups
• Materialized views for infrequently updated tables to cache expensive computations
• Allowing some amount of redundant data for the price of inconsistency
• Introducing stored procedures to reduce roundtrips to the database
• Statement ordering so that concurrent threads access tables in the same order, preventing
deadlocks
Network latency
Even with the lightning speed of fiber networks, transferring data over the wire isn’t nearly as fast
as a program’s local function call. A local function invocation can be handled in nanoseconds, while
network requests take milliseconds. This doesn’t even account for the overhead of establishing an
HTTP connection over TCP.
When designing a distributed system, your first priority should be to minimize the number of
network calls per task. Avoid creating a distributed monolith where each service calls dozens more,
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 353
which in turn access even more services.
Abstractions can also obscure the number of network calls made. An object-relational mapper
(ORM) like Hibernate allows easy traversal of an object graph, but accessing its proxy objects will
perform database queries in the background to lazily load more data. From the developer’s
perspective, they are simply iterating over a list, which can easily add up to thousands of database
queries.
After reducing the number of requests, consider reducing the size of network transfers. While
convenient, transferring entire aggregate roots can be time-consuming, especially in verbose
serialization formats like JSON or XML. Use projections for database queries and query-like APIs
like GraphQL to select which information is returned. Avoid mixing binary with text-based formats
like JSON, where they are encoded as base64; instead, provide a separate endpoint to download raw
data. If size remains an issue, evaluate binary-efficient serialization formats like gRPC and Apache
Avro.
Finally, if network-related issues persist, investigate alternative transport protocols like HTTP/3.
Misconfiguration
Software like Nginx or Kafka and frameworks like Spring come with a large list of defaults, which
can be too conservative for your workload. For example, in the Java Spring framework, the
RestTemplate class for making HTTP requests creates a connection pool by default that allows only
up to ten concurrent connections across the entire application.
Carefully read the documentation of the libraries and software you use. When changing defaults,
document why you did so, and revisit parameters after upgrading major versions of the library.
Task congestion by misbehaving system
A common pitfall when designing for expected load is overlooking the potential for work to queue
up in a system due to a misbehaving producer or a bug in the consumer. For example, a newly
released batch process might contain a bug that causes it to constantly crash, leading to work that is
usually processed in minutes piling up for days.
This may not be an issue if the consumer can process work faster than the producer inserts new
tasks. However, depending on the queue implementation, the sudden increase in work can slow the
system down even more. This is often the case with inefficient database queries designed to operate
on a task table containing a few dozen entries, not millions.
Strategies to improve performance
Once you identified the bottleneck, there are various strategies you can apply to increase your
system’s performance.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 354
Addressing a performance emergency
When facing an immediate performance issue, prioritize these three steps:
1. Discard data or limit traffic if permitted. It’s better to achieve partial success
than none at all.

2. Investigate the root cause. If data is insufficient, deploy a micro-release with
enhanced logging on one instance.
3. Don’t hesitate to use temporary fixes. While it’s tempting to resolve all
performance issues, your main goal is to stabilize the production environment.
You can refine and remove temporary solutions later.
Scale up
Scaling is a common strategy to address performance issues by allocating more resources. The type
of scaling depends on the nature of the task and the resources it requires.
If your task is CPU-bound, upgrading to a more powerful processor can provide a significant boost.
For tasks that are I/O-bound, such as those heavily reliant on disk operations, switching from HDDs
to SSDs can lead to dramatic improvements. If your task can be divided into parallelizable chunks,
scaling out to multiple servers can speed up processing.
Dynamic scaling offers the flexibility to allocate and deallocate resources based on demand. Cloud
providers and orchestrators like Kubernetes provide templates to set up rules for scaling out
(adding resources) or scaling in (removing resources).
Vertical scaling
This involves increasing or decreasing the physical resources allocated to a single instance of the
application, such as doubling the available RAM. As it involves a physical change to the
underlying hardware, downtime is typically expected. However, some cloud providers offer
seamless migration of a running virtual machine to a more powerful host. It’s worth noting that
typical enterprise web applications are rarely CPU-bound and should not consume large
amounts of memory, so vertical scaling may not always be the most effective solution.
App App
Text is not SVG - cannot display
Horizontal scaling
This involves adding or removing instances of the application to distribute the load. Horizontal
scaling is effective when your performance is limited by the number of concurrent tasks a single
instance can handle. However, as Amdahl’s Law illustrates, the effectiveness of parallelization is
limited by the amount of work that must remain serial. Common bottlenecks could include a
relational database or a shared network file system. For batch processing of large data volumes,
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 355
the MapReduce approach can help divide and conquer tasks across a large cluster of worker
nodes.
App App
App
App App
Text is not SVG - cannot display
Design for scalability
Always design your server-side applications in a way that they can be easily scaled
to multiple concurrently running instances, even if they are designed as a
monolith. Apart from being able to scale for performance reasons, this also gives

you more reliability in case of failure, and allows for rolling releases without
downtime.
Note that this may require thinking about employing leader election so that cron
tasks are not run concurrently and can be picked up by another node in case of
failure.
Perform work concurrently
You don’t need to scale entire instances horizontally to achieve parallelization. Even on a small
scale, performing tasks concurrently can be beneficial. For instance, if you need to retrieve data
from two microservices to respond to a user’s request, try executing them concurrently and then
combining the results. This approach requires careful design to ensure that querying one system is
not dependent on the other. Additionally, it can be challenging when dealing with database
transactions or shared mutable state, which may necessitate the use of semaphores.
Asynchronous or reactive programming is a specialized form of concurrency programming that
utilizes only a few operating system threads. When the application needs to wait, such as for a
network call to return, it reuses the thread for the next task and resumes the original task once an
event triggers it. This approach can lead to highly efficient resource utilization. However, if your
programming language does not natively support this style (like JavaScript’s async/await or Java’s
virtual threads), it can result in reactive constructs being scattered throughout your codebase,
including your business logic.
Cache expensive resources
Caching is a powerful technique that mirrors data from slower resources, such as a database, to
more responsive ones, like the application’s memory. This can significantly improve the
performance of your software.
Ideal candidates for caching are data that are infrequently updated, such as country codes or static
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 356
web pages. Content Delivery Networks (CDNs) can be particularly useful in this context. They not
only bring static resources closer to the user’s location, enhancing responsiveness, but also alleviate
the load on your host.
However, caching is not without its challenges. Cache invalidation, famously cited as one of the
‘three hard problems in computer science’ [FowlerTwoHardThings], poses questions such as: How
do you synchronize copies when the source data is updated? Is eventual consistency an acceptable
trade-off? While viewing a slightly outdated report might be harmless, acting on outdated data in a
banking application, for example, could lead to financial repercussions.
Therefore, if users are making business decisions based on cached data, it’s crucial to be
transparent about it. Always display when the data was last updated to ensure users are aware of
the potential for slight discrepancies.
Decouple online transaction processing from scheduled tasks
While it might seem convenient to run scheduled tasks, such as database table cleanups, within the
same instance that handles Online Transaction Processing (OLTP) requests, this approach is seldom
advisable. Performance issues encountered by these tasks can potentially bring down the entire
application. So, what should take precedence - purging old log entries or serving your users?
To avoid such dilemmas, execute these jobs separately from your online server instances. Tasks
that run frequently, say every second, can still be modeled as timed tasks scheduled by a separate
copy of the application itself. However, for tasks that are longer-running and less frequent,
consider modeling them as batch processes running completely independent, i.e., one (operating
system) process per job.
The scheduling of these tasks is flexible. You could use Linux’s native cron, Kubernetes' CronJob
scheduler, or a cloud function, depending on your preference and requirements.
Keep in mind that if you schedule tasks at a resolution of seconds, initiating a new process for each
task will require a startup time in the milliseconds range for the code handling the task.
Implement the circuit breaker pattern
A Denial of Service (DOS) attack is characterized by an overwhelming flood of requests or tasks
directed at a resource, pushing it to its maximum capacity and rendering it incapable of handling
any meaningful work. However, not all DOS attacks are malicious. A misconfigured software within
your own network can inadvertently generate a multitude of requests.
Another potential issue arises from retries following a failure. Consider a system under significant
load due to a high volume of requests. As the load increases, so does the error rate, leading to the
application returning a 503 Service Unavailable status. Now, envision a consumer that naively
retries whenever it encounters an upstream error. Is it beneficial to burden the system with even
more requests?
This is where the circuit breaker pattern proves its worth. After a series of repeated server errors,
the client deems the upstream resource as unavailable. It then treats subsequent requests as
failures without actually executing them. This state is maintained for a predefined time span
(either fixed or exponentially growing), after which actual requests are attempted again. This
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Performance | 357
pattern helps the target system to 'calm down' and recover from its overloaded state. In a way, it is
comparable to the fairness of the TCP congestion avoidance algorithm.

Quality Assurance
We aim to provide with each engagement a high-quality delivery. As noted in the Architecture
Objectives, the definition of quality in a project depends on the requirements of this project. The
Architecture Principles and on top of them all design decisions we made impact the quality of the
software.
Thus it is essential to safeguard quality through quality assurance measures along the whole
process — from designing the architecture to the code itself.
From a bird’s-eye view, Architecture Reviews serve as the highest level of quality assurance. They
can and should be conducted early in an engagement, even before any code exists, or later for
existing software, focusing on specific aspects.
An essential aspect of quality assurance for working software is Test Automation. We give an
overview of different test levels, architectures and types and show how and when they can be used
sensibly.
The ability to react quickly to new insights or changes creates competitive advantages for many
companies. With Continuous Quality, we demonstrate an approach that makes it possible to deliver
features continuously, quickly and with appropriate quality.
Lastly, quality is also about sustainability of delivery. Doing it well once is usually not sufficient,
and we must enable ourselves to accelerate and safeguard our delivery in long engagements. In the
Living Documentation section, we lay the foundations of a documentation practice to reduce the
overhead of creating documentation.
Architecture Reviews
Architecture sets the course for a project at a very early stage. Its rules and measures provide the
foundation for a system or service landscape. Changing these fundamental decisions
retrospectively or overturning them completely is usually impossible due to budget and time
constraints. The initial idea must therefore be as suitable and viable as possible to match the
targeted quality (aspects) by reaching the architectural objectives/goals.
Architectural reviews are the most important means of conducting quality checks and ensuring
assurance. They can be done even at a very early stage of a project. But there are also many reasons
for conducting architecture reviews at later stages. It is valuable to repeatedly put the architecture
to the test in order to recognize necessary adjustments to the course. In another scenario, we might
be asked to take over code from another supplier and assess the associated risks.
The following article caters to both inexperienced and experienced reviewers. First, it will give you
a general understanding of the topic. It will not propose a new methodology, but will refer to
existing tools and methodologies and offer tips and best practices to conduct reviews successfully.
General observations on architecture reviews
Architecture reviews (sometimes also called "architecture evaluation") are a valuable, useful, and
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 359
powerful instrument to assess a software that already exists or that is in the making. I.e. it can be
applied at any time in the lifecycle of a software system. Its goal is to identify risks that can/should
be mitigated, or even uncover existing problems. An architecture review requires investments, but
saves time and money in the long run by preventing wrong or inadequate decisions. Compare this
to your car which you regularly drive to the shop for inspections: You would like to get a check (and
fix) before it breaks down unexpectedly in the middle of the road (i.e. "preventive maintainance").
There you also have to invest into the checkup to avoid bigger and more expensive damage.
The subsequent drawing depicts the importance of architectural objectives/goals for the
architecture itself and all it’s derivate artifacts. Architecture objectives serve as the essential
foundation upon which everything else is built, and they also provide a basis for measuring and
assessing these building blocks.
(Small remark: The graphic shows a simplified view regarding "processes & organization". They
certainly don’t reside exclusively within the architecture domain and cover more than that.
However, within the architecture discipline we at least shape and define the software development
life cycle!)

The rest of this chapter motivates architecture reviews by explaining its basic principles and

What happens during an architecture review?
A bird’s eye view of what happens during an architecture review is depicted in the following
drawing. This scheme intentionally avoids being tied to any specific concrete review method or
tooling. It depicts the general ideas behind an architecture review which is common to all
approaches. The differences lie in the way to conduct these steps. Each method typically places
varying emphasis on different steps compared to other methods.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 360

(as-is) architecture software (code)
Is the architecture impleme...
