Text is not SVG - cannot display
Figure 76. Overview of the tasks and activities of architecture review
Starting point are usually the architecture objectives. Additionally, during a very much
recommended alignment meeting before starting the review with your review sponsors/customers
(cf. Schedule a preliminary alignment meeting), you should listen for special or explicit review
goals or areas. These will also influence your review. The set of objectives provided by these
sources are your basis to assess and measure every artifact (from verbal information through
documents to code) during your review. They are your "north star" as you will always measure
against them whenever you rate something as good or bad.
An architecture review tries to answer three basic questions:
Is the (as-is) architecture appropriate and suitable for the requirements and objectives?
You can perform this core step independently of whether there is already a software or not. The
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 361
architecture objectives and any additional review goals are held and assessed against your
architecture (=architecture documentation): Do your architecture decisions support or impede
certain architecture objectives? Are there risks or problems? The main task of an architecture
review is to uncover those risks/problems and make them explicit in the result.
This step will be an expert review. The architecture documentation as a whole, including the
architecture decision records (ADR), are the main input. This is held and checked against the
architecture objectives (including additionally the focus points from the review sponsors). The
reviewer(s) will study a bunch of documents and lead interviews with stakeholders to come to a
result. Checklists like [ARC360] or [ATAMTemplate] can be very helpful here.
Are the requirements (functional and non-functional, i.e. quality characteristics)
implemented completely and correctly?
Functional incompleteness and bugs are not really the main focus of an architecture review. But
for the sake of completion it’s mentioned here as well. If this is in your scope you more or less
have to rely on the statements from the interviews you conduct and on test result
documentation you acquire. However, the check of quality characteristics like performance or
security is more often in the scope of an architecture review.
Be aware that these often cannot be assessed easily and often require specialist knowledge from
the reviewer. So be cautious before you promise to provide an "expert opinion" on the systems’s
security if you are not by chance a cybersecurity specialist (or have one in your review team and
enough time and resources).
Is the intended architecture implemented correctly and appropriately in software?
If there is software that can/should be assessed during the review, in this step you will check if
the code matches the architectural guard rails. Do your components match the architecture
principles? Are naming conventions met? etc.
In this step tools like SonarQube oder SonarGraph can be very useful. Hopefully, the project has
already something set up for their daily project work that you can just use. See tools and
processes mentioned in the article on continous quality for more on that.
Here you can also uncover organizational, process or knowledge issues: Did the developers not
know what/how they are expected to implement things? Is it lack of knowledge, skills or
communication? What is the issue, why that was not uncovered before your review?
However, it’s not only technology! A systems' architecture is more than just a bunch of diagrams
and code. It covers the project’s organization as well (it shapes it and is shaped by it), including the
(development) processes and operational aspects. The latter is nowadays even more important with
DevOps and a developer team beeing responsible for their own operation in a cloud environment!
Therefore an architecture review can and should cover these apsects as well. Just some examples of
questions in that area:
• What does the build-pipeline look like?
• How do deployments work?
• What environments are there?
• What does the staging & deployment process look like?
• How do logging, tracing and alerting work?
• Are all these solutions appropriate?
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 362
Keep an eye open for these aspects, even if they’re not in your explicit scope of the review.
Particularly the processes and organizational aspects (including people related issues) are often a
source of trouble within the engagements (more on that in Technology is not everything: keep a
holistic view!). How do teams work together? How agile is the "agile process"?
Finally, the architecture review concludes with a "quality report" and usually with at least one
presentation. Your report should state the identified risks and problems in a format tailored to your
audience. A management board needs different abstraction and vocabulary than the customer’s
architects. Keep that in mind, especially for a presentation. Preparation of results will give you
some hints and tips on that.
What is the benefit of an architecture review?
Since architecture significantly impacts project success or failure (e.g. in software development,
acquisition, customization, or retirement), prioritizing sound architectural decisions is crucial.
An architecture review offers a structured evaluation of a system’s design, aiming to ensure that it
meets both current and future business and technical requirements and quality aspects.
The main advantage you get out of architecture reviews is to provide guidance for making both
business and technology decisions, ensuring good product quality, and managing the evolution of
software systems.
Every decision you have made carries the risk of being off the mark. Architecture reviews help
identify risks early on and thus can prevent issues related to quantity, quality, budget, or time. They
can also confirm that the chosen approach is still appropriate.
On top of that, architecture evaluations give you some extra perks. They can lead to better
documentation of the evaluated software system and reveal any implemented violations of
architectural decisions, which can then be fixed. Plus, they increase awareness of the architecture
among all stakeholders involved.
If you conduct architecture reviews regularly, you can safeguard that your system is robust,
scalable, secure, and aligned with your strategic goals (i.e. architecture objectives). This leads to
more successful projects and helps to protect your investments.
When to conduct architecture reviews?
As stated before, architecture reviews can be applied at any time in the lifecycle of a software
system and it’s recommended to execute them regularly. Typical points in time when they are asked
for:
Shortly after the start of a project
Here the lead question will be: Can the solution work that we designed? Architecture reviews
are the only possibility to assess the quality of your architecture at this early stage as there is
(usually) no software to test yet.
When problems arise
If a project team encounters architecture related issues in an running project and wants a
"diagnosis". This kind of review is often combined with asking for the "treatment", i.e.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 363
improvement ideas. Often the focus here lies on these specific issues.
When fundamental things change
There can be many sub-causes here. To name a few examples:
• After a company merger it is necessary to assess which of the now two systems with the
same purpose should be the future lead system
• A company has been (too) successful, now twice the volume has to be processed through the
system: Will it manage it?
• Transformation due to a modernization to the cloud: How cloud ready is the system? Is it
worth moving it or should we rebuilt it from scratch?
• Assessing a software that we want to take over from a competitor: is it worth the risk and
effort?
Getting a regular check-up
Architecture reviews can and should be a recurring regular check-up. This is comparable to
what we do with our cars when we bring them to technical inspections. Also nowadays we
accept the benefits and set up continuous integration pipelines as a standard quality assurance
measure: The code is compiled and runs through several automatic checks like automated tests,
or checks with static code analysis tools. Unfortunately, architecture reviews cannot be (fully)
automated.
An architecture review is about checking if the engagement is still on track as a whole (from
architecture perspective), or to identify issues as early a possible. It doesn’t have to be an
exhaustive review covering all aspects every single time. Try focusing on some important
aspects.
Unfortunately, this trigger for architecture reviews is less frequently acted upon than it should
be.
Who should conduct an architecture review?
As there are not many tools to run a check-up, nor black-and-white hard facts to determine
whether a certain decision is good or bad, the most important factor remains the human element.
Therefore the quality of an architecture review is mostly influenced by the people that perform the
review and their experience. Thus, first of all: You need experienced architects, ideally with the
appropriate business/functional and, above all, technical knowledge.
As we tend to become operationally blind over the time, it is always a better idea to get someone
external to your engagement. So, don’t execute a review on your own if possible as you will not be
as open minded and neutral as someone from outside — as much as you try! Additionally,
employing a project-external team comes with an additional benefit: "A prophet is not without
honor except in his own country." A "neutral" expert opinion that certifies issues and problems
often has more weight for the review sponsors (e.g. project itself, (higher) management, customer’s
stakeholders, etc.) than somebody from the well-known project team — no matter how right they
are. (see also Nobody ever listens to me/Nobody believes me)
In the real world, you will not be able to get the 100% matching domain expert that you can
onboard within a week as there are time and budget restrictions to finish work. The more time you
have as a reviewer, the deeper you can dive into the project and form your own opinion and assess
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 364
"hands-on". The less time you’ve got, the more you have to rely on the interviews and the things you
capture from talking to people. Unusually, it’s something in between. You ask the (hopefully) right
questions to get as much information as possible. With these hints in hand, you drill down into the
seemingly smelly or interesting parts to support or invalidate your theories. But you will not (and
cannot) become a fully familiarized system’s expert in that short time. In spite of that, the outside-
in view from external reviews still remains extremely valuable.
No matter if you manage to get someone from outside or not: Never do a review alone without a
(sparring) partner. So always designate (at least) two architects as reviewers to get better results.
Recommendations for the execution of an Architecture Review
The following section presents some general tips and best practices for conducting a review
without being tied to a specific review methodology.
Schedule a preliminary alignment meeting
It is paramount to establish a joint and clear understanding of expectations with your sponsors
upfront. To avoid any surprises for both parties, it’s advisable to define the boundaries for the
review beforehand. Therefore schedule a preliminary meeting to discuss the setup and scope of the
review.
The following points need to be addressed:
Scope and Goals
Inquire about the specific goals your sponsors have in mind for this specific architecture review.
Goals may include architecture objectives or specific issues that they want you to focus on. It’s
not just one way: Also make clear what you can do or what you won’t/cannot do during the
review. E.g. if you are not versed in cybersecurity, nor have access to an expert, you should not
promise to deliver a "security assessment including a pentest".
Review Effort
The planned effort and time for the review should match the scope. Note that there is nothing
like a "typical length" for a review. Anyhow, it’s never feasible "in a quick brown paper session
during a lunch break". Reviews can last from a couple of hours (in a very narrow scope), over a
few days to several weeks. It depends very much on the size of the software, the goals for the
review and the depth you are required to analyze. Here you might also need to do expectation
management with your peers. If your sponsors have only 2 days budget, the scope of the review
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 365
must be narrowed down accordingly.
Documentation Requirements
Determine the formality required for documenting results and the intended target audience. The
language you have to use in your result documentation will vary if the recipients are customers,
architects or higher-level managers.
Stakeholder Identification and Availability
Identify key stakeholders and (key) knowledge sources. Stakeholders may include customers or
even end users, depending on the review focus and scope. Make clear that stakeholders should
be prepared and available for discussions. Find answers to: how can you approach them? where
do you get the names and contacts from?
Access and Credentials
Clarify necessary access paths to the software and review artifacts or credentials. Who provides
them to you, and by when? As simple and straight forward this might sound, it can be a blocker
for you. Sometimes this is a big source of delays because you wait for days or even weeks until
you get the necessary credentials to start your work.
Write minutes of the meeting
Nobody likes writing minutes of a meeting. Nevertheless, it’s very important: It serves as the
foundation for the review process and should eliminate ambiguity and misunderstandings later
on. Document the discussion outcomes, especially goals, scope, pain points and tasks. Everyone
(sponsors and reviewers) should agree to these minutes. Reiterate the discussion if necessary
until you come to an agreement.
Interviewing your stakeholders is your treasure
Code, documentation and tools are important artifacts during the review. However, the most
important source are the people you will talk to. They are your real treasure. Give attention to the
interviews you conduct, plan to whom you need to speak, prepare yourself upfront, take notes and
structure your data and findings after each interview. See Recording of findings for more on the
documentation of findings.
Listen very, very carefully what you are told or maybe what is omitted. Ask questions and follow
the leads you get. In order to get to the treasure: Create trust. Promise (and keep your promise) to
your interview partners that you will not reveal the source of information except when they
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 366
explicitly agree to be quoted or mentioned. More about that in May I quote your statement?.
Listen carefully
The reviewer’s task is to synthesize and formulate essence of an interview by recording the
findings (cf. see Recording of findings). Therefore, during an interview you have to listen very
carfully. Who better than the people in the project to know where the problems are? And they will
tell you as people tend to speak about their pain points! Keep your ears open: What do people say?
What do they omit (unintentionally or on purpose)? Record pain points and continue drilling where
you think you have to go deeper.
Adapt your plan as it seems appropriate: If necessary schedule a follow up interview with someone
you already have talked to, talk to somebody you didn’t have on your initial list or look into specific
parts of the code/documentation you got pointed to by an interviewee.
Nobody ever listens to me/Nobody believes me
When talking to stakeholders you will sooner or later stumble upon someone who laments:
"nobody ever listens to me!" or "nobody believes me!". You will then not only get a problem
statement (that can go right into your record of findings), but often a proposition how to overcome
the impediment or issue as well, which you should keep track of. This does not mean that you
should always accept these statements and ideas unquestioningly, but they usually contain at least
one rough diamond. So take them seriously and consider them during your work.
If the people inside the project already know the issues and solutions: How can it be that they are
not listened to? There can be several reasons for that. The simplest would be: The idea is not good
or feasible (for whatever reason — usually lack of budget). But assuming the diagnosis is accurate, it
can be a communication issue: The source has not formulated the problem statement, or failed to
convey it in a message appropriate for the target audience. They just didn’t get across with their
message. Or it can be a personal issue: if you know a person or situation very well, you can become
careless and stop listening to each other’s opinions. ("familiarity breeds contempt.") Or the
manager and architect simply don’t get along well on a personal basis.
You in your role as a (external) reviewer have the opportunity to act as an opinion amplifier (if you
agree with your source). As a "neutral observer," your perspective carries more weight than
divisions that have developed over time between teams or individuals. So be aware of that power
and use it wisely.
May I quote your statement?
You have to establish trust during your interview. Ensure (and keep your promise) that everything
said during the interview remains confidential in the sense that the source is not revealed.
On the other hand, quoting someone directly in your report is a very powerful tool. It makes the
result more lively (especially during a presentation) and underlines messages and conclusions. On
top, it gives positive credits to the source of a statement for revealing an issue or making a
suggestion for improvement.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 367
The right quote can inspire people to change their ways.
— Zig Ziglar, author
To reiterate: Never ever break your promise and relationship of trust to quote someone without
their explicit agreement. So if you want to quote some of your interview partners, ask specifically if
you are allowed to do so. If she or he disagrees, refrain from quoting. Additionally, take care to
protect your sources in all the result documents that you will hand over. Never disclose your
sources — not even your sponsors. So take care to anonymize everything when preparing the
result!
Technology is not everything: keep a holistic view!
As a software engineer or architect you tend to look for issues within the technology stack and
code. But technology is not the core of every problem. More than once people, culture and
processes (social-cultural/social-technical) are at least part of an issue or even the root cause.
Therefore, architecture reviews need to be a "breadth-first search": Don’t just look at code-loving
metrics. An architecture review is much more than just starting Sonarqube and looking at the
numbers. That’s also why interviews with real human beings are so important. They give you a
broader view on what’s going on.
There is a lot more to think of: Is testing done correctly (also in the sense of processes, point in time
and team setup)? Is the infrastructure adequate (for development, staging, production)? Are the
technologies used correctly, e.g. is Kafka (not) abused as Message Queue? Is the documentation
adequate? What does the process model for development look like? Does it suit the project? Is it
working and followed? Do the teams have the right skills and seniority level? As it’s a people
business: Are there people that cannot work with each other because they don’t like each other?
How much you cover these aspects depends largely on the scope and objectives you got for the
review. Still, always keep an eye open for all these topics, and keep in mind that one of these might
be the true root cause of problems you see. Include them if you stumble upon one of these in your
review documentation, even if it was not in your explicit scope. Whenever you identify something
worth including in your list of findings: include it in order not to loose it.
Separate problem identification from finding solutions
As engineers we are trained to think analytically and to immediately come up with solutions to
problems we encounter. This sections tries to convince you of exactly the opposite: Postpone the
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 368
search for a solution to a later point in time. Separate the review (= identification of issues) from
solving them or even thinking about potential solutions.
1 2
Text is not SVG - cannot display
Why? Because coming up with good solutions to a problem takes time. During a review you will
have only a limited amount of time. For the point of view of the team that get’s a review, it is much
more valuable that you invest that time into getting an unobstructed, external problem view. This is
why they need the external perspective in the first place: To get a fresh view on their problems. The
additional benefit is that if you wait with the discussion on possible solutions until you have a full
view of the findings, you can think holistically about the issues instead of finding a local
optimization for each issue individually.
Once you have a list of issues, ususally even a resident project team can workshop on possible
solutions. There, the handicap of being an "insider" is not so big as with the identification of issues
itself.
Certainly, if you have enough time and budget, the external reviewer(s) can and should come up
with solution ideas during their work. But do that in a completely separate step after having
identified and enumerated the issues first.
When the real world is very vague — assumptions become your friend
In an ideal world you would always have a fixed scope and predefined, documented goals and
architecture objectives for your review. However the real world is not always that nice to you. You
might encounter a setup for your review like: "We want to take over the system from an
competitor. Can it be developed further or does it have to go?" with no further documentation on
architecture objectives.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 369
Then it’s time to get creative and start working with assumptions where necessary. If you don’t
have anything else, derive architectural goals/principles/quality criteria from the context. Conduct
interviews if possible and get as much input from the conversations as possible.
Hints how to identify architecture objectives
1. In the chapter architectural objectives you can find a simple workshop format
that could help you to identify your objectives. This should be helpful in this
 situation as well.
2. [Toth2024] describes for LASR a quite simple technique how to derive quality
characteristics if they have not been documented upfront. Their homepage
provides a free card deck for that. (unfortunately everything concerning LASR
is in German only)
Recording of findings
The most important part of the review is documenting your findings. How you do that is of lesser
importance — be it in Excel, JIRA, a SharePoint document, or in a database. Consider using a
platform for collaborative editing if you work with more than one reviewer (and, as we stated
above, you definitely should!). Whatever you prefer, use a coherent structure. This allows looking
up information later, be it when you compile your result presentation, or when you just ask
yourself one month later where you got that particular piece of information from.
Standard data you should record
The following example shows recommended apects that should be recorded during an evaluation.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 370
Example 31. Example for a discovered topic during a review
ID Title Description Impact Suggestion Source
of the finding assessment for

/remediation
(optional)

gging of the
platform
ID
Give each finding a unique identifier, so that you can reference them in your documentation. It
also allows you to easily crossreference from one finding to another. A simple number sequence
already suffices. It does not even need to be without gaps.
Title
Give your identified issue a brief an concise title to describe and summarize it. That helps people
reference it when they talk about it, and also forces you as author to think about "what is this
about?" in a crisp way — this is a kind of built-in quality control.
If you cannot find a succinct caption, it may be that you mangled two different problems
together, or that the issue is just inherently complex. If you come to the conclusion that you
mixed up two problems into one, consequently split your finding into 2 separate items. Maybe
you haven’t really understood the issue well enough as you cannot verbalize it in a simple way?
So first take a few extra seconds to check that before coming to the final conclusion that "it’s
very complicated and I can’t simplify further".
Description of the findings
The core of your record. It’s about: "what did you find?"
Capture here whatever you think is helpful. Don’t forget that visualizations like a screenshot or a
drawing, as well as hyperlinks (you don’t have to invent or redraw everything during your
review — reuse what you can!) are very helpful. It also does not need to be very nice, also a
photo of whiteboard might suffice at this stage.
Keep also your review setup in mind: Will you be the only one who will read this, or are you
working on the review together? Are you going to be on vacation and someone else takes over?
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 371
What if you become ill? As with good code, record the issues in such a way that someone else
from your peers will be able to understand them and pick up where you left off! It’s not about
readability for a manager oder a non-architect, it’s about your fellow reviewers. Translating the
findings for other audiences comes later when preparing the final result document or
presentation.
Impact assessment
This section is not easy to tell apart from the description. Here the focus is on a concise
description of the answer to the question: "what is the problem due to the identified issue?". In
other words give an answer what happens or will happen due to the identified finding.
Suggestion for improvement/remediation
Provide ideas or suggestions what need to be done to overcome the issue. This section is
optional. It’s recommended first to just identify and record all issues. Later, you can think of
possible solutions. Even if you run out of time it is more valuable to uncover all relevant issues
(cf. Separate problem identification from finding solutions).
However, do not hesitate to jot down good ideas you have right on the spot, or obtained from
your interview partners! You can and should evaluate later if they are the right ones or not.
Source
Record the source that informed you of the issue. It can be from various kind. Maybe a code
checker like "SonarQube", maybe you found something in a document you have read or during
an interview with a person. Why is that so important? Because you might reread your notes an
come up with additional questions or because you want to quote something in your result
artifact and you need the "source".
Please be aware when your source is a person: Maybe that person told you something under the
cover of confidentiality. (see more about sources and quoting in May I quote your statement?)+
Also think about the storage where you put that information on. It should not be possible later
on to revert to a earlier version from the file history to "de-anonymize" your data! So it might be
a good idea to store your raw data separate form the final result.
additional data you might want to record
The list of possible aspects you can document during a review is infinitely long. They can and will
also vary by the concrete situation you are in and the scope of your work. Subsequently, some
additional aspects you might want to record:
Aspect Area
If your review scope is broad like in large software systems or landscapes, you might want to
further sub-categorize your issues. Which categories you use is up to you and your specific scope
of the review. E.g. it could be by technical building blocks like "SAP, Java Server, Java Frontend,
Kafka-Streaming, …" or by process steps "sales, inventory management, invoicing, document
handling, …" or whatever else suits you best.
Severity of the impact
Maybe you want assign a label expressing severity of impact of that specific issue, like
"threatening, high, low, …". You could rate/categorize it yourself during the review, or ask your
interview partners or other project members about their assessment.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 372
Preparation of results
Every good story comes to an end. And with every good story the end carries much weight in the
conclusion of your readers — or here better said: stakeholders — if it was worth the effort or not.
That should mean especially: don’t underestimate the importance (and the necessary effort) of a
good result preparation and presentation. Take your time to be thorough and deliver a high-quality
report.
"Good result" does not not mean you should hide or sugarcoat your findings. Always keep to the
facts — even if they might be unpleasant or surprising or both. Include everything you deem
important. Especially, don’t omit findings to make the story "more pleasing".
Usually your review sponsor(s) want a presentation and/or a result document. As always, it is
important to think what your target audience understands or not. Are you using an appropriate
level of language and depth so that your readers can understand your points? Maybe you might
need more than one version of your slidedeck if you present your results to different target groups,
e.g. the management vs. the project’s architects. In the same vein, a result document should include
both a management summary and a deep dive.
When preparing or giving a presentation, refrain from trying to squeeze all your 786 individual
findings into it. It’s natural that we want to show our entire work, but have mercy with your
audience. They will die of Powerpoint-poisoning if you do that. You will need to summarize your
findings and compile the result into a manageable, sensible amount of slides. Put the additional
details in a separate documentation (or backup slides) instead, which you give to your sponsors so
that they have all the details to indulge in afterwards.
Finally, never forget about one very important audience: the developers of the project you
reviewed. In typical Capgemini setup they are from "our team". An architecture review should not
be a McKinsey style external consultation. So, if it’s not already planned, convince your sponsors
that the developers (and certainly also other interested project members) should also get access to
the result and/or a result presentation. It’s important that they also get the "diagnosis" for "their"
system as they have essentially built it. They will have to live with any recommendation you made.
That’s why they are very important stake holders as well. Acknowledge their work and motivate
them for the necessary change. Some of them also will have contributed actively to your review
during interviews or by providing artifacts. So it’s only natural that they want to know what was
the outcome.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 373
Methods and tools
The following section summarizes some recommended methods and tools to tackle architecture
reviews. These approaches are very different in how lightweight or heavyweight they are. Thus
they differ much in the necessary time and effort to conduct a review, as well as in the depth of
rules and structures they provide as guardrails.
ATAM (SEI) and ATAM-Template/Matrix (Capgemini)
The Architecture Tradeoff Analysis Method (ATAM) is a structured approach used to evaluate the
trade-offs involved in designing software architectures. It was developed by the Software
Engineering Institute (SEI) at Carnegie Mellon University and helps in understanding the
consequences of architectural decisions in relation to the quality attributes of a system, such as
performance, security, modifiability, and usability. ATAM is designed to identify risks and trade-offs
early in the software development lifecycle, helping stakeholders make informed decisions about
the architecture. More on ATAM can be found in [Clemens2001] and on the SEI pages, cf. links in
Web-Pages.
As the full blown ATAM approach is heavyweight and time consuming, Capgemini came up with a
shorter version which focuses on an expert review that works with interviews. In the center of this
approach is the ATAM-Matrix that focuses on opposing the architecture design decisions (hopefully
documented as Architecture Decsion Records, ADR) to the quality characteristics (aka. "non-
functional requirements, NFR) which are set as mandatory for the software. Then you assess box by
box: Does a design decision positively affect a NFR, does it impede it or is "neutral", i.e. the two are
not related. In the end you get an overall picture that allows you to decide whether and where you
have issues or problems. For more see [ATAMTemplate].
This approach works very good at an very early stage of your system/software, even before you
have code. However, you need to have already an idea about your architecture (decisions).
ARC 360
The "Architecture Review Checklist 360°" (short ARC 360) is a Capgemini service offering for guided
Software Architecture Assessments. Its goal is to assess in a quick and holistic way, whether the
software architecture is a solid foundation to go ahead and to identify weak spots and reveal
practical solutions. It is applicable for all kinds of software: large-scale systems, as well as agile
products, traditional or microservice architecture, etc.
The checklist contains all information and templates for a 1-2 day guided architecture walkthrough
with stakeholders, supported by an Excel checklist. At the end, it provides a 360° view of the most
important architecture topics, utilizing terminology and elements from the arc42 standard.
ARC 360 focuses on the architecture scope itself. It does not cover organizational and human
aspects explicitly.
For more information, including artifacts, see [ARC360].
LASR
LASR (Lightweight Approach for Software Reviews) is a structured approach for lean software
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Architecture Reviews | 374
reviews with low overhead. Activities that are included in many review methods like consolidation
of objectives, consideration of design ideas, identification of risks, etc. are present in the "core
review" of LASR. In addition to the core review, LASR also offers the optional review mode
"Confidence Increase", which offers further methodological possibilities and additional drill downs.
Thus it can improve review results.
The method is a much leaner and pragmatic alternative to ATAM, but also maintaining a goal-
oriented approach where the quality characteristics are in the center at the start. It even offers you
a method what to to if the project you are reviewing does not have documented or established
architectural objectives/goals. It is designed to be feasible with just the involvement of one’s own
team, and it’s even possible to conduct it solo if necessary. Moreover, this method is trimmed on
efficiency and simplicity. It is capable of delivering a preliminary result swiftly, such as within the
span of a couple of a few hours.
More one LASR can be found on the LASR home page or as a podcast on "Software Architektur TV",
see Web-Pages below. The method itself is thouroughly describes in the LASR Book [Toth2024].
Unfortunately the documentation on LASR is entirely in German only.
Capgemini Assets
▪ [ARC360] Architecture Review Checklist for 360° Assessment (formerly known as devonARC) -
The Architecture Review Checklist is a Capgemini service offering for guided Software
Architecture Assessments.
▪ [ATAMTemplate] ATAM Template - (German) a Capgemini specific pragmatic and much less time
consuming approach to evaluate a software according to ATAM ideas. It’s part of the C&CA
Germany Assets collection.
