Test Automation
Developing in an agile way has become normal in our projects. Having a quality assured shippable
product at the end of each sprint can only be achieved with an appropriate amount of automated
tests. As an architect you must ensure that structures for a successful test automation are set up
and constantly improved. This article provides an overview about important design decisions and
some good practices for test automation.
Overview
General rules that support good and maintainable solutions:
• Ensure a clear understanding within the teams who automates what when and how.
• Create and execute tests as close to the commit as possible (fast feedback, clear source of
problem)
• Ensure immediate reaction on "red" (broken) tests to avoid the broken window effect.
• Take continuous improvement seriously (build up monitoring that shows problems, throw away
tests that are not helpful, find ways to avoid flaky tests, …).
• Value maintainability over a quick first success. Tests only needs to be created once, but
maintained over a long time.
Test Levels
Automated tests should exist on different integration levels. For example, devonfw describes four
levels. Often, you only find the three levels Unit, API (or Service) and UI.
A prominent concept how to utilize the integration levels is the test pyramid:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Test Automation | 376
Figure 77. Test pyramid
For microservice architectures you also need to consider the integration between multiple services
within a product and the integration with external systems (System Integration Test - SIT):
Figure 78. Test pyramid for microservices
Test Honeycomb as alternative
In most contexts the test pyramid a good target model for the number of tests per integration level.
For small microservices you can also consider a test honeycomb like proposed by Spotify.
Consumer Driven Contract Test (CDC)
Following the pyramid shape you want to have a very limited number of tests with multiple
services. Additionally, you want to have the possibility to deploy one microservice independent of
each other.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Test Automation | 377
Consumer Driven Contract Tests are the established way to support both objectives. They reduce
the risk of integration problems and can be executed independently. Each consumer of an interface
(synchronous or asynchronous communication) defines its expectations to the provider in form of
a test. Those tests are executed in the provider’s build pipeline. If the provider breaks the contract
with a change, it cannot be released.
Figure 79. Consumer Driven Contract tests
A good introduction can be found here.
What is important for architects?
• Define the integration levels on which there should be automated tests.
• For each integration level you need to mock the parts you do not integrate. Realistic mocks have
a high impact on the value of the tests. So, define rules and tools for mocking on each level.
• Define rules for the test intensity on the different levels:
◦ Define objectives for code and branch coverage for unit tests. For larger applications, unit
tests build the solid foundation of the test pyramid. Additionally, motivate why high code
coverage does not necessarily mean good tests.
◦ Put a focus on black box tests on API level (e.g. REST-API); a good functional coverage will
keep the application changeable, because even after complex refactorings you can ensure
that the application still behaves unchanged to the outside world.
◦ Use as few UI tests as possible. You need them, but limit the number.
◦ You can mix API usage with UI tests. A good practice is to set up preconditions via API and
only use the UI for the core aspect of the test.
• Encourage teams to do test driven development (TDD) on unit test level and behavior driven
development (BDD) for the higher levels. Both techniques have a positive impact on test and
development quality.
Test Automation Architecture
The following generic Test Automation Architecture (gTAA) has been introduced by the
International Software Testing Qualification Board® (ISTQB®):
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Test Automation | 378
Figure 80. Test Automation Architecture
It shows that there is usually not simply one tool to be selected. Instead, there are four layers of test
automation to be considered as well as their integration to Configuration-, Test- and Project-
Management.
In the Test Adaptation Layer you decide how to technically integrate the test driver with the
system under test (SUT) and it’s environment. Tools that fit to your context have a big impact on
speed of automation and maintainability of automated tests.
In the Test Execution Layer the important aspects are Test Logging/ Reporting/ Monitoring. It can
evolve over time, but make sure you start with some support. If you cannot see or analyze
problems in automated tests, automation will slow you down and not provide any benefits.
For the Test Definition Layer the focus is more on business rules and business related changes
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Test Automation | 379
than on the technical solution. The question for the design is how to keep test cases and test data
understandable and maintainable. BDD test scenarios in Gherkin syntax can be a good practice to
keep the test cases readable and the implementation modularized and reusable.
For the Test Generation Layer we usually decide to do Manual Design instead of generating test
cases from models, because the test models for our applications would be too complicated and
time-consuming to be maintained.
An introductory article is Benefits of a Generic Test Automation Architecture.
What is important for architects?
• Don’t only think about green runs, but put effort in ways to analyze the results (Test Logging,
Test Reporting and the connections to Configuration- and Test Management).
• There is not the one tool that fits all. Usually, you will have a mixture of tools (e.g. one
supporting API test, one supporting UI tests, one mocking responses from other systems).
• Reflect the architecture in the test code, e.g. by having a clear Adaptation layer or using the Page
Object Pattern.
Test Types
Orthogonal to the Test Levels (aka Test Stages), there are the different Test Types. Each Test Type is
focused on a particular test objective, typically a quality characteristic like:
• functionality,
• efficiency (load & performance),
• reliability,
• security
• usability.
They are orthogonal to the Test Levels, because each Test Type can be executed on one or multiple
Test Levels.
What is important for architects?
• Functional test automation needs to be started right from the beginning of the project.
• For non-functional test automation you must define the right time when it makes sense to start.
But don’t wait too long, you need the learnings to make tests stable.
• Consider the right Test Level for each test aiming for the lowest possible in the test pyramid.
• There are supporting tools for many aspects of non-functional testing. Investigate before you
build something yourself.
Pipeline integration
General Rules:
• Aim for "Fail Fast". That means as many tests as possible should be automatically executed after
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Test Automation | 380
each commit to show the source of a new problem immediately. Tests are to be excluded from
this rule, if
◦ they are too slow (and this cannot be changed for example by parallelization)
◦ they do not have a clear pass - fail result (an example are performance tests for monitoring
trends without clear boundaries when to fail)
• Ensure that teams immediately fix failures in the pipeline. Otherwise, you will experience the
"Broken Window Effect".
Test Data
The ideal approach would be that each test creates its own data and cleans up afterwards. This
works for unit tests and very simple microservices. For more complex tests and systems creating
the required master data for each test is too complex and too slow. Pragmatic solutions are needed.
Some good practices:
• Use a standard set of master data that is imported before the test run; possible options (can be
combined):
◦ Fill with synthetic data on database level with tools like Liquibase or Flyway
◦ Create automated scripts that create the master data using the application. This should not
necessarily be part of your test suite. The main focus would be the realistic creation of data.
This is only recommended, if your master data functionality is already stable.
◦ Use extracted (anonymized) master data from production or test environment
• Create operational data for each test run:
◦ Create builders that fill structures meaningful defaults, so that each test only needs to
overwrite some relevant fields.
◦ Import the data using the application to make them realistic (API, public interface or
persistence layer, not UI).
• Consider cleaning up the data after each test run (usually on DB level). This makes the tests
truly independent, but might slow down the execution, especially because you cannot run
multiple tests in parallel.
Notable Tools
Some links to tools that have proven very useful in various projects:
• Cucumber: a tool to automate functional tests based on Behavior Driven Development: it allows
specifying and directly automating test scenarios that are understood by developers, testers and
business.
• PACT: a tool to support Consumer Driven Contract tests. If you use it with the Pact Broker, you
get a good overview about the communication between the apps in your product(s). So, it
supports testing as well as architecture management.
• Wiremock: a generic simulator for HTTP-based API. You can simulate any response based on the
request. You can proxy to the rel system, record the traffic and use the recorded messages for
future tests.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Test Automation | 381
• Mr. Checker: an integration of different test tools in the context of devonfw. Covers most aspects
of the test automation architecture for functional tests. Good choice, if you do not have specific
tool requirements and want to have the integration out of the box.
Continuous Quality
The ability to quickly react on new insights or changes in an agile business environment ("business
agility") creates competitive advantages for many companies. This has an impact on how we deliver
software. Delivering fast always implies fast and with appropriate quality. So, automating the
deployment process is a necessary precondition, but not enough. We must establish a way of
working that ensures that the quality status is continuously visible and stays on a desired high level
without major drops ("continuous quality"). This is the environment where teams are really open to
change.
Definition: Continuous Quality
We talk about Continuous Quality, if the product is continuously meeting quality
requirements, which allows to continuously deliver features into production.
In other environments that do not require a short cycle time you can decide to take a more
traditional approach towards quality where you accept the risk of drops in quality during
development. This might save some invests in quality, but you will need to invest in measures to
mitigate the risk that you cannot regain the desired quality in time. Many of the aspects mentioned
below for fulfilling Continuous Quality can be used to risk mitigation in traditional projects and can
add great value there as well.
Typical anti-patterns to Continuous Quality are:
• Waterfall or other projects with test and acceptance phases done after development by
dedicated teams
• A way of working where everyone in the team works on their own feature branch until the end
of the sprint. Then merging results in a drop in quality that is fixed during an end of sprint / end
of release testing phase.
Pillars of Continuous Quality
If you aim for business agility, you need to invest in the following three pillars to enable continuous
quality:
• Technology
• Project Setup
• People
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Continuous Quality | 382
Technology
The most obvious area is ensuring the necessary quality standards through technical solutions. This
allows to gain speed in development while taking appropriate quality into account. The decisive
factor here is that quality checks, deployments and infrastructure are automated as far as possible
and are carried out continuously ("continuous everything"). Typically, the following technical
options should be considered:
• Implement Test Automation for functional and non-functional requirements. Reduce the
necessary manual tests to zero.
• Use of static code analysis (e.g. SonarQube) or linters. These ensure at code level that
previously agreed rules and standards are adhered to. This should include checks for security-
relevant risks (static application security testing SAST). Even architecture guidelines can be
tested with tools like ArchUnit.
• Automatically check open source libraries for weaknesses regularly. Not only direct
dependencies play a role, but also transitive dependencies, which can also contain weak points.
For example, tools like Nexus IQ, OWASP Dependency Check or Clair can be used. In order to
continuously stay on the latest stable versions of the libraries, Renovate Bots can also be used,
which automatically update the respective libraries (using feature branches and pull requests).
• Integrate all quality checks into Continuous Integration pipelines. This ensures that they are
continuously carried out.
• Automate the deployment of the application for all environments. This prevents errors caused
by manual deployment of the application. It does not imply that you must continuously deploy
to production.
• Implement the principle "everything as code" to ensure consistency and maintainability of
your environments and configurations. Especially for applications in the cloud, the
infrastructure should be described by code (IAC). Well-known tools for this are, Terraform or
Helm.
• Make sure your infrastructure code is covered by automated testing. A possible framework for
testing Terraform templates is Terratest.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Continuous Quality | 383
• Chose an architecture style that encourages flexibility and team responsibility:
◦ Microservice architectures ensure small units with clear owners.
◦ Modulithic architectures can also be purposeful, if clear delimitable areas of responsibility
can be defined and if pipeline runtimes can be kept short enough.
• Use trunk-based development or short living feature branches. This reduces the risk of
errors found only late when integrating large changes of the code.
• Use feature flags in the context of Continuous Delivery / Deployment. These allow unfinished
or not yet accepted features to be deactivated on test or production environments, so that they
are not visible to the user. In public cloud environments, there are services available that offer
simple management of feature flags e.g. Azure App Configuration. The use of feature flags also
offers the advantage that new features can only be made available to limited user groups at first
and can therefore be tested live. In conjunction with a clean log, errors can be detected early on,
even if the feature is not yet fully live. It is important to Remove feature flags promptly once
the feature is fully live and no bugs are observed. Otherwise, you will end up having more and
more possible combinations that have to be tested and edge cases have to be taken into account.
Project setup / Organizational
In addition to the technological solutions, the organizational setup can foster Continuous Quality or
make it impossible for teams to get there. The most important factors are knowledge and
responsibility. Teams need the possibility and responsibility to decide about quality measures. And
they need the knowledge to take the right decisions.
• For knowledge of business and operational requirements, keep the distance to business and
operations small. Live BizDevSecOps:
◦ Establish direct communication with business and if possible with end users.
◦ Let developers take part in story writing, e.g. "Pair story writing".
◦ Visualize business goals, functional processes and quality goals.
◦ Make sure the team understands the client’s quality goals, how they are measured and how
they can achieve them. Insist that they focus on the quality goals itself, not on the metrics,
which are "just" indicators. Do not allow KPI-tweaking. Throw away work that only tweaks
KPIs, but does not deliver value (but increase maintenance cost), e.g. unit tests without
proper assertions.
◦ Build up an “agile” knowledge management, i.e. let it grow over time, continuously check
what you need and what you no longer need.
◦ Repeat important knowledge and rules. Saying them just once during onboarding is usually
not enough.
◦ Keep time between development and release as short as possible, so that the team directly
sees the connection to their work, if something goes wrong.
◦ Let teams take part in operations and support, e.g. by having a rotating operator of the
week.
◦ Make sure production monitoring is established and used by the team. Test the production
monitoring before the first go live in test environments.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Continuous Quality | 384
• Give the teams the full end-to-end responsibility for their product.
◦ The team setup must be cross-functional, so that all aspects can be covered within the teams.
This especially includes knowledge about testing, environments and automation.
◦ The teams must have the freedom to decide about necessary quality improvements, e.g.
removing technical debt or doing changes in the build pipeline. A fixed percentage for such
tasks can help to not always give features a higher priority and foster continuous
improvement. External approvals for changes should be prevented.
◦ Establish a climate of psychological safety. It must be allowed to make mistakes and
mandatory to learn from them. Make sure that learnings are actively shared within the
team and with other teams.
• Having full responsibility for every aspect of BizDevSecOps can be overwhelming, especially for
junior teams handling new technologies. So, implement mechanisms to avoid mental overload:
◦ Make sure there are people in the team who can cover topics that are not typical developer-
know-how (e.g. test, CI/CD, production monitoring). No single person can do everything in
BizDevSecOps, a cross-functional team with diverse knowledge is needed.
◦ Provide access to experts, e.g. for setting up load or security testing.
◦ Set-up enabling / support teams in areas where teams wish external help, e.g. a team that
organizes integrated tests with external systems. Make sure that the support teams are good
enough that the teams use them voluntarily.
◦ Encourage teams to start with best practices from other projects / teams and adapt later, e.g.
templates for an initial CI/CD pipeline, coding guidelines like devonfw. It is important that
this is not a top-down decision, but is made by the team.
People
Organization and processes cannot cover every possible aspect. It needs people who fill the
processes with life; people who really care and act when there are problems. Without constant
quality awareness even a good initial setup will not lead to good quality.
Even though this point sounds obvious and easy to implement, it can be very challenging. Here are
some typical points that help everyone in the team to develop a quality mindset:
• It’s part of the role of architects and developers to fight for actions to keep up the quality in
the long run. Fighting for time and budget is the EM’s or the Product Owner’s task. Good
compromises can only be reached, if everyone stands up for their respective area.
• Say "NO" when quality measures are regularly overruled by new or further development. It’s
OK to be pragmatic once, but the risk is high that it becomes a pattern. When you object, make
sure to explain it properly to the client and the rest of team. It must be clear that it is not about
blindly following processes, but that quality must be continuously worked on to ensure agility
in the long run.
• Act as a role model for quality yourself. Others will follow. Some examples are:
◦ Provide quick feedback on good and bad quality.
◦ Share your own mistakes and learnings and thus establish an open failure culture.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Continuous Quality | 385
◦ Reward and promote critical questions or indications of poor quality. It is important that
people are encouraged to be critical and that problems are not suppressed, but solved.
• Let essential quality processes become the DNA of your team:
◦ Quality issues must be understood as build breakers with all the consequences. This means
that the software cannot be delivered without adhering to the quality standards. Fixing
broken builds therefore always has the highest priority, especially before the further
development of new features.
◦ Adapt the rule set that makes a build fail if necessary. It is important to discard rules that do
not require a direct response. These checks can run downstream or nightly, for example.
◦ Propagate and live the "Boy Scout Rule". The rule states that code that is adapted is left
cleaner than it was before. If extensive refactorings are necessary, these can be taken into
account as part of the complexity of stories or implemented separately as a technical task in
the sprint. Ideally, the team automatically identifies technical debt and takes appropriate
steps to reduce it.
◦ Having a dedicated capacity for technical tasks e.g. refactorings in every sprint can help to
keep the technical debt low continuously.
Timeline for enabling Continuous Quality
To achieve the target of Continuous Quality in a new project, quality needs to be considered from
day one. This shapes the mindset of the team and prevents that technical debt are piled up in an
uncontrolled manner. However, this does not mean that all the points mentioned above need to be
implemented from the start. Focus and MVP-thinking is needed to get the best effect:
• Lay the core technical foundations in sprint 0.
• Shape the organizational foundations in the first sprints.
• Continuously improve after MVP based on the foundations you have laid in the previous
phases.
Setup minimum viable quality in sprint 0
We refer to the initial setup of a project as Sprint 0, in which the technical foundations of the
project are laid before the actual feature development can start. A sprint 0 should solely enable
teams to fulfil the DoD for features from sprint 1 on. It should not be used for a big upfront design.
Since you cannot cover every quality aspect from the beginning, prioritize and start with minimum
viable quality measures:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Continuous Quality | 386
• In sprint 0, start with a small selected team to lay the foundation, so that the rest of the team can
produce good quality from day one. This includes:
◦ a minimum viable business and technical architecture
◦ an initialized sprint backlog
◦ code examples (including examples for automated functional tests)
• Focus on “hard to catch up” quality measures (i.e. quality standards that would constantly
add high technical debt for each feature, if not followed right away), at least:
◦ linter and basic code checks
◦ automated functional tests
• Evaluate if there are non-functional requirements that need to be addressed right from day one.
Often they can be delayed until later, but this should be a conscious decision.
• Set up an MVP pipeline (build, test, deploy) and an initial development environment. Use
examples or templates from other projects to get started fast. You can improve later.
• Start monitoring to prepare a clean log:
◦ Set up a first initial monitoring to be extended to production monitoring
◦ Use it on development environments to detect errors and problems early. Starting with a
monitoring as early as possible helps to get a clean log.
Grow towards continuous quality from sprint 1 to MVP
After the technical foundations have been established in the first phase, it is necessary to establish
organizational foundations. Build an organization that promotes the achievement of the desired
quality. Within this organization, the initial quality measures will grow toward continuous quality
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Continuous Quality | 387
• Show a quality mindset:
◦ Say "NO" when overruling quality checks is done. It is better not to deliver features at the
beginning than to establish a mindset that quality rules are optional.
◦ Enforce immediate reactions on breaking builds.
◦ Establish an environment of psychological safety.
• Collaborate closely through BizDevOps. Establish low distance between business and
development (including test) and operations.
• Setup an enabling organization:
◦ Make sure there are people in the team who can cover topics that are not typical developer-
know-how (e.g. test, CI/CD, production monitoring).
◦ Make sure you have enablers, experts or support teams for areas of missing or highly
specific knowledge.
• Ensure transparency:
◦ Visualize project goals, status and quality-related KPIs, so they can be easily viewed at any
time.
◦ Transparency applies within the team and towards stakeholders.
• Go live early. Find and define the smallest MVP scope and go live as soon as possible. The
smaller the releases, the lower the risk. The earlier you go live, the more time you have for real-
life-learnings.
Improve continuously after MVP
Quality is a continuous effort and measures should be reviewed regularly. This means that after the
MVP is in production, there must be continuous adjustments and optimizations in terms of quality
assurance. This phase benefits from the fact that the quality mindset is already present in the team,
technical foundations have been laid, and technical debt has only accumulated to a small extent.
Improvements can either be sprinkled in by the architect, but ideally should be driven and
scheduled independently by a self-organized team.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Continuous Quality | 388
• Close test gaps:
◦ Identify gaps in testing and close them with additional automated testing as needed e.g.
Consumer Driven Contract Tests, Security Tests, Chaos Monkeys, …
◦ Consider tests in production where it makes sense.
• Automate everything:
◦ Consequently, remove manual steps for recurring tasks. An increasing degree of automation
can avoid errors in manual steps, for example during deployment.
◦ Renovate bots that continuously analyze possible version updates and critical
vulnerabilities in dependent packages.
• Constantly reduce technical debt:
◦ Plan explicitly, e.g. fix percentage of capacity for the team to invest in every sprint.
◦ Keep up and live the "Boy Scout Rule" especially when the project team restructures or
members change.
◦ Visualize status of quality KPIs for example through SonarCube or test management plugins
for Jira such as Xray.
• Live continuous improvement:
◦ Keep up learning culture, psychological safety, close collaboration and feedback between
BizDevOps.
◦ Explicitly distribute and build up knowledge in the team.
Steps towards continuous quality in existing projects
If you haven’t started like described above, you might desire a transformation towards continuous
quality.
Understand initial situation and objectives
There is no simple standard transformation path, because the situation in projects can be very
different. So naturally, the journey starts with understanding:
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Continuous Quality | 389
• What objectives are to be reached by moving towards continuous quality? Typically, the goals
are faster deliveries with same or increased quality. But there may be more like lowering
attrition in the development team.
• What existing quality measures / processes are in place?
• How is the effect (or missing effect) of the existing quality measures / processes?
Often teams want to move towards continuous quality, because they face growing quality
problems:
• They spend more and more effort on testing and fixing bugs.
• As a result the time for features is decreasing.
• Fewer features means no space for investment in long-term quality improvements.
• Poor quality causes team members to leave the project. Loosing their knowledge makes it even
harder to keep quality up.
This is a vicious circle. Your technical debt grow constantly, reducing your ability to deliver
features further and further. If this is your initial situation, there are some hard truths:
• The shift to better quality will further reduce the speed in the short term.
• A deep mindset shift is required of everyone in the project; this is a long way.
• Delaying the shift will only make it harder.
Typical starting points
• Reduce distance between Biz – Dev – Ops to increase knowledge and feeling of responsibility:
◦ Enforce ownership for code on team level.
◦ Make developers fix their own bugs.
◦ Reduce time to production (e.g. reduce release scope, make testing part of development,
automate builds and deployments).
◦ Let teams directly talk to business, end-users and operations.
◦ Build cross-functional teams.
◦ Rotate support work within the teams.
◦ Ensure access to production monitoring.
• Enable a mindset shift. People don’t easily change established ways of working and thinking,
even if they are not satisfied with the status quo. They need help:
◦ Bring in people from the outside with the mandate to fight for quality (and the right to say
"No").
◦ Define the future quality measures together with the teams.
◦ Visualize the status and start (real) continuous improvement within the teams.
◦ Coaching is usually needed for a long time so that people don’t fall back into old patterns.
◦ Hand over decisions about quality to the teams; otherwise, nothing will change.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Continuous Quality | 390
◦ Try to grow quality awareness and thus their actions from the teams. It works best when the
willingness to improve quality comes directly from the teams and not top-down. The
reduced distance can help here in many aspects e.g. fix your own bugs.
• Close the test automation gap. A reliable test automation is a necessary foundation for
continuous quality, and it supports the safe removal of technical debt. In most contexts without
continuous quality, it is neglected. As it needs time and effort to reach a reliable and
maintainable state, it is usually one of the first topics to approach:
◦ Map what you have (test pyramid, bugs, technical debt).
◦ Start where it hurts most.
◦ Build up tests in a sustainable way allowing a learning curve within the teams. Letting some
externals implement a huge number of tests to get started is an anti-pattern.
◦ Don’t hesitate to throw away old tests that do not help or documentation that is outdated
and cannot be used anymore.
◦ Automate the build process (if not fully done); virtualize build servers if infrastructure is the
limitation.
◦ Increase team awareness of why automated testing is necessary. Simply meeting KPIs is not
the goal. It is about testing known cases and gaining confidence in the quality of the
application.
Key learnings
• Start on day 1 with quality measures, but don’t try to do everything at once. Start small and
focus on things that are hard to catch up.
• Don’t stop. Continuous quality needs continuous improvements.
• Don’t focus on technology only. They right project setup and people who can fill it are necessary
as well.
• It’s not only for continuous. The described measures are helpful even if you don’t need full
Continuous Quality because Business Agility plays a subordinate role in your context. Use this
article as a toolbox to reduce your risk.
Notable Tools
• Quality Toolbox
Capgemini Assets
• Quality in C&CA Projects (Slides)
• ProductionLine includes OWASP Dependency Checker
• Solicitor
Further information
• OOP 2023 Talk - Continuous Quality (Video)
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Continuous Quality | 391
Living Documentation
Living Documentation is a practice of continuously generating, maintaining, and updating software
documentation. During the software development process, various artifacts are created, such as
source code, configuration files, and test scenarios. This practice focuses on creating and
maintaining documentation sourced from these artifacts, resulting in automatically generated
documentation that is up-to-date. That being said, it is important to note that functional
documentation of software as-is is one area that can significantly profit from implementing the
Living Documentation practice.
Besides Living Documentation, we recognize that other types of documentation exist, which are not
sourced directly from software artifacts. They are widely used, but they are not the focus of this
article. Still, even such documentation can become Living Documentation. One area that is usually
not seen as determined by software is the structure of teams, but later we will see how even such
documentation, far removed from software, may profit from the shift to being a software artifact.
Pillars of Living Documentation
Living Documentation is characterized by the following:
• Automation: Removing repetitiveness from documentation creation.
• Recency: Ensuring documentation stays up-to-date with system changes.
• Completeness: Using system artifacts as documentation to achieve completeness.
• Integration with development: Integrating documentation with development tools to maintain
accuracy.
• Trueness: Documenting the current state of the system, without including unimplemented
features.
Living Documentation differs from regular documentation practices in its focus on the
collaboration between business and development teams, its integration with the development
processes, and its minimal overhead during development. It is meant to supplement existing
documentation practices in specific areas.
In the next subsections, we will discuss the attributes named above in more detail.
Automation
Repetitive work is one of the toils in the software development process. Creating documentation for
recently developed features involves repetitive, mundane work, that demands significant expertise
and effort. As a result, documentation creation and maintenance is often avoided.
Automation should be used to remove the repetitiveness in documentation creation. The
documentation should be maintained continously in the spirit of continuous quality.
Recency
While automation addresses the process of creating documentation itself, there is another source of
work that affects the created documentation throughout its lifecycle. After creation, documentation
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Living Documentation | 392
can quickly lose its connection to the system’s current state. Traditionally, changes in the system
give no clue about related changes, which should be made to the existing body of documentation.
Over time, the documentation becomes outdated and inaccurate. Once in such state, isolated
pockets of incorrect documentation can undermine the validity of the entire body of work, causing
the entire documentation to be perceived as less valuable. These facts are stated at length in the
section about managing documentation.
Achieving high levels of recency is one of the outcomes of applying Living Documentation as a
practice. Living Documentation is characterized by the tight coupling of changes in the system with
changes in the documentation. Changes in the system act as a source of changes to the
documentation, realized with the help of automated solutions. For example, regenerating your
documentation from software artifacts in your automated pipeline whenever a change occurs
ensures recency.
Completeness
It’s impractical to document every aspect of the system from all possible viewpoints. Trying to
cover everything leads to redundant information, making the documentation hard to understand.
One approach to tackling this is stating certain system artifacts to be part of the documentation,
thus indirectly achieving completeness without having to restate the documentation in natural
language.
Usage of system artifacts as documentation lies at the heart of Living Documentation. Instead of
restating system components, Living Documentation relies on the automated transformation of
artifacts into a format suitable for documentation. During this transformation, the artifacts can be
enriched with context and references and combined with other sources of information to achieve
completeness and to make the result usable.
One example is the use of acceptance tests as documentation of the functionality of the system.
While having acceptance tests alone would not be considered Living Documentation, transforming
the tests into a readable format, serving them in an automated fashion to the intended audiences,
and enriching it with appropriate context would.
Integration with development
Where documentation is created, where it is consumed and where the documented software
system is developed are usually three different locations, implemented with different tools. This
makes it difficult to create a sustainable connection between the body of documentation and the
ongoing changes to the software being developed. As a result, the documentation can become
outdated, stale and incorrect. Documentation created separately from where software changes are
made, may be suitable for some aspects of the documentation. However, that is not the case for
those areas where the rate of change in the software is high.
In order for documentation to be properly maintained for software with high rate of change, the
process of documentation has to be highly integrated with the process of development. This means
that sources of documentation are integrated into tools used for development and can be packaged
as single unit of change together with the changes to the system. It is important to reiterate that
Living Documentation is scoped to documenting the functionality of the system at the interface
between business and development.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Living Documentation | 393
Trueness
It’s common for documentation to be created before the actual implementation. This approach is
suitable in many cases. Drafting proposals, creating work packages for development, documenting
thought-experiments and ADRs (Architecture Decision Records) are all valid candidates for creation
of documentation up-front. This does not hold true for documenting the functioning of the system
as it is currently implemented.
Creating functional documentation of the system before it is developed risks documenting aspects
that may later get omitted from implementation or overly focusing on aspects of minor importance.
On the other hand, documenting after development risks omission of important aspects and
redundancy.
Goal of Living Documentation is to always be sourced from the current state of developed artifacts
representing the current state of the system as is. Living Document should include all known
information about functioning of the current version of the system and is not allowed to include
information about those functionalities not yet implemented.
Functionality that is currently undergoing implementation but is not yet completed may be
included in Living Documentation. The generated documentation must clearly indicate that this
included functionality is incomplete and is still undergoing implementation.
Relationship between Living Documentation and other documentation
artifacts
Living Documentation differs from other documentation practices in the following aspects:
• Instead of focusing on a single audience, it focuses on collaboration between business and
development teams.
• It does not have a single abstraction level. It does not focus purely on functionality, technical
implementation, process of gathering requirements or any other single thing. Rather, it
encompasses all these elements in one loop, where changes in the system flow back to the
intended audiences as well-formatted documentation.
• It maintains the connection between changes in developed software and required
documentation.
In conclusion, Living Documentation may be superior in specific areas where collaboration
between business and development is of importance and the rate of change is high. You should not
expect to apply Living Documentation to all of your documentation, as other practices may be
better suited for areas such as planning and high-level architecture. Other documentation practices
are described at length in the section on documentation.
Relationship with Arc42
Arc42 defines and standardizes the structure of documentation but has little to say about how the
documentation gets created. Living Documentation is mostly concerned with practices of creating
the documentation, and much less with the location where it is stored.
Depending on the type of artifact produced by Living Documentation, it may find a suitable place in
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Living Documentation | 394
different sections of Arc42 structure. Here are some examples of potentially generated artifacts and
where they might be properly located in Arc42:
• Documentation of software functionality generated from tests may properly be located in the
runtime view.
• Documentation of APIs automatically generated from OpenAPI documents may be properly
located in the context and scope view.
It is up to you to evaluate where the created documentation is finally stored.
Examples of Living Documentation
By now, you likely have a rough picture of what Living Documentation may be, but let us sharpen
the image with a few examples. In this section, we will explore a couple of areas where Living
Documentation can be employed to reduce the burden of documentation.
Functional documentation with behavior-driven development (BDD)
With Living Documentation, tests and documentation go hand in hand, especially for systems
directly engaging with users. When creating systems directly operated by users, it is often desirable
to ensure that only functionality intended to be changed gets changed, while the rest remains
functional without deviations. Here, behavior-driven development (BDD) enables us to specify the
systems expected behavior and to ensure it remains the same across deployments.
If we decide to use Cucumber and Gherkin syntax to specify our tests, then these artifacts can be
used as functional documentation of the application. Gherkin is a natural-language syntax
accessible to all involved team members. During execution of Cucumber tests, reports can be
generated indicating which functionality still works as expected and which functionality may be
broken. These reports and Gherkin scenarios can be used as inputs for tools that properly format
them and automatically upload them to a destination where documentation resides (e.g.,
Confluence).
With proper behavior-driven development, functional changes should be reflected in the tests and
thus automatically reflected in the documentation, closing the cycle and achieving all attributes
named above.
Functional and technical dependencies across teams
Our large projects often involve more than a handful of teams, dozens of software components and
not rarely many dozens of team members. With time, some of the following things can become
hard to track:
• Who belongs to which team?
• Which software component belongs to which team?
• Which specific roles are assumed by specific team members of each team?
The management of this information can often be offloaded to the teams themselves. However, this
comes with the drawback that such documentation then becomes distributed and scattered across
teams in their own Confluence pages, Github repositories or whatever tools you are using.
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Living Documentation | 395
The solution is to define a schema for a document through which each team can declare this
information for themselves. For example, a simple YAML file containing:
• Name of the team
• List of team members
• Role of each team member
Each team would write such a document and check it into one of their repositories into a file with a
predefined name. Each team is now only responsible for being aware of their structure and
maintaining it properly in the checked-in document.
A central tool would periodically run, checking out all repositories belonging to all teams and
collecting the information from their declaration files. Once all the information is gathered, the
central tool can generate documentation pages containing the team structures.
Note that in this example, there must be a team who is in charge of managing the infrastructure for
all teams. This team would be in charge of developing and maintaining such central tools for
collecting data from repositories and generating the documentation.
Notable tools
Notable tools which help with generating Living Documentation from your project artifacts:
• Cukedoctor for Cucumber is a tool which can help you in formatting your scenarios written in
Gherkin into pretty printed PDF/Asciidoc/HTML documents. Cukedoctor consumes the reports
generated by Cucumber during execution and produces informative reports.
• Maven Plugin for publishing documentation sites to Confluence helps you to bridge the last step
in creation of documentation. This tool can publish entire folder trees as matching tree of pages
in Confluence and can help you automate the distribution of your documentation.
Capgemini Assets
• BDD Living Documentation Generator is our internal tool for converting Cucumber
specification files written in Gherkin into Confluence-compatible Markdown and publishing the
created outputs to your Confluence.
• RealWorld Example implementation of Living Documentation with Confluence, Cucumber,
Cypress, Angular and Spring in Java. This resource is an exhaustive example for implementing
the Living Documentation practice as described in the Functional documentation with
behavior-driven development (BDD) above. It comes with a real world software that is
behavior-tested by Cypress/Cucumber/Gherkin and using the BDD Living Documentation
generator (mentioned above) in combination with Cukedoctor to publish the results to
Confluence.
Further information
• Living Documentation: Continuous Knowledge Sharing by Design, First Edition
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Living Documentation | 396
Pathways
The purpose of the SEAGuide is to be usable in different contexts:
• during the green-field design of a new system
◦ Starting a new project
• to understand the reasoning behind the design of existing systems and to evaluate their quality
• for solutionings during bids
• …
One navigation and structure for all purposes will not be achievable. Hence we will provide you
here with different pathways on how to navigate through the guide depending on your context.
Transitioning from the old guide
For some, the former Architecture Guidelines for Application Design might still be the mental frame
in which they design software. This pathway enables you to find the former, now updated content
here in this new guide. It can serve as a kick-start to understand the new structure and to directly
see what has changed. In particular you can see possible other options for each design step being
taken.
Other than the former Architecture Guide, the SEAGuide focusses on the product only and - by that
- supports a "well-designed" product. Design as task of a delivery model is explicitly carved out, as
there is a big variety and specific customer needs on a well fitting approaches (e.g. Scrum, SAFe,
Waterfall, other delivery models).
1. Architecture Objectives: still valid today, mostly only wording added
2. Architectural principles: still valid today, mostly only wording added and enriched with trade-
off analysis
3. Modular monolith pattern: the core of the old guide was to design software in components
(Component-based architecture). Nowadays this is more commonly referred to as Modular
monolith or Modulith. The new guide contains the essence of that pattern and the idea behind
it, but does not take over the old, rather formal construction model. The pattern is also only
described on a macro architectural level, in that it does not go into the structure of each
component
4. Layered architecture: the old common structure for all application components is described
here in a much more concise way and aligned with the devonfw conventions. In particular the
very formal application component definition is not reused. Also component types are not taken
over, but they are instead related to Strategic Domain Driven design providing a similar more
lightweight concept
5. Interface design: the general definitions and rules regarding the interface design are now
presented as a crosscutting-concept, since it does not relate to only interfaces between
components. The latter content is part of the Modular monolith pattern description
6. Error handling, Data modeling: is taken over as-is with minor rework, broader rework planned
Copyright © 2025 Capgemini Transitioning from the old guide | 397
7. Monitoring: is no described more concisely and with reference to some up-to-date patterns
8. Historzation: has been described on a more abstract level trying to focus on the important
conceptual topics and options you should know
In the future we plan to also migrate some of the other cross-functional topics such as Transactions,
Data Types and Security.
Stay tuned!
